# 三洞群仙录

**The Record of the Immortals of the Three Caverns**

**Title:** *The Record of the Immortals of the Three Caverns* (*Sandong Qunxian Lu*). Twenty fascicles. Compiled by Chen Baoguang of the Song dynasty. Base text from *The Orthodox Daoist Canon* (*Zhengtong Daozang*), Section Zhengyi I. Collated with the *Chongkan Daozang Jiyao* edition.

**Preface to *The Record of the Immortals of the Three Caverns***
Compiled by Daoist Chen Baoguang of the Orthodox Unity Tradition

To dig into the earth in search of a spring—even the most foolish know it may be found. Yet to refine the body and attain immortality—even the wise cannot firmly believe. Thus do the immortals manifest their traces to enlighten the world, teaching the refinement of essence and preservation of truth, the safeguarding of life and nurturing of spirit, that one may transcend the world, escape the dust of clamor, rise above the filth of the mundane, and roam beyond the Eight Extremities. Their intent to benefit beings and aid humanity is vast indeed.

Yet immortals and mortals differ in nature, their aspirations and impurities distinct. When a person is born, they rely on form to house the spirit and on external things to sustain life. But in their folly, they later serve the form at the expense of the spirit, pursue things to the loss of their true nature, passing from youth to old age, from old age to decrepitude, from decrepitude to death. Flesh returns to earth, form and spirit part ways.

The immortal, however, nurtures form to preserve life. Their qi is concentrated, their will unified. They do not burden their heart with likes and dislikes, nor disturb their harmony with gain and loss. Their mind roams in tranquility, their qi merges with the void. In their perfection, the heart is still and the spirit whole, virtue complete and unblemished. Thus can they enter and exit the formless, alone in unity with the Dao, their lifespan matching heaven and earth, ascending into the Great Void to become a true immortal.

Yet Ji Kang claimed that immortality is bestowed by nature and cannot be attained through accumulated study, while Wu Jun asserted that immortality can be learned and achieved—such contradiction! I say: if immortality were not attained through accumulated study, then why did the Celestial Decree to Zhengyang state: *"The Immortal Student Xu Sun, in countless kalpas past, diligently cultivated the supreme Dao, enduring hardship and toil. Of the ten thousand methods and thousand gates, none did he fail to master. He dispelled disasters, relieved suffering, eradicated harm, and vanquished demons. His merit aided living beings, his name inscribed in the registers of immortals. The host of perfected beings have recommended him; he is worthy of promotion. Let him be appointed Grand Historian of the Nine Provinces, Supreme and Illustrious."*

And again, the Edict declared: *"The Immortal Student Xu Sun, you have expiated the sins of your past life—greed, killing, and neglect of ancestral rites. Your present life’s merits are recorded: chanting spells over water, administering medicines, curing illnesses, and suppressing evil poisons. The Lord of Life of Mount Qian has transmitted the Golden Elixir to the mortal realm. Your debts are sealed, your form preserved; your body, household, kitchen, and all possessions shall return to the Three Heavens."* Both edicts refer to him as *"Immortal Student"* and speak of *"diligent cultivation of the supreme Dao, enduring hardship and toil"*—thus is it proven that immortality can indeed be attained through study.

Before Mao Ying’s birth, in the thirty-first year of the First Emperor of Qin, his grandfather Mao Chucheng ascended to heaven in broad daylight upon a cloud from Mount Hua. The villagers marveled, saying: *"The immortal Mao Chucheng has attained the Dao! Riding a dragon, he ascends to the Great Clarity. At times he descends to the Dark Continent, plays on the Red City. His descendants shall follow; among them is our Ying. If the Emperor would learn from him, he too might achieve immortality in the twelfth month."*

According to Ying’s *Inner Biography* and the *Stele of the Nine Bestowments*, he was born in the fifth year of Emperor Jing of Han’s *Zhongyuan* era—seventy-two years before his birth, during the reign of the First Emperor, the prophecy of his future immortality had already spread. In the second year of Emperor Ai of Han’s *Yuanshou* era, the Celestial Court bestowed upon him the title *Supreme Perfected of the Great Prime, Lord of Life and Minister of the Eastern Marchmount*. At that time, he was one hundred and forty-five years old. If this were so, could immortality not be bestowed by nature?

Two hundred and seventeen years before Ying’s conception, the prophecy of his future immortality had already circulated—truly a prediction of what was yet to come. How could this be the result of accumulated study? Those of shallow learning who claim immortality is not attained through study and thus abandon diligent cultivation are those who harm themselves. Those who claim it can be learned and achieved are like those who would grind a brick to make a mirror or sit in meditation to become a Buddha.

The Way of loyalty and sincerity has no other principle but sincerity and unity. With sincerity and unity, one may enter water without drowning, enter fire without burning, part metal and stone, and remain unharmed by tigers and leopards. Witness Shang Qiu Kai or the man of Lü Liang—even with mere unity, such wonders are possible. How much more so for the perfected immortal, whose form and spirit are both wondrous, in perfect union with the Dao? Their sitting is like vanishing, their standing like disappearance. They divide their form, scatter their body, transform in an instant, and fly across the Eight Extremities—such is only fitting.

The scholar of the true cultivation empties the self to preserve truth, embraces unity and simplicity, treasures compassion as their jewel, and takes stillness as their foundation. At dawn, they perceive the solitary; in clarity, they merge with the boundless. Their essential teaching does not depart from the books of Laozi and Zhuangzi. All else is false and deceitful discourse, not the writings of the sages.

When the Yellow Emperor lost the Dark Pearl, the Seven Sages lost their way, yet only *Xiangwang* (Vague Net) found it. When the minnows *Tiao* and *Hu* encountered *Hundun* (Chaos), they bored a hole in him each day, and in the end, caused his death. This shows that those who attain the Dao must abandon form and discard knowledge, embrace emptiness, stillness, and simplicity, remain unmoving in unity, pure and unadulterated. This is the root of nurturing form and spirit, of returning to the workings of creation.

The rulers of Qin and Han indulged in desires and extravagance, swept away by splendor. They admired the arts of immortality, desiring long life to preserve their glory, and trusted in the teachings of the Golden Elixir, relying on the power of medicines to sustain their excesses. Thus did masters of esoteric arts emerge, and the arts of illusion and transformation flourish. Some compounded lead and mercury, concocting cinnabar into the "Great Medicine"; others swallowed saliva and visualized the Dragon and Tiger to refine the "Inner Elixir." There arose names like *Lord of Wood* and *Queen Mother of the West*, titles like *Maiden* and *Infant*, terms like *Yellow Sprout* and *White Snow*, and secrets like the *Seven Returns* and *Nine Cycles*. Above, there were wonders like the *Mystic Capital* and *Crimson Towers*, chronicles of the *Red Clarity* and *Dragon Han* eras, the regulations of the *Three Caverns’ Talismans*, and the rituals of the *Nine Altars’ Offerings*. Below, there were methods of corpse liberation, body reflection, soul transmigration, and possession seizure, as well as flying talismans, black magic, exorcisms, and demon-subduing techniques. All arose from the indistinct and took form, from the formless and measured existence—ten thousand gates and a thousand paths, branching into heterodox byways.

When their work reaches fruition, though paths diverge, all converge as one—hundred deliberations lead to the same end. Scholars of the world must establish their root or they cannot stand; they must refine their pattern or they cannot complete. To guard emptiness and preserve the true, to embrace the One and abide in simplicity—this is the root. Transformations and ascensions, corpse liberation and black magic—these are the branches. Thus it is said: when the root is established, the branches naturally respond; when the foundation is solid, the ornamentation is complete—this is the principle of nature.

Daoist Chen Baoguang of Jingying Jingying Hermitage in Jiangyin, grieved that those of superficial learning grow weary of diligent cultivation, abandon themselves to idleness, live as walking corpses, and die as lowly ghosts, gathered the books of the Nine Schools and the Hundred Masters, even descending to minor officials and vulgar sayings. From all that recorded the affairs of immortals, he compiled this work to enlighten later learners, that they might know the diligence of perfected immortals in cultivating the true, their merit in aiding others, their reverence in serving Heaven, and the arduousness of attaining the Dao—such toil and effort, yet in the end they achieve success, their glory resplendent, becoming companions of Heaven.

Formerly, Sima Ziwei wrote the *Pivot of Sitting in Forgetfulness*, and Chen Bixu composed the *Resonance of the Primordial* to enlighten later generations, both works profound in purport, yet beginners and the dull-witted had no way to enter. Now Master Chen has gathered the deeds of immortals and extolled the mighty exploits of lofty perfected ones, making them clear to those who turn toward the Dao. Upon opening this book, one sees plainly that immortality can be learned, that the traces of sages and worthies through the ages are gathered before one’s eyes, as if viewing them on the palm of the hand. Stirred and encouraged, one is roused to ardent effort, leaping forward with vigor, aspiring to transcend the world—like placing a vessel of fine wine at a crossroads, where all may draw and drink to intoxication. In guiding the lost and teaching the deluded, in aiding others and benefiting the world, his merit is no small thing.

Master Chen’s spirit is empty and still, his virtue pure and harmonious. Clutching the sacred texts of the Three Caverns, his spirit soars to the Azure Heavens; gazing into the secret peaks of the Nine Purities, his name is inscribed on the Cinnabar Terrace. One day, he will join the ranks of immortals and receive his position, borne aloft on clouds to ascend. The Five Emperors will review his register, the Three Officials will record his merit—this is how Heaven repays the toil of those who compose such works, startling the indolent and negligent, that they may know: those who contribute to the world are surely rewarded by Heaven.

Written on the Zhongyuan Festival of the Jiaxu year of the Shaoxing reign, by the Bamboo Studio of Chunli. *Records of the Immortals of the Three Caverns*, Scroll One.

Orthodox Unity Daoist Chen Baoguang compiled and assembled:

**Pangu, Ancestor of All Things; the Yellow Emperor, Founder of the Dao.**

*The Record of Wonders* states: Pangu was the ancestor of heaven, earth, and the myriad things. At his passing, his head became the Five Peaks, his eyes the sun and moon, his fat and marrow the rivers and seas, and his hair and whiskers the grasses and trees. Another account says: his head became Mount Tai in the east, his belly Mount Song in the center, his left arm Mount Heng in the south, his right arm Mount Heng in the north, and his feet Mount Hua in the west. His tears became rivers, his breath wind and thunder, his joy clear skies, and his anger overcast. Furthermore, Xu Zheng’s *Records of the Three and Five Eras* says: Heaven and earth were a chaotic mass like an egg, and Pangu dwelled within it for eighteen thousand years. When heaven and earth split open, the pure yang became heaven, the turbid yin became earth, and Pangu transformed within them, growing nine times in a single day—divine in heaven, sagely on earth. Each day, heaven rose a *zhang* higher, earth grew a *zhang* thicker, and Pangu lengthened a *zhang* taller. Thus, after eighteen thousand years, heaven reached its utmost height, earth its utmost depth, and Pangu his utmost stature. Only then did the Three Sovereigns appear. Numbers began with one, stood firm in three, were completed in five, flourished in seven, and settled in nine—hence heaven is ninety thousand *li* from earth.

Another true scripture says: Before the Two Modes separated, all was a vast, formless expanse, neither shape nor sun and moon yet formed, resembling an egg in its chaotic dark and yellow. Yet Pangu the Perfected, the essence of heaven and earth, already existed, calling himself the Primordial Heavenly King.

*The Transmission of Daoist Learning* states: The Yellow Emperor, son of Shaodian, bore the surname Gongsun and the style Changhong. He was also called Guizang and, due to the auspicious sign of red clouds, Jinyun. As his complexion was more red than white, he was named Jinyun. Another auspicious sign of earthly virtue earned him the title Yellow Emperor. Though young, he could speak, and though a sage, he foresaw all. Fond of the profound and wondrous Dao, he became the founder of the Daoist tradition.

**Shaohao’s Zither and Se, Zhuanxu’s Bestowed Bells.**
*The Supplement to the Records of the Historian* says: Shaohao ruled the world by metal virtue. The imperial consort Huange wove by night in the Swirling Palace or roamed by day on a raft of mulberry wood, traversing the desolate shores of Qiongsang. There, a divine youth of unearthly beauty, called the son of the White Emperor, descended as the essence of Venus to the water’s edge and played with Huange. Another account says: On the shores of the Western Sea stood a solitary mulberry tree, towering a thousand *xun*, its leaves red and fruit purple. It bore fruit once every ten thousand years, and those who ate it remained youthful even after aging. The emperor’s son and Huange drifted together on the sea. Seated side by side, the prince plucked a zither of paulownia wood, while Huange leaned upon it and sang:

*"Heaven is clear, earth is vast, boundless and hazy,
The myriad forms surge and transform without end.
The sky stretches wide, the waves roll far—
Lightly adrift on the raft, I draw near the sun.
Where shall we go when the time comes? To Qiongsang?
My heart knows harmony and joy, never-ending."*

The White Emperor’s son answered:

*"The four corners, eight wastes—too distant to reach,
The net of light chases shadows to the water’s edge.
In the Swirling Palace, the night is still, weaving at the window,
The paulownia’s trunk stands straight a thousand *xun*.
Felled and crafted into instruments—lutes and zithers,
Pure songs flow ceaselessly, joy without limit.
Come, rest by the sea’s shores and hidden isles."*

Huange later gave birth to Shaohao, who was called Qiongsang or Sangqiu.

Zhuanxu, the High Lord of Gaoyang, was the grandson of the Yellow Emperor and son of Changyi. Changyi once walked by the riverbank and encountered a black dragon bearing the Primordial Jade Chart. An old scribe told him: "Your son will rule by water virtue." Ten years later, Zhuanxu was born, his hands marked like dragons, bearing the image of the jade chart. That night, Changyi looked up to see the North Star descend and transform into the old scribe. When Zhuanxu ascended the throne, wondrous omens and blessings gathered without exception. Even those who did not follow the imperial calendar came across mountains and seas to pay homage. The emperor bowed to the spirits of the four directions, while the empress held the jade tablet to receive the feudal lords in proper order. To those who embodied civil virtue, he bestowed bells and chimes; to those of martial virtue, shields and spears. There were floating golden bells and chimes adorned with feathers—when brushed with a feather duster, their sound carried a hundred *li*.

**Tang Yao’s Crane Call, Xia Yu’s Dragon Ride.**
*The Supplement to the Records of the Historian* says: During Emperor Yao’s reign, his sagely virtue shone far and wide. By the banks of the Yellow and Luo Rivers, a red jade tablet appeared, bearing the shape of heaven and earth. He also received a golden jade disk, its inscriptions radiant, recording the origins of heaven and earth’s creation. After the Four Fiends were executed, good people submitted, offices were established, and the proper order of human relations was set. Yao commanded Yu the Great to dredge rivers and drain marshes. In the land of Wu and the northern regions, no calamities or monsters arose, and even birds and beasts were tame. In the ruins of Youzhou, north of Feather Mountain, there was a bird with a melodious cry, its voice like bells, chimes, reed pipes, and zithers. The saying goes: when the azure crane sings, the age is at peace. In the country of Nanxun, there was a cave with a dark spring, its depths connecting to the earth’s veins. Within dwelled a furred dragon, which shed its bones in the open marshes, sharing its den with fish and dragons. The country presented a pair of these dragons, male and female, to the office of dragon keepers. The practice of raising dragons continued unbroken through the Xia dynasty, giving rise to the clan’s name. When Yu tamed the hundred rivers, he rode these dragons. After the four seas were united, he released them into the Yellow River.

**Boyang, Master of Emperors; Zhongni, Perfected Duke.**
*The Extensive Records* says: Laozi’s name was Er, his style Boyang. He was a native of Quren Village in Kuxian County, Chu. His mother conceived him after seeing a shooting star shaped like a tree. Some say Laozi existed before heaven and earth, or that he was the essence of heaven, a divine spirit. He was born by splitting his mother’s left armpit, emerging with white hair—hence the name Laozi. During Fuxi’s time, he appeared as a teacher, called Yuanhuazi, instructing Fuxi in the ancient methods, expounding yin and yang, aligning the eight directions, and establishing the eight trigrams.

In the time of Shennong, he emerged as a teacher, called Master Dacheng, instructing Shennong in tasting the hundred herbs, cultivating the five grains, and teaching the people to sow and plant. During the age of Zhurong, he appeared as a teacher, named Master Guangcheng, teaching the cultivation of the Three Bonds and the alignment of the Seven Governances. In the era of the Yellow Emperor, he served as a teacher, known as Master Limu, regulating yin and yang and composing the *Scripture of Daoist Admonitions*. From Shaohao and Gaoxin to Yao, Shun, Yu, and Tang, Lord Lao appeared as the imperial teacher, each time bearing a different name, transmitting his scriptures, and imparting his Dao. His transformations were boundless, beyond exhaustive explication. In the time of King Zhao, he emerged to enlighten and guide the Western Hu; by the reign of King You, he returned to the Central Lands.

Thus, when Confucius journeyed to Zhou to inquire about ritual, he said: "Today I have seen Laozi—is he not like a dragon?" Laozi knew the origins of ritual and music, the return of the Dao and virtue; truly, he was my teacher.

*The Records of the Grand Historian* states: Qiu, styled Zhongni, bore the surname Kong. *The Supplement to the Records of the Historian* recounts: On the night of Confucius’ birth, two azure dragons descended from heaven, and two divine maidens held fragrant dew in the air to bathe him. Zhen was favored by the Celestial Emperor, who sent down the music of the Grand Harmony, while five elders stood in the courtyard—these were the essences of the five stars. Earlier, a qilin had spat forth a jade tablet at a household in Queli, inscribed: "Child of the Water Essence, bound to Zhou yet a plain king." Thus, the two dragons entered the chamber, the five stars descended into the courtyard, and Zhen tied a brocade sash to the qilin’s horn. When the Master was about to pass, he embraced the qilin, removed his sash, and wept.

*The New Record of the Red Terrace* states: Confucius was the Perfected Duke of the Supreme Ultimate, governing Mount Jiuyi. Fu Yue was likened to a star, while Zoutu dreamed of the sun. *The Zhuangzi* says: Fu Yue attained this and served as minister to Wu Ding, encompassing the realm. Riding the eastern cord and astride the tail of the Winnowing Basket, he was ranked among the stars.

*The Supplement to the Records of the Historian* recounts: The consort of Emperor Ku was a daughter of the Zoutu clan. When the Yellow Emperor subdued the ferocity of Chiyou, he relocated the virtuous among his people to the land of Zoutu and banished the wicked to the northern wastes. Their ancestors took their clan name from the land, splitting into the Zou and Tu lineages. The woman walked without touching the earth, treading upon wind and clouds as she roamed between the Yi and Luo rivers. The emperor awaited her there and took her as his consort. She often dreamed of swallowing the sun, and with each dream, she bore a son. After eight dreams, she had eight sons, known in the world as the Eight Spirits, or the Eight Radiant Ones. *Yi* means "brightness." They were also called the Eight Heroes or the Eight Powers. Their divine power and heroic brilliance illuminated all phenomena, and the multitudes flowed with their divine wisdom.

Gongsun played the zither, while Shi Yan blew the pitch pipes. *The Unofficial History of Jin* states: Gongsun Feng secluded himself on Mount Jiuyu. In winter, he wore a single garment and slept on a bed of earth; in summer, he stored his food in vessels until it spoiled before eating. Whenever he played the zither and chanted, he was content in solitude, and all who saw him marveled. *The Supplement to the Records of the Historian* recounts: Shi Yan was a musician of the Shang. Proficient in the mysteries of yin and yang and versed in celestial patterns, his nature was unfathomable. He mastered the music of the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors; when he plucked a single string of his zither, earthly spirits ascended, and when he blew his jade pitch pipes, heavenly deities descended. Though he had already lived for centuries by the time of the Yellow Emperor, he could still perform the *Qingshang* and *Liudiao* melodies, as well as the tunes that bewitch the soul and seduce the spirit.

Zifang was enfeoffed with ten thousand households, while She Zheng entered the Chamber of the Orthodox Unity. *The Biographies of Immortals* states: Zhang Liang, styled Zifang, having aided the Han Emperor Gaozu to success, declared: "I have served as the emperor’s teacher with my three-inch tongue, been enfeoffed with ten thousand households, and ranked among the marquises—this is the pinnacle for a commoner, and it is enough for me. I wish to abandon worldly affairs and follow Master Red Pine in his wanderings." *The Biographies of Divine Immortals* recounts: She Zheng was a native of Badong. He spoke of events from the time of the First Emperor of Qin as if he had witnessed them firsthand. He often walked with his eyes closed, and his disciples followed him for decades without ever seeing him open them. When someone persistently entreated him, She Zheng opened his eyes, and their light flashed like lightning, illuminating the entire chamber. Li Babai called him "the thousand-year-old child."

Ma Di retreated in obscurity, while Zhao Wei lived in reclusion.

*The Supplementary Biographies of Immortals* records: Master Madi was a man of unknown origin who, together with He Danyang, dwelt in reclusion on Mount Heming in Shu, cultivating the Eightfold Dao’s "Gazing-at-Clouds" method. He lived in profound seclusion for many years. Later, within a grotto sanctuary, he uncovered a stone casket containing the Yellow Emperor’s Golden Crucible Formula. He refined the elixir upon the mountain, and upon its completion, ingested it and ascended to heaven.

The original *Biographies* state: During the Sui dynasty, Li Zhaowei was a man of reclusive aspirations who admired Ge Hong’s character. In his quest for the Dao, he sought masters without regard for the thousand-mile journey. He served as magistrate of Gaotang. During the Daye era, he took his wife and children into reclusion on Mount Song, where he was known as the "Yellow-Crowned Master."

The sagely son of Jiandi, the immortal king of the Lan clan.
Wang Jia’s *Supplementary Records of Antiquity* recounts: At the dawn of the Shang, the divine maiden Jiandi wandered the mulberry fields when she saw a black bird drop an egg upon the earth, inscribed with five-colored patterns forming eight hundred characters. Jiandi retrieved it and stored it in a jade tube, covering it with a vermilion cord. That night, a spirit-woman appeared to her in a dream, saying: "You have conceived this object and shall bear a sagely son to succeed the Metal Virtue." Jiandi then carried the child in her womb for a year, and after fourteen months, gave birth to Xie. The eight hundred years of his reign were foretold by the markings on the egg. Though the land suffered drought and hardship, his descendants flourished thereafter.

The *Extensive Records* states: Lan Gong was a native of Yanzhou, whose household numbered over a hundred. His devotion to filial piety was so profound that it moved the divine spirits. One day, a perfected being descended from the moon into his chamber, calling himself the Luminous King of Filial Piety. He declared: "When you dwell in the sun, you shall be the Immortal King; when in the moon, the Luminous King; when among men, the King of Filial Piety. For when filial devotion reaches the heavens, the sun and moon shine brightly; when it reaches the earth, the myriad things flourish; when it reaches the people, the kingly Way is fulfilled. Moreover, the Three Powers first divided from the Three Qi. These Three Qi are the Three Heavens of Jade Purity. I, dwelling in the Upper Clarity, manifest among men to expound the teachings of filial piety. In the Jin dynasty, Master Xu transmitted my lineage of the Dao of filial devotion, becoming the foremost among the immortals. Now I bestow upon you these secret instructions." With these words, he vanished. Lan Gong suddenly awakened to the true mystery. Having attained the Dao, he ascended in broad daylight.

The jade flute of Emperor Shun and the brocade pouch of Emperor Wu:
The *Extensive Records* recounts: When Emperor Shun ascended the throne, the Queen Mother of the West dispatched an envoy to present him with a white jade ring and a map for expanding the territory. Thus, he extended the Nine Provinces of the Yellow Emperor to twelve. The Queen Mother also sent an envoy to bestow upon Shun a white jade flute, which, when played, harmonized the eight winds. The *Inner Chronicle of Emperor Wu of Han* states: Emperor Wu saw in the Queen Mother’s casket a small scroll encased in a purple brocade pouch. Upon gazing at it, the Queen Mother said: "This is the *True Forms of the Five Peaks*. The immortals of Qingcheng recently entreated me for it, as it was issued by the Supreme One of the Three Heavens. Its script is secret and forbidden—how could your impure form be worthy to wear it?"

The reverent secrecy of Du Zigong and the forbidden formulas of Chang Sang:
The *Collected Writings of Xianji* records: Du Zigong possessed secret arts. Once, he borrowed a melon knife from someone, and when the owner demanded its return, Zigong said: "I shall return it presently." Later, the knife’s owner traveled to Jiaxing, where a fish leapt into his boat. Upon cutting it open, he found the melon knife. Such were the frequent marvels of his divine efficacy. The *Records of the Grand Historian* states: Bian Que was a native of Zheng in Bohai Commandery, surnamed Qin and named Yue-ren. In his youth, he served as an innkeeper. When the guest Chang Sang passed through, Bian Que alone recognized his extraordinary nature and treated him with generosity. Chang Sang, perceiving that Bian Que was no ordinary man, visited him for over a decade before calling him to a private audience. He said: "I possess forbidden formulas and, being advanced in years, wish to transmit them to you. Do not reveal them." Bian Que replied: "I respectfully accept." Chang Sang then produced medicine from his sleeve and said: "Drink this with the water from the upper pool for thirty days, and you shall perceive all things." He then entrusted all his forbidden texts to Bian Que. Suddenly, he vanished—likely no mortal. Bian Que followed his instructions and drank the medicine for thirty days. Thereafter, he could see through walls, and by this means, he diagnosed illnesses, perceiving the obstructions in the five viscera, though he concealed his ability beneath the guise of mere pulse-reading.

The golden elixir of Dou Qian and the jade nectar of the Song elder:
The *Biographies of Divine Immortals* states: Dou Qian was a native of Fufeng. During the reigns of Emperors Huai and Min of the Western Jin, the imperial house declined, and the Central Plains were in turmoil. Nearing the age of official service, he grieved over the chaos and longed to follow the paths of Master Red Pine and Wangzi Qiao. He took refuge in secluded peaks and deep caves, contemplating the supreme Dao through countless trials. One evening, divine radiance illuminated his chamber, and an extraordinary fragrance filled the valley. Celestial music drew near, accompanied by hundreds of attendants. A perfected immortal with a luminous circular pendant descended in a carriage, supported by two maidens and followed by officials in leather robes. Appearing no older than thirty, with the bearing of a crane, he called himself the Longevity Lord of the Shady Side of Pingdu Mountain. Moved by Dou Qian’s diligence, he transmitted the secret of the nine elixirs of golden fluid, administering oaths of transmission before departing. The *Extensive Records* states: The Song elder was an old man of Mount Song during the Jin dynasty. Once, he fell into a cavern in the mountain and wandered through its passages. There, he saw a substance like green mud, which he ate, thereafter feeling no hunger. Continuing through the cavern, he suddenly emerged at his home. When he consulted Zhang Hua, the latter said: "This is a grotto heaven. What you ate was jade nectar. You are destined to become an immortal."

The transcendent Hu Huichao and the reclusive Luo Yuansu:
The *Supplement to Immortal Biographies* records: During the Tang dynasty, Hu Huichao was a man of transcendent virtue. When he stood among others, his head towered above them, and even the tallest barely reached his shoulders. Thus, he was called "Tall Immortal Hu." He excelled at commanding spirits and gods. The *Accounts from the Hall of Manifestation* states: Luo Yuansu of Zhaoqing was a minor official who one day vanished into seclusion. The magistrate, enraged, ordered his arrest with great urgency. Yuansu hid in the mountains, where he suddenly encountered an old man leaning on a staff beneath a towering pine. The old man summoned him and asked: "How did you come here?" Yuansu truthfully recounted his plight and begged for refuge. The old man led him deep into the mountains. After traveling over ten *li*, they arrived at two thatched huts beside a pond, surrounded by rare trees and exotic flowers. Two young attendants resided in the western hut, while the eastern hut contained an alchemical furnace. The old man, who called himself Dongzhen, ordered Yuansu to tend the fire. He gave Yuansu several pellets of medicine, saying: "These will cure hunger." Thereafter, Yuansu abstained from grain. After a year, Dongzhen taught him talismanic arts and the method of absorbing *qi*, imparting all their mysteries. One day, he told Yuansu: "You may return home now." He then escorted Yuansu to a point several dozen *li* south of the county, where they parted. From then on, Yuansu’s talismanic arts became renowned in his village, their divine efficacy too numerous to record.

The golden fish of Liu Zhigu and the jade crow of An Qi:
The *Biographies of Eminent Daoists* states: The Daoist Liu Zhigu, styled Guangyuan, was summoned by Emperor Ruizong, who questioned him on Daoist teachings. Pleased with his responses, the emperor bestowed special honors upon him. During the Kaiyuan era, natural disasters and epidemics spread, afflicting eight or nine out of every ten people.

The emperor summoned Zhigu to treat the afflictions, whereupon he sighed and said: "Even great virtue arises daily, yet perfected ones too may fall ill. Lower methods refine medicines, superior physicians examine voices, but to resort to needles and moxibustion is already far removed from the Way." Thereupon, he substituted color for sacrificial swine and employed *qi* to dispel pestilence, thus curing deep-seated ailments as swiftly as rivers flow and dispersing pathogens like melting snow—so profound was his mastery. The emperor offered him lavish rewards, all of which he declined. At the foot of Huashan in Dongyang stood an ancient abbey, which he renovated and took as his residence. Suddenly, a radiance filled his chamber, and a single stalk of cinnabar *lingzhi* emerged, ringing with the sound of gold and jade when struck. In a dream, a divine being told him: "Within the cliff of the rear mountain lies a golden fish. Mount it, and you may ascend to the heavens—but without this *lingzhi* to strike the stone, it cannot be summoned." At dawn, he searched for the golden fish but found no trace. That night, he dreamed again: "It lies beneath the dripping spring." Arriving at the spot, he struck the stone with the *lingzhi*, and with a thunderous crack, the boulder split asunder. There lay a golden fish, over three *chi* in length. Mounting it, he soared into the sky, enveloped in swirling clouds and mist, and departed.

*The Biographies of Immortals* states: Master An Qi was a native of Fu Village in Langya. He sold medicines by the sea, and all who met him claimed he had lived a thousand years. Qin Shihuang summoned him for an audience and, after conversing with him, bestowed gold and jade worth thousands of *wan*. An Qi left these at the pavilion of Fu Village and departed, leaving behind a letter with a red jade crow as repayment, saying: "Seek me a thousand years hence beneath Mount Penglai." His countenance remained youthful, his complexion fair as jade.

*The Biographies of Divine Transcendents* records: Ling Shouguang, over seventy years of age, encountered an extraordinary person and obtained the art of ingesting *qi*. His appearance reverted to that of a man of twenty. By the first year of the Jian’an era, he had lived over two hundred years before dissolving his form. When his coffin was opened, no corpse remained—only a single shoe. Another transcendent, Yu Zhen, began studying the Dao at seventy-nine. She mastered the method of embryonic breathing and abstained from grains for over thirty years. Though her flesh diminished, her complexion grew fair, and she could run as swiftly as a galloping horse. Emperor Wu summoned her, and she appeared no older than twenty. When he inquired among her villagers, they claimed she was over four hundred years old. The emperor revered her as a divine being, but she declined his offers and returned home, covering three hundred *li* in a single day.

Jingchun achieved nothing; Zinian barely succeeded.

*The Biographies of Immortals* states: Guo Pu, styled Jingchun.

*The Records of the Western Mountains* relates: Perfected Lord Xu Xun, seeing the Jin dynasty’s decline and the frequent outbreaks of war, sought to avert future calamities. He and Lord Wu visited Wang Dun, where Guo Pu—an old acquaintance—was already in attendance. Together, they were granted an audience. Dun said to the Perfected Lord: "I dreamed of wood splitting the heavens. What does this portend?" The Lord replied: "Wood splitting the heavens above forms the character *wei* (未). Should you act rashly?" Dun then ordered Guo Pu to divine the matter. Pu said: "It will come to nothing." Dun was displeased and asked: "How long is your lifespan?" Pu replied: "If you launch your campaign, disaster will strike soon. If you return to Wuchang, your fate cannot be foretold." Dun grew furious and demanded: "How long is *your* lifespan?" Pu answered: "It ends at noon today." Dun ordered his guards to seize Pu and execute him. The Perfected Lord hurled his wine cup into the air, where it transformed into a white dove, circling the rafters. As Dun looked up, the two Lords had already vanished. Later, Dun ultimately could not escape his fate.

From the *Book of Jin*: Wang Jia, styled Zinian, ate no grains, wore no fine garments, and kept no company with worldly men. He wandered and dwelled in seclusion in Dongyang Valley. Those who sought him with sincere hearts would see him, but if their hearts were insincere, he would conceal his form and vanish. When Yao Chang sought to kill Fu Jian, he consulted Jia. Jia replied: "You will *lue* obtain it." Chang grew furious and said: "If you say 'obtain,' then say 'obtain'—what is this *lue*?" He then had Jia beheaded.

Previously, the monk Dao’an had said to Jia: "The world’s troubles are now at their height—you may depart." Jia replied: "You go first; I still have debts unpaid and cannot yet leave." Soon after, Dao’an passed away, and at this time Jia was executed. Now, Chang’s son, Xing, styled Zilüe, had just killed Jian—this was the meaning of "*lue* obtain it."

On the day of Jia’s death, people saw him on the ridges of Long. He drank osmanthus-infused water, and Zhang was granted rich marrow.

From the *Biographies of the Immortals*: Fan Li, styled Shaobo, was a native of Xuzhou. He served the Zhou master, the Grand Duke, and favored osmanthus-scented water and a grain-free diet. He became a minister of Yue. After Goujian destroyed Wu, Fan Li boarded a light boat and entered the sea, changing his name. He then went to Qi, where he was known as Yazi Zipi. Over a hundred years later, he appeared in Tao, amassing wealth in the hundreds of millions, and was called Tao Zhugong. Later, he abandoned his fortune and sold medicines in Lanling. For generations afterward, people continued to recognize him.

From the *Supplement to the Biographies of the Immortals*: Zhang Yunling cultivated the Dao at Zhaoxian Abbey on Mount Nanyue. Through refined contemplation, he achieved spiritual resonance, and Heaven bestowed upon him the true esoterica, teaching him the Way of internal nurturance and harmonizing primordial qi, as well as the practice of silent audience before the Great Emperor. After thirteen years of practice, his spirit roamed the Great Origin, and he faced the August Ultimate in audience. The Great Emperor bestowed upon him the divine elixir of *qiong-yu* and *lang-gao*, a liquid for merging spirit and luminous essence. Having received and ingested it, he underwent wondrous transformations, his spiritual efficacy boundless. In the first year of the Jianxing era, on the third day of the ninth month, he ascended to Heaven.

Lingxiao grasped jujubes, while Wang Ji obtained a peach.
In the *Declarations of the Perfected*, the Ninth Radiance True Consort, styled Lingxiao, once descended alongside the Purple Yang Lady into the chamber of the Perfected Yang. The Lady asked Yang, "Have you ever seen such a person in the world?" Yang replied, "Her spirit is lofty and refined—nothing can compare." The Lady laughed heartily. The Consort held three jujubes, giving one to each person to eat. The True Consort said, "Your master is the Lady of Mount Nanyue, who holds authority over life and destiny. Her Dao is supremely wondrous and complete—truly the lineage of profound virtue." Yang remarked, "These jujubes have no pits, and their flavor resembles that of pears."

From the *Biographies of the Immortal Wangs*: Wang Chai, formerly the magistrate of Wangwu, recited the *Yellow Court Scripture*. Each time he sought to annotate it, he failed to grasp its profound principles. After reciting it over six thousand times, he resigned his post and entered the grotto-heavens to seek the true and visit the Dao, vowing never to return. One day, deep within the cavern, he saw a stone bed and desk upon which lay a scripture, beside which stood a divine personage. The figure said to him, "Is this your aspiration? I am the immortal Wang Taixu. I have annotated this scripture for seven hundred years, and now I bestow it upon you." He then gave Wang a peach, saying, "This peach does not grow in the mortal realm. Having obtained it, whoever eats it will ascend in broad daylight."

Miaoxiang paid audience to Shun, while Liangqing recommended [a worthy] to Yao.
From the *Collected Biographies of the Immortals*: Wang Miaoxiang was a female Daoist from Cangwu. She practiced grain avoidance and qi absorption, focusing her thoughts on the cinnabar palace. Through this, she achieved resonance, and wondrous signs often appeared—radiant clouds, fragrant aromas, celestial music shaking the forests and valleys. Suddenly, a thousand chariots and ten thousand riders descended from the sky. A retinue of a thousand, each over ten feet tall, wielded halberds, weapons, banners, and standards. After a long while, a crane-canopied phoenix chariot, drawn by nine dragons, alighted before the altar. A figure wearing a sword and trailing slippers ascended the dais and sat, his body emanating five-colored light. Hundreds of immortals attended him. Miaoxiang approached to pay her respects. The Great Immortal said to her, "I am none other than Emperor Shun. Once wearied of governing the myriad states, I nurtured the Dao upon this mountain. I often wish to guide and instruct later generations, so that the world may know there is no one who cannot be taught. The Dao resides within, not without; it is in your own person, not in others. As the mysterious scripture says, 'Cultivate it in your person, and your virtue shall be pure'—this is not something others can achieve. I have seen the Earthly Controller’s memorials reporting that you have dwelled on this mountain for over thirty years, steadfast from beginning to end, unwavering in your devotion to the Dao. You have attained perfection." Thereupon, he commanded his attendants to bestow upon her the two scriptures of the *Dao and Virtue* and the *Elixir of Lingering Radiance*, then departed. Thereafter, he descended to the Yellow Court Abbey once every year or three to five years. Several years later, Miaoxiang ascended to immortality.

From the *Compendium of Marvels*: Chen Liangqing, in the fourth year of Jingyou, traveled from Yongzhou to the capital to take the imperial examinations. In the tenth month, he reached Changsha and dreamed of a guide leading him onto a great ship, where he met a Daoist who called himself the Master of Azure Essence. Their discourse was lofty and archaic. The Master said to Chen, "I have already recommended you to Yao as a forthright and thoroughly refined minister." Chen asked, "Where is Yao now?" The Master replied, "He presides over Mount Nanyue." Chen said, "Yao was an ancient sage-king—how could he be ranked among dukes and marquises?" The Master said, "Yao was an emperor among men, ruling by the power of Fire. Having relinquished the world, he became a spirit, taking his place in the South. Why do you doubt this?" Chen declined, citing his unestablished official career, and requested another day to respond. The Master granted him a ten-year reprieve. Upon waking, Chen found the dream strange and recorded it to console himself. The following year, he passed the examinations and was appointed judicial officer of Quanzhou. Passing through the South Pavilion of Yueyang, he dozed off during the day and dreamed again. A messenger arrived with an official summons, startling him awake. He sighed, "Could this be Yao’s command?" His companions encouraged him, dismissing the dream as untrustworthy. Later, while reviewing documents, he suddenly died at dusk as his meal was being prepared.

He knew of Shaluo, while Pei recalled Lanqiao.
From the *Supplement to the Biographies of the Immortals*: He Danyang, a native of Longyou, served as a Gentleman of the Palace Secretariat during the late Han. Between the reigns of Emperors Ai and Ping, the imperial house declined. He said to others, "Today’s affairs are beyond human control—this is the ordained course of history. Long ago, Shaluo collapsed, and a prognosticator declared: 'Five hundred years hence, a sagely woman of Qi shall rise.' Now, the Chancellor is a descendant of the Tian clan of Qi. The sagely empress is destined for this era—it is time to overthrow the Han and restore Qi’s enterprise." Thereupon, he abandoned worldly concerns for the mountains and forests, seeking transcendence. He often consumed pine pollen, which made his body light and his vision keen.

Thus he resigned his post and withdrew into seclusion, dwelling among the famed mountains of Shu. The Supreme Perfected of Great Peace descended to transmit to him the Dao of climbing the stellar chariot and riding the dragon, and later he ascended to the heavens.

From the *Records of Transmitted Tales*: Pei Hang hired a boat on the Xiang and Han rivers. Aboard the same vessel was Lady Fan, a woman of imperial beauty. Hang sent a poem to her maid Niaoyan:

*"Though north and south share this boat, longing lingers still,
How much more when a heavenly immortal stands behind the brocade screen?
If you ascend to the Jade Capital for the morning audience,
I would follow the phoenix and crane into the azure void."*

The lady replied: "I have a husband in Hannan. I pray you do not take these playful words to heart. Though there is a slight affinity between us, sir, we may yet become kin in the future." She answered with a poem:

*"One sip of jade nectar stirs a hundred feelings,
Pounding the black frost away, Cloud Maiden appears.
Blue Bridge is the very grotto of immortals—
Why climb the rugged path to the Jade Capital?"*

Later, passing through Lanqiao Station, he grew parched with thirst. At a thatched cottage, an old woman was spinning hemp. He bowed and begged for a drink. The woman said, "Cloud Maiden, bring a bowl of water." Hang took it and drank—it was pure jade liquid. He said to the old woman, "Your granddaughter is stunningly beautiful. I wish to marry her." The woman replied, "I am old and ill, and this is my granddaughter. The immortals left a dose of medicine, but it requires a jade mortar and pestle to pound it for a hundred days before it is ready. If you wish to marry her, you need only bring the jade mortar and pestle. Gold and silk are of no use to me." Hang departed in frustration.

A month later, he obtained the mortar and pestle. Returning to Lanqiao, the old woman untied the pouch at her waist and handed him the medicine. Hang began to pound it. That night, he peeked in and saw a jade hare holding the pestle, its snow-bright radiance illuminating the room. When the hundred days were complete, the old woman swallowed the medicine and said, "I shall enter the grotto to prepare the bedchamber for Master Pei." Soon, a grand mansion appeared, and immortal youths and maidservants led Hang inside. Among the assembled kin, all were immortals. One woman, claiming to be his wife’s elder sister, said, "Do you not recall meeting me on the same boat at Ezhu, sailing to Xiang and Han?" Those around her said, "This is Lady Yunqiao, wife of the Heavenly Master Liu Gang, and scribe to the Jade Emperor." Hang took his wife into the Jade Peak Grotto, where they consumed the cinnabar snow and jade blossom elixirs, attaining the rank of Supreme Immortals.

The crow nested in the mulberry, the snail peered from its shell.

From the *Unofficial Records of Xiangshan*: During the days when the Ancestral Founder lay hidden in obscurity, he once traveled the passes and rivers with a Daoist. The Daoist had no fixed name, calling himself "Chaotic Warrior." Whenever they lacked funds, he would reach into his sleeve and draw out gold, the more he took, the more it appeared. The three of them often drank to riotous intoxication. The Daoist was skilled at singing, performing *Step Void* as a game, and could extend his voice into the distant void, producing sounds of pure subtlety. At times, two lines of verse would drift down with the wind, heard only by the Ancestral Founder:

*"Golden monkey, tiger’s head—four,
The true dragon attains the true throne."*

When asked upon waking, he would say, "Merely drunken ramblings. How can they be trusted?" On the day he received the abdication and the Mandate, it was the fourth day of the first month of the *gengshen* year. Once enthroned, the Daoist was never seen again. Edicts were issued to search for him throughout the land. Some claimed to have seen him on the road to Bianyuan, or between Song and Luo. Sixteen years later, in the *yihai* year of the Kaibao era, during the Shangsi Festival, the emperor visited the Western Pond. The Daoist sat drunk beneath the shade of a tree by the shore. Smiling, he bowed to Taizu and said, "I am glad to see you well since we parted." The emperor rejoiced greatly and swiftly sent attendants to secretly bring him to the inner palace, fearing he might flee. He hastily returned and met with him. The Daoist was as before, and they clapped hands and drank heartily.

The emperor said, "I have long wished to ask you one thing. How many years remain in my allotted lifespan?" The Daoist replied, "If the night of the twentieth day of the tenth month is clear, you may extend it by another twelve years. If not, you must make swift preparations." The emperor pressed him to stay, lodging him in the rear gardens. The garden officials sometimes saw him spending the night in the treetops, in a crow’s nest.

From the *Soushen Ji*: Xie Duan found a large conch by the riverside in his village and took it home, keeping it in a jar. One day, he glimpsed into his house and saw a maiden emerge from the jar, going to the hearth to kindle the fire. Duan asked her, and she replied, "I am the White Water Immortal Maiden from the Heavenly Han. The Celestial Emperor pitied you for being orphaned young and diligently guarding your virtue, so he sent me to temporarily tend to your cooking and hearth. In a few years, you will grow wealthy and find a wife, and then I shall return. Yet you secretly spied on me, and now that my form has been seen, I can no longer stay. I must leave you, but I shall leave this shell behind—remember this as the tale of *Sha Lu* and *Pei Yi at Lan Bridge*."

From the *Xianzhuan Shiyi*: He Danyang, a man of Longyou, served as a Gentleman of the Imperial Secretariat during the late Han. Between the reigns of Emperors Ai and Ping, the imperial house declined, and he said to others, "Today’s affairs are not governed by human effort, for they are ordained by the cycles of fate. Long ago, when *Sha Lu* collapsed, one who knew the numbers foretold: *Five hundred years later, a sagely woman of Qi shall rise.* Now, the Chancellor is a descendant of the Tian clan of Qi, and the sagely empress is destined for this era—to overthrow the Han mandate and establish the Qi dynasty. This is the appointed time." Thereupon, he abandoned worldly ambitions for the mountains and forests, seeking transcendence. He often consumed pine pollen, which made his body light and his eyes bright.

He then resigned his office and withdrew into seclusion, dwelling among the renowned mountains of Shu. The Supreme Perfected of Great Peace descended to transmit to him the Dao of climbing the celestial pole and riding the dragon, after which he ascended to immortality.

According to the records: Pei Hang hired a boat on the Xiang and Han rivers. Aboard the same vessel was Lady Fan, a woman of peerless beauty. Pei sent a poem to her maid, Niaoyan, which read:
*Though north and south share this boat, longing lingers still,*
*How much more when a heavenly immortal stands beyond the brocade screen?*
*Should you ascend to the Jade Capital for the morning audience,*
*I would follow the phoenix and crane into the azure void.*

The lady replied, "My husband resides south of the Han. I pray you do not take these playful words to heart. Though we share a slight affinity now, we may yet become kin in the future." She answered with a poem:
*One sip of jade nectar stirs a hundred feelings,*
*Pounding away the black frost, Cloud Maiden appears at last.*
*The Blue Bridge is the very grotto of immortals—*
*Why climb the rugged path to the Jade Capital?*

Later, passing the Blue Bridge post station, Pei grew parched with thirst. At a thatched cottage, an old woman spun hemp. He bowed and begged for a drink. The woman said, "Cloud Maiden, bring a bowl of water." Pei took it and drank—it was pure jade liquid. He said to the old woman, "Your granddaughter is breathtakingly lovely. I wish to marry her." The woman replied, "This old, ailing one has this granddaughter, left with an immortal’s medicine—a single *daogui* measure. It must be pounded in a jade mortar for a hundred days before it is ready. If you wish to marry her, you need only bring the jade mortar and pestle. Gold and silk are of no use to me." Pei departed in frustration.

A month later, he obtained the mortar and pestle. Returning to the Blue Bridge, the old woman untied her sash to receive the medicine. Pei pounded it for her. That night, he peeked in and saw a jade hare holding the pestle, its snow-bright radiance illuminating the room. When the hundred days were complete, the old woman swallowed the medicine and said, "I shall enter the grotto to prepare the bridal chamber for Master Pei." Soon, a grand mansion appeared, and immortal youths and maidservants led Pei inside. Among the divine kin was a woman who said, "Do you not recall meeting me on the same boat at Ezhu, sailing to the Xiang and Han?" Those around her said, "This is Lady Yunqiao, wife of the Heavenly Master Liu Gang, and a scribe for the Jade Emperor." Pei entered the Jade Peak Grotto with his wife, where they consumed the cinnabar snow and jade blossom elixirs, attaining the rank of supreme immortals.

The *Wild Records of Xiangshan* states: During the days when the Ancestral Founder’s glory was still hidden, he once traveled the passes and rivers with a Daoist. The Daoist gave no fixed name, calling himself "Chaotic Warrior." Whenever they lacked funds, he would reach into his sleeve and draw out gold, which only multiplied the more he took. The three often drank to raucous intoxication. The Daoist was skilled at singing, performing the *Step of Emptiness* as a game, and could extend his voice into the distant void, producing a sound of pure subtlety. At times, two lines of verse would drift down with the wind, audible only to the Ancestral Founder:
*Golden monkey, tiger’s head—four,*
*The true dragon attains its true position.*

When asked upon waking, the Daoist dismissed it as drunken babble, saying, "How could such words be trusted?" Yet on the day he received the abdication of the mandate—it was the fourth day of the first month of the *gengshen* year—he ascended the throne. Afterward, he never saw the Daoist again, though he issued edicts to search for him far and wide. Some claimed to have spotted him on the road to Bianyuan or in the region of Song and Luo.

Sixteen years later, in the *yihai* year of the *Kaibao* era, during the Shangsi Festival, the emperor visited the Western Pond. There, beneath the shade of a tree by the bank, the Daoist sat drunk, laughing as he bowed to Taizu. Overjoyed, the emperor swiftly sent attendants to secretly bring him to the inner palace, fearing he might vanish. He hastily returned and met with him as before, clapping hands and drinking heartily. The emperor said, "I have long wished to ask you one thing—how many years remain in my allotted lifespan?" The Daoist replied, "If the night of the twentieth day of the tenth month is clear, you may extend it by another twelve years. If not, you must make swift preparations." The emperor pressed him to stay, lodging him in the rear gardens. The garden officials sometimes saw him spending the night in the treetops, in a magpie’s nest.

From *Soushen Ji* (Records of the Search for Spirits):

Xie Duan found a large conch by the riverside in his village and took it home, keeping it in a jar. One day, he peered into his house and saw a maiden emerge from the jar to light the hearth. Duan asked her who she was, and she replied, "I am the White Water Maiden of the Celestial River. The Heavenly Emperor pitied you for being orphaned young and diligently guarding your integrity, so he sent me to temporarily tend to your cooking and hearth. In a few years, you will grow wealthy and take a wife, and then I must return. But you have now secretly spied on me, and my form has been revealed—it is no longer fitting for me to stay. I shall leave you, but I will leave this shell behind. After offering it to a monastery for almsgiving, use it to provide meals for poor scholars."

A destitute scholar, having received a meal, did not leave. Instead, he searched the courtyard, gazing all around, and calmly said to Tan Yu, "Though I am poor, I am always moved by your reverence’s offerings. Today, I have a small gift to bestow—may I ask for one person to accompany me?" Tan Yu did not believe him, but after repeated pleas, he sent a humble, ascetic monk to follow. Together, they walked beyond the city’s north gate.

After traveling over fifteen *li*, they stopped at a small inn. Inside the room, there was nothing but two empty beds. That night, they lodged at the inn, and the scholar asked about the number of monks in the monastery and the renovations of its halls. The monk replied, "If we speak of repairs to the monastery’s halls, they would require no less than a thousand *sheng* of funds."

The scholar then rose, lit a fire beneath the bed, and broke open several corners of a tile. From his bag, he took out medicine and, by lamplight, applied it. Gradually, the radiant light transformed everything into gold. He weighed out ten *jin* and gave it to the monk, saying, "Use this for repairs. Guard it well and do not take it lightly." At dawn, he bid farewell and vanished, never to be seen again.

A song of harmonious melody was sung, and the verses of Duan Gu’s lament echoed.

*The Continuation of the Immortals’ Biographies*: Lan Caihe, of unknown origin, often wore a tattered indigo robe, a wooden clog belt, and a single boot. He would sing in the marketplace, holding a clapper board to beg from passersby. Young and old followed him, their laughter harmonizing in delight, though he seemed mad yet not quite mad. His song went:

*Step and sing, step and sing,*
*Lan Caihe—*
*How long does a life endure?*
*Youth blooms but a spring on the tree,*
*Years cast like dice in play.*
*The ancients, once gone, never return;*
*The living come in endless array.*
*At dawn, riding simurghs to the azure vault,*
*At dusk, seeing mulberry fields turn to white waves.*
*Eternal light gleams in the void,*
*Golden palaces tower, jagged and high.*

One day, as he sang and danced by the bridge at Haoliang, he ascended on clouds from a tavern’s upper floor, leaving behind his robe, boot, and clapper board.

*The Expanded Record of Marvels*: Duan Gu, a native of Xuzhou, repeatedly sat for the imperial examinations and came from a wealthy family. Later, he suddenly grew erratic, donning a scholar’s cap and a plain cloth robe with a white silver belt. He wandered the streets day and night, chanting:

*...A thatched hut, still under repair,*
*Yet the wind howls, tearing the eaves,*
*Rafters sag, beams tilt—*
*Soon, soon it will collapse!*
*At the words “collapse,” he would repeat them three or five times before stopping.*
*The walls crumble to a heap of dust,*
*The master never returns home.*

Whenever he came and went, dozens of idle youths from the alleys followed, echoing his words. People dismissed him as mad, thinking nothing of it. In the final years of the Qingli era, he fell ill and died, his body temporarily interred in the wild. Years later, when his grave was opened for proper burial, only an empty coffin remained.

King Zhao of Yan, free of desire, Zi Xiu ashamed of his mother.
*Miscellaneous Records of the Zi Year*: King Zhao of Yan dozed off and dreamed of a feather-clad immortal who spoke to him. When asked about the path to transcendence, the immortal replied, “Your great wisdom and essence remain unopened. To seek longevity and eternal vision is impossible.” The king knelt and begged to receive the teaching of absolute desirelessness. The immortal traced a finger across the king’s heart, which split open at his touch. The king awoke in shock, his blood soaking the mat. Thereafter, he stilled his mind and dismissed all music and pleasures. Within ten days, the immortal from his dream reappeared and said, “I first sought to replace your heart.” He produced a green silk pouch, the size of a hand, containing *Luminous Cinnabar for Mending the Spleen* and *Essence Powder for Nourishing Blood*. With his hand, he massaged the king’s chest, and in an instant, the wound healed. The king begged for the medicine, storing it in a royal jar sealed with golden cords. When applied to his feet, it allowed him to soar beyond the earth for ten thousand miles as if strolling a foot’s distance. Those who obtained and consumed it would outlive heaven and age no more.

*The Inner Biography of Dongfang Shuo*: When Qin conquered the six states, the essence of Venus, the Weaver Girl’s handmaiden Liang Yuqing, and her companion Wei Chengzhuang fled into the Small Immortal Grotto of Yaocheng, where they remained for forty-six days. Enraged, the Heavenly Emperor ordered the Five Peaks to search and capture them. Venus returned to its place, but Wei Chengzhuang escaped. Liang Yuqing had a son named Zi Xiu, and she was banished to the eternal spring beneath the Northern Dipper. Her son was betrothed to the River Earl, tasked with riding the clouds and summoning rain. Yet whenever Zi Xiu reached the Small Immortal Grotto, he felt shame at the place of his mother’s illicit flight, turning his chariot away. Thus, that land was often deprived of rain.

*Records of the Immortals’ Assemblies, Scroll One* concludes.
*Records of the Immortals’ Assemblies, Scroll Two* begins.

Chen Baoguang of the Orthodox Unity Dao compiled this collection.

Yang Jun, Controller of Destiny, and Zi Jin, Attendant of the Celestial Court.

*The True Sayings* records: Yang Xi, a native of Jurong, was spiritually attuned from childhood. He formed a bond of divine friendship with the two Xus, and after attaining the Dao and becoming a perfected immortal, the immortals descended repeatedly to transmit their teachings to him. The Perfected Consort of Nine Flowers admonished him, saying: "Those who revel in the flourishing of worldly dust are but lowly ghosts hastening toward death, their aged frames burdened with sin. Wealth and indulgence in luxury are the axe and saw that sever the bones, much like the carts and boats that bear the condemned." Thereafter, the host of perfected immortals descended without fail, day after day. After twenty years, Yang Jun ascended to heaven in broad daylight upon a cloud-borne crane, where he was appointed Perfected Controller of Destiny of Huayang.

*The Divine Immortals’ Biographies of the Wang Clan* states: Wang Qiao, styled Zi Jin, was the crown prince of King Ling of Zhou. Born with divine intelligence, he loved the Dao from childhood. Fond of playing the sheng to mimic the cry of the phoenix, white cranes and vermilion phoenixes would gather around him. Later, he met Master Fuqiu, who imparted to him the essentials of the Dao. Taken to Mount Song, he never returned. One day, he suddenly alighted upon a crane atop the mountain’s peak, and a children’s rhyme spread: *"Prince Wang Qiao, fond of divine immortals, on the seventh day of the seventh month ascends to heaven as an honored guest."* Initially, he was appointed Attendant of the Celestial Court as Controller of Destiny of the Southern Marchmount, and later promoted to Perfected Lord of the Paulownia Minister.

Modest in his master’s rank, his Dao soared among the perfected immortals.

*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* records: The Daoist Kou Qianzhi secluded himself on Mount Songyang to cultivate and refine his practice. The Most High Lord of the Dao descended, and the Immortal Elder Wang Chuping led Kou Qianzhi forward, saying: "I have received the memorial from the Lord of the Central Marchmount: Since Celestial Master Zhang ascended as a perfected immortal, those who perform good deeds have had no master to guide them. Now, the Daoist Kou Qianzhi’s conduct aligns with the natural order; he is fit to assume the master’s rank."

*The Declarations of the Perfected* states: Daoxiang was the style of the third son of the Chief Scribe Xu. He cast aside worldly affairs to devote himself wholly to the supreme Dao, and the Chief Scribe held him in high esteem. Profoundly attuned to the numinous and the true, he formed a deep bond with his master. Now, he resides in the Eastern Flower as a perfected immortal, where he has been entrusted with texts and appointed Attendant of the Celestial Court.

Yu the Elder refined the corporeal soul, while Xia Fu refined the ethereal soul.

*The Accounts from the Hall of Manifestation* records: During the Tang dynasty, when Lord Wang Qian governed Jingnan, a young man of the Lü clan, impoverished and destitute, sought an audience with him. The lord paid him no heed. After lodging at an inn for over a month, the young man, in dire straits, sold his donkey in the marketplace. The market warden, Yu the Scribe, summoned him and inquired about his circumstances. The young man replied: "My family is from north of the Wei River. Poor and with elderly parents, I came here because Lord Wang is my maternal uncle of the highest degree. Yet, though I have come from afar, he has not deigned to glance my way. Is this not my fate?" Yu said: "I see hunger in your face. Tonight, I shall prepare a meal for you; you may lodge beneath my roof." He then led him into a humble chamber, where they sat on a worn mat, and coarse rice was served in earthenware. That night, Yu spoke slowly to the young man: "I shall perform a minor rite to secure the funds for your return to Luoyang." He took a small jar, placed it on the ground, and after the time it takes to eat a meal, lifted it to reveal a figure about five inches tall, adorned with a purple sash and golden seal. Yu said: "This is the corporeal soul of Lord Wang." He admonished it: "Lü is your nephew by marriage, poor and come from afar. Yet you have not shown him the slightest courtesy—is this the way of kinship? You must bestow upon him ample gifts: a horse, a servant, and two hundred bolts of silk." The purple-robed figure nodded in assent. Yu then covered the jar again, and after a moment, the figure vanished. The next day, Lord Wang indeed summoned the young man for feasting and outings. After several months, the young man announced his departure, and the lord presented him with a servant, a horse, and two hundred bolts of silk. Upon returning north of the Wei River, the young man dared not speak of what had transpired, and only years later did he recount it to others.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* states: The Controller of Destiny said: "Xia Fu, from his youth, loved the Dao. He journeyed to Wu to seek out Master Red-Beard, who transmitted to him the method of refining the ethereal soul. Later, he encountered the Perfected Lord of the Paulownia Minister and attained the Dao. Now, he dwells among the immortals in the grotto-heavens."

Daorong burned his undergarments; Huizong piled the firewood.

*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* records: The Daoist Gou Daorong, of unknown origin, was renowned in Shu. He befriended Lü Yi, the magistrate of Huayang, and declared: "On the day of my departure from this world, come to the great pine in the eastern outskirts and burn my coffin with firewood as a kindness." Lü Yi, along with his friends Zhang Sheng and Chang Yao, and several dozen others, marked the date to await the event. The evening before the appointed day, Daorong visited the homes of his close friends, drinking, laughing, and conversing.

As dusk fell, he lodged at an inn. Lü Yi secretly observed him and saw that his repose was as usual. At dawn, he had already transformed, yet his complexion remained unchanged. Thereupon, they prepared a coffin and undergarments, conveying him to the great pine in the eastern outskirts. When the firewood was arranged and the flames ignited, the coffin blazed with such intensity that none could draw near. Daorong emerged above the smoke and flames, ascending gently into the void.

Another was Zhao Huizong, who at the end of the Tianbao era suddenly piled firewood in the eastern part of the commandery and immolated himself. Officials and commoners gathered to witness, and Huizong sat serenely amidst the fire, reciting the *Scripture of Salvation*. In an instant, he transformed into auspicious clouds and immortal cranes. When the fire subsided, the grass beneath remained verdant. Two poems were found among his posthumous writings. The first read:

You bore me from the void,
Placed me in nonbeing.
The purest essence becomes spirit,
The primordial nothing, the body.
Scattered yang becomes light,
Conjoined yin becomes tally.
Form turns to ash and earth,
Spirit joins the immortals.
All filth shall end,
All affairs forever cease.

The second read:

I ride the steed of time,
Sun and moon as my escort.
Leaping through the nine skies,
I ascend to audience with the Heavenly Emperor.
Brightly shine, my assembly,
And my disciples too.
The false Dao nurtures form,
The true Dao nurtures spirit.
Diligently, diligently—
I have nothing more to say.
Xu Diao anoints the heart,
Qian Lang mends the brain.

*The Continuation of the Biographies of Immortals* records: Xu Diao, of unknown origin, styled himself the "Angler of Penglai." He often wore a gourd at his waist, poled a small boat across the five lakes, and exchanged his catch for wine, returning to chant and recite. If he encountered the sick, he would take a pill no larger than a sesame seed and instruct them to apply it with wine over the heart—all would recover. When asked whether the medicine could be ingested, he would not answer.

He replied: "It may be ingested, but I fear it would be monk’s fare." Some meddlesome fellow swallowed it and naturally ceased eating—only then did people believe him. One day, he vanished without a trace.

Qian Lang, having passed the imperial examinations, held successive eminent posts, and wherever he governed, he left behind fond memories. Later, he resigned his office and entered Mount Lu, where he encountered a master who taught him the art of *returning to the origin and mending the brain*. At the age of one hundred and fifty, his complexion remained that of a child. Several of his great-grandsons entered officialdom, all with hoary heads. One day, Lang took leave of them, saying: "I have dwelt in the world for many years, but now the Supreme Clarity has summoned me—I must depart." Thereupon, he dissolved into transformation and departed.

Long Bogao of Fangtai, Xi Xuanjie of the True Isle.
*The Declarations of the Perfected* records: The Eastern Minister Lord said: Long Bogao was a man of the Han. The General of the Vanquishing Waves admonished his brother’s son, praising this man’s excellence as worthy of emulation. Later, Bogao received the method of *embryonic breathing* from the immortal Diao Daolin. Feigning death in a drunken stupor, he concealed himself on Fangtai.

*The Extensive Records* states: Xi Xuanjie grew old yet retained a youthful countenance. Emperor Xianzong, marveling at this, summoned and asked him: "Master, though advanced in years, your complexion shows no sign of age—how is this?" He replied: "Your servant has long consumed *lingzhi* upon the sea, and thus it is so." Thereupon, he carved wood into the Three Isles of the sea, adorning them with pearls and jade. The emperor, gazing upon them, exclaimed: "Were you not an immortal of the highest order, you could not have attained such a realm!" Xuanjie said: "The Three Isles lie but a footstep away—allow me to take Your Majesty on a tour." He then concealed himself and entered, gradually diminishing in size until he vanished entirely. The emperor sighed in wonder and named the mountain *True Isle*.

Yin Sheng the Beggar, Han Kun the Impoverished Daoist.
*The Biographies of the Immortals* records: Yin Sheng was a beggar of Chang’an. He often begged in the marketplace, but the people despised and reviled him, even dousing him with filth—yet his clothes remained unstained. Suspecting him of sorcery, they imprisoned him, but he remained as before. Travelers in Chang’an sang a song: "Give the beggar fine wine, lest your house be ruined." Liu Xiang said: Yin Sheng, the beggar, was spurned for his importunity; few recognized his truth. Though shackled and shamed, to me he was untainted—calamity befell their homes instead.

*The Extensive Records* states: Han Kunzi concealed himself on Mount Tiantai, fond of reciting poetry filled with lines on the seclusion of mountains and forests. Master Tongzhi compiled and prefaced his verses, circulating them in the world. During the Xiantong era, he called upon Li He. Seeing his tattered robes, Li treated him with disdain. The next day, the old man returned clad in fine attire, riding a white horse, and Li received him with great courtesy. The elder said: "Do you know of Han Kunzi?" Li replied: "I do." The old man said: "This poor Daoist is he. I thought you might be teachable, but now I see otherwise—you have not yet entered the gate." He continued: "Cultivate the heart within, restrain the body without—thus one avoids transgression. Know softness and guard humility—thus one secures the self. Attribute faults to oneself—thus virtue accumulates. Only thus may one hope to attain the Dao." With that, he whipped his horse and departed.

Xi Fu Refining Melons, Meng Jie Holding Dates.
*The Biographies of the Immortals* records: Xi Fu dwelled in the mountains, where an immortal often came to sell melons at his home. The immortal taught him the method of refining melon seeds, instructing him to consume them at the vernal equinox. After twenty years, he could ascend mountains and enter water. At over a hundred years of age, he ceased dwelling on the mountain’s peak, calling out to his father to speak of matters before departing.

*The Unofficial History of the Western Han* states: Meng Jie was a man of the Western Han, versed in the Dao’s arts. By holding a single date pit in his mouth, he could go ten years without hunger. He could also hold his breath, his body unmoving, for over a year. Scholars and commoners revered him as a divine immortal. Later, he entered the mountains and never emerged.

Shi He with One Meal, Qingjing with Nine Repasts.
*The Biography of the Perfected Lord of the Western Mountains* records: The Perfected Lord, named He, was a native of Julu. In his youth, he entered the Four Bright Mountains, where an immortal taught him the alchemical formula for transmuting gold and jade. He relieved the suffering of the destitute, and the people received his bounty. Later, he could expel malevolent spirits and command ghosts and gods. He was known for his filial piety toward his mother, mastered *embryonic breathing*, and attained the method of *sitting in forgetfulness*. Sometimes he ate but once in a hundred days or slept but once in half a year. Emperor Ming of Jin treated him with great favor. When Master Xu the Perfected ascended, his teacher followed him into heaven.

*The Biographies of the Divine Immortals* records: The Master of Qingjing was a thousand years old yet had the complexion of a child. He could walk five hundred *li* in a day, go a year without eating, or consume nine meals in a single day.

The Three Realms of the Heavenly Master, the Nine Altars of Lan Sheng.
The *Original Biography* states: Zhang Daoling was the sixth-generation descendant of the Marquis of Liu.

He was recommended as a candidate of *worthy, good, upright, and square* yet, though he held office, his heart lay in refining his form. Thus, he retired to seclude himself in the Northern Deng Mountains. Emperor Zhang summoned him with a third-rank seal and cord, but he declined. Entering Mount Song, he encountered a divine person who told him: "Within a stone chamber lies the *Yellow Emperor’s Alchemical Canon*, sealed in a jade casket with a jasper clasp—you shall obtain it by following the ringing bell." The Master followed the sound and indeed found the book. Thereupon, he built an altar to worship the Perfected and refined the Nine Elixirs. When the elixirs were complete, he said to his disciple Wang Chang: "To ingest the elixir is to ascend to heaven, yet I have not accomplished great merit. How could I dare consume it hastily? I should first eliminate harm and promote benefit for the state, and only then take it. Thus, my service to the Three Realms will be without shame."

*The Biography of Ling Sheng* records: In the early *Jianlong* era of the present dynasty, the Perfected Lord Ling Sheng descended and told Zhang Shouzhen: "The altar rites comprise nine tiers. The upper three are established for the state, the middle three for officials, and the lower three for the common people. Each of the nine tiers has its own ceremonial forms."

The *Five Viscera* of Xuanfu, the *Three Passes* of Shuqi.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* states: Wang Xuanfu was taught by an immortal the method of *swallowing solar essence*. After thirty-four years of practice, he could inwardly perceive his five viscera and write in darkness at midnight. When his Dao was accomplished, the Most High dispatched a feathered chariot to welcome Xuanfu. Riding clouds and driving dragons, he ascended to heaven in broad daylight. Now, he resides in the *Mystic Orchard Terrace*, transformed into the Perfected of the Central Marchmount.

Furthermore, Zhao Shuqi studied the Dao on Mount Wangwu. He encountered a diviner who said: "If you wish to enter the Gate of Heaven, visit the Three Passes, visualize the Vermilion Bird, and align with Kunlun." Shuqi found this extraordinary and bowed, entreating for the essential instructions. The diviner presented him with a scroll, saying: "The Three Passes are: the mouth as the Heavenly Pass, the feet as the Earthly Pass, and the hands as the Human Pass. When the Three Passes are harmonized, the five viscera are at peace; when the five viscera are at peace, the entire body is free of illness, and the immortal Dao is achieved."

The Dao Lord bestowed a sword; the Jade Maiden offered a ring.

*The Original Biography* states: When the Heavenly Master’s work was complete, the Most High Dao Lord descended and conferred upon him a pair of male and female swords, each inscribed with the patterns of the sun, moon, and stars. The Dao Lord said: "I pity the beings of the Lower Realm, bound and entangled as wandering souls, with humans and ghosts intermingled.
