Now, you shall divide them into the Five Quarters, separating humans and ghosts into distinct positions. Your merit shall be immeasurable."

Moreover, when the Heavenly Master reached Renshou County, he encountered twelve celestial jade maidens, each presenting him with a jade ring, wishing to serve him. The Master combined them into a single ring and said: "I shall cast this into the earth. Whoever retrieves it first may keep it." The maidens scrambled to take it, but the deeper they grasped, the further it sank, until it was restrained and would not emerge. It transformed into a salt well, from which both public and private parties drew benefit. The county was henceforth named Ling Prefecture, beginning with Daoling.

The Holy Mother pierced the clouds; Zhou Sheng plucked the moon.

*The Extensive Records* states: The Holy Mother was a native of Hailing and married into the Du family as a mother. She encountered someone who could alter her form and transform her appearance, but her husband did not believe her and instead accused her of sorcery. He reported her to the authorities, and she was imprisoned. Yet, she flew out through the window, and people saw her piercing the clouds as she departed. She left behind a single silk shoe beneath the window. The people of Hailing built a shrine for her, calling it the *Shrine of the Holy Mother*.

*The Accounts of the Xuan Chamber* records: Zhou Sheng possessed Daoist arts and traveled through Wu and Chu, where many revered him. Once, he arrived in Guangling during a Mid-Autumn gathering. The moonlight was radiant and clear, and the assembled guests spoke of Emperor Minghuang’s journey to the Lunar Palace. Zhou Sheng smiled and said: "I once studied under a master and can also retrieve the moon." He then took several hundred bamboo poles and lashed them together, saying: "I shall use this ladder to fetch the moon." He closed the door for a long while, and the guests waited in the courtyard. Suddenly, the sky and earth turned pitch black, and upon looking up, not a wisp of cloud was to be seen. Soon after, they heard Sheng call out: "I have arrived!" He lifted the hem of his robe, revealing the moon by half an inch, illuminating the entire room. The cold penetrated to the bone, but after the time it takes to eat a meal, all returned to normal.

Shang sang *Yangchun*; Zhang chanted *Baixue*.

*The History of Poetry* states: Shang Qiqi possessed extraordinary arts. Passing through Runzhou, he drank with guests and said: "I have a skill to entertain you." He then turned to a painted woman on the screen and said: "You may sing the *Yangchun* melody." The woman responded at once and began to sing. Her voice was clear and resonant, as if emerging from the screen itself. She sang: "Sorrowful to hear *Yangchun* sung, it knots the wanderer’s heart. My love has gone and not returned; the willows drift like fragrant snow." She performed over ten such songs.

The *Preface to the Wuzhen Pian* states: Zhang Boduan, styled Pingshu, of Tiantai, exhausted his attainments and composed eighty-one regulated poems, titled *The Wuzhen Pian*. Much of the text is not recorded here. One of his poems, *The Ninefold Transmutation of Golden Fluid and Returned Elixir*, reads:
*"Yellow Sprout and White Snow are hard to find;*
*The adept must rely on deep virtue and conduct.*
*The Four Symbols and Five Phases wholly depend on Earth;*
*How could the Three Primes and Eight Trigrams depart from Water?*
*Refined into numinous substance, none recognize it;*
*Dissolving all yin demons, no ghost may invade.*
*Should you seek to leave the secret formula among men,*
*You’ll not meet a single one who is a true friend."*

Zhang beheld towers and terraces; Feng ascended palace gates.

*The Miscellaneous Notes from the Northern Dream* states: Zhang Jianzhang was fond of classics and history, always cherishing purity and coolness. Once, while traveling to the Bohai Sea, he encountered a storm. A green-clad youth led him ashore and up a mountain. On the island, he beheld resplendent towers and terraces, where immortal maidens resided, each attended by a grand retinue. After a brief stay, they said: "You do not deceive in the dark chamber; you may be called a gentleman of antiquity." They then ordered a green lad to escort him back.

*The Extensive Records* states: Feng Nü was young and abstained from food, her heart yearning for purity and emptiness. Her parents dismissed her words as nonsense. One day, she suddenly saw immortals in the sky, traveling from south to north for over a thousand *li*, conjuring golden cities and jade towers atop a mountain. One figure beckoned her to ascend the palace gates, where a host of immortals stood in solemn array. They said: "You possess the bone record of an immortal and shall become a Perfected of the Upper Realm. The Most High has commanded us to confer upon you the *True Writ of the Numinous Treasure*. Practice it accordingly, and your ascension shall come in time."

When the appointed time arrived, she indeed ascended into the void.

**Dwelling in Grotto-Chambers: The Cave of Xu Gu**
*The Records* state: Xu Qikun passed the imperial examination but aspired to cultivation. Once, while traveling to Shu, he ascended a perilous plank path when suddenly both he and his horse plunged beneath it. Unable to advance or retreat, he followed his horse until they reached a grotto-chamber, where he saw immortals arrayed in ranks. All rejoiced, saying: "This is the grandson of the Prefectural Governor—he bears the physiognomy of an immortal." The perfected ones bade him sit and offered him a drink, saying: "This is stone marrow. You have obtained it—guard it well, neither leaking its secret nor indulging in dissipation. We shall meet again later." Then they sent him on his way with the horse he had ridden, saying: "This is the dragon of our grotto, punished to bear burdens. You possess immortal bones, so you were destined to encounter it. When you return to the world of men, release it to go wherever it pleases." Later, when Xu Qikun happened to pass by his former home, the horse transformed into a dragon and soared away.

*The Extensive Records* state: The immortal maiden Xu was several hundred years old yet appeared no older than twenty. She often wandered sacred mountains, sometimes lodging in forest groves or cave-dwellings. Traveling through the Jiangnan region, she was seen by the people of Wu for thirty or forty years, her complexion unchanged, her steps swift as flight. During the Xiantong era, she arrived in Kè County and told someone: "My late father served in the Northern Qi and accumulated hidden merit, which has now extended to me, granting me prolonged years." Later, people investigated this and realized the maiden was none other than the daughter of Chancellor Xu Zicai.

**Virtue Illumines the Five Peaks; the Mighty Dao Roams the Nine Doubts**
*The True Sayings* record: The Lord of the Eastern Ministerium said: Song Dexuan lived in the time of King Xuan of Zhou. By ingesting the *Six Jia Talismans of the Numinous Flight*, he attained the Dao, walking three thousand *li* in a day and transforming his form at will. Now he dwells on Mount Song, roaming the Five Peaks. Also, Han Weidao, of unknown origin, followed Song Dexuan in and out, serving him as his master. Dexuan transmitted the Daoist arts to him, and now that he has achieved the Way, he often enters and leaves the Nine Doubts Mountains.

**Xu Fu and the White Deer; the Recluse and the Yellow Oriole**
*The Extensive Records* state: The First Emperor of Qin dispatched Xu Fu with three thousand boys and girls to the Eastern Sea in search of the herb of immortality, but they never returned. Later, when Shen Xi attained the Dao, the Yellow Emperor sent Xu Fu in a white deer-drawn carriage to welcome Master Shen the Perfected—only then did people learn that Xu Fu had achieved the Way. Also, Yuan Cangji styled himself the Recluse Yuan. During the Daye era of the Sui, while on a mission across the sea, he encountered divine beings who detained him in a foreign land, where he traversed immortal realms. One day, longing for home, he was swiftly conveyed back, arriving in Donglai in less than ten days. When he inquired about the land, he found it was now the Great Tang. Searching for his descendants, he found no one—only two birds resembling yellow orioles accompanied him in and out, soaring in the sky and descending at his call, able to convey speech from their beaks.

**Pei Chen’s Joyous Reunion; Orchid Fragrance’s Mystic Tryst**
*The Extensive Records* state: Pei Chen once befriended Wang Jingbo and Liang Fang as fellow seekers beyond the world, entering the mountains to study the Dao. After Liang Fang died, Jingbo descended the mountain to take office. During the Zhengyuan era, while on a mission to Huainan, he chanced upon Pei Chen. Mooring his boat, he greeted him: "What do you need?" Chen replied: "We are rustics, our hearts as free as clouds and cranes. West of Guangling lies my dwelling." Suddenly, he poled his boat away. Jingbo visited the dwelling and saw Chen, resplendent in robes and cap, hosting a feast. As Chen grew merry with wine, he told his attendants: "This man was once my companion in the mountains. His resolve for the Dao was not steadfast, and he harmed himself with worldly brilliance, drifting in the sea of birth and death, seeking the shore in vain. Today’s joyous reunion is truly rare—let him depart now."

Also, a fisherman by the Xiang River heard the whimper of a small child. Looking around, he saw no one—only a girl of about three. He took her home and raised her for over a decade, her beauty and bearing extraordinary. One day, a green lad descended from the sky, took the girl away, and told the fisherman: "I am the immortal maiden Du Lanxiang, sent to the mortal world for a transgression. My allotted time has ended—I depart now."

**The Banished Immortal Calls Rats; Master Zhu Raises Chickens**
*The Extensive Records* state: During the Yongming era, a recluse named Cai lived on Mount Zhong. He kept several dozen old rats, which came at his call and went at his command. His speech was strange, and people called him a banished immortal.

*The Biographies of the Immortals* state: Master Zhu was a man of Luo. Dwelling on North Mountain, he loved raising chickens. For over a hundred years, he kept a thousand fowl, each with a name. At dusk, they roosted in the trees; by day, he released them to scatter, and they returned when he called their names. He amassed a fortune of ten million coins, which he spent to dig a pond in Wu for raising fish. Ascending Mount Wu, he was often accompanied by hundreds of white cranes and peacocks.

**Li Jue’s Glittering Trade; An Gong’s Cold Meditation**
*The Continuation of the Immortals’ Biographies* states: Li Jue lived in the city of Guangling, making his living by selling salt. At fifteen, his father passed the trade to him. Li Jue measured out each sheng and dou himself, never considering the price’s rise or fall, yet his clothing and food were always abundant. At eighty, he did not abandon his trade. It so happened that Minister Li Jue was appointed military governor of Huainan, and the salt merchant changed his name to Kuan. One night, the minister dreamed he entered a grotto palace where a golden inscription bore the name Li Jue. Delighted, he suddenly saw two immortal lads emerge from a stone wall. Li Jue asked: "Where is this?" The lads replied: "This is the Huayang Grotto-Heaven. This name does not belong to you, my lord." The minister said: "If not mine, then whose?" The lads answered: "One of your subjects."

Li Jue awoke with realization and searched throughout the streets and alleys until he found one named Li Kuan. He welcomed him into the prefecture and bowed as an elder brother. When asked about the cultivation of the Dao, Kuan replied: "I know nothing of such practices." He recounted only his affairs in selling salt. The minister said: "This is a task most men find difficult—your hidden merit is beyond compare." Later, Kuan departed the world through *shijie*, shedding his mortal form like a cicada.

*Accounts of Immortals*: Tao Angong was a master smith who repeatedly conducted the firing process. One day, the flames suddenly scattered upward, their purple hue piercing the heavens. Angong prostrated himself beneath the furnace and pleaded for mercy. In an instant, a vermilion sparrow alighted upon the furnace and said: "Angong, Angong, your smelting has reached communion with Heaven. On the seventh day of the seventh month, a crimson dragon shall come to carry you away." When the appointed day arrived, the crimson dragon indeed appeared, and Angong mounted it to ascend into the heavens.

Ma Xiang’s paper tricks, Zhang Zhen’s clay steed.

*Further Accounts of Immortals*: Ma Xiang, styled Ziran, was a native of Qiantang. From youth, he loved literature and had the protection of the Dao. One day, passing a vegetable garden, he saw an abundance of radishes and begged for some. The gardener refused, so Xiang tore a piece of paper from his basket, fashioning it into a fox, then another into a heron. The heron flew into the garden while the fox chased after it, trampling all the vegetables to bits. Realizing Xiang was no ordinary man, the gardener knelt in apology. Xiang then retrieved the paper fox and heron, returning them to his basket—the vegetables appeared as before, entirely undamaged.

*The Master Embracing Simplicity*: Zhang Zhen lived during the reign of King You of Zhou. Though repeatedly summoned, he refused to serve. He studied under Master Chang Sang and mastered his teachings, able to divide his form and transform his shadow, turn blades of grass into dragons and tigers, and spray water into pearls and jade. One day, while traveling with his disciples, he molded clay into a horse and rode it, covering a thousand *li* in a day. Later, he entered Kongtong Mountain and ascended to the heavens in broad daylight.

The wife of Cheng provided silk; the mother of Su longed for fresh fish.

*The Biographies of Divine Immortals*: The wife of Cheng Wei, a *qimen* officer, possessed divine transformations. When Wei was to accompany the imperial procession but lacked proper attire, he grew deeply anxious. His wife said, "Why fret over missing clothes when you must depart?" She then produced two bolts of silk, which suddenly appeared before him. Later, Wei attempted alchemical transmutation repeatedly but failed. His wife took a small measure of powder from her pouch, cast it into molten mercury and silver, and in an instant, it became silver. When Wei pressed her for the method, she refused, saying, "Your bones are not yet destined to receive it." When he persisted, she departed through *shijie* transformation.

*The Biography of Immortal Su*: The Immortal Lord, surnamed Su and named Dan, was a native of Bang County in Guiyang Commandery. Born during the Former Han, he lost his father in youth and served his mother, Lady Pan, with such filial devotion that his morning and evening care for her warmth and meals surpassed all others. He was once granted Daoist arts by divine immortals and could conceal his form and alter his appearance. One day, as he warmed his mother’s bed, she said, "I crave fresh fish. Go and prepare it." He replied, "As you wish." Picking up his chopsticks and pausing his meal, he took up gold and left. In a moment, he returned with the fish. His mother, still eating, was delighted and asked, "The market is so far—where did you obtain this so quickly?" He answered, "I bought it in Bian County." She said, "The mountain roads to Bian County are treacherous, a hundred and twenty *li* away. How could you return so swiftly? You deceive me." He replied, "When I bought the fish, I saw my uncle in the Bian market. He told me he would come here tomorrow. Wait for him to verify the truth." The next day, his uncle indeed arrived and recounted their meeting in the market. Only then did his mother realize he was no ordinary man and secretly observed him. She saw him always carrying a green bamboo staff, which all suspected to be a divine rod.

A Daoist with two mouths; the master drew ankles.

*Wang’s Records of Seen and Heard*: Liang Kuangzhi resided in Ezhou when a Daoist suddenly arrived. Kuangzhi drank with him, and the Daoist requested silk, which was given. The Daoist rolled it up and tossed it into his sleeve, saying, "Enter the sleeve." Though he tried again and again, nearly ten times, the silk would not enter. The Daoist sighed, "I cannot escape being Lord Kou of Lai." He then departed, promising to return. Over a month later, he came again. The gatekeeper sought to announce him, but the Daoist said, "No need to see the attendant gentleman. Just report that I have gone ahead." When Kuangzhi arrived later, he was demoted to Huazhou. Long after, a Daoist with two mouths—and a third on his belly—arrived at the hall. He demanded a *dou* of wine, drained it in one draught, and said to Kuangzhi, "Do you recall our meeting in Ezhou?" Though the voice was the same, his appearance was not. He then asked for twenty-seven coins and left.

The Daoist exited the gate, threading the eaves through his belly, and blew an iron flute with his mouth. He took a fishing boat and stood alone upon it, the wind carrying him across the river as he departed. Twenty-seven days later, Kuangzhi passed from this world.

*The Biographies of Daoist Scholars* states: Master Jianji, whose courtesy name was Xiude, was a native of Dongqian in Wuxing and a descendant of Sun Kai, Prime Minister of Wu. His mother, née Yao, dreamed during her pregnancy that an old woman told her, "The child you bear shall become a teacher of humans and gods." When he was born, his ankles bore cloud-like markings, and his palms were inscribed with large characters. Though born into a wealthy family and early engaged in marriage and officialdom, he once remarked to his colleagues, "Such an era is rare indeed." He then abandoned his wife and children, cast off worldly affairs, and devoted himself wholly to the teachings, never slackening in wakefulness or sleep. He chose to dwell on Mount Lu and was summoned to Jinling. One day, he said to his disciples, "I shall return to my former retreat." Soon after, he lay down and transformed, his skin radiant, an extraordinary fragrance suffusing the air. All the disciples in the mountains saw the Master returning to the mountain, his robes and appearance resplendent. They marveled, but in an instant, he vanished.

Fan Ying extinguished flames with water, Zhu Lun rode the mist on high.
*The Book of Han* records: Fan Ying, a native of Jinan, was skilled in divining the patterns of heaven and earth, penetrating the subtle and obscure. He secluded himself on the southern slope of Mount Hu, refusing all summons. Once, a violent wind arose from the west, and Ying said to his disciples, "A great fire rages in the city market." He then took water into his mouth, faced west, and sighed, noting the exact time. Later, a traveler from Shu Commandery arrived and reported that on that very day, a massive fire had broken out, but in an instant, a black cloud rose from the east, and a heavy rain extinguished the flames. Ying once said, "Master Fuqiu once traveled with me atop the Altar of Heaven." One day, he departed and was seen no more.

*The New Record of the Cinnabar Terrace* states: Zhu Lun, styled Deyuan, held fast to the One without wavering. The Most High was moved to descend, revealing the treasures of the jade casket and bestowing upon him the elixir talisman, saying, "If you can cultivate the Supreme Dao with diligence and guard it with steadfastness, I shall ensure your ascension and transcendence, riding the mist in emptiness. Obedience shall bring blessings; negligence shall bring calamity. You must practice this faithfully and never transmit it lightly." When Zhu Lun attained the Dao, during the reign of King Jing, the Most High conferred upon him the scriptures and appointed him an immortal official of Mount Song. He carried the Dragon Staff of Holding the One and wielded the Serpent Whip of Clarity and Emptiness.

*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* records: Zhao Baoyi was twelve years old when, while herding cattle, he encountered an old man who asked, "Are you hungry?" The boy merely nodded. The old man reached into his bag, took out food resembling a radish but sweet in taste, and gave it to him, along with a staff and a gourd. Inside the gourd was medicine—peas.

The medicine, when administered, cured all who suffered from deep-seated or recent ailments. The master himself consumed the radish-like food and no longer craved cooked meals. One day, wandering aimlessly, he arrived at Balou Monastery west of the capital. Passing the Nirvana Hall, he heard groans and asked who was in such distress. A monk replied, "Several novices have fallen gravely ill with an epidemic." The master took peas from his gourd, had them swallowed with fresh water, and immediately the patients broke into sweat as if freshly bathed; by evening, all had recovered. Thereafter, those seeking the medicine thronged like a marketplace, and its fame spread beyond the city walls.

At the time, Emperor Zhenzong had not yet returned from his eastern suburban sacrifice. Prime Minister Xiang, acting as regent in the capital, secretly sent men to verify the reports and swiftly memorialized the throne. When the imperial carriage returned, the emperor summoned the master and, delighted, patted his back, saying, "You share my surname." He bestowed imperial favor, tonsured him as a Daoist, and soon after granted his request to return home. The emperor presented him with a gold-inlaid dragon-headed staff, a bronze seal, a fragrant wooden cane, and medicinal herbs, dispatching the eunuch Zhang Maoxian and the Daoist Hu Taiyi to escort him to Mount Shimen. There, a True Serenity Abbey was specially built as his place of transcendence.

*The Supplement to the Biographies of Immortals* records: Liao Chong, styled Qingxu, was a native of Lianshan Prefecture. Renowned for his talent and virtue, he served as the prefectural registrar. Later, he resigned his office, traveled in search of the essentials of the Dao, and on Mount Heng encountered the Perfected One of Great Peace, who transmitted the Way to him. Upon returning to his hometown, he often rode a tiger, wielding a snake as his whip. In the second year of the Xiantian era, amid darkening winds and clouds, he soared aloft and vanished.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* states: Liu Bang, a native of Pei, rose from commoner status to overthrow Qin and pacify Xiang Yu, laying the foundation of Han. For his merit, the Celestial Emperor appointed him as an honored guest of the Southern Clarity Lord. Master Mao said: "Those whose ancestors possessed hidden virtue may directly assume office as immortals. Some enter the Southern Palace to undergo transformation, regardless of rank, and all swiftly proceed to the Southern Palace for refinement."

Moreover, in the mountains of Huayin lived Yin Qianzi, a practitioner of the Dao during Emperor Wu’s reign. He met an extraordinary person who taught him the Way of Cinnabar Radiance. After fifty years of practice, his complexion remained that of a child. Now, the Supreme Unity Lord has sent for him; he ascended on clouds to the Primordial Realm and became a High Immortal.

Peng Keng’s comings and goings, Zhong Lun’s lingering delight.
*The Declarations of the Perfected* records: Peng Keng was Peng Zu, who lived eight hundred years without resorting to the dissolution of the corpse. The Lady of Southern Marchmount said: "The lords themselves choose whether to appear or withdraw, to retreat into the mountains and forests, to delight in roaming as immortals, or to ascend into emptiness as extinction. It is not that they cannot ascend to heaven—they simply do not wish to."

*The Divine Immortals of the Wang Clan* states: Wang Zhonglun once resided on Mount Heming. A stone there emitted sounds of its own accord. Tian Xuan saw it and asked, "Who is that?" He replied, "I am the immortal Wang Zhonglun. I love the strange resonance of this stone, so I linger here to listen."

Here ends the second scroll of *Records of the Immortals of the Three Caverns*.
The third scroll of *Records of the Immortals of the Three Caverns* follows.

Chen Baoguang of the Orthodox Unity Dao, compiler. *Mingqi’s Maple Chamber* and *Lady Zhen’s White Terrace*.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* states: On Mount Heng there was a Dao practitioner named Ye Mingqi. During the late Wei dynasty, he entered the mountains to ingest herbs and elixirs. Together with Zhang Lizheng, he dwelled in a secluded cave, where he received the cinnabar method from the Lord of Western City. Forty-three years had passed, and his inner and outer vision was thoroughly clear. He could walk five hundred *li* in a day. Now he resides in the Fangzhu Chamber as an Upper Immortal.

The Lord of the Register of Destiny further states: The Halls of Yiqian and Tongchu are the male and female abodes. Those who preside over the earthly realms and rank highest among them may attain the halls of the perfected immortals. Now the Female Perfected Zhao Sutai has resided in the Yiqian Hall for four hundred years and refuses to leave, saying that nowhere under heaven is more delightful than this place.

Master Mao’s kestrel chicks and the Saintly Maiden’s goose eggs.

*The Records of the Immortals* states: Master Mao studied the Dao in Qi. After achieving the Way, he governed Mount Mao. When the sick came to make vows, he would often boil ten kestrel chicks and place them within his curtain. After a moment, Master Mao would toss the chicks back to them. Upon breaking them open, if they were found to be without yolks, the patient would recover. If there was earth inside, the illness would not be cured—this was his constant method. The kestrel chicks remained as before, with no sign of having been broken.

*The History of the Southern Dynasties* states: Xiao Ang was the governor of Pengcheng. At the time, there was a woman of about twenty, with unbound hair and yellow robes, dwelling in a stone chamber on Mount Wuque. She practiced no particular cultivation, ate very little, and occasionally went out to drink a little wine and eat a goose egg, calling herself the Saintly Maiden. Those who came seeking children often had their wishes fulfilled, and visitors filled the mountain valleys. Xiao Ang summoned and questioned her, but she gave no response. Taking her for a demonic deluder, he had her flogged thirty times, whereupon her wounds festered. Later, she vanished without a trace.

Daoist principles tame deer; the lord’s friend leads hounds.

*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* records: Daoist Wang Daoyi knew that Mount Zhongnan was the site where the Perfected Master Yin Xi had ascended to transcendence. During the Taihe era, he led six or seven disciples from Mount Gushe to reside at Louguan. In the third year of Yongping, his master ascended through transformation; white clouds filled the chamber, and an extraordinary fragrance suffused the courtyard. A white deer appeared outside in the yard, sometimes visible, sometimes hidden, for ten days. After the burial, it vanished entirely. Villagers later saw Daoyi riding a white deer as he soared into the heavens.

*The Tales of the Thatched Pavilion* relates: In Shicheng Township of Xiao Han County in Suizhou, a villager named He Junyou encountered a Daoist at the Cloud-Top Mountain Temple. The Daoist, clad in plain robes and deer-leather shoes, led a black hound and instructed Junyou to carry a gourd and staff to Mount Qingcheng. At first, they followed an overgrown path, where strange rocks flanked the way, slender bamboo and peach blossoms lined the route, and flying springs murmured over rapids. Upon reaching a Daoist abode, the Daoist told Junyou to place his bag and staff on the steps, saying, "You bear the marks of a transcendent." He then gave Junyou a single pellet of medicine to swallow, saying, "On the eighth day of the ninth month, you must return here." He sent Junyou home, who thereafter felt no hunger or thirst.

On the appointed day, as evening fell, the valleys were bathed in bright moonlight and clear wind, and Junyou awaited something with quiet anticipation. At dawn, clouds and mist intertwined in five-colored hues. Junyou gazed upward, treading the void, as auspicious winds and radiant mists arose in abundance. He then crossed towering ravines and layered peaks, vanishing from sight as one strained to follow.

Gan Pu recognized Tao; He Pu laughed at Guan.
*The Record of the Cinnabar Terrace* states: Once, a man returning from beyond the Yangtze suddenly encountered someone in a tiny boat shaped like a deer’s head, with a single attendant, gliding straight toward him over the waves. The stranger called the man by name, saying, "Gu Daodu is going to the capital. I wish to send a letter to Maoshan for Tao the Recluse." He was just about to write it when he suddenly said, "Never mind. You will not go to the mountain after all. I shall deliver it myself." When the man reached the capital, he indeed hurried off to Guangling. Later, when Zi Liang asked Master Hong about this, Hong replied, "That was Gan Pu, an immortal of Mount Song. He had known Tao in a former life, not in this one. How could he come here again?"

*The Lives of Eminent Daoists* records: Xing He Pu, of unknown origin, lived in seclusion by the Bohai Sea. At the time, Fang Guan served as magistrate of Lushi County and treated He Pu with great kindness. One day, as they strolled hand in hand, they unknowingly walked dozens of li. Arriving at Xia Gu Village, they came upon an abandoned Buddhist hall, where pines and bamboos cast deep shade. They sat beneath them, and He Pu struck the ground with his staff, instructing his attendant to dig. After several feet, they unearthed an earthen jar containing a letter from Lou Shide to Yong the Elder. He Pu laughed and said to Guan, "Do you recall this?" Guan, his temples shaved, remembered that in a past life he had been a monk named Yong. He Pu added, "You are destined to become a grand minister. Take good care of yourself."

Mei Gu walked on water; Dao Hua ascended the pine.
*The Garden of Marvels* relates: During the Qin dynasty, by a lake in Danyang County stood a shrine to Mei Gu. At times, a Daoist named Daoyan would appear, wearing shoes and walking upon the water. To this day, on the dark and full moon nights at the shrine, one can see the faint outline of shoes amid the water’s mist.

*The Lives of Eminent Daoists* records: Hou Daohua, whenever he found damage in the abbey where he lived, would take up axe and adze to repair it, enduring hardship without complaint. One day, while renovating the main hall, he suddenly found a cinnabar pellet in the rafters and swallowed it. Before the abbey gate stood a pine tree, and Daohua climbed it to trim its branches. When others reproached him, he replied, "I fear they will hinder my ascent." All thought him mad. Seven days later, at dawn, Daohua climbed to the top of the pine, waved his hand in farewell, and said, "I have received a decree from the Jade Emperor, appointing me Attendant of the Immortal Terrace and Keeper of the Faithful Chapel of the Upper Clarity Palace. I depart now." He left behind a poem: *"The Great Return Elixir, wrapped in silk, / For many years its hue unchanged. / Last night, stolen and swallowed— / Today, I fly into the azure sky."* Moments later, music arose from the clouds, and banners and pennants appeared faintly as he ascended into the heavens.

Lingwei’s immortal crane; Song Xian’s dragon-man.
*The Records of Spirits* states: At the eastern gate of Liaodong stood a stone pillar, upon which perched an immortal crane. Unaware of its nature, people tried to shoot it. The crane took flight into the sky and sang: *"There is a bird, there is a bird—Ding Lingwei! / Gone from home a thousand years, now I return. / The city walls remain, but the people are no more. / Why not learn the Dao, that your tomb may lie empty?"* Thereafter, people placed two cranes upon the pillar, and this practice began.

*The Unofficial History of Jin* records: Song Xian was a man of literary talent who resigned from office and refused all social intercourse, living in seclusion in Jiuquan. Three thousand disciples studied under him. The governor of Jiuquan had his portrait painted in the pavilion and, whenever he entered or left, would bow to it, reciting: *"What stone do you pillow? What stream do you rinse? / Your form may be seen, but your name cannot be sought."* He then visited Song’s gate and sighed, saying, *"Your name may be heard, but your person cannot be seen. / Your virtue may be revered, but your form cannot be beheld. / From this day forth, I know that you, Master, are the dragon among men."*

Wang Ding dwelt beyond the world; Guaiya dreamed within.
*The Miscellaneous Records of Elegant Words* states: Wang Ding composed songs and poems and was fond of immortals, his heart roaming beyond the material world. Some of his contemporaries believed he had attained realization, but when asked, he never revealed anything. Over a hundred of his poems circulated among people, such as: *"The wind shakes the rotten branch, startling the crane to flight; / The water carries mountain fruit, drifting toward men."* To Cheng Mingfu, he wrote: *"Before the ancient county, rapids murmur; / With office idle, the Dao is at ease. / Holding a cup, I listen to mountain crows sing; / Drying herbs, wild apes watch. / A brush lies across the stone crevice where I play the qin; / A fishing rod is tucked behind my ear. / I wonder when Master Tao Yuanming / Will ascend into the clouds."*

*The Records of the Loyal and Steadfast Duke* states: Zhang Guaiya was in Chengdu when he dreamed of visiting the Purple Mansion of the Perfected Lord. Their conversation had barely begun when a report came: "The Honorable Huang Jianji of the Western Gate has arrived." Huang entered wearing a scholar’s kerchief and Daoist robes, and the Perfected Lord descended the steps to greet him, showing great courtesy. He then seated Zhang below Huang and spoke to him with warm regard, as if in admiration. The next morning, Zhang dispatched an official to the Western Gate to summon Huang Jianji, instructing him to come in his usual attire. When Huang arrived, he appeared just as in the dream. Zhang then told him of the dream and asked, "What hidden virtue do you possess, to be so honored by the Perfected Lord?" Huang replied, "I have no special talent. Each year, when rice and wheat ripen, I use money to buy and store grain. When the next year’s harvest is not yet ready and the common folk face hardship, I sell it back to them at the same price, without raising the cost or altering the measure. For me, there is no loss, yet the people are helped in their time of need." Zhang said, "This is why you were seated above me."

Then he ordered the scribe to support Huang and bow to him.

Those who cultivate hidden merits in their youth may ascend by daylight. The *New Record of the Cinnabar Terrace* states: Master Li, styled Shunxing, was a native of Jingzhao. On an autumn equinox evening, a thousand celestial riders suddenly descended and hovered in the air. Two figures appeared in his courtyard and said to him, "The Supreme Emperor has commanded that immortal officials await you in the stone chamber atop the Purple Pavilion. We two are the gods of the Direct Day, sent to summon you. You should prepare your carriage at once." Master Li followed them, crossing peaks and valleys as if riding on flying paper, and in an instant they arrived. There he saw three Perfected Ones, each seated in a different posture, naturally enveloped by five-colored clouds and mist. Their attendants and officials radiated brilliant light, and their banners, pennants, and feathered canopies were beyond description. Master Li bowed and inquired about the Dao of ascending by daylight. The Perfected Ones replied, "You possess accumulated virtue and are destined for an immortal office. To ascend with both form and spirit is no difficulty for you."

The *Divine Immortals of the Wang Clan* records: Wang Shaodao accumulated hidden merits by aiding others, and through the virtue of his deeds, he attained immortality. He often said to people, "When merit reaches three thousand, one ascends to heaven in broad daylight. With surplus goodness, one descends in a cloud chariot. To propagate the Dao without cease is to attain deathlessness. These words are true indeed." The *True Instructions* states: In the Palace of Youthful Beginnings, there was Wang Shaodao, a man of the Han era.

Du Sheng’s dragon soars with sand, Ma Fu’s rats cower at his command. The *New Record of the Cinnabar Terrace* states: Perfected One Du Sheng’s age was unfathomable. He abstained from grain and drank only water, yet appeared as a man of twenty. He could write the character for "dragon" in sand, float it upon water, and with a shout, transform it into a small dragon that would soar over ten feet high, trailing mist and clouds. When called, it would descend at once. The *Divine Immortals* records: Ma Xiang wandered through famous mountains, and none knew he possessed the Dao’s protection. When the prefect of Changzhou, Lord Ma, heard of him, he summoned him and treated him as an elder brother. The prefect said, "There are many rats in the city." Ma Xiang then wrote a talisman and had it posted beneath the wall. With a long whistle, all the rats gathered. He called the largest forward and said, "You are a creature of heaven, provided with grain to eat. Why do you disturb the lord? I do not wish to kill you, so you should leave at once." The largest rat turned, and the smaller ones prostrated themselves before the steps, then departed in a single file.

Han Xiang at Blue Pass, Yin Xi at Hangu Pass. The *Green Jade Case* states: Han Xiang, styled Qingfu, was the nephew of Han Yu. Free-spirited and unrestrained, he once drunkenly recited:
*"Green mountains, home of clouds and streams,
This place is my abode.
At night, I drink the flowing jade,
At dawn, I chew the crimson mist.
My zither plays the tune of jasper,
My furnace refines cinnabar.
The golden tiger guards the cauldron,
The dark field nurtures white crows.
A gourd holds the world,
A three-foot blade slays demons.
I brew wine in an instant,
And make flowers bloom in a moment.
Whoever can learn from me
Shall together behold the immortal blossoms."*

Han Yu asked, "How can you alter creation and make flowers bloom?" Han Xiang replied, "This is quite easy." He took soil, piled it in a basin, and in an instant, two blue lotuses bloomed, resembling peonies. Their leaves bore small golden characters: *"Clouds veil the Qin Ridge—where is home? Snow chokes Blue Pass—my horse cannot advance."* Han Yu did not understand the verse’s meaning. Later, when Han Yu was demoted to Chaozhou for his memorial on the Buddha’s relics, he encountered a great snowstorm on the road. There he met Han Xiang, who said, "Do you recall the lines on the flowers?" When he inquired about the place name, it was Blue Pass. Han Yu then realized and continued the poem:
*"A memorial submitted at dawn to the nine-tiered heavens,
By dusk, I was banished eight thousand leagues to Chaoyang.
I sought to purge corruption for a sagely reign,
Daring not, in my frailty, to cling to these fading years.
Clouds veil the Qin Ridge—where is home?
Snow chokes Blue Pass—my horse cannot advance.
I know you have come from afar with deep intent—
To gather my bones by the miasmic river’s side."*

Later, Han Xiang accompanied Han Yu to the Yuan and Xiang rivers, and none knew where they went. The *Extensive Records* states: Lord Xi, magistrate of Hangu Pass, divined the winds and knew a divine person would pass through. He swept the road for forty *li* and recognized Laozi upon seeing him. Laozi had not yet transmitted his teachings in the central lands, but knowing Yin Xi’s destiny, he tarried at the pass and imparted the essentials of the Dao.

Yu the True Mother’s retinue, Huang the True Lord’s paternal line. The *Records of the Western Mountains* state: True Lord Yu, whose personal name was Lie and style Daowei, was known for his filial piety toward his mother. His mother was also devoted to cultivating the Dao. Together they journeyed to the Western Mountains to see Perfected Lord Xu. They kowtowed and pleaded for instruction. Moved by their sincerity, Perfected Lord Xu had a chamber built to the west of his residence for them to dwell in and taught them the Dao’s arts. When Perfected Lord Xu ascended, Daowei and his mother both received the Jade Emperor’s decree, and all of Perfected Lord Xu’s household ascended to heaven.

Additionally, True Lord Huang’s personal name was Renlan, and his style was Ziting. His father’s name was Fu, styled Wanshi, renowned for his lofty conduct and filial piety toward his parents. The prefecture and commandery recommended him as a filial and incorrupt candidate. During Emperor Mu’s reign, he served as a censor. Ziting was skilled in minor arts and possessed a refined disposition. Perfected Lord Xu gave his daughter to him in marriage. Wanshi served Perfected Lord Xu with the rites of a disciple. When Ziting received the Jade Emperor’s decree, he ascended to heaven with his parents and household. Today, in Xiangfu Abbey of Gao’an County, a cinnabar well remains—it was once his residence.

Xiu the Duke transforms into a sheep, Yin Cheng pities the deer. The *Arrayed Traditions of Divine Transcendents* states: Duke Xiu Yang secluded himself in a stone chamber on Mount Hua, consuming yellow essence. The chamber had a stone stool, and as he sat upon it, the stone wore through. When Emperor Jing heard of his fame and summoned him, the duke transformed into a white sheep upon the stool, inscribing its flank: *"The sheep duke thanks the Son of Heaven."* Later, the emperor placed the stone sheep on a terrace, but the duke transformed again and vanished, his whereabouts unknown. The *New Record of the Cinnabar Terrace* states: Yin Cheng possessed the Dao’s arts. Though Qin Shi Huang repeatedly summoned him, he refused to appear. Weary of the clamor, he delighted in the mountains and forests, changed his name to Yin Linzi, and fled deep into the woods.

At times, Yin Cheng would encounter a lame deer hobbling painfully, unable to advance; moved by its suffering, he applied medicine to dress its wound. Later, caught in a great storm with provisions exhausted, he found himself stranded in the thickets. Just then, a doe approached, and in hunger he suckled its milk, while in cold he embraced it for warmth, the two remaining together for months on end. A divine being then imparted to him secret formulas, enabling him to regenerate bones and shield the sick. At the age of three hundred and forty, he ascended to heaven in broad daylight. The *Declarations of the Perfected* refers to him as Yin Linzi, who in the late Han entered Mount Mao, where he was later revered as a Perfected One.

*Devoted to the Real, Riding Tigers; Ziying Catching Fish.*

The *Transmissions of Eminent Daoists* states: Lü Zhi Zhen, a disciple of the Master of Broad Accomplishment, dwelt in a stone chamber for over a decade, skilled in healing others with medicinal stones. When he entered the forests and valleys, tigers and leopards followed him, yet when asked about the Dao, he remained silent, offering no response. Once, on the road through Mount Shang, he suddenly paled and halted. Those around him were astonished, but he said: "Ahead, bandits approach." Soon after, armed men appeared, and Zhi Zhen addressed them: "All who pass here are my disciples—do not disturb them." The bandits bowed and stepped aside, no longer daring to advance. Later, arriving at a valley’s mouth, he declared: "Here dwells something that harms men."

The companions wished to see it, so they entered the forest, where Zhi Zhen bound two tigers with rope and led them out. From this, all knew that Zhi Zhen had deeply attained the Dao of Broad Accomplishment.

*The Traditions of Immortals* states: Ziying was a man of Shu Village who excelled at diving into water to catch fish. Once, he caught a red carp, and admiring its color, took it home and placed it in his pond, feeding it grains of rice and millet. Within a year, it grew over a *zhang* in length, sprouting horns and wings. Terrified, Ziying apologized to the fish, which said: "I have come to welcome you. Mount my back, and I shall carry you away." Suddenly, a violent storm arose, and Ziying climbed onto the fish’s back, soaring into the sky and vanishing. He left behind two dogs and one donkey.

*The Traditions of Immortals* states: Zixian, surnamed Hu, was a diviner of the Han era who lived over a hundred years. Fond of wine, he was once generously hosted by an old woman who never asked for payment. One day, he suddenly told her: "Prepare quickly—we must depart together." That night, a divine being arrived bearing two straw dogs. Zixian and the woman mounted them and entered Mount Huayin, where he cried out: "The wine-seller’s mother is here!"

*The Supplementary Traditions of Immortals* states: Shen Jian possessed the arts of prolonging life and reversing death. Once, while traveling afar, he entrusted a donkey, a dog, and sheep to a host’s home, giving each a single pill before departing. The host remarked: "This guest is strange—he leaves fifteen creatures and not a single coin. What am I to do?" Three years later, Jian returned, and the servants, donkey, and sheep remained unchanged. He ascended lightly, sometimes departing, sometimes returning, for over three hundred years, though none understood him. Later, he vanished without a trace.

Han Zhong’s sesame and red cups; Tingrui’s calamus.

*The Comprehensive Records of Immortals* states: Le Zichang of Qi, fond of the Dao from youth, entered Mount Huolin, where he met Han Zhong, who taught him to ingest the *Sesame and Red Cup Elixir*. Though eighty years old, his complexion remained like a maiden’s. Later, he ascended as an immortal and was appointed Keeper of the Gate of Cultivation.

*The Elegant Talks of Prefectural Pavilions* states: The Daoist master Shen Tingrui, son of the former Minister of Rites Bin, dwelled on Jade Bamboo Mountain. Whenever he encountered deep mountains or ancient caves, he would forget to return for days. One day, he bid farewell to a singing girl and sat in the Pavilion of Assembled Immortals. After reciting the verse *"How fleeting is human life!"*, he passed away. Two years after his burial, Zeng Zhaoying of Mount Gezao encountered him several *li* from the mountain gate. Though Gezao and Jade Bamboo Mountain lay a hundred and sixty *li* apart, when Zeng asked where he was bound, Tingrui replied: "I am briefly visiting friends on Mount Lu." He left a poem in parting:

*Paths stretch north and south, east and west,*
*Yet human lives must meet at times.*
*Once I dwelt on Gezao’s slopes,*
*Then ascended to the Capital of Yuan.*
*Clouds drift vast across the sky,*
*A spring’s voice falls on lone stones.*
*Who could have foreseen this early meeting?*
*Together we boil calamus and herbs.*

When Zeng returned to Jade Bamboo Mountain and spoke of this, they realized the master had achieved liberation in the flesh.

Ju Feng’s unkempt mane; Chiman’s dwarfed frame.

*The Miscellaneous Records of the Northern Dream* states: During the Tongguang era of Later Tang, a mad scholar named Sang Ju Feng, filthy and disheveled, acted contrary to human norms, his strange powers beyond measure. When he visited the prefectural office to pay respects to the Prince of Nanping, he wore one straw sandal and one hemp shoe, earning the nickname "Wild Man."

*The Records of Heyang* states: Prince Qianyang of Tang, Li Qian, was a heavy drinker, surpassed only by the scholar Ye Fanneng. The prince pressed him to drink, but Fanneng refused, saying: "I have a disciple whose capacity rivals yours. Tomorrow, I shall send him to meet you." The next morning, a calling card arrived bearing the name *Daoist Chang Chiman*. The prince hastily summoned him. Though small in stature, his spirit shone with radiance, and his voice rang clear. After sitting, he discoursed brilliantly on cosmology. The prince ordered wine, and the Daoist drank readily. The prince poured rich wine into a large goblet, filling it with a massive cuttlefish. Though the prince grew flushed with drink, the Daoist remained poised and refined. After a long while, he suddenly said: "I am drunk after just this one cup." The prince urged him on, but the Daoist replied: "Do you not know that capacity has limits? Why press me further?" When pressed again, he collapsed to the ground—revealing himself to be a large wine jar, holding five *dou*.

Jade Pool’s white oranges; Cangzhou’s green dates.

*The Traditions of Divine Immortals* states: King Mu of Zhou met the Queen Mother of the West at Jade Pool, where they feasted on water lotus, black dates, jade-like lotus roots, and white oranges.

*The Miscellaneous Records of Guiyang* states: During the Sui dynasty, the recluse Yuan Cangji served as an adjutant to a maritime envoy. When his ship was wrecked in a storm, he drifted on a plank to an island, where the locals said: "This is Cangzhou, ten thousand *li* from the realm." The flowers and trees there always bloomed as in the second or third month, and few ever died. The land bore twin-lobed melons two *chi* long, green dates and red grains as large as pears. In the ponds swam four-legged golden fish and golden lotus flowers, which women plucked for adornment—without a golden lotus in her hair, none could approach the immortals’ abodes. Longing for the Central Kingdom, Cangji was sent back by the islanders in a wind-cutting boat that sped like an arrow, reaching Donglai in ten days. When he asked about the realm, he learned it was now the Tang dynasty—two hundred years had passed since the Daye era.

Youying’s five mushrooms; Fenggang’s hundred herbs.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* states: Lady Youying chanted:
*"With heart I pledged to the Woodcutter’s Son,*
*Promising to seek the five mushrooms.*
*If the magic herbs elude your grasp,*
*You too shall never come."*

*The Extensive Records* states: Fenggang was a man of Hanyang who gathered the flowers of a hundred herbs, steeping them in water and sealing them in mud. Beginning in the first month and ending in the ninth, he buried them for a hundred days, then pressed and decocted them into pills. When placed in the mouth of the recently dead, they revived instantly. After consuming the medicine for several years, Fenggang never aged. Later, he entered the Earth’s Lung Mountain.

Gongcheng’s ease; Songlai’s sweeping.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* states: Zhao Gongcheng once suffered from a foot ailment that prevented him from walking. In his heart, he silently bowed to the Most High.

After thirty years of unwavering devotion, he was moved by the Most High to bestow a dose of medicine, which, upon consumption, cured him instantly. Later, he attained the Dao and now resides on Mount Heming. During his practice, he never once wavered in his focus—this is what is meant by "inwardly refining the Great Primal" and "reposing in the divine palace."

Furthermore, Songlaizi served as Market Overseer during the reign of Duke Zhuang of Chu. He would often sweep the marketplace when he encountered a yellow-robed beggar singing in the streets:

*"Twin blooms burst from Heaven’s Court,*
*The mountain’s source wards off yin’s harm.*
*At dawn, I rein the celestial steed,*
*And journey to the home of the Great Perfected."*

The beggar sang this as he begged in the marketplace, yet none recognized him—only Songlaizi understood. Thereupon, he honored him with the rites of a master. After thirty years, the beggar transmitted to Songlaizi the Way of the Middle Immortals, and he now dwells on Central Peak. It was then known that the beggar was none other than Feng Yanshou, the Perfected of Western Peak. The annotation states: *"The hands are the celestial steeds; the nose, the mountain’s source."*

**Gongsong’s longevity; Dongguo’s agelessness.**

*The Master Embracing Simplicity* states: Gongsong possessed literary talent and lived for several centuries, his complexion like that of a child. He encountered the immortal Gan Ji, from whom he received writings that extensively discussed the waxing and waning of yin and yang. There is the Way of Heaven, the Way of Earth, and the Way of Man. Those who govern the state employ it to achieve great peace; those who cultivate the body employ it to preserve longevity—such is its Way.

*The Biographies of Divine Transcendents* states: Dongguo Yan, a native of Shanyang, consumed the *Lingfei Powder*. When he wrote at night in a darkened chamber, his body emanated light. He could also gaze afar and discern small objects dozens of *li* away on level ground, perceiving even their colors.

Those throughout the world destined to die—whether acquaintances or strangers—he could foresee with unerring accuracy, and all came to pass as he predicted. He remained ageless in his village for four or five hundred years, until one day dozens of men riding tigers and leopards arrived to welcome him. All his neighbors witnessed this. He bade them farewell and departed, entering the Kunlun Mountains.

Cai the maiden embroidered phoenixes; Zhihe carved simurghs.

*The Extensive Records* states: Cai the Immortal Maiden was a native of Xiangyang. From childhood, she was clever and skillful, excelling in embroidery, which earned her renown among her neighbors. One day, as she finished embroidering a pair of phoenixes, their five-colored radiance shimmered like rosy clouds. Suddenly, an old man arrived at her door, requesting to see them, and offered to set their eyes. Once the eyes were placed, the phoenixes took flight. The old man and the maiden each mounted a phoenix and departed, descending into the forests south of Xiangyang Mountain. Later, the mountain was renamed Phoenix Grove Mountain, and Phoenix Grove Pass was established. Her dwelling was consecrated as the Phoenix Grove Serene Truth Abbey, where an image of the female Daoist was enshrined.

Additionally, Han Zhihe was a foreigner who entered the Middle Kingdom and served as an imperial guard. He carved wooden figures of simurghs, cranes, crows, and magpies, installing mechanisms within their bellies. When activated, they soared a hundred paces through the air. He could also craft dragon-drawn chariots; when one stepped upon them, their fangs and claws all moved, twisting and coiling as if in flight. Emperor Xianzong admired his work and bestowed lavish rewards upon him. Yet Zhihe distributed all his gifts to others and later vanished, his whereabouts unknown.

Yue Ba shattered the temple, and Gu Qing’s coffin burst open.

*Extensive Records* states: Yue Ba served as the Merit Officer of Chengdu. The prefect asked him, “Does the Merit Officer possess the Dao? May we test it?” Yue Ba replied, “Very well.” He then entered the wall, ascending slowly like mist or cloud, and vanished without a trace. Later, he was appointed Gentleman of the Palace and promoted to Prefect of Yuzhang. On a mountain there stood a temple of great efficacy, capable of parting the winds for boats on the river. Upon Yue Ba’s arrival, the spirit vanished. Yue Ba declared, “This temple spirit is deceitful.” He then demolished the temple. The spirit fled, transforming into a scholar who traveled to the State of Qi, where the prefect gave his daughter to him in marriage. Yue Ba journeyed to Qi, met the prefect, inscribed a talisman, and let out a long whistle, summoning the scholar to appear. He rebuked the spirit, which turned into a raccoon dog, and Yue Ba slew it.

*Biographies of the Immortals* records: Gu Qing was a Gentleman-in-Attendance during the reign of Emperor Cheng. Stricken by illness, he died, yet his body did not stiffen. He was placed in a coffin without sealing it. Two years later, he was seen wearing a green cap, sitting composedly atop the county gate. His family came to welcome him, but he did not descend. When the coffin was opened, no corpse remained. Later, he entered Mount Taibai, where the people built a shrine for him, and Gu Qing would occasionally appear within it.

Liang Bo sought Wei, and the Filial and Benevolent Emperor honored Han.
*The Biographies of Divine Transcendents* states: Emperor Xiao dispatched the emissary Liang Bo into the mountains to seek Wei Shuqing, but he found only his son, Du Shi. Together, they entered Mount Taihua to search. Du Shi glimpsed his father playing a board game with several others upon a stone, seated on a white jade platform. Du Shi asked who the players were, and his father replied, “Master Hong Ya, Xu You, Chao Fu, and Wang Zijin.” He added, “I have a formula for transcendence hidden beneath a pillar in my dwelling.” Du Shi returned and dug there, uncovering a jade casket sealed with the imprint of a flying immortal. Inside was five-colored mica. Du Shi consumed it and ascended as an immortal.

*Records of Forgotten Tales* states: During the reign of Emperor Hui of Han, the Daoist Han Zhi, a man from the State of Jue, spoke of an envoy from the Kingdom of Nili who had come to court. The envoy stood four feet tall, with two horns like seals, teeth protruding from his lips, and dwelled in deep caves. His lifespan was immeasurable. The emperor sent Han Zhi to ask how many dynasties he had witnessed. The envoy replied, “Five generations have succeeded one another, dying and being reborn like motes of dust or fine rain—existence and oblivion cannot be reckoned.” He also recounted events from the eras of Nüwa and the Yellow Emperor as if they were before his eyes. Han Zhi reported this to the emperor, who sighed, “Vast and obscure! Without divine insight or profound understanding, one cannot speak of such distant matters.” Han Zhi then withdrew, and his whereabouts became unknown. The emperor commanded various alchemists to establish an immortal’s hall north of Chang’an, naming it the Shrine of Han.

Ruo Shan cast off his sandals, and Ren Dun abandoned his office.
*Extensive Records* states: During the Kaiyuan era, Tang Ruo Shan governed Runzhou. Daily, he would set sail upon the river with a few colleagues and guests to visit Jinshan Temple. Midway across the current, a dense fog obscured all, rendering even nearby objects indistinct. Ruo Shan alone saw an old fisherman rowing a boat toward their vessel. He boarded the fisherman’s boat and departed in a transcendent manner. Upon the table in his quarters, a letter of farewell and a memorial to the throne were found. The memorial read in part: “Though my fate aligned with an enlightened age and I was repeatedly honored with rank, I awakened to the principle of rise and fall and deeply understood the rule of knowing when to cease. My heart dwelled at the mysterious gate, and I early attained the true formula. Gold can indeed be wrought, as the ancients of Huainan once declared; the white sun’s span can be extended, as the wondrous use of the true scriptures reveals. Having obtained this, what more could I seek? Thus, I cast off the red dust and soared to the azure vault. Penglai is not distant, and Fusang lies within sight. Gazing afar at the imperial palace, I am overwhelmed by the devotion of a dog or horse.” When Emperor Ming read the memorial, he marveled at it.

*The Biographies of Daoist Scholars* states: Ren Dun, styled Zishang, lived at the beginning of the Yongjia era when the realm was in turmoil. He abandoned his office, crossed the river southward, and resolved to live beyond the mundane world, dwelling in the mountains and forests. Suddenly, a man over ten feet tall appeared. When Ren Dun questioned him, the man claimed to be a messenger sent by Lord Anaku to inquire after him. Ren Dun asked, “Where does the Lord usually reside?” The man replied, “He is often in the heavens but also roams the mortal world. When he passes through, he sometimes lingers in the Great Smelter or the Immortal Charts.” Later, Ren Dun foresaw the calamity of Sun En and underwent corpse liberation upon Mount Muzhao.

Stilwell’s golden palace, Gongyuan’s azure vault.
*The Original Biography* states: The Jade Emperor decreed to Perfected Ge...

Long had Hong transmitted the essential teachings of the heart, excelling in nurturing the prenatal true qi. He expounded divine remedies to heal the ailments of living beings and widely disseminated the ancient classics amidst the mortal world. Through hidden merits, he aided all creatures, and by secret practices, he aligned himself with the perfected. His name was inscribed in the Jade Capital, and his person ascended to the Golden Portal. Thus, it was fitting that on the third day of the third month at the hour of *yin*, he mounted his chariot and ascended to heaven.

*The Original Biography* states to Perfected Luo: Gongyuan could dispel water spirits, succor living beings, exorcise malevolent forces, and expel demons. He summoned dragons to bring forth rain, possessed the virtue of administering medicinal cures for convulsions, and the merit of deploying talismans to dispel illness. Within, he cultivated the Three Ones; without, he nurtured the Four Kinds of Life. His name was renowned in the Immortal Capital, and his person returned to the Azure Vault. Thus, it was fitting that on the fifteenth day of the first month at the hour of *wu*, he rode a chariot drawn by a red dragon to return to heaven.

Shen Xi rode the dragon and tiger; Gongyang soared with phoenix and crane.

*The Master Who Embraces Simplicity* records: Shen Xi, a man of Wu, studied the Dao in Shu. He saved people and benefited all beings, his virtue moving the Celestial Thearch. One day, while traveling, he suddenly beheld a chariot drawn by a green dragon and another by a white tiger, each attended by a dozen figures in crimson robes, bearing staffs that lined the road. They asked, “Have you seen Master Shen the Daoist?” Xi replied, “I am he.” The attendants said, “You have rendered service to the people. The Yellow and Lao Lords have dispatched immortal officials to welcome you as Attendant of the Azure Vault.” Thereupon, he ascended to heaven.

*The Records of the Western Mountains* state: Zhongli Jia, styled Gongyang, was the nephew of Lord Xu the Upright. Fond of dwelling in forests and peaks, Lord Xu cherished his aptitude for receiving the Dao and entrusted him with secret instructions, bidding him cultivate them in secrecy. When Lord Xu ascended, he foretold the day of Gongyang’s transcendence. On that day, purple clouds descended from the heavens, and green phoenixes and white cranes danced in his courtyard. Immortal youths and jade maidens descended to welcome Gongyang, who ascended in broad daylight.

Lord Bo’s silk writings; Lord Gan’s immortal elixir.

*The Biographies of Divine Transcendents* record: Master Yu had suffered from leprosy for decades, and no medicine could cure him. One day, he saw an old man selling remedies in the market, surnamed Bo. He approached and inquired, and the man said, “I can save you.” He bestowed upon him two scrolls of silk writings, saying, “This will not only cure your illness but grant you long life.” Master Yu bowed and received them. Pondering their meaning, he applied them inwardly to cultivate his nature and body, and outwardly to dispel calamities and heal illnesses—none failed to be cured. Having perfected the Dao, he departed as a transcendent.

*The Records of the Western Mountains* state: Perfected Lord Gan, styled Bowu, was esteemed in his village for his filial conduct. Wielding his sword, he followed Perfected Lord Xu to subdue demons, contributing greatly to their efforts. Lord Xu transmitted secret instructions to him, and Lord Gan concealed his form and shadow, eluding all perception. One day, celestial music suddenly resounded from the heavens, and in an instant, auspicious clouds and radiant rosy mists shone forth as he ascended to heaven.

Lord Zheng of Kun Terrace; Zilian of Wei Pavilion.

*The Green Locks* records: The presented scholar Niu Yi departed the capital and rested beneath the shade of a willow. He dreamed he arrived at a grand mansion, where an attendant said, “This is the Jade Palace.” Upon closer inspection, he recognized his old friend Lü Zhen, who said, “Wu. Presides over this office.”

Niu beheld a white jade tablet upon the hall, inscribed in vermilion seal script and veiled in crimson gauze, bearing in large characters: *Register of Heavenly Immortals of the Central Realm*. Below were listed several thousand names, among which he recognized only a few—Lü, Yi Jian, Li Di, and Yu Jing. Niu inquired about the details of these heavenly immortals, and Lü replied, “There is an order and true mandate to these things, which are not for one such as you to know.” Niu asked, “Are the present-day ministers and chancellors mostly immortals?” Lü answered, “Seven or eight out of ten.” Niu said, “Minister Fu Bi is the state’s elder statesman—could he truly be an immortal?” Lü replied, “Minister Fu is the Perfected Lord of Kun Terrace; at ninety-three years of age, he returned to the Kun Terrace Palace.” Niu asked, “What office do you now hold?” Lü said, “After three hundred more years, I shall be appointed as the overseer of the earthly realm.” Niu asked, “And what office does the Son of Heaven hold?” Lü answered, “He presides over the Five Peaks, Four Waterways, and the famous mountains and great rivers.”

*The Comprehensive Record of Marvels* states: The Daoist of Mount Heng, Shuai Zi Kang, was dissolute and fond of wine, his nature rough and quarrelsome, quick to insult others with words. Few associated with him, and so he was nicknamed “Ox.” He dwelled in the Wei Pavilion upon the mountain. When the Vice Minister of Rites, Lord Wang You, was serving as Prefect of Tanzhou, at the onset of summer he was to offer sacrifices to Zhu Rong. Arriving at Mount Heng, he sought out the so-called Wei Pavilion. Upon arrival, he found Zi Kang drunk and asleep. Lord Wang spoke with him and found him most extraordinary, so he took him back to the prefectural seat and drank with him daily. When Zi Kang asked to return to the mountain, he once more took up residence in the Wei Pavilion. One day, he suddenly told others, “I shall soon depart on a distant journey and must bid farewell to Lord Wang.” That very day, he took a small boat downriver to Tanzhou to pay his respects, saying, “I am bound for another place.” Lord Wang detained him to drink, and after two days, Zi Kang took his leave. On the appointed day, he wrote a letter to Li, the abbot of the Hengshan Daoist temple, then bathed, donned his robes, burned incense, held his ritual tablet, and shed his mortal form. Those who heard of it were astonished, and Li purchased a coffin to bury him with full honors. Nearly half a year later, a monk from Mount Heng arrived at the Anshang Gate in the capital. Zi Kang came to see the capital, saying, “On my journey here, I was most generously treated by Abbot Li.” From his sleeve, he produced a letter to be delivered to the monk in thanks. When Li opened the seal, it was indeed a letter in Zi Kang’s own hand. All marveled at Lord Wang’s silent discernment.

*Records of the Multitudes of Immortals of the Three Caverns*, Scroll Three—concluded.
*Records of the Multitudes of Immortals of the Three Caverns*, Scroll Four.

Chen Baoguang of the Orthodox Unity Dao, compiler and editor, reveres the masters:

**The Master He**
The *Biographies of Divine Immortals* states: On Mount Heng there lived a Daoist who never revealed his name. When asked his surname, he would say, “He,” and when asked his given name, he would say, “He.” Thus, the people of his time called him Master He. Someone once asked, “Master, you never speak—how then do you enlighten those who come after?” He replied, “To know and yet claim not to know is supreme; to not know and yet claim to know is a flaw. Who could carve open the apertures of primordial chaos to grasp the principle of spontaneity?” Then, staff in hand, he entered the mountains, and tigers and leopards followed him. Master Sima remarked, “This may be called one whose talents are complete yet whose virtue remains unmanifest.” Later, he underwent liberation by means of the corpse, and with a clap of thunder, his body vanished.

**The Venerable Pupupu**
The *Extensive Records* states: The Venerable Pupupu’s origins were unknown. He claimed his surname was Pu, and so the people of his time called him the Venerable Pupupu. He dwelled on Huangtu Mountain in Guangzhou for over thirty years, consuming apricot elixirs while eating and drinking like an ordinary person. He sold medicines for a living, yet no one recognized him. When he encountered Wang Bian, he transmitted to him the secret of the apricot elixir, and in that very moment, the master ascended into the clouds. Prefect Li Xiuguang heard of this and deemed it inauspicious. The master then descended to Xiuguang’s residence, whereupon Xiuguang said, “If you are an immortal, you should depart and not return—this is demonic.” The master replied, “Both Lady Ma Gu and Lord Mao sought the Dao from me. Why do you call me demonic?” Xiuguang ordered his attendants to seize him, but suddenly the Lords of the Dragon and Tiger appeared before them. The master ascended, clouds and mist swirling on all sides, thunder and lightning shaking the earth. The onlookers fled in terror, and Xiuguang apologized for his offense. Emperor Minghuang issued an edict to establish an official abbey, which stands to this day.

**Three Pines of Master Hou’s Abbey; Two Bamboos of Su’s Hermitage**
The *Biographies of Eminent Daoists* states: The Daoist Hou Kai, styled Faguang, was a native of Jingzhao. During the Wei dynasty’s Zhengshi era, he became a Daoist and was transmitted the secret formulas of astronomy. His morning and evening devotions grew only more diligent with time. He chose to dwell in Hangul Valley, where he practiced the Three Transformations of the Primal Qi, recited the *Great Cavern Scripture* and the *Inner Writings of the Three Sovereigns*, and mastered the methods of summoning and exorcism. His abode was encircled by clear springs, and three pines spread their branches overhead, creating a secluded and tranquil retreat—his true dwelling place. Thus, it was named Three Pines Abbey.

The *Collected Writings of the Bang River* states: Su the Perfected One’s mother lived to be over a hundred years old. Without illness, she passed away suddenly, and the villagers prepared a coffin and buried her with proper rites. That day, on the summit of Niupi Mountain northeast of the prefecture, purple clouds enveloped the trees, lingering without dispersing. It seemed as though a white horse galloped among the groves, and wails of mourning were heard from the mountain ridges—all saying that Lord Su had returned to observe mourning for his mother. The villagers rushed to the site, where before his thatched hermitage, the ground was level and smooth, and two bamboos swayed without wind. When swept, the earth remained perpetually clean. After three years, the wailing ceased, and the white horse was no longer seen.

**Hu Fang’s Golden Exhortations; Yongbo’s Jade Cultivation**

**The Biographies of Divine Transcendents** records: During the Tang dynasty, Chen Xiufu, styled the Seven Masters, resided in Baocheng during the Zhenyuan era. He tilled the land and gathered firewood like an ordinary man, yet possessed many arts of transformation. Five to seven curious youths sought to learn his arts, practicing diligently without rest. Mid-lesson, he suddenly collapsed and died, his body swiftly decaying. The youths fled in terror, none daring to look back, and from then on, no young man dared disturb him. Hu Fang of Changming once became his disciple. When Hu Fang was about to take office, he left five thousand coins for Xiufu to buy wine. Xiufu laughed and declined, saying, "I have an abundance of gold and jade—what I regret is being unable to use it all." He handed Hu Fang a hoe and instructed him to till the earth. After digging no more than two or three inches, gold, jade, coins, and goods emerged with each cut. Xiufu said, "The things of this world are indeed like this, but each person’s allotted share is fixed. One should not take more than their due—if one were to use them, would there be any limit?"

**Extensive Records** states: Yang Yongbo was renowned for his filial piety. When his parents died, he buried them on a high mountain and built a hut beside their grave, weeping day and night. A sweet spring welled forth, providing water for travelers. One day, a man leading a horse arrived and gave Yongbo a pint of white stones, saying, "Plant these, and fine jade will grow." Indeed, white jade disks, over two feet long and countless in number, sprouted. One day, while traveling, Yongbo chanced upon the sea and met an immortal named Wei, who said, "This is the jade-planting Yongbo." Another immortal remarked, "Your filial devotion has moved the divine spirits. This office will one day be your dwelling." Years later, Yongbo and his wife ascended together. Their former residence is now called the Jade Field Quarter.

Dao achieved, he rode a mule; reverence paid, he bid farewell to the deer.

**The Bangjiang Collection** recounts: During the Qingli era, a scholar-recluse traveled to Mount Tai and called upon Registrar Guo Jifu. After taking his seat, Guo examined the visitor’s calling card, which bore the name Luo Daocheng. Upon inquiring about his origins, the scholar replied, "I am from Bangzhou." Guo invited him to stay for a drink, and the scholar said, "I have long wished to journey to Shuzhou. I arrived at Mount Tai a few days ago and have already explored it thoroughly. I will soon return south." He then borrowed brush and paper to compose a poem:

*Drawn by the thought of numinous splendor, I came to wander,*
*Emerald jade peaks rise in ten thousand autumnal layers.*
*Climbing to the heights of Great Peace, I gaze afar—*
*Roots entwine, linking over a dozen provinces.*

He then wrote a matching verse:

*My tracks drift with water and clouds, my days spent in idle roaming,*
*The summer valley’s chill surpasses autumn’s clarity.*
*Even apes and birds cling to old affections—*
*Turning back, I depart for the seaside provinces.*

Guo was deeply moved by the poems. After the scholar left, Guo sent a servant to escort him to his lodging and gave the servant another poem to deliver:

*White mule swift as racing clouds, his steps unhurried,*
*Wherever the wanderer goes, poems mark his trace.*
*If you wish to know his name and origins,*
*Look upon the stone beneath Bangyang Mountain.*

Later, Guo inquired of the people of Bangzhou and learned of the White Mule Perfected Lord’s Abbey. There, Luo Daocheng had attained the Dao and ridden a white mule across stone cliffs, leaving hoofprints that remain to this day.

**Records of the Western Mountains** state: Perfected Lord Xu, named Xun and styled Jingzhi, was originally from Xuchang but later moved to Yuzhang. From birth, he was quick-witted and handsome, unrestrained in his youth, fond of hunting. Once, he shot a fawn, which fell to the ground. Its mother licked it with her tongue, and before long, the fawn died. Moved by this, Xu destroyed his bow and arrows and devoted himself to study. By his early twenties, he had mastered the classics and histories, as well as music, astronomy, geography, and the Five Phases. He was especially drawn to the Dao of divine transcendents and later ascended with his entire household.

Liao Fu of the northern outskirts; Wang Xu of the eastern marsh.

**Biographies** record: Liao Fu of the Later Han, indifferent to worldly affairs, devoted himself to the classics, particularly astronomy, apocrypha, and divination by wind and celestial patterns. Though official posts were offered, he declined them all, earning the name "Master of the Northern Outskirts."

**The Tang Dynasty’s Biographies of Recluses** state: Wang Ji, styled Wugong, was appointed Assistant Magistrate of Liuhe County in Yangzhou but resigned to return home. He owned several dozen *qing* of land on an islet in the river, near which lived the hermit Zhongchang Zixian, who practiced dietary and meditative arts. Admiring his purity, Wang wished to live nearby. He built a hut on the islet, delighting in zither and wine, and was thus called "Master of the Eastern Marsh."

Dong Feng ate millet; Manqian stole peaches.

**The Biographies of Divine Transcendents** record: Dong Feng, styled Junyi, was from Houguan. Living on Mount Lu, he did not farm but treated illnesses without accepting payment. Instead, he asked those cured of serious ailments to plant five apricot trees and those with minor illnesses to plant one. Over the years, the apricot trees grew to ten thousand, forming a dense grove where birds and beasts played beneath, and no weeds ever sprouted, as if perpetually tended. When the apricots ripened, Feng told people, "Those who wish to buy apricots need not announce themselves—simply take them, exchanging one measure of grain for one measure of fruit." Once, someone brought little grain but took many apricots, and a tiger roared and chased them away. The grain he received was used to aid the poor and travelers, consuming three hundred *hu* annually, yet much remained. One day, he ascended to heaven.

*The Han Emperor Wu’s Inner Chronicle* records: Emperor Wu suddenly saw a girl in green robes and was told, “On the seventh day of the seventh month, the Queen Mother of the West will briefly descend.” The Emperor asked Dongfang Shuo who this was, and Shuo replied, “She is the Purple Orchid Chamber Maiden of the Queen Mother of the West, who conveys messages to and fro.”

On the appointed day, the Emperor, clad in his finest robes, stood upon the steps. In the deep of night, he heard the sound of flutes and drums in the sky. The Queen Mother arrived in a chariot of purple clouds, drawn by nine-colored striped dragons. Other celestial immortals, each a *zhang* in height, accompanied her. The Queen Mother ascended the hall and personally arranged a refined feast, presenting seven luscious peaches in a golden bowl. The Emperor ate them and found them exquisite. The Queen Mother said, “This peach bears fruit but once every three thousand years. Dongfang Shuo has stolen them three times already.”

Mountain fathers with raven-black hair, Yao Hong with green fur.

*The Master Who Embraces Simplicity* states: Bo Xianfu dwelled on Mount Hua, cultivating his spirit without eating. After two hundred years, his hair remained dark as youth. Later, he transmitted his teachings to a woman, whose complexion bloomed like peach blossoms. An old man with snowy beard and brows once arrived, and the woman struck him with a switch. When asked why, she said, “This is my son. He refused to practice Xianfu’s method, so he aged prematurely. That is why I punish him.”

*The Tang Annals* recount: During the Tang dynasty, a monk lived on Mount Nanyue. One night, he saw a creature with green fur covering its body approach his seat. The monk said, “This humble monk abides in meditation and harms no living thing.
