If spirits and gods have awareness, do not disturb me.” The creature replied, “Do you know of the Jin and Song dynasties?” The monk said, “From the Jin to the Tang, four hundred years have passed.” The creature asked, “Do you know of Yao Hong?” The monk said, “I have heard that Hong is dead.” The creature said, “I am Hong. At the time, I feigned death to escape, roaming the blessed lands. Only the cold cypress leaves caused fur to grow over my body.”

The Queen Mother tapped time; Zideng played the zither with pride.

*The Collected Records of Immortals* states: The Nine Radiance Primordial Lady, the Queen Mother of Mount Gui, the Three Origin Ladies, and various perfected immortals descended into the Crimson Chamber of the Lesser Existence Pure Void Palace. The Lady and the Perfected Lord acted as host and guest, each commanding their attendants to arrange the celestial zither’s melodies. The Nine Voids harmonized in rhythm, and the Eight Sounds resonated with divine resonance. The Queen Mother tapped time and sang.

*The Han Emperor Wu’s Inner Chronicle* records: The Queen Mother commanded her attendant Wang Zideng to play the Eight Jade Pillars, Dong Shuangcheng to blow the Cloud Harmony Flute, Xu Feiqiong to strike the Numinous Void Drum, and An Faying to sing the *Mystic Numinosity* melody.

One asks the long-lived scholar—he is the mountain’s prime minister.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* states: The Long-Historian was surnamed Xu, named Mi, styled Sixuan, a descendant of the seventh generation’s progenitor. The Lord was broadly learned and of the highest rank, first serving as Magistrate of Yuyao, later ascending through the ranks to become Palace Secretary and Protectorate Long-Historian. He cultivated the Supreme Dao in secret, and perfected immortals descended frequently. A True Consort bestowed upon him a scripture, saying: "Jade nectar and golden broth, life-nurturing divine pears, square-patterned fire jujubes—these shall be given to the mountain-dwelling Daoist Xu, not to the worldly Long-Historian Xu."

Furthermore, Tao Hongjing, styled Tongming, passed the imperial examinations and was promoted to Reader-in-Attendance. At the age of thirty-seven, he harbored no ambition for officialdom, declaring: "Confucius said, 'Dwelling in tranquility to pursue one’s aspiration, practicing righteousness to fulfill the Dao.' Now that my righteousness is fulfilled, I have no further direction. I shall follow the path of pursuing my aspiration." Thereupon, he styled himself the Recluse of Huayang, removed his court robes and hung them at the Gate of the Divine Tiger, donned a deer-fur cap and Daoist garb, entered Huayang, and dwelled on Mount Jijin, where he compiled the *Declarations of the Perfected*. Emperor Wu of Liang repeatedly summoned him, but he did not go. Whenever the emperor had matters to discuss, he dispatched envoys to inquire, and the people of the time called him "the mountain’s prime minister." He presented the Dao before the emperor and served the immortals in the celestial realms.

*The Divine Immortals of the Wang Clan* records: Wang Fajin, from his youth, was devoted to the Dao. One day, he suddenly encountered two immortal youths who told him: "You possess the bones of an immortal and have not forgotten the Dao. The Celestial Thearch has commanded us to come and welcome you, to serve in the heavens above." Unaware, he followed the two maidens, ascending through the void until he stood before the Thearch, who said to him: "To dwell amidst the Three Powers is not easily attained. I entrust you with the *Code of Atonement* in one scroll. Descend and instruct the people—this, too, shall be your merit." During the Tianbao era, he ascended to heaven in broad daylight.

Moreover, Wang Fengxian was a maiden of the Xuan people. In her youth, she encountered over ten youths clad in azure, playing and laughing with her from dusk till dawn. Her parents suspected them of being demons and questioned her. Fengxian replied: "What I have encountered is the Dao, and those I have seen are celestial immortals. At first, I beheld in heaven the ranks of heavenly beings, and one immortal said: 'You possess the bones of an immortal. Fifty years hence, you shall return here. However, the grains of the hundred crops harm one’s true qi.'" Thereafter, Fengxian abstained from food. She often told others: "The divine immortals I have seen in heaven differ not from those of the Daoist tradition." She then painted a scroll of heavenly beings in courtly assembly, naming it the *Chart of Heavenly Convergence*.

Yuan Yi compressed the cosmos into a gourd; Fei Changfang cast his staff aside.

*The New Record of the Alchemical Terrace*:

Xie Yuanyi, styled Master Gourd, numbered among the three thousand disciples of Confucius. He often hung an empty gourd in the marketplace to sell medicines. After the day’s business concluded, he would compress himself into the gourd, unseen by anyone in the market—save for Fei Changfang, who observed him from an upper story. Changfang went to pay homage and became his disciple.

Furthermore, when Fei Changfang of Runan served as a market inspector, Master Gourd, recognizing his sincere faith, said to him: "When I compress myself into the gourd, you must imitate me, and you shall enter as well." Once inside, the gourd vanished from sight, revealing only towers of jade and palaces of gold, their forms exquisitely wrought, with jade boys and jade maidens attending in pairs. The Master said to Changfang: "I am an immortal. Do you cherish the Dao?" Changfang pleaded earnestly, and the Master bestowed upon him the arts of exorcising ghosts and healing illnesses, though he could not attain immortality. He also gave Changfang a bamboo staff, instructing him to ride it home and then cast it into the Ge Marsh. Changfang followed his words, throwing the staff into the marsh, whereupon it transformed into a dragon and departed.

Wei Mountain’s Wang Qiao, Fan Brook’s Lü Shang.

*The Biographies of Divine Immortals*:
Wang Qiao, styled Zijin, encountered Master Fuqiu and attained immortality. His friend Huan Liang met Zijin upon Wei Mountain, where Zijin said: "On the seventh day of the seventh month, I shall ascend to heaven. You may gather with my old friends to bid me farewell." On that day, Zijin and his former companion Wei Guan climbed the mountain, where they saw Zijin abandon his horse by the ravine below before ascending to heaven. At that moment, as Guan bowed in farewell, he turned to see the horse he had ridden also soar into the sky. The place is now called the Ravine of the Bidding Horse.

Moreover, Lü Shang, a native of Jizhou, was wise from childhood and foresaw life and death. To escape the chaos of King Zhou’s reign, he concealed himself in Liaodong for over twenty years. Later, he journeyed west to Zhou, hiding in Fan Brook, where he fished for three years without catching a single fish. A passerby remarked: "You should give up." Lü Shang replied: "This is not for you to understand." Soon after, he caught a fish whose belly contained a book on military strategy, said by some to be the *Jade Chime*. King Wen dreamed of a sage and, hearing of Lü Shang’s virtue, carried him back to jointly govern Zhou. After his achievements were complete, Lü Shang announced his departure. When his coffin was opened, no corpse remained—only six scrolls of the *Jade Chime* lay within.

Stone Master of the Eastern Palace, Guangli of the Southern Palace.

*The Declarations of the Perfected*:
The Great Lord Mao said: "Long ago, there was White Stone Master, who refined stone as his essence, hence the world called him Master White Stone. He was a perfected being. Now he has been appointed Right Immortal Minister of the Eastern Palace. He entered the mountains, severed himself from grains, and boiled stones for sustenance. The method for boiling white stones was devised by Stone Master himself."

Furthermore, the Middle Lord Mao said: "Han Taihua was the younger sister of An Guo and the wife of General Li Guangli of the Han. Guangli, having rendered meritorious service to the world in his lifetime, now resides in the Southern Palace to undergo transformation."

Ziyang’s summoned attendants, Lu Lian’s soaring ascent.

*The Preface to the Tianpeng Rites*:
Deng Ziyang entered Mount Magu, reciting the divine incantations of Tianpeng day and night. A golden-armored divine being appeared and spoke to him: "I am the Messenger of the Six Heavens of the North. Your devout recitation of the sacred scripture has been noted by the Lord Emperor, who has already recorded your merit." Thereupon, he bestowed upon him the true form of the Black Seal divine talisman, which possesses the protection of immortals above, the power to command spirits and gods in the middle, and the efficacy to heal diseases below. "You must keep this secret," he said. "In time, you shall become the teacher of kings." The next day, Deng indeed found the true form talisman within a stone chamber, and its practice proved efficacious.

*The Divine Immortals of the Wang Clan*:
Wang Lulian was the daughter of the immortal Wang Gang. Having inherited her father’s Dao, she entered the mountains of Luhun and never emerged. Later, she encountered the Perfected One of Grand Unity, who transmitted to her the method of *soaring ascent*. Through cultivation, she ascended to heaven in broad daylight.

Guo Wen tamed tigers; Qu Jun rode dragons.

*Supplement to the Biographies of Immortals*:
Guo Wen, styled Wenju, was a man of Luoyang who dwelled in the Great Wall Crag. One day, a tiger opened its jaws before him as if to make a plea. Guo Wen reached into the tiger’s mouth and removed a bone. The next morning, the tiger brought a dead deer and placed it outside his stone chamber. Thereafter, the tiger remained docile at his side, so gentle it could be stroked and played with. When Guo Wen left the mountains, the tiger followed him. Even in the bustling city among crowds, the tiger walked beside him, its gaze meek, never daring to act fiercely—no different from a sheep or dog. At times, it carried books, salt, and rice back to the mountains. The Jin Emperor, having heard of this, summoned him to court and asked, "Master, do you possess some special method for taming tigers?" Guo Wen replied, "It is only natural. If humans harbor no intent to harm beasts, beasts will have no intent to harm humans. What method could there be? Treat me well, and even tigers become like the people; treat me harshly, and even people become like tigers. What difference is there?" The Emperor admired his words and offered him an official post, but Guo declined and retired to Mount Aoting.

Qu Jun was a man of Nan’an who, during the reign of Emperor Zhang of Han, lived in seclusion on Pinggang Mountain. The Yellow Emperor descended and transmitted to him the *Dragon-Riding Dao*, enabling him to control and ride cloud-borne dragons. Later, he entered Mount Emei to cultivate the *Luminous Mirror of the Grotto Chamber*. When he was about to ascend to heaven, he bid farewell to his family and friends, then departed riding a dragon. Today, on Pinggang Mountain, there remains Dragon Crag, said to be the tethering post of Qu Jun’s dragon.

Yellow Stone beneath the bridge; Li Zheng within the grotto.

*Records of the Grand Historian of the Former Han*:
Zhang Liang passed by an old man who dropped his shoe beneath a bridge. Turning to Liang, he said, "Boy, fetch my shoe." Liang retrieved it and presented it to him. The old man said, "You are teachable." He then set an appointment, saying, "Meet me here in five days." This occurred three times. Each time Liang arrived as promised, the old man said, "It should be so." He then produced a book and gave it to Liang, saying, "Study this, and you shall become the teacher of kings. Thirteen years later, you will see me beneath the mountain north of Qi, at the place called Yellow Stone. That stone is I."

The Lady of the Southern Marchmount said: "Sincerity is the hinge of gain and loss. Zhang Liang’s three appointments demonstrate his unwavering devotion to the Dao and clarity of heart."

*The Declarations of the Perfected*:
The Middle Lord Mao said: "Li Zheng of Henei once practiced the *Guardian Unity Dao*. Initially, he resided in Luoyang, but later moved to the grotto of Huayang, where he oversees the investigation of matters concerning the people."

Zhao Sheng slept in the open; Ma Xiang slept upon a wall.

*Biographies of Divine Immortals*:
The Celestial Master Zhang Daoling possessed the *Nine Cauldrons of the Great Essentials*, which he entrusted only to his disciple Wang Chang, saying, "Afterward, there will be one who comes from the east. He shall receive this. This person will arrive at noon on the seventh day of the first month." He described the man’s appearance in detail. Sure enough, Zhao Sheng arrived from the east. Though they had never met before, his form and countenance matched exactly what Daoling had foretold. Daoling tested Sheng seven times, and each time he passed. Only then did he transmit the *Scripture of the Elixir* to Sheng. The first test: when Sheng first arrived at the gate, he was not announced. Daoling had others curse him for over forty days, yet Sheng remained, sleeping in the open without leaving.

*Continuation of the Biographies of Immortals*:
Ma Xiang, styled Ziran, possessed the Dao. Once, while traveling through the borders of Changxi County, he sought lodging for the night. The host jested that there was no place to stay, saying, "You may sleep on the wall if you wish." As dusk fell, his disciples were anxious for shelter, but Ma Xiang said, "You may sleep among the crowd. I shall sit until dawn." Everyone else slept, but Ma Xiang leapt onto a beam, dangling upside down by one foot, his body suspended as he slept. The host rose in the night and, seeing this by candlelight, was astonished. Ma Xiang said, "If I can sleep on a beam, what difficulty is there in sleeping on a wall?" Soon after, he entered the wall. The host begged his forgiveness, and only then did Ma Xiang emerge.

Ji Qiu played the zither; the fisherman strummed his fishing line.

*Biographies of Immortals*:
Ji Qiu was a Daoist of Mount Tai. During the reign of Emperor Wu of Han, he received rewards for his Daoist arts, his white hair turning black again and his lost teeth regrowing. When the Emperor made his eastern inspection tour to Mount Tai, Ji Qiu came to greet him wearing a *zhangfu* cap and yellow robes, holding a zither.

*Unofficial History of the Five Dynasties*:
Sun Miao, the governor of Xunyang, saw a light boat gliding over the waves near the islet, now appearing, now vanishing. Soon, a fisherman was seen angling, strumming his fishing line, and whistling in the clear emptiness. The governor asked, "Have you caught any fish?" The fisherman replied, "This is not fishing as you know it. How could there be fish?" The governor, struck by his words, gathered his robes and waded through the water to address him: "Sir, I perceive you possess the Dao. Now, in this era of civilized governance, why do you not assist in the bright and flourishing rule?" The fisherman answered, "I am but a madman of mountains and seas, ignorant of worldly affairs. I have not yet escaped poverty and lowliness—how could I speak of wealth and honor?" Then he sang:
*"The bamboo rod, the tangled line,
The river flows on, time’s design.
To forget each other brings delight,
The baited hook, the fish’s plight.
Neither Yi nor Hui am I,
Yet care and sorrow pass me by."*
With that, he strummed his fishing line and departed.

Dao Kai ate coarse food; Shi Tan wore tattered robes.

*The Record of the Thatched Pavilion*: Shan Daokai despised fine food and fine clothing, fearing neither hunger nor cold. He favored dwelling in the mountains, where mountain spirits and tree essences repeatedly tested him, yet he remained unafraid. Later, he moved to Mount Luofu, living alone in a thatched hut, remote from worldly concerns. Over a hundred years later, he underwent liberation through the corpse.

*The Unofficial History of Jin*: Shi Tan claimed to be a man of Beihai, with no fixed abode. He did not seek a permanent dwelling nor fine garments; when his clothes wore out, if someone gave him new ones, he would in turn bestow them upon others. When attending funerals, he would lean on his staff to offer condolences, regardless of distance or weather, arriving at the same time as others—no one could fathom this, deeming him divine. Later, his whereabouts became unknown.

Sima Baiyun, Wu Tan of the Purple Qi.

*The Biographies of Divine Immortals*: Sima Chengzhen was skilled in seal script, creating a distinct style known as the "Golden Scissors Script." He dwelled in seclusion on Yuxiao Peak of Mount Tiantai, styling himself the Master of White Clouds. Emperor Ruizong summoned him; upon his return, court officials composed volumes of poems to see him off, which he titled *Records of White Clouds*.

Also, during the Han, the Emperor’s son-in-law and Commandant of the Cavalry, Wu Tan, styled Zidu, was a native of Beihai. When Emperor Wu of Han was out, Zidu appeared at the Wei Bridge, a purple qi hovering above his head. The Emperor summoned him and asked, "How old are you?" He replied, "Your servant is one hundred and thirty-eight years old." The Emperor questioned Dongfang Shuo, who said, "This gentleman possesses the arts of yin and Dao." Emperor Wu dismissed his attendants and inquired further. Zidu said, "In my sixty-fifth year, I sometimes suffered from back pain, dry mouth, and a runny nose. Since obtaining this method seventy-three years ago, I have fathered thirty-six sons and remain as vigorous as a youth." Emperor Wu received his teachings but did not fully master them; nevertheless, his longevity surpassed that of other emperors.

Qin Gao rode a carp, Huang An sat upon a turtle.

*The Master Embracing Simplicity* states: Qin Gao often wandered through the Jiang and Zhe regions, moving through the waters as he pleased, so that people of the time regarded him as a water immortal. Formerly, when he refined the elixir south of the Zhe River, upon its completion he scattered the dregs into the river, where they transformed into small fish. People thereafter called them "Qin Gao fish." Later, he roamed to Wu, where he rode a red carp and ascended to the heavens.

*The Biographies of the Immortals* records: Huang An was a soldier from Daijun. He was always seated upon a turtle, some three feet wide. When asked how old the turtle was, he replied, "Long ago, when Fuxi wove the first nets and traps to bestow upon me, its shell was already smooth. It emerges only once every three thousand years to avoid the brilliance of the sun and moon, and I have sat upon it through five such emergences."

Pearl garments and peach kernels, letters written on pomegranate rind.

*The Immortal Biographies of the Wang Clan* states: Wang Chu served as magistrate of Wangwu. He constantly recited *The Yellow Court Scripture* six thousand times, yet its profound meaning remained beyond him. After resigning his post, he ceased eating grain and swallowed qi. Entering a grotto, he found a stone chamber where an ancient scroll lay upon a stone table. Wang Chu bowed twice and said, "This humble servant has trespassed into this grotto-heaven, a meeting rare across ten thousand kalpas. Now beholding this mysterious scripture, I humbly beg to cast my mortal eyes upon it but once." Suddenly, a man appeared and said, "I am Wang Taixu, the Perfected One of the Eastern Pole. I composed the commentary on *The Yellow Court Scripture* and now bestow it upon you." He also gave Wang Chu a peach kernel as large as several *dou*, instructing him to grind and ingest it to cure illness and extend his years. "You may not dwell here yet," he added. "Wait another twenty years." Wang Chu carried the kernel and the scripture home with him.

From *A Comprehensive Survey of Divine Marvels*:

The scholar Shen Xie of Huzhou had a father who, in his later years, styled himself "Elder of the East" and frequently entertained guests. He brewed much wine to accompany the viands, and whoever arrived—regardless of high or low station—was invariably offered a cup. One day, a wandering visitor called upon him and drank face-to-face with the Elder of the East. Their lofty discourse rang clear and resonant, penetrating subtle mysteries. Day gave way to night, and though the wine jars were repeatedly drained, the guest remained composed and unruffled. When asked his name and lineage, he gave no answer but instead inscribed on the wall with pomegranate peel:

*The western neighbor, though rich, frets over insufficiency;
The Elder of the East, though poor, delights in abundance.
White wine is brewed to welcome fine guests;
Yellow gold is spent to gather rare books.*

Below, he signed himself "Recluse of the Sun-Return Mountain." The Elder of the East, drunk, lost track of him. After his departure, many took him for Lord Lü. The characters he had written vanished and then reappeared. Su Dongpo’s poem on the matter is quite detailed.

*Jiao Guang’s stone taro,
Yang Xi’s cloud lichen.*

From *The Master Who Embraces Simplicity*:
Jiao Guang dwelt in the mountains, where he would boil white stones until they were as soft as taro and eat them. At times, he would enter the mountains to gather firewood, which he would give to others. When the Wei dynasty received the Mandate, he went to live by the river, building a thatched hut where he sat without a mat. His body was filthy, caked with grime like lacquer. Sometimes he ate only once in several days, and he maintained silence. His appearance was neither old nor young.

Such was his life among men for over two hundred years, after which his whereabouts became unknown.

From *Sequel to the Biographies of Immortals*:
Yang Qing often dwelt on Mount Kuocang. Later, he traveled to the cliffs of Ruan Lang Pavilion, over ten *zhang* above the ground, where large seal-script characters were carved into the stone. Tradition held that these were inscribed by Ruan Zhao of the Han, but upon examination, they proved to be the work of Li Yangbing, who had once served as magistrate of Jinyun. While visiting the pavilion, he had composed a poem:

*From whence comes the traveler Ruan?
The clouds of immortals stretch across the cave’s mouth.
A place untrodden by mortal men—
Today, I walk within.*

Yang Qing, drinking wine with a Daoist of Jinyun during the flowering season beside the pavilion, suddenly collapsed as if dead. He was carried home, where he revived after seven days. He recounted: "At first, a person clad in dark-green robes with crimson trim, who called themself Lingying, invited me into a grotto palace. There, I beheld wondrous towers and halls, phoenixes and cranes, and from a stone cavern, something took flight. Lingying pointed and said, ‘This is the Azure Cloud Lichen. Those who consume it attain transcendence.’ I ate it and felt my body grow light, my steps as swift as flight." Later, he withdrew into seclusion on Mount Weiyu.

*End of Scroll Four of The Assembly of Immortals of the Three Caverns*
*Scroll Five of The Assembly of Immortals of the Three Caverns*

Chen Baoguang of the Orthodox Unity Dao, compiler of *The Lives of Eminent Daoists*, records:

Shanjun the Black Dragon and Shuqing the White Crane—

**The Biographies of High Daoists** states: The Daoist Wei Shanshi sought the Dao, wandering through famous mountains, where he encountered immortals who bestowed upon him the *Summons of the Three Sovereigns*. Thus he attained the Way of divine transcendence. He always carried a black dog named Wulong, "Black Dragon," sharing his food with it wherever he went. One day, intending to visit Shaolin Temple, the monks were at their midday meal when Shanjun led the dog to their side and divided his portion to feed it. A monk said, "Is it right to feed a dog before men have eaten?" Shanjun replied, "I was in error," and made amends. Soon after, he left the temple. The assembly watched as the master walked farther away, and the dog grew ever larger, until it transformed into a black dragon. The master mounted it and departed.

**The Traditions of Divine Transcendents** states: Wei Shuqing consumed mica and attained transcendence. In the second year of the Yifeng era of the Han, on the renchen day of the eighth month, Emperor Wu saw a man riding a cloud chariot drawn by white cranes alight before his palace hall. Startled, the emperor asked who he was. The man replied, "I am Wei Shuqing of Zhongshan." The emperor said, "You are my subject—come forward and speak with me." Shuqing had originally sought an audience with the emperor, believing he would be received with great courtesy, for the emperor was devoted to the Dao. But when the emperor called him a subject, Shuqing was deeply disappointed. He fell silent and did not respond, then suddenly vanished. The emperor deeply regretted his words.

**Ge Xiang sold medicines, Ji Zhu divined fortunes.**

**Sequel to the Traditions of Divine Transcendents** states: Luo Wanxiang, of unknown origin, dwelled long on Mount Wangwu. Later, he journeyed south to Luofu Mountain and sighed, "This mountain is the Vermilion Brightness Grotto-Heaven—Ge Zhichuan once dwelled here. Though Deng Tai did not detain him, I shall remain." He built a hut and secluded himself. Sometimes he wandered the markets, selling medicines and drinking wine, coming and going without fixed abode. If he ate a single meal, it might suffice for several men, yet he did not call it much; if he fasted, none knew the passing of months. In a day, he could travel three or four hundred *li*. Later, he no longer emerged.

**New Records of the Elixir Terrace** states: Sima Jizhu divined fortunes in Chang’an. Song Zhong and Jia Yi, both on leave, went to see him. Jizhu’s aspirations were lofty and wondrous; Zhong and the others suddenly felt lost, their faces blank, mouths agape, speechless. Then they adjusted their robes, rose, bowed twice, and took their leave. Upon exiting the market gate, they could barely mount their chariots, leaning on the rail and bowing their heads, utterly unable to speak. Three days later, Zhong met Jia Yi outside the palace gate and said, "The higher the Dao, the greater the peace; the higher the power, the greater the peril. Dwelling in resplendent authority, one’s downfall is but a matter of time."

**Tai Jingxian shattered a cauldron; Zhao Ming set a thatched roof aflame.**

**Supplement to the Traditions of Divine Transcendents** states: Tai Jingxian, of unknown origin, lodged in the Kuaiji market during the Huichang era. His dwelling was not fixed; sometimes he drank wine and feigned madness, thus passing ten years. Those intrigued by his ways began to suspect his strangeness. The Zhang family of Kuaiji often treated him with reverence. One day, he took a steelyard weight, pressed it in his hand for a long while, and stretched it like taffy. Taking an iron pestle, he hammered it into a cake, and after a time, it reverted to its original form. Zhang had long been poor. Jingxian said, "I shall depart." He told Zhang to take a cauldron and shatter it, binding the pieces with charcoal and piling them in a furnace. He kindled a fire, added elixir, sealed the door, and said to Zhang, "After the charcoal fire dies, you may retrieve what has been transformed. Use it to enrich your household. By aiding others through the Dao, your descendants may enjoy its wealth for generations." The next day, Zhang saw it was all purple gold.

**Baopuzi** states: Zhao Ming and Zuo Ci both used *qi* to restrain water, causing it to flow backward. They also kindled a fire atop a thatched hut to roast a quail; the quail cooked, yet the thatched roof did not burn. They restrained water in the central courtyard—though exposed to bitter cold, it did not freeze. Within a *li*, they could prevent the scorched from feeling heat.

**Qin fled to Peach Blossom Spring; Tian dwelled in Willow Valley.**

**Record of Peach Blossom Spring** states: During the Taikang era of the Jin, the fisherman Huang Daozhen of Wuling rowed his boat up the Yuan and Li rivers and entered a place where peach blossoms lined the banks, their fallen petals swirling. He saw a stone grotto from which a stream issued, its cold waters murmuring like jade. Dwellings stood in rows, pavilions and ponds linked together. Though the men’s caps and women’s robes resembled those outside, their garments were fresh and bright, their complexions radiant. Seeing Daozhen, they were delighted and invited him home, preparing wine and food. When asked about the present era, Daozhen told them all truthfully. They sighed, "How fleeting are the changes of the human world!" Daozhen took his leave, but when he later sought the path to the blossom source again, he became lost and never found it.

**True Reports** states: North of Mount Leiping was once called Willow Valley. A man named Tian Gong came to dwell there. To its north lay Willow Aid Water, also called Tian Gong Spring. It was said that this water was the flowing essence of jade sand; drinking it expelled the three worms from the abdomen.

**Weaver Maiden and Green Crow; Woman Transcendent and White Ape.**

**Record of Duchen** states: The Weaver Immortal Maiden was from Changsha. She entered the Dao and dwelled on Mount Heng. Though over eighty, her complexion remained youthful. She cultivated incense and fire offerings at the immortal altar of Lady Wei, living alone without companions. After several years, a green crow—shaped like a turtledove but with a red crest and long tail—flew to her dwelling. It spoke, "I am the messenger of the Southern Peak Lady. Because you cultivate the Dao with such diligence, dwelling alone in these desolate woods, she has sent me to keep you company." It also said, "The Queen Mother of the West is surnamed Wei—she is your ancestor. The Wei clan of Henan is the former mountain where the Queen Mother cultivated the Dao." Whenever people traveled to the mountain, the green crow would foretell their names. Later, learning of the many troubles across the four seas, she secluded herself on Mount Jiuyi.

**Supplement to the Traditions of Divine Transcendents** states: The Woman Transcendent Xue, of unknown origin, lived during the troubled times of the Jin, when the capital was in turmoil. Those who followed the Dao often took refuge in mountains and forests to avoid the world. She dwelled on Mount Heng at the Seeking Truth Terrace, appearing ordinary yet inwardly cultivating the true Dao. When she went out, yellow crows, white apes, and white leopards often followed her. Later, she ascended to heaven.

**Nie discoursed on inherent nature; Zhang expounded on returning to the origin.**

**Traditions of Divine Transcendents** states: During the Five Dynasties, the alchemist Nie Shaoyuan, styled Master of No Name, built a hut on Mount Wen Zheng, shunning worldly customs. He once composed the *Treatise on Inherent Nature* and *Secret Essentials of Cultivating Truth*. Xu Zuanzhen greatly admired them, saying, "Even Wu Yun and Shi Jianwu could not surpass them."

**Biographies of Eminent Daoists** states: The Daoist Zhang Wumeng, styled Master Hongmeng, once traveled to Tiantai, ascended Red City, and lodged at Jade Terrace. He practiced the guiding and pulling methods of Red Pine and the cyclically transformed elixir of Anqi. Emperor Zhenzong summoned him to lecture on the *Book of Changes*. He expounded on the hexagram *Modesty*, and the emperor asked, "Why speak only of *Modesty*?" He replied, "When things are at their greatest abundance, one should guard them with modesty." The emperor then commanded him to lecture on the *Returning to the Origin*. Wumeng said, "The state is like the body. When the mind is without action, *qi* is harmonious, and harmony gathers the myriad spirits. When the mind acts, *qi* is disordered, and disorder scatters the essence and splendor. This is the great purport of returning to the origin." The emperor was pleased with his words and bestowed upon him the title *Master of Peaceful Abiding*.

**Dongbin on Penglai Isle; Jing Shi in the Cloud Cake.**

**The Elixir Formulae** states: Lü Dongbin sat for the imperial examinations twice at the Ministry of Rites but met with no success. He declared, "Since worldly examinations bring no advantage, I shall cultivate the celestial examination." At the end of the Tang dynasty, he traveled to Mount Lu, where he encountered Master Zhongli and received the Dao. Thereafter, he cherished his *qi* with care. Peddling brushes and ink, he wandered between the capital region and Mount Heng, though few recognized him as Dongbin. The master imparted to him a secret formula:

*"The Three Flowers harmonize and transform into the Fire Dragon,
Emerging straight from the twilight crossroads—a thousand days’ work.
When the proof is complete, the crane-drawn chariot
First returns to Penglai Isle."*

**True Sayings** records: On Mount Shan, two Daoist practitioners, Zheng Jingshi and Zhang Chonghua, received oral instructions from the immortal Meng Deran during the early Jin dynasty and entered the mountains. They practiced the method of guarding the Five Viscera in accord with the days, consuming sesame and elixirs. Over time, they no longer required food or drink, their bodies grew light and strong, and they reverted to a youthful form. One day, the Northern Lord Lao dispatched Taiyi to welcome them with a cloud-plow, and they ascended to heaven in broad daylight. *Fang Feng met West White; Xu Yu encountered East Special.*

**Records of Verified Immortals** states: In the *Preface to the Great Classic of the Yin Talisman* by the Tang-era Prefect of Fang Mountain, it is written: "From youth, I loved the Dao and longed for longevity. Upon reading the *Yin Talisman*, I saw that it spoke of the immortals’ Way of Embracing the One, yet later generations only studied its methods for pacifying the state and governing the realm, seldom grasping the immortals’ supreme joy. In the third year of the Zhenguan era, I traveled to Mount Tai and encountered an elder who called himself West White, though his surname remained unknown. Discussing the *Yin Talisman*, he revealed that its essence lay entirely in the immortals’ Great Elixir—something the world could not comprehend. He then transmitted to me the commentary by the Mother of Mount Li, wherein the Way of Embracing the One was fully revealed."

**Biographies of Divine Immortals** records: Xu Dingci, a native of Pengzhou, served as a laborer in the commanderies and states during the Xianping era. While transporting silk and paper through the pass, he lodged at an inn in Huayin. One night, a scholar who called himself East Special greeted Dingci and sat with him. They found great affinity, drinking together for a day. As Dingci prepared to depart, the scholar said, "I am Chen Tuan. Because your bones are no ordinary ones, I chanced to meet you here." Overjoyed yet awestruck, Dingci earnestly sought esoteric teachings. The scholar replied, "The teachings do not prize the extraordinary; simply conserve your spirit, and do not let likes and dislikes inwardly harm you—this is most excellent." Then, from his sleeve, he produced a *daorui* of medicine and said, "Consume this, and you shall live a hundred years." The next day, Dingci sought him again but found no trace. Later, he too achieved *shijie* (liberation by means of a simulated corpse).

The Three Pengs severed, the Five Natures of Shangyuan perfected.

*Xuanwang Zhi* records: Qixu, a man of solitary and refined spirit and bone, dwelt in a Chang’an Buddhist temple. Fleeing the rebellion of An Lushan, he entered Mount Taibai, where he suddenly encountered a perfected one who urged him to journey to the Zichuan Prefecture. One day, ascending the mountain’s summit, he beheld a city with palaces and towers. In the hall sat a figure of imposing presence, adorned in regal cap and robes, leaning upon a jade table, surrounded by attendants. He asked, "Have you cut off the enmity of the Three Pengs?" Qixu had no answer. The figure warned, "Take care—you must not remain here." Then he led him away. Later, Qixu vanished without a trace. Zichuan was none other than the Perfected Lord Ge.

*The Inner Tale of Emperor Wu of Han* states: The Lady of Shangyuan said to the Emperor, "Do you love the Dao? You have frequently summoned masters of esoteric arts and ascended mountains to offer sacrifices, and the spirits have been moved. Yet your inborn nature is violent, your inborn nature is licentious, your inborn nature is extravagant, your inborn nature is cruel, and your inborn nature is treacherous—these five dwell constantly within your *rongwei* and the five viscera. If from this day forth you cast aside these five natures, return to gentleness and goodness, and ever perform yin virtue by rescuing those in mortal peril, refrain from spilling essence, observe abstinence and diligence, strike the Heavenly Drum, drink Jade Nectar, bathe in the Flowery Pool, and knock upon the Golden Beam—practicing these disciplines, you shall surely witness wonders."

You Jian served as *sizhi*; Lü Huai corrected and rectified.

*The Biographies of the Immortals* recounts: You Jian initially served alongside Zhou Fu as a gatekeeper. Later, he was promoted to Marquis of the Numinous Pass of the Northern Emperor, and then advanced to the office of Illustrious *Sizhi*.

The office of Illustrious *Sizhi* is comparable to the post of *Shangshu Puye* in the mundane world.

*The Eminent Discourses of the Hanlin Academy* records: Lü Huai was deemed worthy to memorialize the throne and was later posted to Anzhou. One day, as he sat alone in a small pavilion and closed his eyes, he beheld a vision—a youth clad in azure who announced: "The Jade Emperor is journeying south to the Scorched Continent, and you, sir, are to accompany him as *Douzheng Weixian*." From that moment, he partook of the Celestial Kitchen’s fare and wandered the Purple Palace. As the Scorched Continent was oppressively hot, the Supreme Deity bestowed upon him a single pellet of *Qingdong* Elixir. Lü bowed in gratitude and swallowed it; as it descended his throat, it was as though ice and snow had melted within. He knew then that his days in the mortal realm were numbered. Later, a certain Zhu Mingfu encountered him on the road to Xiangjiang, where hundreds of armored clerks and soldiers stood in attendance. Lü rode a jade-horned azure deer, and Mingfu bowed, asking: "Are you to become an immortal?" Lü smiled without answering and extemporized a poem:

By chance my merit was inscribed in jade towers high,
While countless robed in dust lie buried ‘neath the sky.
Now I follow the Emperor as *Sizha*—
Who else remains to stand guard at the Cypress Terrace nigh?

Jiwei was appointed *Dinglu*, and Sihe *Baoming*.

*The Records of Lord Mao* state: The Middle Lord Mao, whose personal name was Gu and courtesy name Jiwei, was nominated as *Xianliang Fangzheng* and successively promoted to *Jinwu*. Hearing that his elder brother, the Great Lord Mao, had attained the Dao, he resigned his post and entered the mountains. There, his brother led him to meet the Lord of the Western City, whereupon he achieved immortality and was appointed Perfected of the *Dinglu*. Furthermore, the Lesser Lord Mao, whose personal name was Zhong and courtesy name Sihe, rose through the ranks to become *Wugeng Dafu*. Together with the Middle Lord, he entered the mountains in search of the Lord of Life’s Mandate. The latter introduced him to the Queen Mother of Mount Gui, who transmitted the essentials of the Dao and appointed him Perfected of the *Baoming*. The Great Lord Mao declared: "Now that I depart to assume my duties, we may no longer visit one another frequently. On the eighteenth day of the third month and the second day of the twelfth month each year, I shall summon fellow immortals to descend upon Mount Juqu. If any aspiring immortals or virtuous men arrive on those days, I shall attend to them and instruct those who have yet to awaken." Thus began the tradition of the pilgrimage to Mount Mao.

Dong was revived after death, and Gan healed the sick.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* recount: Lord Dong, a native of Linhuai, practiced *qi*-circulation and alchemical refinement, remaining youthful past a hundred years. One day, he was imprisoned on false charges and feigned death—his body rotted and teemed with maggots. When his remains were returned home, he revived and later ascended as an immortal. Moreover, Gan Shi, a native of Taiyuan, excelled in *qi*-circulation and abstained from food and drink. He received the *Yuansu* method from Master Rongcheng and applied it with efficacy, healing illnesses without acupuncture, moxibustion, or herbal decoctions. He lived among people for over a century before entering Mount Wangwu and ascending as an immortal.

The *Congshi* of Qingzhou and the Perfected of the Purple Palace.

*The Records of the Western Mountains* state: The Perfected Lord Huang, whose personal name was Renlan and courtesy name Ziting, hailed from Wuling. Renowned for his diligent scholarship, he later resigned his office to pursue the Dao. Ziting became a disciple of Lord Xu and attained his master’s teachings, serving for a time as *Congshi* of Qingzhou. Upon achieving perfection in the Dao, he ascended with Lord Xu the Perfected.

*The Lofty Discourses of the Green Jade Case* record: Sun Mian, a *You Shijin*, served as an official overseeing the women’s quarters in Yuancheng. The riverbank there was prone to collapse, and one of the women remarked: "A great thunder spirit dwells beneath it, emerging whenever the sky clears." Mian lay in wait and, when the spirit appeared, loosed an arrow that struck its neck. That night, he dreamed an officer summoned him, saying: "You have slain the thunder spirit; you are now summoned to testify." He followed the officer to an official residence, where the attendant declared: "This is the office of the Perfected of the Purple Palace." Mian asked: "What is the Perfected’s name?" The reply came: "He is the Duke of Wei." Mian recalled how he had once received the duke’s patronage and thought to seek his aid. Upon entering, he saw the duke seated upon a dais, flanked by azure-clad youths. Mian prostrated himself and implored the Perfected’s protection. The duke turned to his attendants and took a yellow document from a green satchel; a youth read aloud: "Five hundred kalpas are required to attain the preciousness of a human body. The damage to the women’s quarters was the fault of Mian’s duty." The duke ordered him dismissed.

Wang Hui plowed with tigers, and Lu Yu tilled with crows.

**The Biographies of Eminent Daoists** records: The Daoist Wang Hui dwelled at the Cavern of True Reality in Bear’s Cage Ridge on Mount Hua. He often cultivated yellow essence by the streamside, where tigers and leopards plowed and tilled for him. He would ride them in and out, wielding a whip and bridle just as one rides a horse, with no difference. He composed esoteric instructions to teach others the arts of self-cultivation, yet his methods were so recondite that none could fathom them.

**Unofficial Histories of the Tang** states: Lu Yu’s origins were unknown. A monk found him by the waterside as an infant. When he grew to adulthood, he proved intelligent and skilled in letters. Divining his fate with the *Book of Changes*, he drew the hexagram *Gradual*, and so took "Lu" from "the wild goose alights upon the land" and "Yu" as his given name. He secluded himself among the tea groves of the Han River, styling himself the Recluse of Mulberry and Ramie. He shut his door to write, though at times he would wander the wilds, singing and striking wood in rhythm, so that contemporaries called him the modern Jieyu. Lu Yu was devoted to tea and composed the *Classic of Tea*, earning him the title of Tea Sage. It is also said that Lu Yu plowed with cranes while crows tilled for him.

Wang Xianke gave charcoal; Xuanping carried firewood.
**Supplement to the Biographies of Immortals** recounts: Wang Xianke, a native of Hengyuan in Qingcheng, was kind and benevolent, his family wealthy beyond measure. The mountains near his home were rich in firewood and charcoal. One day, hearing the sound of thieves chopping wood, he went to investigate and found a Daoist, who explained: "I am refining the elixir in the valley, but I lack charcoal. I took this wood to burn." Xianke asked how much he needed and offered to provide it all, sparing him the labor. The Daoist gratefully replied: "When the medicine is complete, I shall repay you." Thereafter, Xianke visited often. One day, the elixir was finished, and the Daoist departed, leaving behind several grains of cinnabar. After consuming them, Xianke felt his qi flow freely and his body grow light. Beside his gate stood a great cypress. He leapt into its branches and ascended to the treetop, then soared away as an immortal.

**Extensive Records** tells of Xu Xuanping, a native of She County in Xin’an. During the Jingyun era, he secluded himself in the southern valley of Chengyang Mountain. Sometimes he sold firewood, and when drunk, he would chant:
*"Firewood sold at dawn’s first light,*
*Wine bought as the sun sinks west.*
*None ask where my path may lead—*
*Through white clouds, into emerald peaks."*

Fei’s ink turned stone black; Geng’s silver was forged from snow.
**Records of Mount Mao** state: Fei Changfang encountered the Venerable Pot and received his talismans, which he used to heal the world. Residing east of Mount Mao, he inscribed talismans to aid others. One day, he poured water upon a stone in a mountain stream, turning it black. Henceforth, it was called "stone ink." Even now, ink made from it can be used to write talismans.

**Biographies of Female Immortals** records: The female Daoist Master Geng was the daughter of Geng Qian. Once, during a great snowfall, she ordered courtesans to gather snow in golden platters and mold it into silver ingots. When cast into a blazing furnace, it instantly turned to gold, the imprint of her fingers still visible. **Records of Extraordinary Persons** adds: The master took snow, packed it tightly, and carved it into silver rods. When placed in searing coals, it melted into pure, gleaming silver.

Xu Ji’s horse neighed; Da Liang’s ox panted.
**The Biographies of Eminent Daoists** states: Shu Xuji, styled Dezhen, dwelled at the Bronze Horse Abbey in Xinfan. He often told others: "Long ago, the Yellow Emperor met with Master Xiao and the Heavenly Perfected Sovereign, along with a host of immortals, at this very place. They named it the Terrace of Three Assemblies. A bronze horse once hid among the trees here, and the Dragon Bridge marks its old path." Later generations built a terrace where the horse had vanished. On clear autumn evenings, the bronze horse would suddenly leap and neigh, and those who saw it would attain the Dao. Xuji lived there for thirty years, fortunate enough to witness it once. One day, he told a neighbor: "Tonight, I shall depart for another place. I entrust my affairs to you." He pointed to a spot and instructed: "Bury me here, three feet deep. I will surely repay you richly." That night, he passed away. The neighbor followed his wishes and buried him, discovering a full *yi* of gold. When officials investigated, they found only a staff and sandals in the grave.

**Supplement to the Biographies of Immortals** recounts: Feng Daliang was poor but devoted to the Dao, though he had no particular practice. Whenever Daoists or masters of methods passed his door, he would detain and entertain them. His sole possession was an ox that turned a millstone to sustain him. One day, the ox died. His wife, née Yang, wept in despair: "Our livelihood depends on this ox. Now that it is dead, how shall we feed ourselves?" At that moment, a Daoist from Mount Cimu arrived, staying for days as was his custom. The couple told him of their plight, and the Daoist said: "The hide and horns are all that matter." "They remain," they replied. He took them and stitched them into the shape of an ox, adding wooden legs. When he tied a rope to it and drove it forward, it rose and grew as sturdy as before. The Daoist then vanished. Years later, in the scorching heat, the ox panted heavily. A shepherd boy, seeing this, secretly loosened its mouth, only to find it reduced to hide and bones.

The Taihe Immortal’s crane-drawn chariot; Master Fa’s dragon carriage.
**The Divine Immortals of the Wang Clan** records: The Perfected Wang loved the Dao. He and his wife entered the mountains, cutting off all worldly ties and devoting themselves to incense and meditation. After decades, a divine being bestowed upon him a sacred text, saying: "Your name is already inscribed among the immortals. Follow this Way, and your son shall also become a Perfected." One day, the Celestial Emperor sent a dragon carriage and crane-drawn chariot to welcome him. He ascended in broad daylight and was appointed the Perfected of Taihe.

**The Biographies of Eminent Daoists** states: When Master Ye Fashan resided in Siming, an old official suddenly appeared, weeping and begging for help. The master asked what troubled him, and the man replied: "I am the Dragon King of the Eastern Sea. The Celestial Emperor commanded me to guard the treasures of the eight seas. Recently, a Brahmin from Lǜpó has been practicing sorcery, casting spells day and night. On the fifth day of the fifth month, the sea will run dry. The treasure that governs heaven and anchors the sea is a divine artifact of the Celestial Emperor. It is about to be stolen. I beg you to save me with your cinnabar talismans."

The master then dispatched a flying talisman, and the sea waters returned to their former state. Another day, the Dragon King presented treasures and precious goods in gratitude, but the master declined, saying to the dragon: "Upon those rocky heights, the waters lie far away. If you could bring forth a clear spring, that would be most excellent." That very night, the sound of wind and rain arose in a rushing torrent. By dawn, a stone channel encircled the mountain retreat, its waters flowing in a ring—known to this day as the Celestial Master’s Channel.

Jade tablets record worthy names; golden scriptures hold wondrous teachings.

*The Extensive Records* states: Lady Wei, named Huacun and styled Xian’an, was the daughter of Wei Shu, Duke Wenkang of the Jin. From childhood, she studied the Dao, mastering the Nine Classics and all histories without exception. At fifteen, she yearned for the immortal arts of breath cultivation, sparing no effort in her practice. At twenty-four, her parents compelled her to marry Liu Ju, with whom she bore two sons. After instructing her sons to maturity, she bid farewell to her bedchamber and devoted herself to rigorous cultivation. The Perfected of the Supreme Ultimate and the Azure Lad of Fangzhu descended, addressing her: "The Most High has inscribed your immortal name upon the jade tablets. Strive onward!"

Another wondrous scripture tells of Lu Miaodian, a female adept of Mount Jiuyi. Born with keen intelligence, she was given *The Yellow Court Scripture* by a passing traveler, who told her: "This scripture is the golden text of the Great Emperor of Fusang’s palace. Recite it ten thousand times, and you shall become a divine immortal—if only you apply yourself diligently." The scripture declares: "Recite it ten thousand times to ascend the Three Heavens; a thousand disasters vanish, and ten thousand illnesses heal." Dwelling in the mountains, she chanted it as though joined by a thousand voices, yet only the indolent fail to cultivate. Miaodian entered Mount Jiuyi and recited the scripture for a decade. True immortals descended, and she ascended to heaven in broad daylight. To this day, her footprints remain upon the stone altar.

An old man steams a child; a youth boils a dog.

*The Biographies of Divine Immortals* recounts: The Ten Friends of Weiyang were wealthy men who gathered as Daoist companions, taking turns hosting feasts for amusement. An old official, clad in tattered robes, often joined their gatherings, and they never turned him away. One day, as the wine flowed freely, he said: "Though I am poor, I wish to host a gathering in return. May I?" The others agreed. The next day, he led them to a thatched hut where several beggars sat in a circle. He lifted a massive plank covered in oilcloth, revealing a steaming child. The group recoiled in disgust, refusing to eat. The old man said: "This is a thousand-year-old ginseng, rare indeed. I wished to repay you with it, but if you will not eat, so be it." He and the beggars divided the meal among themselves, then ascended to heaven.

*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* tells of Zhu Ruzi, who studied under the Daoist Wang Yuanzheng in the Great Answer Cliff. One day, he spotted two small spotted dogs near his dwelling and, intrigued, pursued them into a cluster of wolfberry bushes. There, he and his master unearthed two wolfberry roots, plump as dogs. The master ordered them boiled. Ruzi tended the fire for three days, tasting the broth first. Seeing the roots had softened, he ate them. Suddenly, he rushed outside, feeling his body grow light. He soared to the mountain peak, enveloped in clouds, and departed. Yuanzheng ate the remainder and thus avoided death. The peak is now called Boy’s Peak.

Gao Wen laughs at the crab; Cao Cao marvels at the perch.

*The Comprehensive Records of Marvels* relates: Gao Wen had mastered the arts of nourishing life and could drink several *dou* of wine without becoming drunk. When Shen Langzhong served as transport commissioner of Jiangdong, he often invited Gao to accompany him on his rounds. Once, while traveling by boat, they stopped at the Guichi Pavilion, where a recluse named Li of Jiuhua was an old friend of Gao’s. Shen invited Li to join them for drinks, and each consumed over two *dou* without showing signs of intoxication. Gao took up a fishing rod and said: "Let each of us catch a fish to amuse ourselves—but no crabs!" He cast his line into the brazier before them. Moments later, Li pulled out a crab. Gao laughed: "I said to catch a fish, yet you got a crab. You must be penalized!"

*The Biographies of Recluses of the Later Han* records: Zuo Ci, styled Yuanfang, once sat among the guests of Minister Cao Cao. Cao leisurely surveyed the assembly and remarked: "Today’s feast is lavish, with every delicacy present—save for perch from Wu’s Song River." Yuanfang, seated below, replied: "That can be arranged." He requested a bronze basin filled with water and a bamboo rod with bait, then cast his line into the basin. In an instant, he pulled out a perch. Cao and the guests were astonished. Cao said: "One fish is not enough to serve everyone. Can you catch another?" Yuanfang cast his line again, and soon pulled out another. Both were over three *chi* long, fresh and delightful. Cao ordered them cooked and shared among the guests.

Feng Liang abandons the world; Du Qi retreats into seclusion.

*The True Sayings* records: Feng Liang, once a county clerk, felt shame for his lack of aspiration. He smashed his carriage, slaughtered his ox, and tore his tattered robes, then devoted himself to studying the Daoist arts, his resolve stern and unyielding. The provincial and commandery authorities extended him ceremonial summons, but he declined. When the imperial court learned of him, the Three Excellencies vied to yield their positions to Liang, yet he refused. A man of the Later Han, at sixty-seven he abandoned the world, bound his belongings, and entered Mount Mao, where he now dwells in the Deer Track Grotto.

Another figure, Du Qi, crossed the Yangtze at the beginning of the Jian’an era and followed Sun Ce into Kuaiji, serving as Sun Quan’s commandant. In the twentieth year of Huangwu, he encountered Master Jie, who transmitted to him the arts of guarding the primal and refining the white. Thereupon he retreated into seclusion east of Great Mao Peak, where he could conceal his form and often appeared in bodily manifestation. Sometimes he would gather firewood or trade for clothing and grain, yet none recognized him. The Precious Grotto of Taiyi, the Immortal Ruins of Yuanzhen.

*The New Record of the Cinnabar Terrace* states: Zhou Liang, styled Taiyi, studied the Daoist arts under Yao Tan. When others displayed such feats as flying sand or hurling stones—all manner of demonic marvels—Liang would hold the scriptures, recite, and chew the sacred words, whereupon the malevolent beings reverted to their true forms or perished at his side. He often roamed the precious grottoes with immortals, whistling and chanting through the day.

*The Extensive Records* tell of Xue Yuanzhen, who from youth cherished the Dao and at times dwelled among the Five Ridges. He said to others: "The Nine Doubts and Five Ridges are the abodes of divine immortals, where mountains and waters are secluded and wondrous, mists and clouds surpassingly strange—like the towering peaks of Yangshuo, the verdant spires, or the pure emptiness of the grotto-palaces of Boluo. One cannot forget them. Thus did Zhurong lodge his spirit upon Mount Heng, and Emperor Shun ascend as an immortal at Cangwu. The Radiant Ancestor left his traces at Goufeng, and the Yellow Emperor soared in his wheel-chariot at Dinghu. Why have so many lofty immortals, perfected beings, divine persons, and transcendent companions roamed these places in carefree delight? It is because the mountains are secluded and numinous, the waters deep and clear, pines and bamboo intertwined, clouds and creepers veiled in mystery—surely not a realm beloved by those of mortal frame or dust-laden heart. Moreover, within the profound grottoes, another heaven and earth unfold: jade nectar drips like milk, spirit herbs and magic mushrooms flourish. How could worldly eyes perceive them? Yet those who tread such paths, who attain the Way of extended years and wander there in ease—truly, what joy is this!"

The world speaks of feathered fans and dark-hued maple chariots.

*The Lives of Eminent Daoists* records: Wu Meng, styled Shiyun, was returning from Zhongling when he wished to cross the great Yangtze. There he encountered a furious gale and raging waves, yet he drew a white feathered fan and traced the water, whereupon he crossed over. All who witnessed this marveled.

*The Collected Records of Immortals* states: Xue Xuantong, styled Master Xuantong, recited the *Scripture of the Yellow Court* without cease. Divine immortals descended to him and declared: "Your recitation of the *Yellow Court* has borne merit; the earthly officials have repeatedly memorialized your name, and it is now recorded in the Purple Void’s archives." Thereupon they transmitted their secrets to him.

In the years of Xiantong, the Purple Void Primordial Lady descended to transmit the Nine-Flowered Elixir, saying: "Take this, and a paper chariot shall be sent to welcome you back to Mount Song." That very night, he dissolved into transformation, leaving no corpse. A memorial was submitted, and Emperor Xizong marveled at it.

*Explanations of Music Bureau Pieces: "The Water Immortal's Melody"* records: Boya studied the qin under Master Cheng Lian, who said: "My teacher, Fang Zichun, dwells in the Eastern Sea and can shift a man’s essence." Thereupon he took Boya with him. Arriving at Mount Penglai, he left Boya, saying: "I shall fetch my teacher." He poled his boat away and did not return for ten days. Boya heard only the surging of waters, the deep stillness of mountains and forests, the mournful cries of birds, and sighed: "The master shall shift my essence." He took up his qin and sang, suddenly grasping its wondrous purport. Cheng Lian poled his boat to welcome him, and Boya’s art thereafter became peerless in the world.

*The Lives of Divine Women* states: The Lady of Great Primordiality, surnamed Zhuan and named He, practiced the Dao from youth. She could unlock gates with a gesture, topple mountains with a point of her finger, and fell trees with a glance. Once, traveling through mountains with her disciples at dusk, she struck a stone with her staff, and it split open to reveal curtains, tables, and wine and delicacies. Thus she traversed ten thousand li in an instant, ageless and untouched by time. Later, she ascended to heaven from Mount Baodu.

Praise begets renown; seclusion stills the breath.

*The True Sayings* records: The Eastern Minister said: Long ago, Guo Chongzi and his three brothers were struck and wounded in the arm by evildoers. The three brothers flew into a rage and wished to punish them, but Chongzi said: "Anger is useless." He sent them away. Later, one of these men entered officialdom, and Chongzi’s praise for him was frequent and unstinting. The man came to thank him, yet the praise continued unabated. He said: "I am a wicked man; I cannot accept your kindness." Thereupon he took his own life. Chongzi later attained the Dao, but the Supreme Ultimate Perfected One declared that Chongzi bore the fault of causing a death and thus could not ascend as a Perfected Immortal of the highest rank.

The Eastern Minister also said: Liu Fenglin studied the Dao on Mount Song for four hundred years. Thrice he compounded the divine elixir, only for it to be ruined by malevolent spirits. He then moved to Mount Weiyu, where he sealed his breath and ceased respiration. To this day, over a thousand years later, he has yet to ascend as an immortal. It is said that he has merely attained undying life, without the power to command spirits or forces.

Harmony with the azure and purple; purity of the yellow and vermilion.

*The Lives of Eminent Daoists* records: He Zhizhang served as Director of the Palace Library. During the Kaiyuan era, he encountered Master Tonghe, who transmitted an elixir to him, saying: "First comes the covenant, then the transmission. Yet the ranks of immortals, like the azure and purple of officialdom, cannot be attained in haste. The first principle is to retreat from worldly ties. Retreat fosters few desires; few desires bring spirit at ease; spirit at ease enables non-action that accomplishes all. To seek the Dao otherwise is like spurring a horse in reverse to chase the galloping steed—take heed of this." Later, Zhizhang resigned his post and petitioned to become a Daoist.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* records: The Perfected of Pure Emptiness told Lord Yang: "The Way of the Yellow and Vermilion, the method of blending qi—these are but Zhang Ling’s means of bestowing progeny upon the world, not the true work of Perfected Ones. I have seen many who practiced these arts and perished without descendants, but none who gained progeny thereby. To fix the mind on sensual forms while pursuing the supreme Dao is like embracing jade to leap into fire, or interring a dog in a golden coffin. The sensual is called the Yellow and Vermilion; the supreme Dao is called the Hidden Writ."

The jade casket of Juanzi; the stone wall of Gongbi.

*The Life of Su Lin* records: Juanzi was an immortal of antiquity. Once, while angling by the river, he encountered the Lesser Lad of the Eastern Sea, who said to him: "You diligently pursue the supreme Dao, yet outwardly borrow fishing and fowling as a guise. To bait the hook without catching is the perfection of nurturing life. If you should catch a carp, try cutting it open." With these words, he departed. Juanzi soon caught a carp, and upon slicing it open, found within a jade casket. Opening it, he beheld the method of the Three Primordial Perfected Ones, bestowed by the Lord of the Golden Watchtower. Juanzi cultivated this teaching and gained the power to summon clouds and rain, riding the void to ascend the heavens.

*The Lives of Eminent Daoists* records: Zhang Gongbi’s origins are unknown. Master Liu resided at Cloud Terrace, refining his qi for over twenty years. At each of the Three Primes, he would attend the assemblies, departing in silence. The master inquired of him, and he replied: "Gongbi dwells beneath Lotus Peak." The master went with him to a place where they saw a stone wall towering a thousand fathoms high. Gongbi knocked, and it split open to reveal a world within—myriad phenomena arrayed in splendor. Zhang said to his disciples: "The master is here; prepare a feast and perform for his entertainment." His disciples struck the water, and soon a pair of blue dragons and a pair of white elephants danced with exquisite grace, while a pair of vermilion phoenixes and a pair of green luan-birds sang with crystalline clarity. They then offered the master a bowl of water and a knife-tip of powder, blending them for him to drink. The taste was sweet and fragrant. After a while, Gongbi bade the master farewell and departed. Looking back, they saw only the stone wall.

*Records of the Multitudes of Immortals of the Three Caverns, Scroll Five* concludes.
*Records of the Multitudes of Immortals of the Three Caverns, Scroll Six* begins.

Chen Baoguang, Orthodox Unity Daoist, compiled and noted:

Jade vessels fill themselves; clay gourds often stand empty.

*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* records: Daoist Wang Yan, styled Ziyuan, studied under Master Jiao Kuang of Cloud Terrace Abbey on Mount Hua, receiving the esoteric scriptures and true teachings of the Three Caverns. During the Later Zhou dynasty, Emperor Wu, revering the Dao, dispatched envoys to summon him. Master Jiao said to him: "The Daoist teachings have declined, long bereft of support. You must strive to propagate them, lest they fail to flourish. From this moment, I shall depart." Wang arrived at the capital and, after some time, obtained leave to return to his abbey. The emperor then ordered its expansion for his residence. Yet the mountain’s rocks lacked soil, making its transport exceedingly laborious. Through silent prayer to Heaven, soil suddenly surged forth beside the abbey, abundant and inexhaustible. Often lacking oil, he set out a vessel, which by morning filled itself, and though used for long, never ran dry.

*The Unofficial History of Jin* records: Tao Gang, from youth, possessed lofty aspirations and lived with unfeigned contentment. By his dwelling stood five willows, so he once composed *The Biography of the Master of Five Willows* to describe himself: "The Master—no one knows his origins or name—lived beside five willows, hence his sobriquet. Tranquil and reticent, he coveted neither glory nor profit. Fond of reading, he sought no deep understanding, yet whenever insight arose, he rejoiced, forgetting even to eat. He loved wine but, being poor, could seldom obtain it. Relatives and friends, knowing this, would invite him to drink, and he would drain every cup, determined to become drunk. Once intoxicated, he would depart without reluctance. His walls were bare, offering scant shelter from wind and sun; his coarse robe was patched and threadbare; his gourd bowl often empty—yet he remained at ease."

Song’s prayers brought rain; Wu’s talismans stilled the wind.

*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* records: Daoist Song Yuanbai, one day during a great drought in Yuezhou, exposed the dragon tablets in the courtyard to pray for rain. Yet the drought worsened. Yuanbai said to others: "The rain we seek requires Heaven’s decree; without an offering above, it cannot be summoned." He retired to Yuanzhen Abbey, burned incense, and supplicated all night. By dawn, a heavy rain fell, and the people of Yue marveled. Later, in Xinzhou, another drought arose, and the prefectural governor specially requested his prayers. Yuanbai then inscribed a talisman and presented it before the city god’s statue, whereupon rain promptly arrived.

*The Records of West Mountain* state: Wu Meng, styled Shiyun, once saw a violent wind arise. He wrote a talisman and cast it onto the roof, where a blue-black crow seized it in its beak and flew off. Moments later, the wind ceased. When asked the reason, he replied: "On South Lake, a boat was caught in this storm, and two Daoists cried out to Heaven for aid. Thus, I used this talisman to still it." Investigation confirmed his words.

The revered master tamed tigers; the reclusive scholar reared dragons.

*The Rustic Tales* recount: Yuntai Abbey in Guizhou was once a site graced by Lord Lao and Celestial Master Zhang. Among its residents was a Daoist named Pei Haozhong, whose origins were unknown. Though over a hundred years old, he subsisted on pine branches or refined his qi alone. Often, after clenching his fists and regulating his breath, he would sit in silent meditation with eyes closed, while fierce tigers docilely gathered at his side—fellow residents sometimes witnessed this. One day, he told his disciples: "I must depart. You remain here and diligently cultivate." Though his disciples urged him to stay, he wrapped his robe and ascended the sheer cliff as if treading level ground, swift as a flying bird or nimble ape, scaling the peak until he vanished from sight. Later, when tigers grew rampant in the countryside, villagers painted the revered master’s likeness to subdue them, calling him the Tiger-Taming Venerable.

*The Minister’s Former Affairs* state: When Chancellor Niu governed Xiangyang during a prolonged drought, a recluse—rumored to rear dragons—was summoned to summon rain. The recluse said: "Between the Yangtze and Han rivers, no dragons dwell. Only in a single backwater lies a black dragon, forcibly driven to wreak havoc, difficult to control." The chancellor insisted, and indeed, a great rain fell, flooding ten thousand households.

Gao Xian on the water, De Run in the jar.
*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* record: Gao Xiaoxian, known as the Immortal Elder, once accompanied the King of Wu on a voyage to the Three Rivers’ mouth. When winds stalled the fleet, many boats capsized, and the elder’s vessel vanished without trace. The king sighed: "Though the elder possesses the Dao, how could he not escape this?" He sent men to search. That night, the elder suddenly appeared, walking atop the water with robes and shoes untouched by moisture. Upon arrival, his face bore the flush of wine. Asked the reason, he replied: "Yesterday, Wu Zixu forcibly detained me for drinks, delaying me here to Your Majesty’s inconvenience." The king listened in silence.

*The Inner Transmission of the Celestial Masters* states: Zhang Zhongchang, styled Derun, was a great-grandson of the Celestial Master. Once summoned to court, he secretly returned, sighing: "I nearly fell into the world’s snare." In his chamber, he buried a jar. Though he ate meat and drank wine with his wife, by night he would enter the jar, remaining inside for days without emerging.

Red Pine the Rain Master, Yuan Zhi the Water Mother.
*The Biographies of the Immortals* say: Red Pine, the rain master of Shennong’s era, ingested water jade to instruct the Flame Emperor. He could enter fire unscathed. He journeyed to the Kunlun Mountains, dwelling in the Queen Mother of the West’s stone chamber, ascending and descending with the wind and rain. The Flame Emperor’s youngest daughter pursued him, attaining immortality and departing together. In the Gaoxin era, he served as rain master.

*The Unofficial History of Jin* records: Zhao Yuan Zhi once encountered a Daoist who bowed and led him into the water. They traveled through the night beneath the moon, mud clinging without soiling their feet. Beside them appeared a serpentine creature aglow with five-colored light. Astonished, Yuan Zhi asked what it was. The Daoist replied: "This is called the Water Mother. Those who see it become immortals."

Dong Yuan’s tolling bell, Jian Ming’s resonant drum.
*The Principal Biography* states: Qu Baiting studied under Master Huang Dong Yuan of Taoyuan. One day, he took leave. Dong Yuan asked: "Where do you depart to?" He answered: "To my grotto palace." Though the master wished to detain him, he could not. Baiting’s countenance radiated extraordinary brilliance; clad in a short hemp robe and black silk headscarf, he retreated three steps, then vanished. Dong Yuan and his disciples were stunned. Searching yielded no trace. The abbey’s caretaker rang the bell to assemble the community for a forest search. At the northeast edge of the woods, they encountered a great serpent blocking the path.

*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* recount: Daoist Zhang Jian Ming, well-versed in the *Laozi* and *Zhuangzi*, was summoned by the Gaozu Emperor, who asked: "Can Daoist principles govern a state?" He replied: "The Dao speaks of the wondrous subtleties of the myriad things. One who grasps its essence may govern the realm from the stillness of an ancestral shrine." The emperor praised his words, inviting him to the inner palace to lecture on the *Dao De Jing* and appointing him as teacher. One day, hearing the palace’s timekeeping drum, he remarked: "Does Your Majesty hear the drum? Its sound is singular. Though the five tones and twelve pitches exclude it, all are harmonized by the drum. The One is the root of all affairs. He who holds to the One may govern the realm." The emperor approved, bestowing the title Master of Penetrating Mystery. Later, he vanished without trace.

Wu Guang who clipped chives, Gui Fu who ate mallows.
*The Biographies of the Immortals* state: Wu Guang, a man of the Xia dynasty, had ears seven inches long. Fond of the zither, he ate the roots of wild chives. When Tang of Yin overthrew Jie, he consulted Guang, who said: "This is not my affair." Tang asked: "Who then is suitable?"

He replied, "I know not."
Tang asked, "What of Yi Yin?"
Guang said, "He is strong and enduring, patient of disgrace—yet I know nothing beyond this."
After Tang conquered Jie, he offered the realm to Guang, saying: "The wise devise the plan, the martial execute it, and the benevolent occupy the throne—such is the ancient Way. Why do you not fulfill this role? I shall serve as your minister."
Guang declined, saying: "To depose a ruler is unrighteous; to kill men is unbenevolent. Others bear the guilt, while I enjoy the gain—this is no peace. I have heard that one who is not righteous does not accept its emoluments, and in an age without the Dao, one does not tread its ranks—how much less when it exalts me? I cannot endure it."
Thereupon, he carried a stone and drowned himself in the Liao River, then concealed himself. Over four hundred years later, he reappeared in the time of Wu Ding, who again sought to appoint him as minister. Guang refused. Wu Ding sent a carriage to fetch him, but Guang fled, deeming it improper. He then cast himself into the river at Floating Mountain. Later, he wandered to Mount Shangfu.
*The Declarations of the Perfected* states: Wu Guang clipped chives and entered the limpid springs of Qingling—such was his way.

As for Gui Fu, he was a man of Xianglin. His complexion shifted between black and white, red and yellow, and the people of Nanhai revered and served him. He regularly consumed cassia and mallows, blending them with tortoise brain to form a thousand pills, each weighing ten *jin*. Gui Fu was seen across generations, and to this day, cassia pills remain in the south of Jingzhou.

The immortal stream flows for Tan Yi; the hired hand becomes Master.
*The Supplementary Biographies of the Immortals* states: Tan Yi of the Tang was born at the end of the Kaiyuan era. As soon as he fell to the earth, he could speak, and within a few years, his body surpassed six feet, his beard and hair flourishing. His bearing differed from that of his elder brothers; he neither ate nor drank, and his stride matched that of a galloping horse. Later, he vanished without trace, and those near and far marveled, deeming him a divine being. His village built a temple to honor him. During the Dali era, he suddenly returned home, clad in a rosy cloud cap and feathered robe—a true immortal of the celestial stream. After bidding farewell to his parents, he soared into the sky and departed.

*The Biographies of the Immortals* states: Of Zi Zhu, no one knew his origins. He visited the King of Jiangdu, saying, "Master Xiao hired me as a laborer for over two hundred years, yet I have not attained true immortality."
The king thought him mad and asked where the master resided. Zi Zhu replied, "Atop Dragon’s Brow Mountain."
The king dispatched an envoy to ascend the mountain, and indeed, at its summit, they found Master Xiao—his body covered in hair, his ears broad, his hair unbound as he plucked a zither. Zi Zhu prostrated himself before him, and the envoy conveyed the king’s command. The master said, "This lord is the ninth-generation descendant of my neighbor. Do not meddle in my servant’s affairs."
The envoy then descended the mountain.

The master’s scripture gushes like a spring; Chang Hong’s snow flies.

From *Liezi*: When Hu Ba played the zither, birds danced and fish leapt. Zheng Shiwen, hearing this, abandoned his home to follow Master Xiang. Master Xiang asked, "How fares your zither?" Shiwen replied, "I have grasped it. Pray, let me demonstrate."

Thus, in spring, he plucked the *shang* string to summon *Nanlü*—suddenly, a freezing wind arose, and grasses and waters congealed into solidity. In autumn, he struck the *jiao* string to rouse *Jiazhong*—a warm breeze gently swirled, and plants and trees burst into bloom. In summer, he plucked the *yu* string to invoke *Huangzhong*—frost and snow descended together, and rivers and pools froze instantly. In winter, he struck the *zhi* string to stir *Ruibin*—scorching sunlight blazed, and hard ice dissolved at once.

As he neared the end and commanded the *guan* note to harmonize the four strings, a balmy breeze soared, auspicious clouds floated, sweet dew fell, and sweet springs gushed forth.

From *Shiyi Ji*: In the twenty-third year of King Ling’s reign, he built the Terrace of Kunzhao—also called Xuanzhao Terrace—one hundred *zhang* high, from which he observed the colors of clouds. At the time, Chang Hong could summon rare marvels. The king ascended the terrace and gazed at the dense, swirling clouds when suddenly two figures arrived riding the clouds. Their beards and hair were yellow, and they wielded brushes of soaring dragons and flying phoenixes, drawn by chariots of green glass. Their robes were stitched from feathers and fur.

The king welcomed them to his seat. As the land suffered a great drought—earth cracked and trees burned—one of the figures sang and summoned frost and snow. With a single breath, clouds arose and snow flew, chilling all who sat there. The palace’s pools and wells froze solid, their ice fit for carving. The king then presented robes of fox fur and white fox pelts, along with purple bear-skin cushions—a tribute from the Western Regions.

Another figure sang, commanding the seat to blaze with heat. He flicked his finger upon the cushion, and a warm wind filled the chamber. The furs and cushions were cast from the terrace.

Xiao followed Nongyu; Du paired with Lianmei.

From *Liexian Zhuan*: Xiao Shi lived during the time of Duke Mu of Qin. He could make peacocks and white magpies dance. The duke had a daughter named Nongyu who admired him, and the duke gave her to him in marriage. Xiao taught Nongyu to mimic the cry of the phoenix. After decades of living together, when she played the flute, a phoenix would alight upon their roof. The duke built the Phoenix Terrace, where the couple remained for years without descending.
