One day, they both departed with the phoenix. Thus, the people of Qin built the Phoenix Maiden’s Shrine at the Yong Palace, where the sound of flutes could still be heard.

From *Shenxian Zhuan*: The Black Mountain Immortal, Duzi, was a man of Zheng. Dwelling on Black Mountain, he gathered pine nuts and *fuling* to consume, and after hundreds of years, he would appear now youthful, now aged, now comely, now plain—revealing his immortal nature. Duyang’s daughter was born with joined eyebrows, her ears fine and long. The people marveled at her, calling her a celestial being. When Duzi sought a companion, Duyang’s daughter delighted in him and stayed to serve him. Whenever they went out, they would grasp the calf’s ears and run, leaving no pursuer able to catch them. Zuo Taichong’s *Wei Capital Rhapsody* says: "Chang Rong refines her hues; Du pairs with Lianmei."

Zhang Lao’s bamboo hat; Meng Qi’s grass cloak.

From *Shenxian Zhuan*: Zhang Lao was a gardener in Liuhe County, Yangzhou. He married the daughter of his neighbor Wei Shu. One day, he took his wife away, saying, "I have a small estate at the foot of the mountain. I shall return by tomorrow morning. If you miss me in years to come, send your elder brother to seek me south of Tian Tan Mountain." He left for several years without a trace. Wei, longing for his daughter, sent his son Yifang to find him. Upon reaching the south of Tian Tan, a Kunlun slave greeted and bowed to him. They arrived at a grand estate with extraordinary pavilions, flowers, and trees. There, a man wearing a *yuanyou* cap and vermilion shoes, his bearing majestic, appeared. Upon closer look, it was Zhang Lao. He led Yifang into the hall, where his sister sat behind beaded curtains of jade, adorned in finery unseen in the mortal world. The feast was exquisite, and after days of hospitality, Yifang took his leave. Zhang Lao gifted him twenty *yi* of gold and a bamboo hat, saying, "If you ever lack money, go to the medicine-seller Wang’s home north of Yangzhou and take a thousand *wan* in cash—use this as your token." Years later, when the gold was spent, Yifang visited Old Man Wang, who indeed handed over the money upon receiving the hat. Thus, he knew Zhang Lao was a true immortal.

From *Xianzhuan Shiyi*: Meng Qi was a hermit of Qinghe, over seven hundred years old. He spoke of affairs from the Zhou dynasty as if they had just occurred, claiming he once attended the Duke of Zhou’s ascent to the altar, where he touched the duke’s foot, and the duke gave him a jade branch in return. Meng Qi always carried it, though it has since vanished. He often chewed cassia leaves for sustenance. When Emperor Wu of Han sought immortals, Meng Qi came clad in a grass cloak. The emperor found him extraordinary.

Li Mu subdued wood; the Guest twisted the loom’s shuttle.

From the *Guangji*: Li Quan journeyed to a stone chamber on Mount Song, where he obtained the *Yellow Emperor’s Yin Confluence Classic*. Yet he did not grasp its principles. Passing by Mount Li, he saw flames rise from burning wood by the roadside. An old woman said, “Fire is born from wood, yet disaster strikes and overcomes it.” Quan, astonished, exclaimed, “These are the secret words of the *Yellow Emperor’s Yin Confluence Classic*—how do you know them?” The woman replied, “I have studied this scripture for three *yuan* and six cycles of *jiazi*.” Quan bowed and begged for its profound meaning. The woman said, “I received this talisman and was listed in the registers of immortals—only then could I speak of the subtleties of the supreme Dao. The *Yin Confluence* is a secret of the Upper Clarity, revered by the Mystic Terrace. In governing a state, it brings great peace; in cultivating the self, it grants the Dao. It must not be recklessly transmitted to unworthy men. Those who leak heavenly secrets will sink through three kalpas—how can one not be cautious?”

From the *Bowu Zhi*: A traveler lived by the river. Every year on the fifteenth day of the eighth month, a floating shuttle would drift past, never failing its appointed time. The traveler, secretly intrigued, prepared ample provisions, boarded the shuttle, and let it carry him wherever it went. Suddenly, he arrived at a walled city. Gazing ahead, he saw a weaving woman. When he asked her a question, she did not answer but gave him a small stone from the loom’s shuttle, saying, “Take this to Yan Junping in Shu.” The traveler returned and asked Junping, who said, “On the seventh day of the seventh month last year, a guest star trespassed against the Oxherd and Weaver—it was you.”

Cultivating mushrooms in the clouds, reclining in mist;
Eating pine seeds, drinking from the mountain stream.

From the *Xianzhuan Shiyi*: Wu Yun styled himself Master of Cave Sun. At fifteen, he devoted himself to the Dao, skilled in composition and proficient in regular and clerical scripts. He sat for the imperial examinations and mastered the Three Teachings and Nine Schools without exception. He withdrew to live in seclusion on Mount Yidi in Nanyang, cultivating mushrooms beyond the clouds, untouched by fame or profit.

From the *Biographies of Eminent Daoists*: The Daoist Wang Yan, styled Ziyuan, loved the Dao from the age of nine and became a disciple of Master Zhengyi, Chen Baochi. At eighteen, he studied at Louguan and befriended the Perfected One Li Shunxing. Soon after, he visited Yuntai Abbey on Mount Hua and again took Fierce Void as his teacher, receiving the secret scriptures and true instructions of the Three Caverns. He nourished himself only with pine seeds and mountain stream water, seeking the truth of the Perfected.

The Controller of Destinies’ precious chalice;
The Old Man’s divine pillow.

From the *Supplement to the Biographies of Immortals*: The Lord of Destinies was ever present at the people’s gates. In his youth, he studied alongside Kang Yuanhuan, the Imperial Censor, and once bestowed upon Yuanhuan a drinking vessel—neither jade nor not-jade, its name unspoken. After parting, they never met again. One day, a foreign merchant from the Western Regions arrived at Yuanhuan’s home in the Eastern Capital and sought an audience, saying: “A rare treasure’s qi emanates from this residence; I wish to see it.” Yuanhuan showed him other objects, but the merchant dismissed them all. Only when the vessel from the Lord of Destinies was brought forth did the merchant prostrate himself, exclaiming: “This is the Celestial Emperor’s *Flowing Blossom Treasure Chalice*! When placed in sunlight, it emits a white mist that ascends to the heavens; when set upon a jade stand, its crimson radiance fills the chamber. This is the Supreme Lord’s treasure for guarding the Central Realm, yet it will not long remain among mortals—soon it shall take flight. He who possesses this treasure shall receive blessings for seven generations.” Yuanhuan placed it upon a jade stand, and that night, a red glow suffused the room.

The *Biographies of Divine Immortals* states: The Supreme Elder, whose name is lost, was seen by Emperor Wu of Han during his eastern progress. The Elder was hoeing by the roadside, a white light crowning his head. The Emperor, astonished, questioned him, and the Elder replied: “Your servant was eighty-five and near death when a Daoist taught me to ingest pine seeds and drink from mountain streams, along with a divine pillow containing thirty-two medicinal substances—twenty-four corresponding to the twenty-four solar terms, and eight toxic drugs to counter the eight winds. Now my years have reversed, and this is the reason.” The night march of Yan Qing; the midday slumber of Guo Zhen.

From the *Biographies of Divine Immortals*: Yan Qing of Kuaiji was poor and often made charcoal in the mountains. One day, a stranger spoke with him and, upon parting, presented him with a book, saying: “Your bone structure befits the Dao.” He also taught him the method of ingesting *shinao* (mineral essence). After receiving the divine text, Qing constantly sensed dozens of attendants following him. Once, as a regional governor passed by at night, he ordered his soldiers to seize Qing. Qing, in turn, commanded his divine retinue to detain them, and neither the governor nor his men could move. At dawn, passersby said: “This is surely Master Yan.” The governor’s family came to kowtow and apologize before he was released.

From the *Biographies of Eminent Daoists*: Master Guo, styled Guozhen, was devoted to the study of divine immortals, forgetting both fame and profit, relinquishing joy and anger. He ate coarse food and wore tattered clothes, yet his resolve grew ever firmer. When suddenly afflicted by illness, others urged him to take medicine, but he said: “When the Dao prevails, illness vanishes—what cause for concern?” Such was his unwavering faith. One day, as he napped at midday, his disciples suddenly heard the sound of carriages and horses approaching. Moments later, the tiled roof trembled, and the master departed this world.

The celestial granaries of cavernous palaces; the numinous altar where stone stands forbidding.

From the *Records of Divine Encounters*: Xue Fang, Prefect of Mianzhou, dreamed of entering a cavernous palace where an abundance of delicacies was laid out. Someone told him: “This is the *Celestial Granary*.” Upon waking, he dispatched the Daoist Sun Lingfeng and a trusted official to investigate. Arriving at Changming County on the prefecture’s border, they found a cavern called *Celestial Granary*. The Daoist entered and saw stone formations arrayed like provisions—an abundance of food and drink, each with a distinct name, their flavors sweet and fragrant. He gathered some to bring back for Xue, but upon exiting the cavern, all had transformed into stone.

From the *Records of Xiangchuan*: The *Numinous Altar of Vermilion Mound* and the *Precious Cavern of the Great Void* align with the stellar lodge *Wings* and the celestial pivot, hence the name *Mount Heng*. The mountain has five peaks, one of which is the *Stone Treasury*. Many scholars, woodcutters, and boatmen here are versed in poetry. Once, an official from Guangzhou was traveling by boat when he heard someone recite:
*A lone oar west of Wild Magpie Shoals,*
*Moonlight stretches far to Dongting Lake.*
*Loath to pass before the jagged peaks,*
*My homeland vanishes beyond the horizon, wordless.*

The silent stone chamber; the spirit’s thatched hall.

From the *Biographies of Eminent Daoists*: The Daoist Chen Guayan dwelled in seclusion on Jade Cloud Peak, playing the zither, drinking wine, and composing poetry, following his inclinations without restraint. His mountain-dwelling verse reads:
*Drunk outside my thatched hut, the gate left ajar,*
*Awakening to green mountains filling my gaze.*
*Where pine blossoms fall, gibbons still slumber;*
*Deer tread softly through the forest’s edge.*
And again:
*Ice forms a mirror upon the water,*
*Snow swept into jade-like dust.*
*Why ask of past or present?*
*Here is a man of the primordial age.*

When he was about to undergo liberation through the corpse, he instructed his disciples, saying: "Enshroud me in a blue cloth sack within the stone chamber—take care not to use earth or wood." At the hour of his passing, he presented his followers with a poem:

*I was formless, then briefly took form,
By chance came to the world, chasing after its bustle.
Now the debts of rebirth are all repaid,
I scratch my head in emptiness and return to the Upper Clarity.*

Another Daoist, named Xu Lingfu and styled Master Silent Hope, dwelled on Cloud-Cover Peak of Mount Tiantai. He built a thatched hut to live in, delighting each day in cultivation and refinement. He once composed a poem:

*Silent and still, spirit congealed at the Great Ultimate’s dawn,
Responding to things without intent, descending in a cloud chariot.
Cultivating one’s nature is not a matter of seeking—
To know the true essence is simply to recognize it as oneself.*

And again:

*To master the Dao and fulfill the True is in this life,
Those lost on the path wait for death before seeking rebirth.
If this life does not comprehend the principle of non-birth,
Even if you gain life, where will you be born?*

In the early Huichang era, Emperor Wuzong of Tang summoned him through the prefectural governor of eastern Zhejiang. Xu declined and never emerged, instead presenting the governor with a poem expressing his resolve:

*My rustic nature sings of the Three Joys,
Imperial grace descends from the ninefold heavens.
The purple palace’s decree is transmitted,
Sent down to White Cloud Peak.
I am shamed by the crane in the written scroll,
Deeply abashed by the dragon painted on paper.
How can I assist the enlightened lord?
I would rather grow old among the pines and crags.*

The governor reported his frailty and emaciation, and the emperor exempted him from service. Thereafter, Xu abstained from grain for a long time, entering stillness and transforming. Liu Kuan served as a prefectural commander, and He Kang as a gentleman of the Ministry of Works.

From the *Declarations of the Perfected*: Liu Kuan, styled Wenrao, was a man of the Later Han. Now, in the grotto-heavens, he serves as the commander of the Youthful Prime Prefecture, overseeing those who first embark on the Dao.

From the *Biography of Chen Wuji*: He Kang’s age was unknown. In his hometown, he served the Later Jin as a gentleman. When Emperor Zhangsheng made his eastern imperial progress, a commoner in the marketplace approached him by the roadside, claiming to be He Kang, former gentleman of the Ministry of Works under the Jin. The emperor, recognizing him as an immortal, secretly examined the records of officials that night and, finding the name, was greatly astonished. He sent men to seek him, but they failed to find him. He often spoke of this to Empress Zhuangxian, lamenting it as a regret.

During the Tiansheng era, He dispatched his disciple Yu Cheng to the capital to present golden, silver, and bronze Daoist statues worth tens of thousands. The empress, surprised, summoned Yu and questioned him. Yu replied that his teacher had sent them. When asked who his teacher was, he said, "He Kang." The empress was again astonished and asked, "Where is he now? Can I see him?" Yu replied, "He is in Huainan. Since your servant has come to offer these, he naturally wishes to meet you." Overjoyed, the empress sent envoys to accompany Yu to Huainan to seek him, and they returned together. The empress visited Hongfu Temple to see him. His words addressed matters difficult for others to articulate in that age, and the empress, displeased, dismissed him.

He Chong attained the ranks of immortals, and Ding Yi transmitted divine formulas.

From the *True Sayings*: He Chong, a native of Lujiang, rose through the ranks to become a minister. His family had long upheld the teachings, and he performed many acts of kindness and merit. In the second year of Yonghe, he underwent liberation through the corpse, transformed in the Southern Palace, and ascended to the ranks of immortals—all because of his many acts of kindness.

From the *Records of the Western Mountains*: The Perfected Lord of Wu, named Meng and styled Shiyun, displayed filial piety from the age of seven. In summer, he would not drive away mosquitoes, fearing they might leave him and bite his parents. At forty, a villager named Ding Yi taught him divine formulas. He later studied under Bao Guan, the governor of Nanhai, and obtained his esoteric methods. During the Huanglong era, a white cloud descended with a talisman, and thereafter the Dao flourished between Wu and Jin.

The Vermilion Talismans of the Lady of Xiang, the Green Satchel of Master Guo.

From the *Biographies of Female Immortals*: During the Zhenyuan era of the Tang, there lived in Xiangtan an elderly woman who never revealed her surname, known only as the Lady of Xiang. For over a decade, she would lodge with others, healing illnesses with vermilion talismanic script. No affliction in the village failed to respond to her remedies. Her hair was as thick as clouds, her skin as pure as snow. Leaning on her staff and dragging her sandals, she could traverse hundreds of *li* in a day. One day, she encountered a Daoist, and their meeting filled them both with joy. When asked about the Daoist, he replied, "This is Lady Fan, wife of the Perfected Lord Liu Gang." Only then was it known that the Lady of Xiang was none other than Lady Fan.

From the *Biographies of Divine Immortals*: The Jin scholar Guo Pu was deeply versed in the classics, broad in learning, and possessed of great talent, though he was reticent in speech. His *fu* poetry stood as the finest of the Restoration era, and his passion for ancient wonders was matched only by his mastery of yin-yang arts. There was a certain Master Guo who resided as a guest in Hebei, excelling in divination. Guo Pu studied under him, and the master bestowed upon him the nine scrolls of the *Green Satchel Writings*. From that time, Guo Pu’s command of the Five Phases, celestial patterns, divination, and the arts of averting disaster and transforming fortune became boundless—surpassing even Jing Fang and Guan Lu. His disciple Zhao Zai once stole the *Green Satchel Writings*, but before he could read them, they were consumed by fire.

"When the child’s spring is exhausted, one wanders into the throes of illness."

From the *Records of the Uncanny*: Du Zichun had squandered his family’s fortune and fallen into destitution. An old man gave him three million coins without revealing his name before departing. Within a year, Du had spent it all, descending from horse to donkey, then from donkey to foot. The old man appeared again, giving him ten million coins. Years later, Du was poorer than before. The old man gave him thirty million coins, saying, "If this does not cure you, your poverty has entered the inmost marrow."

From the *Hidden Recluses of the Tang History*: Tian Youyan, a native of Sanyuan in Jingzhao, was initially appointed as a student of the Imperial Academy. His mother and wife both harbored aspirations beyond the mundane world. Later, he entered Mount Ji, where he built a hut beside the shrine of Xu You and resided there, calling himself "Xu You’s eastern neighbor." During the *Tiaolu* era, Emperor Gaozong visited Mount Song and dispatched the Vice Director of the Imperial Secretariat, Xue Yuanchao, to inquire after his mother. Youyan emerged in a field-worker’s cap and gown to pay his respects. The emperor ordered his attendants to support him, saying, "Has the Master’s cultivation of the Dao in the mountains fared well?" Youyan replied, "Your servant’s love for springs and rocks has entered the inmost marrow, and his passion for mist and clouds has become a chronic malady. Now that I have encountered this sagely age, I am fortunate to wander freely." The emperor said, "In obtaining you today, how is it different from the Han’s acquisition of the Four Hoary Ones?" Xue Yuanchao said, "When Emperor Gaozu of Han sought to depose his heir and install a concubine’s son, the Yellow and Qi elders only then emerged—how could it compare to Your Majesty’s reverence for recluses, personally inquiring after those dwelling in caves and cliffs?" The emperor was greatly pleased.

Wang Zhi’s axe-handle rotted; Xu Jia’s bones withered.
From the *Divine Immortals of the Wang Clan*: Wang Zhi, a native of Liyang, once entered the mountains to fell trees. By chance, he came upon a stone chamber where several youths were playing chess. Zhi sat upon his axe-handle to watch. The youths gave him jujubes to eat, and he felt neither hunger nor thirst. Before the game concluded, one youth said, "You may go now—you have already been here too long." Zhi rose to find his axe-handle decayed to rot. When he returned home, not a single relative remained alive.

Later, he entered the mountains and ascended to heaven, and today in Quzhou there is Mount Lanke.

From the *Accounts of Divine Immortals*: When Laozi crossed the western pass, the magistrate Yin Xi, knowing he was no ordinary man, followed him to inquire about the Dao. At the time, a retainer named Xu Jia had been hired for a daily wage of one hundred coins, and over time, Laozi owed him seven million two hundred thousand coins. When Xu Jia saw Laozi departing far beyond the pass, he sought repayment in vain and composed a complaint to submit to the magistrate. Yin Xi, greatly alarmed upon receiving it, went to see Laozi. Laozi asked Xu Jia, "You should have died long ago. I once hired you because my office was humble and my household poor, with no one to serve me, so I gave you the talisman of Great Mystery to sustain you until today. Why do you now accuse me?" He then had Xu Jia open his mouth toward the ground, and the talisman of Great Mystery immediately emerged, its cinnabar script as fresh as new. Xu Jia at once crumbled into a heap of withered bones.

Ping Zhongjie studied the Dao, observing how disciples served their masters.

From the *Declarations of the Perfected*: There was a Daoist student named Ping Zhongjie, a man of Hezhong, who crossed the river to enter Mount Kuocang and received instruction from Master Song. He cultivated the Way of the Mind-Mirror, mastered the hundred spirits, and practiced the affairs of the Grotto Chamber. After forty-five years of such devoted refinement, his body grew younger, and his form was filled with true qi. On the first day of the fifth month this year, the Yellow Emperor and Laozi sent an envoy to welcome him, and that very day he rode the clouds and mounted a dragon, ascending to heaven in broad daylight. He now dwells in the Cloud Terrace of Canglang.

Another, Huang Guanzhi, from youth was fond of studying the Dao, and his family revered his master, bowing in homage morning after morning and praying for long life. After forty-nine years of such devotion, the Supreme Perfected tested him with one hundred forty trials, all of which he passed. Thereupon, he was given the Golden Elixir and entered Mount Jiao to recite the *Great Cavern Scripture*. He was later appointed to the office of Right Minister of the Grand Ultimate—a man of resolve, whose attainment was not due to his master’s power but to the steadfastness of his own heart.

The Garden Guest’s silkworm cocoons; the giant oranges of Baqiong.

From the *Supplement to the Accounts of Immortals*: The Garden Guest was a man of Jiyin, handsome and virtuous in appearance. The people of his village offered him their daughters in marriage, but he never accepted. He often cultivated five-colored fragrant herbs and, after decades of consuming them, one day saw a five-colored moth alight upon the tips of his plants. He clothed it and provided it a bed, and from it emerged silkworms that spun cocoons. When the time came, a woman appeared at midnight, calling herself the Guest’s wife, and explained the state of the silkworms. The Guest went with her and obtained one hundred twenty cocoons, each as large as a jar. It took sixty days to unwind a single cocoon, and when all was done, they departed together—none knew where they went. The people of Qi and Yin later established a shrine to the Flower Silkworm in his honor.

From the *Records of Marvels*: A man of Baqiong, whose surname is unknown, owned an orange grove. After the frost, he gathered all the fruit, save two enormous oranges, each as large as a three- or four-dou jar. The man of Ba, astonished, cut them open and found within each a pair of elderly gentlemen, their hair and brows white, calmly playing chess. They showed no alarm. One said, "What a pity we did not take root deeply enough to enjoy the full pleasure of this game." The other replied, "You owe me: the seventh daughter of the Dragon King of the Sea’s hair, ten taels; Zhihuang’s yellow forehead ornaments, twelve pieces; a purple brocade quilt, one set; two bushels of rosy cloud fruit from the Crimson Terrace Mountain; nine *hu* of jade dust from Yingzhou. Also, four jars of marrow-congealing wine from Mother A; eight pairs of cloud-soaring silk stockings from Mother A’s daughter, the flying maiden Xiong Feiniang. Repay me later at Master Wang’s thatched cottage on Mount Qingcheng." One said, "The joy of the orange grove is no less than that of Mount Shang." The other said, "I am famished."

From his sleeve, he drew a piece of dragon-root jerky. It resembled a grass root, roughly an inch in diameter, its form twisting and coiling like a dragon, every scale and whisker rendered in perfect detail. He pared and ate it, and as he shaved it away, the piece replenished itself. After finishing, he rinsed his mouth with water, and it transformed into a dragon. The two immortals mounted it together, and beneath their feet, clouds billowed forth. In an instant, wind and rain darkened the sky, and their whereabouts became unknown.

Golden walls and crimson gates, the Clear Capital and the Purple Tenuity.

*Lost Histories* records: There was a scholar named Cui who, at the foot of Mount Qingcheng, discovered a cave leading to golden walls and crimson gates. A transcendent elder, clad in feathered robes and a radiant cloak, offered him wine and food, then gave his daughter in marriage. He presented Cui with two pills from his green satchel, instructing him to take them. Thereafter, on the first and fifteenth of each month, Cui would ride a crane to the celestial pharmacy for his audience. After a year, he requested to return home and was given a talisman of invisibility. He then roamed the imperial palace in secret, stealing brocades and silks. The emperor ordered Luo Gongyuan to divine his location, and indeed, Cui was found behind the palace. The emperor commanded his flogging to death, but Gongyuan said, "This man already dwells in the upper realms. To kill him would not bring fortune to the state." The emperor dispatched guards to escort him to the mouth of the cave on Mount Qingcheng, where they beheld the golden walls and crimson gates. Cui’s wife tossed a scarf, which transformed into a five-colored rainbow bridge, bidding him cross. With each step, the bridge vanished behind him. Soon, clouds and mist enveloped all, and only the sounds of phoenixes, cranes, and celestial music lingered.

*The Book of Liezi* states: King Mu of Zhou visited the palace of the Transformed Man. Its halls were wrought of gold and silver, adorned with pearls and jade, rising above the clouds and rain, with no earthly foundation in sight. From afar, it resembled a bank of piled clouds. All that his eyes beheld, his ears heard, his nose inhaled, and his mouth tasted were beyond mortal ken. The king truly believed it to be the Clear Capital and the Purple Tenuity, the abode of the Celestial Emperor, where the vast music of the Highest Heaven resounded. Yet when he looked down, the palaces and pavilions appeared no more than stacked clods or heaped straw.

To partake of cypress and pine, to guard subtlety and consume lingzhi.

*The Tang Historical Anecdotes* records: Wang Xiyi secluded himself on Mount Song, subsisting on pine and cypress leaves. At over seventy, his sinews remained supple and strong. When Emperor Minghuang toured the region, he summoned Xiyi to his temporary court and consulted him on governance. Their conversation left the emperor deeply impressed.

*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* states: The Daoist Li Shouwei, of unknown origin, often wandered in Shu. His speech was witty and unrestrained, unburdened by petty conventions, and people often slighted him. Suddenly, he announced, "I shall journey to the Five Sacred Mountains. I depart now." When asked about seeking profit or advantage, he replied, "This floating life is but a fleeting instant. Why not seek truth and visit the Dao, casting off the dusty net of the world? As for refining qi, concocting elixirs, consuming lingzhi, and fasting—all these are beneficial. Why cling to trifling gains?" With that, he vanished. He once shared a close friendship with Han Yu of the Ministry of Rites, who gifted him a poem:

*"A youthful face beneath aged years,
Poor and cold, yet you wander noble halls.
Though your cultivation of primal qi is great,
Your heart is lost, and your spirit’s path strays further still.
A staff carved with dragon scales, lean and jagged,
A cloak of crane feathers, tattered and worn.
Why not return to your old hermitage,
Where the peach blossoms have bloomed and fallen times untold?"*
*(Note: "Fallen" pronounced in the departing tone.)*

Bo Yu took a wife; Ji Zi returned a child.

*The Pearl Satchel of the Three Caverns* records: Chu Boyu, styled Yuanqu, was a native of Qiantang in Wu Commandery. When his father arranged for him to take a wife and brought her through the front gate, Boyu slipped out the back and went to Mount Pubu in Kuaiji to cultivate the Dao. He also traveled to Mount Heng, journeying into Min, where rushing torrents plunged into perilous gorges. As evening fell, Boyu’s boat moored at the riverbank, but a sudden gale and mountain flood surged forth, hurling the vessel into a chasm thousands of feet deep. The boatmen ahead were certain it had shattered to pieces. Yet when they searched along the banks, they found Boyu sitting calmly, unperturbed. Later, he went to Mount Huo to refine qi and nourish his radiance, fasting for years on end.

*The Biographies of Divine Transcendents* records: Ji Zixun, a native of Qi, was recommended for filial piety and appointed Gentleman of the Palace, then Prefect. Though over two hundred years old, his complexion showed no sign of aging. Once, he asked to hold a neighbor’s infant, but accidentally dropped it, and the child died. The family, who revered Zixun, buried the child at once. Twenty days later, Zixun returned from outside, carrying the child and returning it to them. Fearful it was a ghost, they dug up the grave, finding only mud within.

The layman wears straw sandals; the Daoist dons hempen robes.

*The Supplement to the Biographies of Transcendents* records: Zhu Taochui, a native of Chengdu, dwelled in seclusion outside the city, weaving a hut of grass. Sometimes he feigned madness, at others, he remained silent all day. The governor of Yizhou, Dou Gui, summoned him to office, but he refused. When Dou sent him clothing, he cast it aside and fled. He often wove straw sandals and left them by the roadside. Travelers who saw them would leave rice in their place, and Taochui would collect it at dusk. People called him the Layman Recluse.

*The Nocturnal Discourses of Lingzhai* records: There was a man named Shi Zong, known as the Hemp-Robed Daoist. He conversed with Bai Tudi of Guangling and Tan Qi of Jiangdu, his words boundless and unfettered. He asked for paper and composed a poem:

*"Desire’s hunger is never sated,
But desirelessness brings no sorrow.
Better still is the man of pure emptiness,
Who girds himself with rope and dons a dark robe.
Roaming freely through the land,
Adrift like an untethered boat.
One must extinguish worldly cares,
And dwell in peace on an ancient mountain."*
Tan Qi marveled at his words. Tao Yuanming’s *Record of the White Earth Embankment* notes that Bai Tudi encountered three extraordinary monks—this was one of them.

There was a mad Daoist who borrowed salt from Haiyan. He had his young attendant climb a small hill, where stood several thatched huts. Three or four Daoists greeted them warmly. Their speech was incomprehensible, but the boy was given a bowl of something sweet as malt sugar. Another Daoist asked, "When will the banished one’s term end?" The reply: "At the White Earth Embankment in Guangling, north of the river in Xuzhou. By my reckoning, his exile should be complete." He then wrote a letter and gave it to the boy, saying, "Deliver this. Tie it to the boy’s belt and send him back to Haiyan." The master joyfully asked, "What is in the letter?"

The letter contained only a few written words.

Again he called out to ask, "Where has the boy gone?"
The young attendant replied, "A Daoist ahead seized his staff and drifted away. I heard only the sound of waves beneath his feet as he reached a mountain. Someone in the mountain entrusted a letter for the White Earth Embankment, which he tied to my belt and showed to me, though I could not understand a word of it."
The boy returned to Shi Zong, who exclaimed in astonishment, "Have you truly come from Penglai Mountain?"

*Record of the Immortals of the Three Caverns*, Scroll Six—concluded
*Record of the Immortals of the Three Caverns*, Scroll Seven

Compiled by Zhengyi Daoist Chen Baoguang

**Clerks of the Underworld, Ghostly Immortals of Mansheng**

From *Beimeng Suoyan*:
The Daoist Qin Baoyan was diligent in his devotions and once addressed the Southern Peak Perfected Lord, saying, "Why do the Highest Perfected require paper money? I do not yet understand."
That night, he dreamed the Perfected Lord replied, "Paper money is what the clerks of the underworld rely upon—what need have I for it?"
Thereafter, those in the prisons placed even greater faith in this.

From *Zhiyi*:
In western Shu, Cui Cun sought the Dao and pursued the Perfected on the western peak of Wangwu. There he saw Shi Yannian Mansheng and Su Shunqin Zimei seated facing each other across a narrow stream. Cun bowed twice and said, "I have long since abandoned worldly rank and profit to seek the Great Dao. Today, I am fortunate to encounter two immortals here. The Han River appears shallow at a glance, yet its depths cannot be fathomed—why may I not stand in attendance at your side?"
He then asked, "Have the learned scholars truly become ghostly immortals?"
Su replied, "Absurd! Pure yang is immortal; pure yin is ghost. How can one be both immortal and ghost?"
The two immortals then commanded a blue lad to take a bamboo writing tube and compose a poem for Cun. As Cun received the poem, he suddenly saw a kingfisher bring a letter and place it before the two immortals. Su said, "The Lord of Yingzhou summons us."
They then soared to the summit of Mount Yu, vanishing in flight. With a single bound, the humble clerk was swiftly promoted.

*The Biographies of the Immortals* records: Zhu Zizhu, of unknown origin, once ascended Rock Mountain and declared, "This mountain holds cinnabar—several hundred catties may be obtained." Magistrate Zhang, having heard this, immediately sealed the mountain, whereupon the cinnabar flowed forth like fire. Zhu Zizhu gathered the cinnabar and gave it to the magistrate to consume. After five years of ingesting the elixir, Zhang’s body grew light and he could soar aloft, whereupon he departed in flight with Zhu Zizhu.

*The Extensive Records* states: Lady Xu, mother of Wei Xiaozhen, maintained widowhood to serve her parents-in-law with such filial devotion that her reputation spread far. She had but one daughter, twelve years of age, who was bright and clever. Though free of illness, the girl died, yet before her burial, she revived and said: "At first, I heard a summons for Wei Xiaozhen to ascend to heaven. There, I saw that all the celestial beings wore brocade and embroidered robes. I was led before the Lord of Life and Destiny, who declared, 'Your nine-times-great-grandfather rendered service to the state and was recently promoted to govern the underworld. Now he has been elevated to the rank of earthbound immortal. Your mother’s filial virtue has already raised her to the ranks of the immortals, while your three previous lives have been reborn in heaven.'" Thereafter, Xiaozhen devoted herself to the Dao, and in the Changqing era, she ascended to immortality.

Guo carried burdens; Huang towed boats.
*The Declarations of the Perfected* recounts: Guo Guan, orphaned in youth and without support, served Master Zheng of the Sui as a porter for seven years, enduring hardship without complaint. Moved by his toil, the master taught him methods of guiding and pulling *qi*, extending his lifespan to three hundred years. Later, he encountered Red Pine Master, who transmitted the Daoist arts, and now dwells in the Great Existence Grotto as a perfected one.

*The Extensive Records* tells of Huang Qi, a minor general of Shu, who long pursued the Dao and performed hidden acts of merit for many years. While crossing Chaotian Ridge, he met an old man whose complexion was like that of an infant and whose skin gleamed like jade. The elder said to him, "Since you cultivate the Dao, in five years you will face great peril, and I shall come to your aid. Strive to accumulate hidden virtue and do not waver in your resolve." Later, as Huang towed a boat down the gorge, it capsized and he nearly drowned. As he reached the rapids, it seemed as though someone rescued him, and he made it to shore. Looking around, he saw it was the old man he had met before, who then vanished without a trace.

Fei Changfang shrank the earth; Nüwa mended the heavens.
*The New Record of the Cinnabar Terrace* relates: After Fei Changfang of the Later Han met the immortal elder, he wished to seek the Dao but was troubled by concern for his family. The elder cut a green bamboo stalk to match Changfang’s height and had him hang it behind the door. When his family saw it, they took it for his corpse and buried it. Changfang followed the elder deep into the mountains, into a tiger’s den, where he was left alone. Yet he felt no fear. Later, he was placed in an empty chamber with a rotting rope suspending a ten-thousand-catty stone above his heart. When a host of serpents came to gnaw the rope, it snapped, but Changfang did not flinch. The elder said, "You are teachable." He was then made to eat excrement, which contained long worms. Changfang found it repulsive, and the elder sighed, "You were nearly perfected—what a pity this remains unaccomplished." Changfang took his leave and returned home. Thereafter, he could chant to shrink the earth, bringing distances of thousands of *li* instantly before him, then release it to restore its expanse.

*The Master of Huainan* states: The goddess Nüwa smelted stones of five colors to mend the azure heavens, severed the legs of the giant turtle to prop up the four pillars, slew the black dragon to save Jizhou, and heaped up reed ashes to stem the flooding waters. The heavens were mended, the four pillars set aright, the floods receded, Jizhou was pacified, the monstrous creatures perished, and the people of Zhuanxu flourished.

Lan Fang’s warmth and care, born of compassion.
*The Green Locks* records: Lan Fang, styled Yuandao, was said by the elders of Bozhou to have appeared unchanged from childhood to the present day. Gentle and kind in his dealings, he won the affection of all, great and small. During the reign of Emperor Renzong, he was once lodged in Fanglin Park. When he announced his departure, the emperor bestowed upon him the title "Master of Nurturing Simplicity of the Southern Marchmount."

At that time, the scholar Jia Changchao presented the Master with a poem, which read:
*"The sage’s grace deeply soaks the hermit’s frame,
A Daoist’s robe should be crowned with hempen cloth.
Beneath Zhurong Peak, drunk beneath the bright moon,
At the source of the Xiang’s waters, angling for purple scales.
Once you saw the sea-peach bear thrice its fruit,
Yet how many springs have passed since the ritual vessels last gleamed?
When one day my work is done and I depart,
I would be the spirit-bridge’s kneeling servant, bearing your sandals."*

The Master replied in kind:
*"Of late, I begged the enlightened lord to free this body,
No harm in forest seclusion, donning a gauze kerchief.
Wild wine brims, floating jade-like foam,
Fresh-caught Han fish gifted, their scales like brocade.
After the night the crow fled the Dark Palace’s nest,
The dragon’s child in the grotto nurtures eternal spring.
If you, sir, should share this aspiration,
Then the pot-bound heavens will hold two men more."*

One day, the Master bathed and suddenly passed away. To this day, travelers on the lakes and rivers of the Xiang ask after him, and some claim to have seen him.

*The Extensive Records* states: Mao Meng, styled Chucheng, was the great-grandfather of Shuqing Jun. By nature compassionate, he delighted in performing hidden acts of virtue. When the Zhou declined, he entered Mount Hua to study under Master Guigu, attained the Dao, and ascended on a dragon. Thus the children’s rhyme sang:
*"The divine immortal scholar Mao Chucheng,
Rode a dragon to heaven, entered the Great Clarity."*
This refers to him. Prosperity and wealth flourish alike, while poverty and hardship share the same fate.

*The True Reports* states: Ma Ming and Ma Han, in serving the scriptures and treasures, had once offended their lord and father. They were thus made to keep two servant boys with hearts of their own—one named White Head, the other Flat Top—who constantly attended the incense and fire, sweeping and dusting. Often, divine radiance and numinous qi would manifest within the chambers. Ming’s wife, who possessed some ability to perceive the unseen, reported that several jade-clad maidens in azure robes flitted through the air like soaring birds. The Ma household soon grew wealthy, amassing vast resources and property. In their old age, they passed away. Ming’s son Hong, Hong’s younger brother Zhen, and Han’s son Zhi continued to uphold their legacy, though in their later years, their devotion to their master slackened and faded.

*The Miscellaneous Morsels from Youyang* records: The scholar Quan Tongxiu, having failed the imperial examinations during the Yuanhe era, wandered destitute between Suzhou and Huzhou, stricken by illness and poverty. He had employed a messenger, a rustic villager, for a full year. One day, he said to the man, "You are as impoverished as I—what progress can I offer you?" He handed him a soiled garment, saying, "Take this and exchange it for a little wine and meat. You may gather the village elders to beg for a small sum to aid your journey." The servant smiled faintly and replied, "This is hardly enough, but I shall provide." He then cut a withered mulberry branch into several pieces, bundled them on a tray, and sighed over them. Instantly, they transformed into beef. Next, he drew water from several bottles and poured it out—it became fine wine. The village elders feasted until drunk and sated, and the scholar obtained bolts of silk and other goods. The servant then took his leave.

Beyond Xue Chang’s urn, within Shentu Ping’s hidden vessel.

*The Supplement to the Biographies of Immortals* tells of Xue Chang, a man of Youji, who sought the Dao and visited strange places. In the seventh year of the Tianbao era, he lodged for months at the Cavern-Heaven Abbey. One day, an old mountain dweller arrived carrying a large root of *Dioscorea nipponica*, its form resembling a giant tortoise, with patterned shell, head, and limbs all perfectly intact. He said to the abbey’s Daoists, "Slice this herb finely, dry it, and brew it with rice and yeast to make wine. After half a year, those who drink it will ascend as immortals." The Daoists followed his instructions and prepared the wine. One day, when all the Daoists had gone to attend a ritual feast, only Chang remained. Three days later, the Daoists returned to find Chang collapsed beside an urn outside, his ears and nose bleeding. After several days, he revived, his body light and his eyes bright, as if about to take flight. Mountains, rivers, cliffs, and walls could no longer obstruct him. Later, he entered the Great Face Mountain and was never seen again.

*The Record of Tree and Fern* states: Shentu Youya, a man of unrestrained spirit, often wandered forests and springs, always carrying a bottle. One day, he leapt into the bottle and was thereafter known as "Bottle Recluse."

Yuan Tai’s dragon chariot, Gongdu’s phoenix-worm.

*The New Record of the Cinnabar Terrace* recounts: Yao Tan, styled Yuantai, was a man of Pingyang. Of refined conduct and lofty bearing, his roots ran deep within. He withdrew to the divine realm, seeking true reclusion, and vanished into the secluded mountains, reciting the *Five Thousand Character Text* daily. He encountered Master Xu, who transmitted to him the Way of Reversing Yellow through Primordial White. After practicing it, rain no longer dampened his robes, nor mud sullied his shoes. His eyes emitted divine radiance like lightning. During the reign of King Jian, he ascended to heaven in a dragon chariot.

Also recorded is the True Man Yin Gui, styled Gongdu, a native of Taiyuan. He abstained from grain and practiced qi-cultivation, dedicating himself to the supreme Dao. His transformations were boundless—now a Daoist, now a scholar, now a youth, now an elder. He once joined the immortals Wei Zhen and others to test dragons, worms, and phoenixes, riding the void, driving clouds, and racing like lightning. He moved between existence and non-existence, dispersing his form and scattering his shadow, appearing and gathering wherever he pleased. He declared, "Five." By now, he had lived thirteen hundred years, his journeys too numerous to record in such a brief account. One day, he refined his body and entered the clouds, ascending slowly into the sky. Only the sound of reed pipes lingered, and only his robes and fine silks remained behind.

Qin Shi Huang raised his terrace, the Yellow Emperor established his abbey.

*The Records of the Forgotten* states: The First Emperor of Qin built the Cloud-Bright Terrace, gathering rare woods from the four directions and summoning the most skilled artisans from across the land. From the south, he obtained azure wood from Yanqiu, burning sand from the Li River, vermilion mud from Guidu, and white bamboo from Yungang. From the east, he procured brocade cypress from Congluan, dragon pines from Yansui, star-patterned wood from the Cold River, and catalpa from Ruanyun. From the west, he acquired floating gold from the Leaking Sea, feathered earth from Langyuan, mist-woven mulberry from Dizhang, and round bamboo from the Chen Pond. From the north, he gathered dry lacquer from Mingfu, patterned catalpa from Yinban, black jade from Zhai Stream, and fragrant jade from the Converging Sea. These treasures were assembled, and the artisans worked suspended in midair, wielding axes among the trees. Construction began at midnight and was completed by noon. The people of Qin called it the Midnight-Noon Terrace.

*The Inner Transmission of the Yellow Emperor* states: The Queen Mother of the West served the Emperor the sap of azure mist and the fruit of crimson essence. She then bestowed upon him five white jade statues, saying, "These are the true visages of the Primordial Heavenly Worthy." She also gave him the *Diagram of the Original Forms of the Two Modes* and nineteen verses on returning cinnabar. The Emperor performed obeisance and placed them in the high abbey, personally making offerings. His consorts, ministers, and concubines were forbidden to approach. Above the abbey, clouds of strange hues often gathered, and rare fragrances wafted for hundreds of paces. The people of that time called it the "Dao Abbey." The term *Dao abbey* originated from this.

Huang An plowed with his tongue, He Pu calculated with his heart.

*The Biographies of Immortals* states: Huang An, considering himself lowly and unfit for human company, took up a whip and recited books, tracing calculations on the ground. One night, the earth split open, and people called it "Huang An plowing with his tongue." At eighty, his complexion remained that of a child.

*The Supplement to the Biographies of Immortals* states: Xing Hepu dwelled in reclusion among the seas and rivers, having attained the Way of divine transcendence. He could direct his mind to focus on any object, perform calculations, and know its nature without fail. Living between Mount Song and the Ying River, he composed three scrolls titled *The Book of Yingyang*, containing the secret of "revolving the heart through emptiness."

Guangcheng’s profound obscurity, Lu Ao’s boundless vastness.

*The Biographies of Divine Transcendents* states: Guangchengzi was an ancient immortal who dwelled in a stone chamber on Mount Kongtong. The Yellow Emperor crawled forward on his knees, bowed twice, and inquired about the Way of nurturing life. Guangcheng replied, "The essence of the supreme Dao is profound and obscure; its ultimate state is dim and silent. See nothing, hear nothing, embrace the spirit in stillness. The body will then right itself. Be ever still, ever pure. Do not weary your form, do not agitate your essence. Only then can you attain long life."

Additionally, Lu Ao encountered a scholar with deep-set eyes and a knotted throat, hunched shoulders and an elongated neck, his upper body broad and lower body slender. Carrying a tortoise shell and eating crabs, he said to Lu Ao: "I have an appointment with Vast Obscurity beyond the Nine Heavens. I cannot tarry long and must depart."

The Jade Hook of the Qi Maiden, the Life-Transmitting Wooden Drill.

*The Biographies of Female Transcendents* states: Lady Hookwing was a woman of Qi, surnamed Zhao. Fond of stillness and purity, she fell ill for six years, her right hand clenched into a fist, and she ate little. During the reign of Emperor Wu of Han, a diviner observing the qi declared: "In the northeast, there is the qi of a noble person." Upon investigation, she was found and summoned. Her bearing and beauty were extraordinary. The emperor unclasped her hand, revealing a jade hook within. Favored by the emperor, she bore Emperor Zhao. Later, Emperor Wu sought to harm her, but when her body was laid to rest, it did not decay and emitted fragrance. When Emperor Zhao ascended the throne, he reburied her, but her coffin was empty, containing only a pair of silk slippers. Thus, her palace was named Hookwing.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* records: Long ago, there was a Master Fu who, from youth, was devoted to the Dao. He entered a stone chamber on Mount Jiao and, after seven years, was received by the Perfected of the Great Ultimate. Given a wooden drill, he was instructed to bore through a stone disc, five *chi* thick, with the promise: "When you pierce this stone, you shall attain the Dao." His heart unwavering, he drilled day and night for forty-seven years without pause. When the stone was finally pierced, he obtained the divine elixir and now serves as the Perfected of the Western Marchmount.

The Old Man of Huaiyang, the Nine Transcendents of Kaihuang.

*Primary Records* state: During the Former Han, Ying Yao secluded himself in the mountains of Huaiyang. When the Four Hoary Ones were summoned, Yao alone did not appear. The people of the time composed a verse: "The Four Hoary Ones of South Mountain cannot compare to the Old Man of Huaiyang." He was the eight-times-great-grandfather of Ying Shao of the Later Han.

*The Supplement to the Biographies of Transcendents* recounts: The Nine Transcendents of Kaihuang were Kong Qiuming, Yang Yuanzhong, Zhang Fashu, Wu Tianyin, Chen Zhikong, Luo Fatong, Zou Wujun, Xie Youyan, and Zhou Xianyong, along with Deng Xiyuan. During the warfare between Han and Chu, when there was no place to find sustenance, each harbored the aspiration to cultivate the Dao. They retreated into the mountains, pledged brotherhood, and wandered together among the Five Marchmounts. Later, they dwelled on West Mountain in Hongzhou. All attained transcendence. By the Kaihuang era of the Sui, phoenixes and cranes with their ceremonial escorts gathered at Nine Transcendents Terrace on Yushun Mountain. They lingered for a day before ascending to heaven in unison.

The Master of Wen Shi was styled so in the Zhou, and titled *Wei Bian* in the celestial ranks.

*The New Record of the Cinnabar Terrace* states: Yin Xi initially served as the magistrate of Hangu Pass. One day, he saw an old man riding a blue ox, drawing near in a humble carriage. Xi prostrated himself and approached, asking, "Where does the Sage intend to go?" The old man replied, "My home lies east of the pass, my fields west of it. I come now and then to gather firewood. I possess no virtue or power, yet you trouble yourself to inquire." Though the old man declined repeatedly, Xi persisted. Finally, the old man said, "Since you know the Five, I also know you harbor a heart faithful to the Dao." Thereupon, he bestowed upon Xi the *Scripture of the Dao and Its Virtue*. Xi’s cultivation perfected, his virtue complete, he was styled Master Wen Shi and appointed as the Supreme Perfected.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* records: Zhuang Zhou studied under Master Chang Sang, who transmitted to him subtle teachings, whence he became known as Zhuangzi. He concealed himself on Mount Baodu, refining his practice and composing texts. Having ingested the Northern Nurturing Fire Elixir, he ascended to heaven in broad daylight, where he was appointed as the *Wei Bian* Gentleman of the Supreme Ultimate.

Xuan Ji transmitted the leaf, Ma Xiang grasped the coin.

*The Lives of Eminent Daoists* recounts: Master Xuan Yuan Ji of Mount Luofu dwelled on the mountain for several centuries, his complexion unaged, his hair trailing to the ground. In darkened chambers, his gaze stretched for yards. When he ventured into cliffs and valleys to gather herbs, poisonous dragons and fierce beasts guarded him. Over a hundred households invited him to vegetarian feasts, and he appeared at each without fail. Emperor Xuanzong summoned him to the palace and asked, "Can longevity be governed?" Ji replied, "Sever sensual indulgence, moderate rich flavors, regulate joy and sorrow as one, and bestow virtue without partiality. Naturally, you will unite with heaven and earth in virtue, match the sun and moon in brilliance. Even Yao, Shun, Yu, and Tang could attain this—how much more so longevity?" The master could press paulownia or bamboo leaves into coins.

*The Traditions of Divine Transcendents* states: Ma Xiang, styled Ziran, possessed Daoist arts. Once, at the seat of Governor Ma Zhi of Jiangnan, he filled a wine cup with soil and planted melon seeds. In an instant, vines sprouted, flowers bloomed, and fruit ripened—its taste was exquisite. He could also shrink his body, pluck green coins, and cast them into a well, summoning them forth at will.

The numinous chariot and blessed land, the mountain chart and grotto-heaven.

*The Lives of Eminent Daoists* records: Wang Lingyu, a Daoist of Jiujiang, resided on the Five Elders Peak. One night, a divine person told him, "Those who attain the Dao each have their proper place, just as planting the five grains in sand and stone yields no harvest. Since you possess the bones of a flying transcendent, you must find a blessed land and numinous abode to achieve transformation. Without it, demons will disrupt your practice, and your aspirations will come to naught." The master asked, "Where may I dwell?" The divine person replied, "The summit of Vermilion Mound, the neighboring peak of Purple Canopy—these are a grotto-heaven and blessed land, where you may ascend to heaven." The master followed this advice and relocated to Mount Heng. After twelve years, his work complete, he ascended in broad daylight during the Tianjian era.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* states: The Mountain Chart Master was a grandee during the reign of King Ai of Zhou and also a transcendent. He transmitted the Nine Cloud Water Method to Zhang Jizi, who cultivated this Way and was appointed as the Assistant of the Nine Palaces. Now, the Mountain Chart Master dwells in the grotto-heaven, playing chess with Jizi.

Da Ling regrew his beard, Zhang Guo knocked out his teeth.

*The Inscription on the Wall of Chaohua Temple* records: During the Tang, the palace attendant Jian Da Ling wrote: "In the year I came to know my fate, I followed the imperial carriage on its western journey. In the twelfth month of the *dinghai* year of Tianbao, I received the True Origin Elixir Formula from the Old Man of the City, though its meaning eluded me. As the imperial progress halted and official duties pressed, I set it aside. In the *dingyou* year of Zhide under Emperor Suzong, while on a mission to Song Hill, I encountered the Old Man again and at last fully grasped the Way of Divine Water and Yellow Sprouts. Upon completion, I sought leave to return to the imperial court, then retired to my fields. Coincidentally, Minister Zhang Qufei of the Southern Bureau and Left Historian Cheng Taixu, both acquainted with my former abode, jointly prepared a sacred chamber. With the aid of spring blessings from august heaven, the elixir crucible radiated light. After ingesting the medicine for a full cycle, my flesh and complexion became radiant, my hair lustrous. I soared through the void without fear, my mind ever more attuned to the divine. From the time I first ingested the numinous elixir in the *dingyou* year of Zhide until the *jiayou* year of Qianfu under the present emperor, one hundred and eighteen years have passed. Having served twelve reigns, I have regained my youth and regrown my beard."

*The Miscellaneous Records of Emperor Ming* states: When Emperor Ming summoned Zhang Guo, he once offered him wine poured by Dong Yin. Guo drained three goblets in succession, his face flushed with intoxication. Turning to his attendants, he remarked, "This wine is not of fine quality." He then reclined outward, and after the time it takes to eat a meal, he awoke. Suddenly, he gazed into a mirror and saw his teeth mottled and blackened with decay. Seizing an iron ruyi, he struck his teeth, and they all fell out.

He applied medicine to his teeth and again slept. After a long while, he drew the mirror to look—his teeth had regrown, firm, lustrous, and more radiant than before.

The Cock Master Cures Illness; Ao Ling Channels the Waters.
*The Idle Talks of the Military Camp* records: In Tang-era Shu, there lived a man called Fei Jishi, "Cock Master," whose eyes were red without pupils. He could foresee events and perform exorcisms for others. When someone afflicted by illness came to seek his aid, Jishi would carry a rooster to the courtyard and set up an offering. He would then take a stone the size of a chicken’s egg, place it in the patient’s hand, and stride in zigzag steps, exhaling and shouting sharply. The rooster would spin and die, the stone shatter into four pieces—and the patient’s convulsions would cease. Hence his name, Cock Master.

*The Supplementary Biographies of Transcendents* states: Ao Ling was a man of Chu who, after death, was cast into the river. His body drifted upstream to the foot of Mount Wen, where he suddenly revived and concealed himself among the mountains of Shu. His mastery of transformation and command over ghosts and spirits became widely known. At the time, landslides in the gorges had dammed the river, causing the waters to stagnate. Emperor Du Yu, distressed by this, heard of Ao Ling’s abilities and sent him to Jintang Mountain at Qutang Gorge. There, Ao Ling channeled the waters, restoring their original course so that people could once more dwell on dry land. Du Yu abdicated several hundred years later, wandering to Mount Tianzhu, where he encountered a gathering of celestial perfected. He then ascended to heaven and departed.

Xuan Ji Bestows Leaves; Ma Xiang Plucks Coins.
*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* recounts: Master Xuan Yuan Ji of Mount Luofu was said to have lived for several centuries, his complexion unaged, his hair trailing to the ground. In dark chambers, his gaze could stretch for yards. When he ventured into the valleys to gather herbs, poisonous dragons and fierce beasts guarded him. He would visit over a hundred households to accept vegetarian offerings, yet his presence remained undivided. Emperor Xuanzong summoned him to the palace and asked, "Can one govern longevity?" The master replied, "Sever desire for sensual pleasures, moderate rich flavors, equalize sorrow and joy, and practice impartial virtue. Then you will naturally align with the power of heaven and earth, match the brightness of sun and moon. Even the sages Yao, Shun, Yu, and Tang could attain this—how much more so longevity?" The master could press paulownia or bamboo leaves into coins.

*The Biographies of Divine Transcendents* states: Ma Xiang, styled Ziran, possessed Daoist arts. Once, at the residence of the Jiangnan governor Ma Zhi, he filled a wine cup with soil and planted melon seeds. In an instant, vines sprouted, flowers bloomed, and fruit ripened—its taste was exquisite. He could also shrink his body, pluck green coins, and toss them into a well, summoning them back at will.

The Divine Chariot’s Blessed Land; The Mountain Chart’s Grotto-Heaven.
*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* records: Wang Lingyu, a Daoist of Jiujiang, dwelled on Five Elders Peak. One night, a divine being told him, "Those who attain the Dao each have their proper place, just as planting the five grains in sand and stone will yield no harvest. Since you possess the bones of a flying transcendent, you must find a blessed land and numinous cavern. There, you may transform; elsewhere, demons will corrupt your practice, and no hope of success remains." The master asked, "Where may I dwell?" The being replied, "The upper peak of Zhu Ling, the neighboring summit of Zi Gai—these are a grotto-heaven and blessed land, where you may ascend to heaven." The master followed this advice and moved to Mount Heng. After twelve years of cultivation, during the Tianjian era, he ascended in broad daylight.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* states: Shan Tuzi was a minister during the reign of King Ai of Zhou and also a transcendent. He transmitted the method of the Ninefold Cloud Water to Zhang Jizi, who practiced this Way to ascend as an assistant to the Nine Palaces. Now, Shan Tuzi resides in the grotto-heaven, playing chess with Jizi.

Da Ling Regrows His Beard; Zhang Guo Strikes His Teeth.
*The Inscription on the Wall of Chaohua Temple* records: The Tang eunuch and palace attendant Jian Daling wrote: "In the year of my fate’s decree, I followed the imperial carriage on its western journey. In the twelfth month of the *dinghai* year of Tianbao, I received the true elixir formula from the Old Man of the City, though its meaning eluded me. As the imperial retinue halted, my duties grew burdensome. In the *dingyou* year of Zhide under Emperor Suzong, while on a mission to Song Hill, I encountered the Old Man again and at last fully grasped the Way of Divine Water and Yellow Sprouts. Upon completion, I sought leave to return to the imperial court, then retired to my fields. The Southern Bureau official Zhang Qufei and the Left Historian Cheng Taixu, both familiar with my former abode, jointly prepared a sacred chamber. With the aid of spring blessings from august heaven, the elixir crucible glowed, and after ingesting the medicine for a full cycle of days, my flesh and complexion became radiant, my hair lustrous. I soared through the void without fear, my mind ever more attuned to the divine. From the time I first ingested the numinous elixir in the *dingyou* year of Zhide until the *jiayou* year of Qianfu under the present emperor, one hundred and eighteen years have passed. Having served twelve reigns, I have regained my youth and regrown my beard."

*The Miscellaneous Records of Emperor Ming* states: When Emperor Ming summoned Zhang Guo, he once offered him wine poured by Dong Yin. Guo drained three goblets in succession, his face flushed with intoxication. Turning to his attendants, he remarked, "This wine is not of fine quality." He then reclined outward, and after the time it takes to eat a meal, he awoke. Suddenly, he gazed into a mirror and saw his teeth mottled and blackened with decay. Seizing an iron *ruyi*, he struck his teeth, and they all fell out.

Applying medicinal paste to his teeth, he then slept. After a long while, he again took up the mirror and saw that his teeth had regrown—firm, lustrous, and more radiant than before.

The Rooster Master healed the sick; Ao Ling channeled the waters.
*The Idle Talks of the Military Camp* records: In Tang-era Shu, there lived a man called Fei the Rooster Master, whose eyes were entirely red, devoid of pupils. Skilled in divining the future, he could perform exorcisms for others. When someone afflicted by illness came to seek his aid, the Rooster Master would carry a rooster to the courtyard and set up an altar. Taking a stone the size of a chicken’s egg, he would have the patient grasp it, then pace in zigzag steps, exhaling sharply and shouting incantations. The rooster would spin in circles until it died, and the stone would shatter into four pieces—whereupon the patient’s convulsions ceased. Thus he earned the name Rooster Master.

*The Supplementary Biographies of Immortals* states: Ao Ling was a man of Chu who, upon his death, was cast into the river. His body drifted upstream to the foot of Mount Wen, where he suddenly revived and concealed himself among the mountains of Shu. His mastery of transformation and command over spirits and gods became renowned. At the time, landslides had dammed the gorges, blocking the flow of the river, causing great distress to Du Yu. Hearing of Ao Ling’s abilities, Du Yu dispatched him to Jintang Mountain at Qutang Gorge to channel the waters and restore their original course, allowing people to dwell once more on dry land. Du Yu abdicated his throne and, centuries later, wandered to Mount Tianzhu, where he encountered a gathering of celestial beings. He then ascended to heaven and departed.

Ge You carved wood; Zhang Ci cut paper.
*The Biographies of the Immortals* recounts: Ge You was a man of Shu who, during the reign of King Cheng of Zhou, often carved wooden sheep to sell. One day, he rode a sheep into Shu, and the kings, marquises, and nobles welcomed him to Sui Mountain. Those who followed him each received a peach, and none returned. The villagers remarked, “To receive a peach from Sui Mountain is not to become an immortal, yet it is enough to be esteemed.”

*The Extensive Records* states: Zhang Ci was a learned man who cultivated qi and abstained from food. Fond of wine and obsessed with chess, he once composed a poem:
*What can be done about the golden crow,*
*That flies ceaselessly overhead?*
*The red furnace guards the elixir’s flame,*
*Yet how can jade-like beauty be preserved?*
*This year, flowers bloom upon the branch;*
*Next year, they cover the entire tree.*
*Better to drink wine without cease,*
*From dawn to dusk, and dawn again.*

At times, he would cut thousands of paper butterflies, blow upon them with his breath, and send them flying in formation. At his clap, they would descend. On another day, he cut a paper crane, sighed upon it with water, and suddenly it took flight. He wrote: “You go first; I will follow later.”

Ma Jian subdued evil; Liu Gen summoned ghosts.
*The New Records of the Alchemical Terrace* states: Ma Jian was well-versed in the *Book of Songs* and the *Book of Rites*. At seventeen, he encountered a master who taught him the arts of *dunjia*, the Raven’s Horn, and the Raven’s Essence, along with the secrets of celestial portents. Skilled in summoning and commanding spirits, he could subdue evil and vanquish demons. One day, a celestial deity descended and addressed him: “Master, you possess accumulated merit and are destined for transcendence. Why do you summon and command spirits? Conceal your arts, sever all ties, cultivate serenity, and embrace the One—true immortality will come of its own accord.” Ma Jian heeded this advice and ascended to heaven in broad daylight.

*The Biographies of Divine Immortals* records: Liu Gen could heal the sick and command spirits. When Du Xin, the governor of Yingchuan, heard of him, he suspected him of sorcery and summoned him to the prefectural office. “What powers do you possess,” Du Xin asked, “that you so delude the people?” Liu Gen replied, “I can make ghosts appear.” Du Xin said, “Prove it now. If no ghost appears, you will be executed.” Liu Gen replied, “Ghosts are easily summoned. Lend me your brush.” Du Xin complied. Liu Gen inscribed a talisman and let out a long whistle. In an instant, the southern wall of the hall split open several feet, and four or five hundred men in red garments, wielding swords, emerged from the breach. They bound the governor’s parents and brought them forward, weeping: “Why have you offended the divine immortal, causing us to be bound and humiliated thus?” The governor kowtowed and begged for mercy. Liu Gen admonished the spirits and dismissed them, and the ordeal ended. A month later, the governor and his wife, Lady Guo, both died.

Chen Chang built houses; Yan Qing pulled boats.
*The Master Who Embraces Simplicity* states: Chen Chang had dwelled on Ramie Mountain for over six hundred years. The mountain folk built him a house and made seasonal offerings. Though he neither ate nor drank, his complexion remained that of a man in his fifties or sixties. Ramie Mountain spanned a thousand *li*, with over a thousand households atop it. Their customs were the same as those of Wu.

Yan Qing was a man of Kuaiji who, though poor, made a living selling charcoal. One day, while on the road, he encountered an extraordinary person who gave him a scroll of plain silk. Yan Qing said, “I cannot read.” The divine being replied, “You need not read it; simply keep it in a quiet place.” Thereafter, Yan Qing gained spiritual powers. When someone pulled his boat, he would disappear, and only the charcoal-laden vessel would move on its own. Later, when he treated the sick, he would bring the scroll to their homes, and they would recover. He entered Mount Xiao Huo and attained immortality.

Xiyi embodied Yao and Shun; Hongya emulated Chao and You.
*The Biographies of Divine Immortals* records: Chen Tuan, styled Tunan and known as Master Xiyi, once encountered a golden-armored divine being who guided him to seclude himself on Mount Hua. Emperor Taizong summoned him and asked, “I wish to govern the realm with the Way of Yao and Shun. Is this possible?” Chen Tuan replied, “I have heard that Yao and Shun had earthen steps three feet high and thatched roofs untrimmed. If Your Majesty can do likewise, then you shall truly be a Yao or Shun of the present age.”

*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* states: The Daoist Zhang Yun, known as Master Hongya, secluded himself on Mount Yuzhang. During the Kaiyuan era, Emperor Ming summoned him and asked, “How do I compare to Yao and Shun? How do you compare to Xu You?” Zhang Yun replied, “Your Majesty’s Way surpasses that of Yao and Shun, while my virtue falls short of Xu You’s. In ancient times, Yao summoned Xu You, yet Xu You did not come. Today, Your Majesty summoned me, and I have come.” The emperor praised him and offered the title of Master and the rank of Minister of Ceremonies, which he repeatedly declined, eventually rising to Minister of Works. He then said, “Why does Your Majesty begrudge a single mountain and stream, preventing me from following in the footsteps of Chao and You?” The emperor granted his request, and he dwelled on the great cliffs of the Western Mountains, the very place of his former seclusion.

*The Records of Yuzhang* notes: During the Kaihuang era of the Sui, the region was renamed Hongzhou in honor of the mountain where the master resided.

Programming two tigers, painting two oxen.

*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* records: Cheng Taixu, a native of Xichong in Guozhou, was drawn to the Dao from childhood. At fifteen, he ascended the eastern mountain near his home, where a lofty aspiration to transcend the mundane arose within him. Soon after, clouds of five colors enveloped his body. In adulthood, he ceased grain and sat in forgetfulness. Two tigers would accompany him wherever he went. He named them Good Speech and Good Conduct, then stroked their backs and administered the Three Refuges. The tigers knelt and listened attentively. Thereafter, they would come whenever he called their names.

*The Biographies of Recluses* states: Master Tao the Recluse dwelled on Mount Jijin, compiling the *True Instructions*. Though Emperor Wu of Liang repeatedly summoned him, he never responded. Instead, the master painted two oxen and presented them to the emperor—one roaming freely amidst water and grass, the other fitted with a golden bridle and held by a man. The emperor remarked, “The master’s intent is like the tortoise dragging its tail in the mud. He cannot be compelled.” Thus, he ceased his summons.

Xu sought the crescent moon, Du bowed to the autumn courtyard.

*The Biographies of Immortals* recounts: Xu Cuo wandered through famous mountains, leaving inscriptions wherever he went: “Here passes the Seeker of the Crescent Moon.” One day, he composed a poem:
*“Before the flowers of the celestial garden lies the land of drunkenness,
Mistakenly I drained the Queen Mother’s ninefold rosy goblet.
Venerable Wei clapped his hands, deeming me frivolous,
And banished me to the mortal world as a wine-mad fool.”*
Thereupon, he ascended upon clouds from a tavern.

*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* records: Du Guangting, styled Binsheng, was also known as Master Guangcheng. At the end of the Tang, a reclusive Daoist whose name was obscure sought lodging with him. Though the master assented, he never granted the man an audience. The Daoist sold medicines in the market by day, spending his earnings on wine, and would sing only *“Lamenting the Autumn Courtyard.”* The people called him the *“Autumn Courtyard Lamenter.”* For nearly half a year, none suspected his strangeness. One evening, deeply intoxicated, his singing grew louder. Those who peered in saw lamps and embroidered banners, tables laden with vessels, and azure youths standing by to pour wine as he sang. The onlookers reported this to the master, who then knocked at his door, saying, *“Guangting’s capacity and knowledge are shallow. I never imagined a transcendent would deign to visit. I am deeply at fault. I humbly beg to bow before your radiant presence, that my worldly obstructions may be dispelled.”* The Daoist replied, *“Why such earnestness? I shall come out to meet you.”* In an instant, he stowed the banquet and attendants into his cap, opened the door—and the room was empty.

Dongfang Peeped at the Window, Zhang Ping Dug a Well.

*The Tales of Emperor Wu of Han* states: On the seventh day of the seventh month, the emperor was in the Cheng Hua Hall when two azure birds alighted before it. The emperor asked Dongfang Shuo, who replied, *“The Queen Mother of the West is about to arrive.”* Soon after, she appeared. Someone was peering through the southern window. The emperor, startled, asked who it was. The Queen Mother said, *“That is your attendant, Dongfang Shuo. He is a witty fellow—just a mischievous boy from my neighborhood.”*

*The Extensive Records* recounts: During the Yuanhe era, Prefect Zhang Shiping of the Tang and his wife both went blind in middle age. They shut their doors, performing rituals and prayers without cease, their devotion growing ever more fervent. One day, a scholar appeared and said, *“This affliction requires no medicine. Simply dig a well in a blessed site and wash your eyes with its water.”* The prefect followed his advice. The moment he washed his eyes, his vision cleared. The couple bowed in gratitude and offered rich gifts, but the scholar refused, saying, *“I am an official of the Star of Venus. Because you have endured illness without forsaking the Dao, your devotion unwavering, the Celestial Emperor sent me to impart the Dao’s mysteries in repayment for your diligence. Gold and silk are not what I seek.”*

Bai Reached the Immortals’ Abode, Li Trod the True Realm.

*The Extensive Records* states: Bai Youqiu failed the imperial examinations during the Zhenyuan era. He sailed into the sea, where wind and rain drove his boat to the foot of a mountain. That night, he heard the wind rustling the leaves like the sound of someone reciting poetry:
*“A jade canopy spans the azure void—
This is the dwelling of immortals.”*
Suddenly, over a thousand riders appeared, mounted on dragons, cranes, turtles, and fish. They pointed to the water, which seemed to flow beneath the moon. Someone called out, *“Water Bureau Messenger Bai Youqiu!”* and handed him a document from the Water Bureau. Youqiu followed them and soon reached an island. Gazing into the distance, he saw signs of human habitation. When he returned home, none of his family remained—generations had passed.

*The Supplement to the Biographies of Immortals* records: Li Lin, a native of Yan, was traveling with a friend on Mount Wutai during the Baoli era when he fell into a wind cave. A lion-like figure led him into a cavern and gave him water to drink, saying, *“Though you nearly perished, you have entered my grotto and trodden my true realm—this is your destined affinity with the Dao. If you harbor the desire for long life, return home for now. You may come again to drink this divine nectar, which will extend your years.”*

King Zhao Treasured the Pearl, the Dark Emperor Buried the Cauldron.

*The Supplement to the Biographies of Immortals* states: In the time of the Yellow Emperor, Wuchengzi wandered the ridges of Mount Hanshan when he saw a black clam on a high cliff, proving that black clams could fly. By the era of King Zhao of Yan, the kingdom presented one to the king. He washed its sand and mud with water from a jade vessel, then sighed, *“Since the dawn of sun and moon, I have seen black clams produce pearls eighty or ninety times—but this clam yields a pearl only once in a thousand years.”* The king carried this pearl in his bosom during the summer, and his body felt light and cool. It was called the *“Heat-Dispelling, Coolness-Summoning Pearl.”*

*The True Instructions* records: On Great Mount Mao stands a bronze cauldron from the time of the Dark Emperor, large enough to hold four or five *hu*. It rests on the mountain’s highest point, buried eight *chi* deep, with a slab of stone covering it. The Dark Emperor ordered the Spirit of the Eastern Sea to conceal it there.

Wu Ding Was Summoned, Master Shao Requested Audience.

*The Supplement to the Biographies of Immortals* states: Cheng Wuding, a native of Guiyang, became a county magistrate at thirteen and was sent to deliver goods to the provincial capital. The governor, Zhou Ting, recognized his exceptional qualities and retained him as a literary aide.

Upon his return from the capital, he passed through Changsha Prefecture. As he missed the postal relay’s closing hour, he lodged beneath a tree in the wild. Suddenly, he heard voices saying, “We’re heading to Changsha’s market for medicine.” At dawn, he saw two cranes. Finding this strange, he followed them to the market gate and waited. Sure enough, two elderly men appeared. He trailed them for several *li* before one asked, “What do you seek by following us?” He replied, “I heard of your life-saving arts and came to serve as your attendant.” The elders exchanged glances and smiled, then drew a jade case from their sleeves. Upon examining a plain silk scroll, they found Cheng Wuding’s name inscribed. Each gave him a single pill, and by this he attained the Dao.

One day, he told his younger brother, “On the seventh day of the seventh month, the Herdboy visits the Weaver Girl. I have been summoned back to my post and cannot stay long.” With these words, he passed away. After his burial, the prefect ordered the coffin opened, but his body was gone—only a green bamboo staff and a black crow remained.
