*The Traditions of Divine Transcendents* records: Li Shaojun once compounded an elixir, but before its completion, he told Emperor Wu, “Your Majesty cannot forsake extravagance, distance yourself from sensual pleasures, cease warfare, or temper your wrath. Lost souls wander beyond the ten thousand *li*, and ghosts of the slain haunt the marketplace. The great elixir and the supreme Dao cannot be achieved under such conditions.” Suddenly, Shaojun fell ill. That night, the emperor dreamed they ascended Mount Song together when a messenger riding a dragon descended from the clouds, bearing a tally, and announced, “The Great One summons Shaojun.” The emperor awoke and told his attendants, “Shaojun will soon leave me.” The next day, Shaojun’s condition worsened. The emperor visited him personally, only to find he had already transformed. The emperor said, “He has merely departed in this manner.” When they prepared to encoffin him, his body had vanished.

*Records of the Multitudes of Transcendents from the Three Caverns*, Scroll Seven—concluded.
*Records of the Multitudes of Transcendents from the Three Caverns*, Scroll Eight.

Chen Baoguang of the Orthodox Unity Dao, compiler.

The Proud Clerk of Qiyuan, the Fisherman of Misty Waves.
Jin poet Guo Pu’s *Wandering Immortal Poems* states: “Qiyuan has its proud clerk, Lai has its reclusive wife.” The annotation explains: Zhuangzi once served as clerk of Qiyuan. King Wei of Chu sent generous gifts to summon him, offering the position of minister. Zhuang Zhou laughed and said to the envoy, “Begone! Do not defile me, the proud clerk of Qiyuan.”

*The Fisherman’s Songs Record* recounts: Emperor Xianzong sought the *Fisherman’s Songs* of Master Xuan Zhen. Li Deyu, then prefect of Runzhou, obtained them. Master Xuan Zhen was Zhang Zhihé, who styled himself the Fisherman of Misty Waves. His song reads:
*By West Ford Mountain, white gulls take flight,*
*Peach blossoms drift on waters where mandarin fish grow plump.*
*In blue hat and green raincoat,*
*Through slanting wind and fine rain, no need to return.*

Second song:
*The fisherman’s father, clad in coarse brown,*
*Pairs or trios in skiffs light as water striders.*
*Skilled at sculling, accustomed to riding the current,*
*On the Yangtze’s white waves, never a care.*

Third song:
*In the bend of the Milky Way, fishing from a granary boat,*
*Skiffs as homes, drifting west and east.*
*Snow on the river, wind on the shore,*
*Laughing in lotus robes, never sighing at poverty.*

Fourth song:
*At the crab shack by Song River, the host is full of cheer,*
*Wild rice for rice, water-plant soup—all shared alike.*
*Maple leaves fall, reeds wither dry,*
*Drunk, he slaps the fishing boat, unaware of the cold.*

Fifth song:
*On Green Grass Lake, the moon is full and round,*
*At Baling, the fisherman’s oar sings of lotuses.*
*With a fishing reel and a flat-headed boat,*
*Joy lies in wind and waves—no need for transcendence.*

Su Dan’s Crane Cabinet, Sun Zhen’s Oxcart.
*The Bangjiang Collection* records: Lord Su Dan of Su County one day swept and tidied his courtyard, donned his robes and cap, as if awaiting someone. Suddenly, from the northwest, clouds and cranes gathered and descended from the sky. He entered, knelt, and told his mother, “The Supreme One summons me to fill a post as a perfected official. The ceremonial escort has arrived; I cannot remain to care for you.” Having spoken, he bowed in farewell. Mother and son sang playfully for a long time. His mother said, “After you leave, how shall I sustain myself?” He left behind a cabinet, its lock and hinges exceptionally sturdy, saying, “When you lack anything, knock on the cabinet and call for it—what you need will arrive at once. But never open it.” Thereafter, whenever his mother lacked something, she knocked on the cabinet, and the object would appear immediately. One day, his mother grew curious and opened the cabinet. A pair of cranes flew out, and though she knocked afterward, there was no response.

*The Master Who Embraces Simplicity* states: Sun Zhen studied the Dao under Master Bo He and attained it. One day, he announced his departure. The master gave him a sealed talisman box, saying, “Ahead, an oxcart will come to welcome you—board it. All provisions and traveling kitchens will be provided; eat without doubt. But under no circumstances open the box.” Sun Zhen knelt and received it. As he set out, an oxcart indeed arrived. The driver asked, “Are you Daoist Sun? Master Bo has sent me to welcome you.” Sun Zhen boarded, and the cart flew like the wind. Whenever they crossed water, they did not use bridges. Wine and food were fully supplied. One day, his disciple secretly opened the box and saw only a painted oxcart. Immediately, Sun Zhen vanished without a trace. Later, he was seen several times riding out and about with Bo He.

Mi Ming’s Cauldron Rhapsody, Tao Bai’s Gourd.
*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* records: Daoist Xuan Yuan Mi Ming traveled between Heng and Xiang, befriending Liu Shifu. Mi Ming, passing through Mount Tai Bai, learned Shifu was in the capital and arrived at his residence that night. The collator Hou Xi was renowned for his poetry and was discussing verses with Shifu by the brazier. Mi Ming sat among them, his appearance exceedingly plain. Hou Xi looked at him with disdain. Mi Ming pointed to the stone cauldron in the brazier and said, “Can the two of you rhapsodize on this?” Though Shifu had known him for some time, he did not realize Mi Ming was a man of letters. Master Liu recited:
*A skillful craftsman carves new mountain bones,*
*Shaping within the affairs of boiling and brewing.*

Next, Hou Xi said:
*The straight handle does not grasp authority,*
*Blocked and constrained, it can only swallow its voice.*

Mi Ming laughed soundlessly and said, “Is this the extent of your poetry?” Then he chanted in a loud voice, composing over ten verses in succession. Mi Ming responded as if echoing, while the two men exhausted their thoughts but could not continue. They rose and bowed, saying, “Venerable master, none in this world can surpass you.” Their peers were humbled.

*The Extensive Records* states: Lord Tao Tai Bai often made gathering herbs his vocation. One day, he carried a gourd and invited a friend to wander Mount Song. Sitting beneath the trees, they heard laughter and conversation from the pine branches above. Looking up, they saw two figures. Lord Tao said, “You must be divine immortals—might you descend and drink with us?” Soon, a man and a woman in ancient attire descended, saying, “I was a laborer of Qin, and Mao Nü was a palace woman of Qin. Together, we escaped the disaster of Mount Li and have hidden here.” Tao said, “Now that we have met true immortals, may we hear of the great elixir and golden medicine?” They replied, “We are but ordinary people. By cutting ourselves off from the world and eating pine nuts, we came to soar through the void, unaware of the difference between life and death, the mundane and the transcendent.” Then they broke a pine branch, tapped the gourd, and sang:
*Feeding on cypress, the body grows light, leaping among cicadas,*
*Right and wrong hold no meaning when one reaches the human realm.*
*Caps and robes briefly adorn the floating world,*
*For a moment, cloud-wandering through the azure heavens.*

Zi Liang’s Green Bamboo Slips, Yong Shu’s Cinnabar Script.

*The True Records* states: Zhou Ziliang, disciple of Tao the Recluse, was refined and solemn from youth, lofty in bearing. During the Tianjian era, true immortals descended repeatedly to his chamber, saying, “Zhou has cultivated merit and accumulated virtue—he has not betrayed his aspirations.” Ziliang replied, “I am unworthy of the High Perfected’s descent; joy and awe mingle in my heart, leaving me at a loss.” The Lord of Life and Destiny said, “Recently, I journeyed to the Eastern Flower Capital and saw your name already inscribed on the Azure Slips, recorded in the registers of the Dawn Bureau.”

*The Green Jade Pendant* records: Ouyang Yongshu and Mei Shengyu roamed Mount Song. Drunk, they gazed toward the western peak, where four great cinnabar characters on the cliff read *Grotto of Divine Clarity*. Yongshu pointed them out to Shengyu, who saw nothing. Yongshu fell silent. Later, when he petitioned to withdraw from the world, he composed a poem:
*Four cinnabar characters on a cliff ten thousand fathoms high,*
*The Grotto of Divine Clarity, its towers wrought in jade.*
*Gazing through mist and clouds, none reach that distant place—*
*Phoenix and crane must now await my coming.*

A few days later, he passed away.

Transformation by washing the intestines, renewal by rinsing the marrow.

*The Later Han Records* state: Hua Tuo, styled Yuanhua, was an immortal. Magistrate Chen Gui of Pei recommended him for filial piety and incorruptibility, and Grand Commandant Huang Gai summoned him to office, but he declined all appointments. Proficient in prescriptions and remedies, his formulas never exceeded a few ingredients. When illness arose deep within the body, beyond the reach of acupuncture or medicine, he would first administer a dose of *mafei* powder dissolved in wine. Once the patient was insensate with drunkenness, he would incise the abdomen or back to excise accumulations. If the affliction lay in the intestines or stomach, he would sever, rinse, and cleanse them, removing the diseased filth before suturing the wound and applying a divine ointment. Recovery came in four or five days, with full restoration within a month.

*The Tales of Emperor Wu of Han* recount: Dongfang Shuo lost both parents three days after his birth. Someone found him but knew not his surname, so—observing that dawn first brightened in the east—named him thus. As he grew, he often spoke to himself in empty air. Later, while wandering the Vast Obscurity Marsh, an old woman gathering mulberry leaves claimed to be his mother. A yellow-browed elder arrived and pointed at Shuo, saying, “This is my elder brother. I abstain from grain and ingest *qi*, returning every three thousand years to wash my marrow and every two thousand years to flay my skin and shed my hair. In my life, I have already washed my marrow thrice and flayed my skin five times.” Hao Gu picks vegetables; Xu Pu buys rice.

*The Biographies of Female Immortals* state: The shrine of Hao Gu stands northwest of Mo County in Mo Prefecture. Popular tradition holds that Hao Gu, styled Nüjun, was gathering vegetables by the Minyan River with a neighbor during the Qinglong era of the Wei dynasty when two azure lads appeared before them, saying, “The Duke of the Eastern Sea seeks Nüjun as his bride.” With these words, they spread a brocade mat upon the water, where it moved as if on solid ground. The lads attended her as the current carried them downstream.

The neighbor girl ran to inform her family, who rushed to the scene but could find nothing. From afar, Nüjun called out: “By the grace of the Water Immortals, I pray you do not grieve or fear.” Later, a shrine was erected by the water’s edge, and before it suddenly appeared two stones—one vertical, one horizontal—each about three feet wide and over two feet high. An old inscription read: *This is the Lady’s husband’s mounting stone.* They remain there to this day.

*The Imperial Anthology of Categories* states: On West Mountain in Hongzhou lived a couple, Master and Madam Xu, who wandered in and out of the mountains. Tradition holds they were the servants of Xu Jingyang. Once, while purchasing rice with his wife at West Ridge, they returned to find their master’s entire household had ascended to immortality. Master Xu left a poem:

*Since the Prefect departed for the immortals,*
*I’ve wandered the mortal world to this very day.*
*Not to deceive the world by blending with俗,*
*But to sell firewood and buy wine, to settle the heart.*

Xu Jingyang had conferred upon them the arts of earthbound immortality, changing their surname to *Gan Da*. To this day, many claim to have seen them.

*Playful ministers drum their lips; mad scholars cover their ears.*

*Miscellaneous Morsels from Youyang* records: Xing Hepu once hosted a guest who, laughing uproariously with his beard bristling and lips stretching to his ears, engaged Xing in spirited conversation before departing. When asked about it, he replied: “The Celestial Emperor was toying with his minister.”

*The Biographies of Divine Immortals* states: Outside the southern gate of Hezhou, a ragged madman was seen selling bottle gourd seeds, claiming: “In a year or two, these will prove most useful.” Yet no one understood their purpose. At times, he would clap his hands over his ears and dash away, muttering: “Why is the sound of wind and water so deafening?”

The children followed him, and the people of the time called him "Master Cover-Ears." The following autumn, the Yangtze River swelled and flooded, submerging hundreds of households. Many witnessed the madman sitting atop a large gourd on the water, hands pressed over his ears, crying out, *"Why is the sound of wind and water so deafening?"* before drifting away on the river.

*Crown hung at Beihai, shoes left at Nanyang.*

*The Unofficial History of the Later Han* records: Feng Qian, styled Zikang, was a native of Duchang in Beihai. Impoverished, he served as a pavilion chief in the county. When the magistrate passed by his post, Feng greeted him with bows. Soon after, he cast aside his official tablet and sighed, *"How can a true man be another’s servant?"* He immediately removed his cap and hung it on the gate of the Eastern Capital, then returned home to take his family across the sea. They settled as guests in Liaodong before journeying to Mount Lao in Langye, where he cultivated his will and refined the Dao. The people were all transformed by his virtue.

*The Collected Biographies of Immortals* states: Princess Nanyang of the Han married Wang Xian. When Wang Mang usurped power, the princess, long admiring purity and revering the supreme Dao, often reflected on the governance of Emperors Wen and Jing. Knowing that immortals had descended multiple times during Emperor Wu’s reign, she told Wang Xian: *"With the fate of the nation thus, it is not for a woman to prop it up. One should preserve tranquility, withdraw to cultivate the Dao, and distance oneself from strife to prolong life. If one blindly follows the times, I fear one cannot escape the breath of decay."* She then entered Mount Hua, built a hut, and devoted herself to meditation. True spirits descended frequently, and upon achieving the Dao, she ascended on clouds. Only a pair of red shoes remained on the peak, which turned to stone when retrieved. Thus, the place was named Princess Peak.

*Minister Wang’s white hare, Master Lü’s green snake.*

*The Records of Origins and Transformations* recounts: Minister Wang guarded the alchemical crucible for the Celestial Master. When he secretly broke the seal and peeked inside, a white hare leapt out. The crowd exclaimed, *"The elixir is gone!"* A Daoist transformed into a crane and flew away, but in an instant, he seized the hare and returned it to the crucible.

*The Green Pivot* records: Langzhong Jia Shixiong treasured an ancient iron mirror and long wished to polish it. A recluse named Hui claimed skill in mirror-polishing. Taking medicine from his bamboo tube, he piled it on the mirror and said, *"The medicine will be ready shortly—I will return for it."* After he left and did not return for a long time, Jia sent someone to inquire about his lodging. He was found in a temple, where he had inscribed a poem on the gate:
*"In hand, a green snake soars beneath the white sun;
In the cave, immortal fruits bloom with eternal spring.
Know this: the mist-and-clouds wanderer beyond the world
Is no mere mortal who polishes mirrors in the dust."*

When Jia examined the mirror, the medicine had vanished, leaving only a single luminous speck. The recluse also gifted a poem to Zhang Ji:
*"At dawn I roam Nanyue, by dusk I reach Cangwu;
In my sleeve, a green snake—my gall bold and fierce.
Thrice I entered Yueyang, yet none recognized me;
Chanting high, I soared past Dongting Lake."*

*Qian the True’s flying silk, Lady Bao’s washing gauze.*

*The Records of Mount Mao* tell of two sisters of the Qian family, female immortals who dwelled in seclusion on Mount Mao, reciting the *Scripture of the Yellow Court* for thirty years. One day, they bid farewell to their master, who asked, *"Where are you going?"* They replied, *"To the golden gates of the celestial palace."* The master composed a poem to send them off:
*"A Daoist sees off immortal guests,
Escorting them to the eastern peak of Great Mao.
A hundred thousand li to Mount Taihua—
Gazing afar at the dim and distant swan."*

The True Ones answered with a poem:
*"Stay well, Master, stay well;
Do not trouble yourself with this distant farewell.
The immortal roster bears our names—
Our names are recorded in Penglai’s grotto."*

At that moment, they ascended into the cave on flying silk. When the younger sister arrived, the cave had already sealed itself. This is the present-day Swallow Cave, where purple calamus and jade peach trees grow. Thus, Tian Lin wrote:
*"Swallow Cave and Dragon Spring, their aura pure and bright;
Here, the True Qian left her lingering spirit.
Though the elder sister departed, the master remained;
When the younger returned, the cave had sealed tight.
Clouds still unfurl like white silk,
The spring’s voice forever recites the* Yellow Court.
*Jade peaches bloom, calamus turns purple—
Left for the mortal world as a painted screen."*

*The Supplementary Records of Immortals’ Biographies* states: The Lady of Bao was a native of Hanzhong, dwelling between the Lu and Mian rivers. From childhood, she yearned for emptiness and stillness. Once, while washing silk by the water’s edge, clouds and rain darkened the sky, and she conceived as if moved by some unseen force. Her parents reproached her, and in her distress, she fell ill. On her deathbed, she told her mother: “After I die, place me in an ox-cart and send me to the western mountains—this is my wish.” With these words, she passed away.

Her parents laid her in the cart, but before the oxen could be harnessed, the cart moved of its own accord. It crossed the Han and Jin rivers, which surged and flowed sideways to let it pass, ascending directly to the plains and ridges of the mountains. When her family pursued her, they saw only five clouds like a canopy, celestial music resounding through the sky, and banners and standards leading the lady as she ascended to heaven. Looking into the cart, they found only an empty coffin.

Zhang Bai drank wine, while Qiao Qing brewed tea.

*The Comprehensive Record of Marvels* states: Zhang Bai, styled Xubai, called himself the Master of White Clouds. Fond of solitude and tranquility, he was learned and well-versed in literature. Though he sat for the imperial examinations twice, he never passed. He often drowned his sorrows in wine, but after his parents’ deaths, he wept and said: “Office is for nourishing one’s parents—now that they are gone, why seek rank?” He then abandoned grain, cultivating qi and preserving his spirit, and cast off his scholar’s robes to become a Daoist.

In Yanzhong, he often rode a scolding horse, his words piercing the hidden faults of others. In bitter wind and snow, he would break the ice, sit deep in the water, and emerge with steam rising from his body, his clothes dry in an instant. He frequented the Cui family’s tavern, where he once inscribed on the wall:
*“At the Cui family’s tavern by Wuling Stream,
Such wine exists not on earth but only in heaven.
A southern Daoist drinks a dou—
Beyond drunkenness, white clouds fill the deep cave’s mouth.”*
Later, the inscription vanished.

*The Empty Sayings* states: Zhang Zhihe had a servant named Fisher Lad and a maid named Woodcutter Green. When asked the reason, he replied: “My servant tends the fishing line and plucks the zither among the reeds; my maid gathers orchid firewood and cassia kindling, brewing tea in bamboo.”

Wang the Elder threshes wheat, Zhang Ji cracks the melon.

*The Biographies of Divine Transcendents* states: Wang the Elder was a native of Yijun County in Fangzhou. Dwelling in the countryside, he was deeply fond of the Dao and cherished guests. One day, a ragged Daoist arrived at his door. Wang and his wife received him with courtesy. After staying for over a month, the Daoist suddenly broke out in foul sores all over his body. Wang sought physicians and tended to him with increasing diligence, but the Daoist said, “No need for medicine—just prepare several *hu* of fine wine, and I will soak in it to heal myself.” Wang then brewed the wine. When it was ready, the Daoist ordered it stored in a large jar, added medicine, and immersed himself within. After two days, he emerged with hair and beard turned black, his complexion restored to youth, his skin smooth as congealed lard. He then bade Wang drink it. At the time, Wang was threshing wheat, so he shared the wine with his wife, children, and fellow laborers. All grew deeply drunk. The Daoist drank as well, asking, “Would you like to ascend to heaven?” Suddenly, an auspicious wind arose, and colorful clouds billowed forth. The entire household—people, chickens, and dogs—flew away together, the sound of threshing wheat still lingering in the air. Today, the village of Shengxian remains west of Yijun County.

*The Miscellaneous Records of Yang Wen* recounts: Zhang Ji lived outside the city walls when a recluse—none other than the immortal Lü Dongbin—visited him. Zhang received him with utmost reverence. The immortal asked for paper and brush, writing a seven-character poem in clerical script, leaving it for Zhang. The verses hinted at the fleeting nature of high office and prestige, concluding: *“Success will come in the year you crack the melon.”* The common folk interpret “cracking the melon” as the age of sixteen (two eights), but Zhang indeed lived to sixty-eight, proving the immortal’s foresight.

Clouds and rain of Mount Wu, ice and snow of Guye.

*The Collected Records of Transcendents* states: The Lady Yunhua was the twenty-third daughter of the Queen Mother of the West, named Yaoji. Once, while traveling to the Eastern Sea, she passed by the Yangtze River and came upon Mount Wu, where peaks and ridges soared, forests and ravines lay in serene beauty, and a massive stone resembled an altar. She lingered there for a long time. At that moment, the Great Yu was controlling the floods and had halted at the mountain’s base. A sudden gale arose, shaking cliffs and valleys, its force uncontrollable. Yu encountered Yaoji and bowed, beseeching her aid. She commanded her attendants to bestow upon Yu a talisman to summon spirits, then ordered her divine servants to assist him in cleaving rocks and dredging waves, blocking side channels to preserve the main flow. Yu later visited her at Chongyao, but in the blink of an eye, she transformed into stone. At times, she would swiftly ascend, scattering into light clouds; at others, she would linger, gathering into evening rain. Sometimes she became a flying dragon, or a soaring crane—assuming a thousand forms in ten thousand postures, untouchable and elusive. Later, the Chu minister Song Yu spoke of her to King Xiang, but the king, unable to seek the essence of the Dao for immortality, built a terrace at the Gaotang Palace and established the Changtai Temple to worship her. Song Yu composed *The Rhapsody of the Divine Woman*, imbuing it with licentious sentiment and base rhetoric, wrongly dragging down a lofty transcendent.

*Zhuangzi* states: On the distant Guye Mountain dwells a divine being. Her skin is like ice and snow, her demeanor supple as a maiden’s. She eats no grain, drinking only wind and dew. She rides the clouds and qi, driving flying dragons, roaming beyond the four seas.

Yuan Jia’s six feats, Suqing’s three perfections.

*The Court and Countryside Gatherings* records: In the Tang dynasty, Yuan Jia was a youth of extraordinary intelligence. With his left hand, he drew circles; with his right, squares. While reciting classics and histories, he counted sheep’s vertebrae, simultaneously composing a forty-character poem—all accomplished in an instant. With his foot, he wrote a perfect verse. These six feats performed at once earned him the title “Divine Child of the Immortals.”

*The Records of Verified Worthies* states: During the Five Dynasties, the Shu Daoist Zhang Suqing painted twelve scrolls of divine transcendents. Ouyang Jiong composed eulogies, and Huang Judong inscribed them in clerical script. Together, they were hailed as the “Three Perfections.”

Le Ba beheads the fox-spirit, Changfang scolds the giant turtle.

*The Biographies of Divine Transcendents* states: Le Ba heard that the deity of the Lushan Temple spoke to people from behind a curtain. Before Ba arrived, the deity fell silent for over ten days, then vanished without a trace. Ba pursued and captured ghosts, transforming into a scholar who traveled to Qi. The governor of Qi, struck by his striking beauty and eloquence, married his daughter to him. They had a son. When Ba arrived in Qi, he visited the governor, saying, “I hear you have a fine daughter, and her husband is well-versed in the Five Classics. May I meet him?” The governor summoned his son-in-law, who refused to appear. Ba persisted until the son-in-law told his wife, “If I go out today, I will die. What shall I do?” She found this strange. Knowing he would not emerge, Ba gave the governor a talisman. The son-in-law received it, wept, and departed. Ba rebuked him sternly, and he transformed into a fox. Ba beheaded him, and his son also turned into a fox, both slain.

Furthermore, Fei Changfang served as a market inspector when he met the Venerable Hu, gaining the Dao and the power to subdue ghosts. In Runan Commandery, ghosts often appeared each year, parading with the pomp of a governor, drums sounding as they circled the prefecture before departing in sorrow. Later, Changfang visited the prefectural office and encountered one such ghost. He shouted sternly, ordering it seized. The ghost dismounted, kowtowed, and begged for a chance to reform. Changfang scolded, “You show no kindness, recklessly parading before the prefect. Return to your true form!” Instantly, it became a giant soft-shelled turtle. Changfang commanded it to take a talisman to the Lord of Ge Marsh. It left in tears. Those sent to follow saw it reach the marsh and die.

Lei Jian’s sword pierces the Dipper, Yao Zha’s arrow pierces the moon.

*The Book of Jin* records: Between the Dipper and Ox constellations appeared a purple vapor. Lei Huan said, “This is the essence of a treasure sword, its brilliance reaching heaven.” He surmised the sword lay in Fengcheng of Yuzhang. Digging beneath the prison’s foundation to a depth of four *zhang*, he found a stone casket containing a pair of swords—one named Longquan, the other Tai’e. Huan kept one and sent the other to Zhang Hua. After Hua’s execution, the swords vanished.

When Huan died, his son Chong carried the sword and passed by Yanping Ford. Suddenly, it leapt from his waist and fell into the water. Divers sent to retrieve it saw two dragons coiled together, their radiance illuminating the water as they departed. Huan had once said, “Such numinous and wondrous things must eventually transform and depart.” Indeed, it came to pass.

*The Gleamings of Immortal Biographies* records: In the thirtieth year of Yao’s reign, a giant raft floated upon the Western Sea. Upon the raft shone a light that gleamed by night and faded by day. Those who dwelt by the sea observed its glow waxing and waning like the rising and setting of stars and moon. The raft perpetually circled the Four Seas, completing one revolution of the heavens every twelve years, returning to its starting point without end. It was named the Moon-Piercing Raft.

Zhang Ge calls butterflies; Chuping commands sheep.

*The Biographies of Scattered Immortals* states: During the Qingli era, there lived a man named Zhang Jiuge in the capital, who possessed the arts of the Dao. One day, the Prince of Yan ascended a tower and summoned him. After bestowing wine upon him, Jiuge said, “I have a small marvel I wish to present to Your Highness.” The prince asked, “What trick is this?” Jiuge replied, “I require a bolt of silk and a pair of golden scissors.” The prince provided them. Jiuge took the silk, cut it into shapes of bees and butterflies, and with each snip, they took flight—countless in number. Some alighted upon the prince’s robes, others clustered upon the hairpins and ornaments of his beauties. The prince, astonished and delighted, turned to gaze in wonder. Jiuge said, “I fear Your Highness may lose some of your silk.” He then called to them, and one by one, they returned, reassembling into the bolt of silk. Only one section remained incomplete, marked by the trace of a butterfly—one had been caught by a palace attendant. The prince asked, “Can this butterfly be restored?” Jiuge replied, “It cannot. Had it returned at my call, it would have been possible. But now, too much time has passed, and it shall remain here as a marvel.” With that, he took his leave.

*The Biographies of Divine Immortals* records: Huang Chuping was sent by his family to herd sheep. A Daoist took him into Golden Flower Mountain, and he never returned. His brother, Chuqi, searched for him in vain. Later, in the marketplace, he encountered a Daoist who asked, “Is that not the young shepherd from beneath Golden Flower Mountain?” Chuqi followed the Daoist to see his brother. When he asked where the sheep were, Chuping replied, “The sheep are east of the mountain.” Chuqi went to look but saw only white stones. Chuping shouted at them, and the stones all transformed into sheep.

Shunxing repels the Rong; Jinxian reviles the Qiang.

*The New Record of the Red Terrace* states: Li Shunxing, at the age of eleven, became a disciple of a Daoist. One day, thousands of flying immortals gathered in the sky above, their divine radiance illuminating the chamber. The Celestial Attendant of the Scriptures was commanded to open the repository of nine-colored flowing clouds, from which two scrolls were brought forth and bestowed upon him. Emperor Wen of Wei once summoned him to the capital, where both court and common folk revered and trusted him, calling him Master Li the Alchemist. At the age of thirty-eight, in the sixth year of the Datong era, he feigned illness and announced his passing. On the day of his burial, his nephew encountered him on the road to Mount Li and said, “If the Son of Heaven has not yet forgotten me, you may erect my image here, facing north, and I shall ward off the corrupt customs of the northern Rong for the nation.” His nephew returned home only to learn of his death. He reported the matter in full to the throne. Envoys were sent to exhume the coffin, but it was found empty. A shrine was then established in Shayuan, named the Sacred Li.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* records: Wang Yan served as President of the Secretariat under Emperor Wu of Jin, and his daughter, named Jinxian, was the consort of Crown Prince Minhuai. When Luoyang fell, Liu Yao and Shi Le abducted Jinxian and attempted to cross the Meng Ford. There, they sought to take her as a wife. Jinxian cursed them, saying, “I am the wife of the Crown Prince, daughter of the Minister of Works. How dare you, barbarian dogs, presume to touch me?” With these words, she threw herself into the river. Her maidservant, named Liuchu, then declared, “If the great one has done so, the lesser should follow.” She, too, leapt into the river. At that moment, the Female Perfected of Mount Song, Han Xihua, was traveling and took pity on them. She rescued the two, concealing their true fate while outwardly appearing as if they had perished. Their bodies were secretly preserved, and they were taken into Mount Song, where they were installed in the Grotto of Flourishing Yang to assume new ranks among the immortals. Liuchu was then twenty-two or twenty-three, of comely appearance and resolute character. Her original surname was Tian, and she hailed from Yuyang. She was the granddaughter of Tian Feng, former magistrate of Junyi during the Wei dynasty. Feng had once performed a hidden act of virtue, which extended to Liuchu.

Meng Qin’s whirlwind spins; Qiu Lin’s clouds soar.

*The Unofficial History of Jin* states: Meng Qin mastered the arts of Zuo Ci and Liu Gen, and the people flocked to him. The ruler Fu Jian, displeased by his sway over the masses, sought to execute him. Suddenly, Qin appeared before him. Jian detained him, and as they drank, the ruler ordered his attendants to seize him. Qin transformed into a whirlwind and departed. Someone reported he was east of the city, but when pursued, he was suddenly before the Han River, which could not be crossed.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* records: Fan Qiulin served as the Lifeguard Assistant in the Grotto of Flourishing Yang. Skilled in the art of whistling, his notes resembled the mingling of a hundred birds, now like the wind stirring the forest, now like the beat of drums. In an instant, clouds soared above him, and vital qi stirred the woods.

Xiu Fu’s vengeance on the dancing girl; Xu Gu’s wrath upon the lecherous monks.

*The Gleamings of Immortal Biographies* states: Chen Xiufu, known as Chen the Seventh Son, once attended a banquet hosted by the governor of Banan. There, a dancing girl insulted him. Xiufu merely smiled at her, and in an instant, the girl’s beard grew several feet long. She wept and complained to the governor, who apologized on her behalf. Xiufu then sipped a mouthful of wine and sprayed it upon her. After a long while, she returned to her former state. He then took his leave.

*The Extensive Records* states: Immortal Lady Xu was the daughter of Xu Zhicai, the Grand Preceptor of Northern Qi. Though she lived for several centuries, her appearance remained that of a woman of twenty-four or twenty-five. Skilled in the arts of restraint and incantation, she wandered all famous mountains and scenic landscapes, often lodging in the caves and foothills. Once, while staying at a monastery, she was subtly insulted by several arrogant monks with cunning words. The lady rebuked them, but the monks grew only more enraged. She laughed and said, “I am but a woman, yet I have abandoned home and family to roam the rivers and clouds, unafraid of dragons and tigers. How could I fear the likes of you, mere rats?”

The lady then removed her outer garments and suddenly extinguished the candle. The monks rejoiced, believing they had gained the upper hand. By dawn, as the lady prepared her staff to depart the mountain, all the monks stood frozen in place like corpses, their mouths unable to speak. Only after she had gone several *li* did they return to normal.

The Eight Lords of Huainan and the Ten Elders of Tiangu.
*The Biographies of Divine Transcendents* states: King Liu An of Huainan humbled himself to honor scholars. Eight lords arrived at his gate, and the gatekeeper said, “His Highness seeks to extend his lifespan and attain the Dao of agelessness. Yet all of you are already old.” The lords replied, “If the king wishes to see youths and call them ‘possessors of the Dao,’ we shall oblige by becoming young.” With these words, they transformed into boys, their complexions like peach blossoms.

*The Biography of Master Gao*: The Dharma Master Yan Da, styled Daotong, harbored aspirations beyond the mundane from childhood. Together with Wang Yan, Su Daobiao, Cheng Faming, Zhou Huasheng, Wang Zhenwei, Shi Daole, Yu Changwen, Zhang Facheng, and Fu Daochong—ten men who vied in their mastery of the Dao and shared the same path—he was known to the world as the Ten Elders.

Zhao Sheng plucked the peach; the field master descended with jujubes.
*The Biographies of Divine Transcendents* states: The Han Celestial Master led his disciples to the summit of Cloud Terrace, a sheer cliff. Below grew a single peach tree, its branches like human arms, clinging to the stone wall above an unfathomable abyss. The peaches were large and ripe. The Celestial Master said to his disciples, “Whoever can retrieve these peaches shall be taught the essentials of the Dao.” Over two hundred disciples crouched to peer down, their legs trembling and sweat streaming, yet none dared linger. All retreated, claiming they could not do it. Only Zhao Sheng cast a wooden plank from above, stepped onto it without stumbling, and gathered an armful of peaches. The Celestial Master then divided them among the disciples. Later, he transmitted to Sheng the ultimate Dao, and Sheng ascended to the heavens in broad daylight.

**Records of Eminent Daoists**: Master Tian, whose given name was Shiwen, hailed from Gulao. He received the teachings of the Three Caverns and practiced qi refinement through ingestion and fasting, never once neglecting his devotions. Whenever he performed rituals to beseech blessings or heal the sick, his prayers were invariably answered, and the afflicted recovered. Once, when Chen Gang invited him to conduct a jiao offering, spirits suddenly descended with several jujubes onto the altar—fruits unlike any found in the mortal realm. Dreams of blossoms and grass: Yi Mengchang wore flowers, while Ziwei was clad in reeds.

**Elegant Talks from the Prefectural Pavilion**: Yi Mengchang’s origins are unknown; he took his name after dreaming of two suns. In the late Tang, he declined office, donning feathered robes to wander mountains and rivers in carefree abandon, ever fond of carrying flowers. In *Later Collection of Green Locks*, Mengchang once inscribed a poem on the immortal’s altar at Sikong Temple in You County:
*"Only green pines idly play with the sun’s light,*
*No cloud-borne cranes ever lead men astray."*
And on a yellow hibiscus:
*"Dew congeals on golden cups, dripping the last of the wine,*
*Sandalwood dots the beauty’s lips, exhaling rare fragrance."*
Later, Mengchang achieved liberation in death; when his coffin was opened, no corpse remained.

**Extensive Records of the Taiping Era**: During the reign of Duke Jing of Song, a master of astrology was offered the rank of grand minister. He was stationed in a towering pavilion to observe celestial omens, provided with delicacies and adorned in precious robes. One day, a wild man clad in reeds and bearing brambles knocked at the gate and requested an audience. The duke received him in the grand hall, where the stranger spoke of future portents and past events with unfailing accuracy. By night, he studied the stars; by day, he calculated with charts and scrolls. He refused fine garments and rare foods. The duke bowed and asked, *"Now that the state faces ruin and chaos, how may you aid me?"* The man replied, *"When virtue is uneven, disorder draws near. Cultivate virtue to draw the people, and heaven will send auspicious signs, while men will praise your transformation."* The duke praised his words and bestowed upon him the surname Zi and the name Wei—thus was Ziwei born. Wan transmitted the eight sounds, while Wei bequeathed three treasures.

**Supplement to Immortal Biographies**: Wan Baochang’s origins are unknown. From childhood, he excelled in music. One day in the countryside, he encountered a group of ten or so figures in resplendent carriages and robes, their banners arrayed in grandeur. They summoned him, saying, *"The Celestial Emperor has deemed you naturally attuned to music. He will transmit the eight sounds to the latter age, for the music of this declining era must be rectified to restore the true tones of the Zhengshi reign. You have not yet fully grasped them."* They seated him and taught him the music of past dynasties, the sounds of order and chaos—nothing was omitted. Baochang committed it all to memory. Thereafter, the immortals ascended into the void. From that time, no earthly music escaped his mastery.

**Records of Immortal Responses**: Wei Na, styled Jingzhao, failed the imperial examinations and traveled to Shu. At the Zheng family’s garden pavilion, he saw dozens of immortals in attendance, their embroidered skirts and radiant robes beyond mortal ken. They told him, *"We have a new melody called 'Purple Clouds.' Today, the Son of Heaven reveres the Dao of immortals. We wish to entrust this music to you, that you may present it to the sage ruler of the Tang. May we rely on you?"* Wei replied, *"I am but a scholar, unversed in music. I cannot fulfill this."* The immortals said, *"If you cannot, we shall convey it to the emperor in a dream. Yet your presence here is fated by the Dao. We gift you three treasures, the sale of which will enrich you for life."* They ordered an attendant to bring forth a cup called the Jade-Green Chalice, a pillow resembling jade but veined like millet, and a purple jade casket—all luminous and translucent. Wei accepted them with bows and thanks, then took his leave. When he turned back, the pavilion had vanished.

**Hidden Immortal and Ghostly Officials**: Qin Shuang concealed himself as an immortal elder; Zhou Yan served as a ghostly marshal.

**New Records of the Elixir Terrace**: Qin Shuang now dwells as an immortal elder on Mount Hua.

**True Sayings**: Zhou Yan was appointed marshal of the ghostly bureaucracy.
*Note*: Zhou Yan, styled Boren, was a native of Ancheng in Runan. He served the Jin and crossed the Yangtze, rising to the rank of Vice Director of the Imperial Secretariat. In the first year of Yongchang under Emperor Yuan, Wang Dun marched south. Zhou was arrested at the southern gate of Stone Citadel and executed at age fifty-four. He was posthumously enfeoffed as Marquis Kang of Jiyin.

**The Thief of Books and the Pilferer of Elixirs**: Jia Dan stole scriptures; divine mastery purloined cinnabar.

**Unofficial Histories**: One day, Jia Dan ordered his guards to retrieve documents from a dry well. They recovered several scrolls—all Daoist texts. He dispatched over ten men to copy them. As they finished, a Daoist suddenly burst in, shouting Jia’s name and berating him for daring to steal the books. Jia humbly apologized, and the Daoist took them back.

**Records of Eminent Daoists**: Fu Shentong was orphaned young and impoverished, herding cattle to support himself. At his pasture, he often saw a Daoist coming and going. Over time, they grew familiar, and the Daoist asked, *"Could you become my disciple?"* Shentong replied, *"I could."* The Daoist then led him into the water.

You should follow him without fear. Upon entering, he saw the dwelling was solemn and pristine, with medicine pouches and alchemical furnaces. The Daoist had Shentong tend the fire and taught him the arts of *huangbai* transmutation. After three years, Shentong often longed for human contact. One day, when the Daoist was away, he secretly took some elixir and hid it elsewhere. Upon returning, the Daoist asked where the elixir was, but Shentong concealed the truth. The Daoist sighed, *"I originally intended to transmit the essential Dao to you, but now, how can I teach you further? Though you have mastered all these methods, they will not aid your longevity."* He then departed.

**Crane-Boned Liao, Jade-Faced Ping of Ping’a**:
**Records of Precious Guests**: During the Tang, Jiang Fang composed the stele inscription for Master Liao of Jingfu Mountain in Lianzhou. *Chong* was his given name, *Qingling* his courtesy name, and *Jingfu* his residence. His name was inscribed on the jade halls and golden tablets; his home lay beyond the crimson clouds. As for his crane-like bones and pine-like visage, his pure demeanor and empty heart—dwelling among mortals yet unburdened by heaven and earth—he counted among his teachers and friends Fusang Gong, Tao Yinju, and Celestial Master Zhang.

**Divine Immortals’ Biographies**: Liu Ping’a did not reveal his given name. During the late Han, he served as Magistrate of Ping’a in Jiujiang, adopting the title as his sobriquet. He practiced medicine to save others, feeling their illnesses as his own. Later, he encountered a divine being who taught him the Way of Concealed Preservation. He ingested the essence of the sun and moon, residing in the celestial palaces, his complexion like jade.

**Three Swords of Guarding the True, Four Rings of Yang Bao**:
**Biographies of the Wondrous Sage**: In the early Jianlong era, the Black Killer descended and said to Zhang Shouzhen, *"You are a man of integrity and purity, fit to expel evil. I shall first teach you the sword arts to rid the people of demons."* The sword techniques numbered three: one of bronze and iron, tempered into a sharp blade—with but a glance from his eyes, he could wield it. For the afflicted, a single flourish dispelled malevolent *qi*. Earth spirits causing calamity and aquatic creatures turning monstrous were subdued by the upper sword. Strange phenomena in mountains and marshes were quelled by the middle sword. *Wangliang* harming humans were vanquished by the lower sword.

**Sequel to Qi Xie’s Records**: Yang Bao saw a yellow sparrow struck down by a crow. He took it and placed it upon the rafters, feeding it yellow flowers. Once its feathers regrew, it would depart at dawn and return at dusk. One night, a yellow-robed youth appeared, saying, *"I am the Queen Mother’s messenger. Once, en route to Penglai, I was struck down by a crow. Thanks to your rescue, I gift you these four rings, that your descendants may remain pure and ascend to the rank of the Three Dukes—just as these rings."* Bao’s son Zhen fathered four generations of eminent officials.

*End of Scroll Eight of the Comprehensive Records of the Immortals of the Three Caverns*
*Scroll Nine of the Comprehensive Records of the Immortals of the Three Caverns*

**Compiled by Orthodox Unity Daoist Chen Baoguang**

The Barefoot Immortal, the Yellow-Haired Elder.

*Extending the Records of Marvels*: Academician Le of the Bureau of Historiography, Shi Jingde, once served as Supervising Censor of the Western Capital. He dreamed of a man in full court regalia who announced, *"I come by imperial command to summon you."* Soon, he beheld magnificent palaces and asked the messenger, *"Whose residence is this?"* The reply: *"This is the Emperor’s abode."* Ascending the steps to meet the Emperor, he was told, *"Your lord seeks an heir. I shall select one for him."* After a brief interval, a man appeared, and the Emperor said, *"The Central Plains seek an heir. Go, and do not refuse."* The man bowed repeatedly, pleading for exemption, but the Emperor insisted, *"Go."* He assented and departed. A courtier standing nearby remarked, *"This is the Barefoot Immortal Li of Mount Heng, who often revels in wine."* The following year, Emperor Renzong was born.

*Records of the Forgotten*: Li Pin, at the end of the declining Zhou, dwelled on Mount Fanjingrishishi, cutting all ties with the world. Only five yellow-haired elders visited him—some riding swans and cranes, others clad in feathered robes. Their ears emerged from the crowns of their heads, their pupils were square, their complexions lustrous as jade. Each grasped a staff of green bamboo, and together with Pin, they discoursed on the reckonings of heaven and earth. When King Ling later sought the *Yi* from the Archivist beneath the Pillars, desiring to gather all who cultivated the Dao under heaven, scholars from the four seas flocked to him without exception. The Five Elders were the essences of the Five Directions.

Jing Tang’s Jade Table, the Bright Star’s Stone Mortar.

*Investigations of the Spirit Realm*: Cui Jingtang of Ruyin once hosted a Daoist who claimed the surname Mei. After several months as a guest, Jingtang acquired a jade table and intended to present it to Gao Shensi, Military Commissioner of Shouchun. He told Mei, *"Master, remain here while I journey to Shouchun."* Returning within ten days, he instructed his nephews to attend to Mei, assuring him of no cause for concern. Mei replied, *"I am from Shouchun and was traveling north to visit a relative. I, too, shall return soon. You may go ahead. After staying here so long, I wish to repay your family. Do you have mercury?"* Jingtang said, *"Yes."* Mei took ten taels, placed them in a cauldron, and refined them with fire. In moments, the mercury turned to silver. He said to Jingtang, *"Take this as provisions for your journey. When you reach Shouchun, seek my home east of the city."* They parted ways. Later, Cui searched for Mei’s residence in the eastern part of the city but found nothing for days. Villagers all said, *"There is no Daoist here surnamed Mei. Only in the Temple of the Prince of Huainan stands an image of Master Mei. Could it be him?"* Following their suggestion, he found the image—indeed, it was the True Lord Mei. Thereafter, he never encountered him again.

*The Collected Records of Immortals*: The Radiant Star Jade Maiden dwells on Mount Hua, partaking of jade nectar, and ascends to heaven in broad daylight. Atop the mountain lies a stone terrace, spanning several acres and rising three *chi* in height. There are five stone mortars, known as the Jade Maiden’s Hair-Washing Basin, wherein the water remains emerald-clear, neither swelling with rain nor dwindling in drought. Within the shrine stands a jade stone horse. He Gu was an old acquaintance; Li Sheng, a former friend.

*Gleanings from the Past*: The scholar Yuan Xia of Hongzhou, while accompanying his parents through Yongzhou, encountered He the Immortal Maiden and said, *"My homeland has a Pavilion of Old Acquaintances, and Yongzhou has one as well. If this is genuine, that is false; if this is false, that is genuine. Pray, Immortal, settle the matter."* The Immortal replied, *"This pavilion’s name derives from a selected poem: ‘By Dongting Lake, I meet a returning traveler; by Xiao and Xiang, I encounter an old friend.’ The waters of Dongting and the currents of Xiao and Xiang share a single source. Now, within Yongzhou’s borders, the Xiang River flows from the left, and the Xiao River converges on the right—hence the name ‘Yong.’ Thus, Yongzhou’s pavilion bears the true name, while the other does not."* She then bestowed a poem:
*"Yong has long been called the old prefecture,
By Xiao and Xiang’s source, a new pavilion stands.
Before its gates, flowing waters have run since ancient days,
Yet now, no old friends remain upon its terrace.
A gentle breeze, the slanting sun—southern Chu at dusk,
Crows cry, blossoms fall—eastern Zhe in spring.
Since you ask of former times,
The pavilion’s name by the Jiang’s left is not the true one."*

*The Collected Biographies of Immortals*: Li Sheng, styled Yunju, cultivated the arts of refining *qi* and nurturing form. When Yuan Qiao governed the east of Zhe and Bai Juyi served as prefect of Qiantang, both—being old friends of Sheng—admired his literary grace and Daoist attainments. They invited him to their banquets and asked, *"In this age of peace, why not seek rank and emolument instead of remaining a commoner?"* The Master slowly intoned:
*"Born into a Confucian family in an age of peace,
The profound and mysterious weighs heavy, while commoner’s garb is light.
How could I chase fame and profit along the world’s paths?
I serve the Jade Emperor and return to the Supreme Clarity."*

Cheng Zi was bitten by a serpent; Chen Chun vomited a crane.

*The Declarations of the Perfected*: Long ago, Cheng Zi of Leisure, from youth enamored of longevity, studied the Dao for over forty years. Later, he entered the depths of Mount Jing, where, after seventy years, the mountain spirit tested him. Mistaking the spirit for a Perfected One, Cheng Zi bowed and sought the Dao—only to be seized and nearly devoured by a great serpent. He narrowly survived by swiftly awakening, invoking the Supreme, and visualizing the Seven Stars to repel it.

*The Green Locks*: Chen Chun reached Peach Blossom Spring and, captivated by the unmatched beauty of its Han Mountains, packed provisions and followed the Han River in search of wonders. After nine days, he arrived beneath a towering cliff. That night, he heard voices within the rock face. Exhausted and out of food, he suddenly detected a sweet fragrance, and more than ten petals of a giant flower drifted out. He gathered and ate them. Soon after, a green-clad maiden appeared on the bank below, gathering water plants. He inquired of her, and she replied, *"This is the domain of the Lady of the Three Springs. On the evening of the Mid-Autumn Festival, three Immortals will gather here."* Presently, the Three Ladies invited him in. He beheld crimson doors and jade windows, unlike anything in the mortal world. A feast commenced, and music played as he exchanged verses with the Ladies in perfect harmony. They cautioned him, *"Beware of going to the southern pavilion."* Chen Chun stealthily entered the pavilion and found a jade flute upon the table. He took it and blew, whereupon his hometown, its people, mountains, and rivers appeared vividly before him—his wife and children laughing and conversing. After a long while, the vision vanished. Chen Chun, unaware, vomited an egg, which fell to the ground and hatched into a crane that flew away. The Immortals reproached him: *"You heeded not our warning—was this not fate? Thirty years hence, you shall return here."* They then sent Chen Chun home by boat.

The Guests of Siming; the Immortal Maidens of the Nine Palaces.

*The Declarations of the Perfected*: Emperor Wu of Wei served as Grand Tutor to the Northern Lord; Sun Ce, Emperor Gaozu of Han, Emperor Wu of Jin, and Xun Yu were the Guests of Siming.

*The Biographies of Female Immortals*: During the reign of Emperor Gaoxin, the land of Shu had no ruler or governor, and its people devoured one another in turn. The father of the Silkworm Maiden was seized by neighbors, leaving only his horse behind. The maiden, grieving for her father, neglected food and drink. Her mother consoled her and vowed before all, *"Whoever brings my husband home shall have this daughter as his wife."* Yet none could retrieve him. One day, the horse broke free of its tether and departed, returning with the father. Thereafter, the horse neighed incessantly and refused to eat. The father said, *"I vowed to give my daughter to a man, but how can a horse be matched with a human?"* Enraged, he shot and killed the horse, then dried its hide in the courtyard. As the maiden passed by the hide, it suddenly wrapped around her, and she flew away.

After ten days, the hide came to rest upon a mulberry tree, and the maiden transformed into a silkworm, feeding on the leaves and spinning silk into cocoons, which clothed the people of the world. Her parents grieved for her without end, until suddenly the Silkworm Maiden descended from the heavens upon a cloud, riding that very horse. She spoke to her parents, *"The Supreme One, in recognition of my filial devotion, has appointed me as a celestial consort of the Nine Palaces. You need no longer sorrow."* Then she ascended once more into the clouds and departed. From this arose the custom in Shu: in official temples and private shrines, statues of a maiden draped in horsehide were fashioned, called the Horse-Head Maiden, to invoke blessings upon sericulture.

**Jade Mist of Yuyi, Pearl Dust of Cangwu.**
*The Record of Forgotten Tales* states: East of Penglai lies the land of Yuyi, where golden mists often drift, rising and falling like mountains or towering pavilions, their doors and windows ever facing the light. When the mist dissipates, the doors all turn northward. South of Daiyu Mountain stretches a thousand *li* of level sands, golden as powdered flakes, flowing endlessly like a river. When birds or beasts tread upon it, their feet sink into it; when the wind stirs, the sand rises like mist—this too is called *golden mist*. In the time of Emperor Shun, birds like sparrows came from Danzhou, exhaling vapors of five colors that billowed like clouds, called *Pingxiao*. These birds could fly ceaselessly, carrying green sand-pearls. When a light breeze blew, the dust rose—this was called *pearl dust*. Today, beyond Cangwu, mountain hermits gathering herbs sometimes find round green stones like pearls. Those who consume them do not die; those who wear them feel their bodies grow light. Thus, an immortal’s verse in praise of the Southern Marchmount reads:
*Pearl dust, round and still, light and bright—*
*Those who follow the Dao and consume it gain long life.*

**Ma Ming’s healing of illness, Qiao Yan’s relief of poverty.**
*The Biographies of the Immortals* records: Ma Mingsheng was a minor county clerk in his youth when he was slain by bandits. As he lay dying, a divine being saved him with medicine, restoring him to life. Realizing then that the path to immortality was proven true, he followed the immortal, carrying medicinal herbs to Mount Lu to receive the essential teachings. After mastering the arts, Ma Ming traveled the world, enduring hardship and toil. He chose to refine medicines to heal the sick rather than ascend to heaven. Though he dwelled among people, few knew he was a transcendent.

*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* records: Tan Qiaoyan was a Daoist of Mount Mao during the Baoli era. He wandered through Tiantai, Jiangsu, and Zhejiang, appearing no older than twenty. None knew he possessed the Dao. He devoted himself to secret acts of merit, often leaving gold along the road to aid the poor. If someone returned it, he would not acknowledge it; when asked why, he replied, *"The Perfected Lord of the Hidden Realm transforms earth into gold to relieve those in need. I regret I cannot do the same. What is the harm in leaving useless things for others?"* In time, it became known that he possessed the divine elixir that transmutes gold.

**Yu Zhang’s exorcism of malevolence, Yuan Ze’s chastisement of spirits.**
*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* records: The Master Yu Zhang, styled Changwen, received from the Yellow Hua Elder and the Perfected Lord of the Supreme Ultimate the sixty cyclical signs, the talismans and seals of the Five Emperors and Five Marchmounts—one hundred thirty-six in all—as well as treatises on the origins of heaven and earth, the fundamentals of talismans, and the methods for setting up altars. He recorded the dates of talisman transmissions, the lunar phases, and the oral instructions of the perfected immortals in meticulous order. Thus, his understanding of the unseen deepened daily, enabling him to dispel demons and sever malevolence. His divine transformations were boundless, and the common people who benefited grew ever more numerous.

*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* further records: Zuo Yuanze, at the Green Screen Abbey in Wenzhou, found that the local earth god was customarily offered blood sacrifices. If the rites were neglected, the god would wreak havoc. Yuanze struck the spirit’s back three times with his staff. The next day, a large raccoon dog was found dead in the courtyard, its back bearing three staff marks. The villagers later dreamed the god said, *"The one who attached himself to me was beaten to death by a celestial official. Beware of offering me blood sacrifices again."*

**Yu Jun’s five branches, Ji Qing’s single leaf.**
*The Records of Dou Lianyi* states: Dou Yujun once dreamed his ancestors told him he would have no sons and his lifespan was short. Ten years later, he dreamed again that his ancestors said, *"Thirty years ago, you were indeed fated to be childless and short-lived. I secretly informed you of this. But in recent years, your name has been inscribed in the celestial and netherworld registers, for your hidden virtues have extended your allotted years by three cycles and granted you five sons, all of whom will achieve honor and longevity. You will die in peace, and afterward, you will reside in a grotto-heaven as a perfected immortal."* Thus, Feng Dao composed a poem for Yujun:
*Dou the Tenth of Yanshan,*
*Taught his sons the righteous way.*
*A sacred trunk stands old and tall,*
*While five branches of the immortal cassia bloom.*
This is the tale to which he referred.

*The Supplementary Records of Immortals’ Biographies*:
Chen Jiqing once traveled to the Qinglong Monastery in Chang’an, where he met an old man in yellow who sat with him by the brazier. On the wall hung a *Map of All Under Heaven*, depicting the lands of Hua and Yi. Jiqing sighed, *"Ten years I’ve been away from home, enduring hardships for ten thousand li—how might I cross the Huai and float the Luo to return to my native hills?"* The old man laughed and said, *"This is no difficult matter."* He ordered a servant boy to pluck a bamboo leaf from before them and place it upon the Wei River on the map. *"Fix your gaze upon this boat, and your long-held wish shall be fulfilled."* Jiqing stared intently—suddenly, the Wei’s waters stirred, and the bamboo leaf swelled into a mighty vessel. In a daze, he found himself sailing upon it, soon arriving home. There, his brothers, wife, and children joyfully welcomed him with bows. Afterward, he bade them farewell, boarded the boat once more, and reached the Wei’s banks—only to awaken as if from a dream, still seated before the painted map, the immortal elder tending the brazier as before. Jiqing expressed his gratitude and asked the old man’s name. The elder replied, *"I do not delight in revealing my surname to mortals. I have dwelt on Mount Zhongnan for seven hundred years. You possess the bones of one destined for the Dao, and thus we met."*

Feng Jun carried the sack; Wang Yao bore the basket.
*The Records of Original Transformations*:
Feng Jun of Guangling earned his livelihood as a hired laborer. One day in the market, he encountered a Daoist purchasing herbs. The Daoist placed them in a sack weighing over a hundred *jin* and hired Jun to carry it. They traveled to Liuhe, where they boarded a small boat. In an instant, they arrived at Xingzi Bay on Mount Lu, where a vast lake stretched before them, its surrounding peaks rising in layered beauty. The Daoist disembarked and walked five or six *li* to the foot of a mountain, where a massive boulder, several *zhang* square, stood. He struck it with a stone, and the boulder split open. Two young boys emerged from within, while inside the grotto, several Daoists played chess and laughed. The Daoist said, *"The one who carried the load is very hungry."* They gave him sesame rice to eat, then told Jun, *"You have labored far for us—here are a thousand coins as reward."* Jun declined and prepared to leave. The Daoist pointed to a stone shaped like a tiger and instructed him to mount it. With a flick of his whip, the stone flew away like the wind, and in the blink of an eye, Jun found himself outside the gates of Guangling. By the time he reached home, dusk had fallen. He lit a candle and untied the money from his waist—only to find it transformed into gold.

*The Biographies of Divine Immortals*:
When Wang Yao was caught in the rain, he had his disciple carry a nine-jointed staff and a basket, yet neither became wet.

Lu rebuked the bandits; Liu and Feng halted the robbers.
*The Youyang Miscellany*:
A man named Lu was traveling to Fuzhou with several companions when they encountered six or seven men in fine clothes, their breath reeking of wine. Lu suddenly shouted at them, *"Your deeds are unrighteous—your lives will not last long!"* The men prostrated themselves in the dust, bowing their heads in submission. His companions were astonished, but Lu said, *"These are river bandits."* Such was his extraordinary power.

*The Biographies of Divine Immortals*:
Liu Feng of Pei had earned military merit and studied the Dao. Once, merchants from Chang’an accompanied him, carrying goods worth ten thousand in gold. Suddenly, in the mountains, they were ambushed by hundreds of bandits wielding blades and bows, surrounding them on all sides. Feng addressed the robbers: *"You serve in office and eat the emperor’s grain, while I plow the fields and my wife weaves—why do you block the roads, endangering others for your own gain?"* The bandits grew even angrier, but Feng shouted, *"The Heavenly Soldiers strike first!"* Instantly, the bandits turned their hands to bind themselves, blood gushing from their mouths as they collapsed, near death. Those still able to speak begged for mercy, vowing to reform. Feng said, *"I originally intended to kill you all."* Then he commanded the Heavenly Soldiers to release them, and the bandits all rose to their feet.

The rustic wielded a crutch; Zizhi bowed twice.
*Cold Studio Night Talks*:
Liu Yefu was lame and leaned on a crutch. Every year, he would hobble to Luoyang to admire the flowers, regaling others with his witty and engaging tales. He once composed lyrics in varying meters:
*"The lame man these years—what does he resemble?
A full beard, dignified as can be.
In this vast world, his crutch holds all his skill.
No matter north or south, wherever he goes,
he fills his gourd with wine and drinks himself drunk.
Who knows where tomorrow will take him?
After admiring the Luoyang flowers by dusk,
he returns to the imperial city—
with nothing at all.
Passing the gourd-bowl, he takes on disciples anew.
Suddenly, he thinks of a light boat on the water,
a reed mat spread across it.
Laughing heartily, beyond Suiyang’s gates,
there lies a fine West Lake."*

*The Records of Divine Immortals’ Manifestations*:
Wang Zizhi, styled Xianmiao, often traveled between the capital and Luoyang. When Pu Chongying, Duke of Langye, first took up his post as military governor, Zizhi resided at the Purple Ultimate Palace, where the duke treated him with great generosity. Hearing of his fondness for wine, the duke sent him two chests of it daily. Zizhi claimed to be from the Wei clan of Henan. Though his appearance remained that of a man in his forties, he cultivated *qi* and none knew his true age.

Li angled without bait; Tao strummed the stringless zither.
*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists*:
The Daoist Li Daosheng befriended Zheng Ao and Luo Yinzhi. Zheng farmed the land, while Yinzhi sold herbs to support himself. Daosheng had a fishing rod—he angled without bait. He could also transform stones into gold, a feat Zheng once witnessed and believed, though he never sought such power. All three loved wine, poetry, and the games of chess and whistling.

They possessed a large gourd, said to ward off cold and heat. Wine stored within it would not spoil over time. Daily, they carried it among flowers, trees, waters, and rocks, drinking a cup and reciting a verse. Once, in the midst of wine’s warmth, they composed linked verses. Zheng said:
*“A gourd holds heaven’s famed treasure,*
*Two men wander the world carefree.”*
Luo replied:
*“Drunk, Yinzhi leans on clouds behind—*
*What then is true nature’s design?”*

*The Biographies of Jin Recluses*:
Tao Yuanming, styled Yuanliang, had no ear for music. He kept only a stringless zither, which he would stroke in harmony when gathering with friends over wine. He grasped the zither’s essence—why need the sound of strings?

*Yan the Flame Emperor drilled fire; Fengzi followed the smoke.*
*The Supplementary Biographies of Immortals*:
When King Zhao of Yan ascended the throne, he was devoted to the Dao of immortals, and the immortal Gan Xu served him. After the king had practiced the Way for many years, Gu Jiangzi arrived by emptiness and told him: “The Queen Mother of the West will descend to observe your cultivation and reveal to you the essentials of the numinous origin.” A year later, the Queen Mother indeed came. She wandered with the king beneath the kindling woods, speaking of *Yan the Flame Emperor’s drilling of fire*—how he lit green cassia fat to illuminate the night. Suddenly, a moth bearing fire alighted in the king’s palace, yielding a round pearl of Yuanqiu sand. From it he fashioned a pendant. Ascending the Terrace of Grasping the Sun, he obtained a luminous pearl carried by a divine crow, which dispelled oppressive heat. Thereafter, the Queen Mother descended thrice to the Yan palace, but King Zhao, obsessed with conquest, failed to heed Gan Xu’s counsel of clarity. The Queen Mother never returned.

*The Biographies of Immortals*:
Xiao Fengzi lived in the time of the Yellow Emperor. Legend holds he was the Yellow Emperor’s master potter. A man once passed by and tended his kiln fire, able to emerge and enter through five-colored smoke. In time, he taught Fengzi, who piled fire and burned himself, rising and descending with the smoke.

*Boren received a western posting; Gengsheng was transferred east.*
*The True Sayings*:
The Recorder of Destinies said: “Recently, I saw Zhou Boren appointed as the Western Bright Duke and Protector of the Central Metropolis—a role akin to the Grand Tutor of the mortal realm. Also, Gengsheng, the Grand Marshal Yu Liang of Jin, was assigned by the Great Emperor to govern the spirits of Wu and Yue, overseeing their salt and labor—truly a taxing task.”
Yuanzi said: “Gengsheng has now been transferred to become the Marquis of the Eastern Sea.”

*Changling leaned on his staff; Shiyun rode a boat.*
*Western Clarity’s Poetic Discourse*:
Stone Peak of Yingyang, a towering and elegant summit, bore a stone chamber where Xing Hepu once calculated the heart. During the Zhiping era, Xu Changling Anshi, having early attained the arts of immortality, came to dwell there, staff in hand. The world was awestruck. Later, he traveled to the Palace of Grand Clarity. At the time, Ouyang Xiu, the Cultured and Loyal Duke, governed Bozhou. Though he had never spoken of Buddhism, upon hearing of Changling, he invited him to his official residence. Their conversation brought sudden enlightenment. He presented a poem:
*“Green hair, blue eyes, a frame so light,*
*Soaring away on a crane, playing the flute’s song.*
*Seated in the prefectural hall, bamboo stirs with wind—*
*Could this be Sun Deng’s long whistle?”*
The Duke also once wrote of Changling:
*“Southern Manor faces Northern Manor’s abode,*
*Then deeper into the mountains, ten li more.*
*On woodcutters’ paths I often tread,*
*True feeling parts from worldly lore.*
*Clouded peaks, a dog’s bark as a shooting star flies,*
*Beyond the sky, a cock crows as dawn’s sun rises.*
*Yesterday, someone came to ask after me—*
*Swiftly, I wrote my reply on fallen leaves.”*
Reading this, one can envision the man.

*The Records of the Western Mountains*:
Wu Meng, styled Shiyun, once rode an iron boat atop Mount Lu. Suddenly, a crimson dragon bore him away to roam the sea.

*The Daoist of Yue Creek; the Immortal Lord of Shaoshi.*
*The Gleanings*:
Atop the sheer summit north of Qinchuan City stood the Palace of Po and Xiao, its magnificence beyond description. Its thresholds were carved from green jade, lustrous as glass. A Daoist from Shu said: “An ancient immortal left a poem at the base of the threshold.” Upon searching, they found it:
*“A Daoist from Yue of Han, unknown to men,*
*Ascends and descends heaven on a single crane.*
*The cavern’s gate is locked, jade windows cold,*
*Dripping dew, grinding cinnabar, he marks the *Zhou Changes*.”*

*The Divine Immortals of the Wang Clan*:
The Immortal Lord Wang, in the early Tianfu era, wandered from Shangdang. Passing through the Wei clan of northern Deng, he entered Mount Song, roaming forests and valleys until he lost his way. Over a year later, his disciples and kin traveled from Huguan and Taihang, journeying south through Song and Shaoshi. They inquired at the temples and sites they passed, seeking him. At last, they found him in a secluded cliff of Mount Song’s northwest, seated solemnly within a cavern, rapt in meditation. His disciples burned incense and paid reverence, reluctant to leave. The Lord said: “The Supreme One, deeming me to have slight merit, has summoned me to become the Immortal Lord of Shaoshi. The paths of immortals and mortals diverge—do not grieve.” With these words, he soared into the void.

Bi Ling pulled the ship, and Xian Ke’s house ascended.
*According to the* Jin History*: Bi Ling, a native of Jianchang, was reticent by nature. Though he lived among common folk and endured frequent insults, he never showed resentment, earning the village’s nickname of “the fool.” At the time, Fan Changbin, the magistrate of Shunyang, was conscripting laborers to build official boats in the mountains of Jiancheng. When the boats were completed and ready to launch, two hundred men could not budge a single vessel. Just as more workers were being summoned, Bi Ling said, “This is not a matter of insufficient strength, but rather of improper coordination.” He volunteered to pull the boat himself, and with only a few men, it glided away like flowing water. The crowd was astonished, hailing him as divine.*

*From* Miscellaneous Notes from the North*: During the Yifeng era of the Tang, beneath the Cuiwei Mountain in Hengyuan County of Qingcheng, a villager named Wang Xianke consumed an elixir left by a Daoist. His entire household ascended to immortality, as recorded in* A Supplement to the Biographies of Immortals*.*

Qu Sheng fled under the rod, and Luo Yu was exiled for his sins.
*From* Extensive Records*: The Daoist Qu Sheng, beaten by his master, fled in haste into a cave. Moments later, he emerged holding a chess piece, saying, “Just now, I encountered Qin men playing chess and stayed for a drink. This piece is from Qin.” His master examined it—small as a turtle, lustrous as jade.*

*From* True Sayings*: E Lü Hua, a perfected female immortal, descended to the home of Yang Quan during the Jin dynasty’s Shengping era. She declared, “I am Luo Yu, an immortal from Mount Jiuyi who attained the Dao. For my transgressions, I was temporarily exiled to this turbid world to atone.” Turning to Quan, she said, “Have no thoughts, no worries, no demands, no desires, no affairs, no actions. Walk paths others cannot walk, learn what others cannot learn. Cultivate inner serenity and diligent austerity—this is how I have practiced for nine hundred years. Now, I bestow upon you the secret of liberation through apparent death.” Quan, too, attained the Dao and now resides in Xiang Mountain, never emerging.*

Qian Shao received celestial scriptures, and Wang Bao wielded divine strategies.
*From* Continued Biographies of Immortals*: Ye Qianshao studied under a Daoist of the Western Mountains, mastering the teachings of the Twelve Perfected Lords. While dwelling in seclusion, he encountered a divine general bearing a sword and the Tiger-and-Dragon Talisman. Two attendants in yellow and green robes, holding registers, bowed before him, saying, “Heaven commands that you receive this register. Divine generals and soldiers are at your service to aid the world.” Qianshao accepted the celestial scriptures, which resembled military rosters. When summoned, the spirits would arrive. Thereafter, malevolent forces would vanish at the mere mention of his name, and those who received his talismans remained free of illness for life. All regarded him as divine.*

*From* Divine Immortals of the Wang Clan*: Wang Bao entered Mount Hua when, one evening, he heard the sounds of flutes and drums. A thousand chariots and ten thousand riders descended from the void. A divine figure declared, “I am the Perfected Lord of the Great Ultimate. Hearing of your toils in the mountains, though you have yet to grasp the essentials of the Dao, I pity you.” Later, Guo Linggai was commanded to bestow upon him the Jade Seal of Divine Flourish, appointing him Perfected Lord of Pure Emptiness to govern the affairs of the Lesser Grotto-Heaven.*

From the east, Wei struck the serpent; Zhao Yu beheaded the flood dragon.
*From* Tales of the Marvelous*: Wei Zidong encountered a Daoist who said, “I am refining the Dragon-Tiger Elixir. In two nights, it will be complete, but many demons and monsters will appear. I need a brave man to wield a sword and stand guard. When the elixir is done, I will share it with you.” Zidong followed the Daoist to a stone chamber on a high peak where the elixir was being refined. The Daoist instructed, “At the fifth watch, stand at the cave’s entrance with your sword. Whatever spirits or monsters appear, strike them without fear.” Soon, a giant serpent approached, and Zidong struck it with his sword, driving it away.*

*From* Records of Extraordinary People*: Zhao Yu studied under the Daoist Li Jue on Mount Qingcheng. Emperor Yang of Sui, recognizing his worth, appointed him governor of Jiazhou. At the time, an ancient flood dragon in the Jianwei Pool was wreaking havoc, sinking seven hundred boats in five months. Enraged, Zhao Yu led a thousand armored soldiers to the riverbanks, drumming and shouting until the earth trembled. He plunged into the water with his blade. After a while, the river turned crimson, and the cliffs shook with thunderous roars. Zhao Yu emerged from the waves, his left hand gripping the dragon’s head and his right wielding the knife.*

Wei saw the severed brush; Cao beheld the eastern reeds.
*From* Extensive Records*: Wei Ziwei, nephew of Wei Xingwu, the Tang dynasty’s inspector of Xichuan, had a subordinate named Ding Yue who served in his ranks. One day, Ding announced his departure, refusing to stay. “We shall meet again near the capital in fifty years,” he said. Ziwei searched for him afterward but found no trace. Later, when Ziwei was traveling to the capital for an official posting, he lodged at an inn on Mount Li. Hearing commotion in the thoroughfare, he went to investigate and saw a grand procession with armored guards and fluttering banners. Among them was Ding. Startled, Ziwei recognized him, and Ding smiled secretly, whispering, “Do you remember our parting at Linyi? Fifty years have passed in an instant.” Ziwei asked how he had achieved this. Ding replied, “I told you long ago—how could I escape it?” When Ziwei offered what he needed, Ding requested a brush. Ziwei searched his bag and handed one over. On the day of execution, Ziwei went to observe. As the blade fell, he alone saw the brush’s tip snap like frost, and in that instant, the condemned Ding leapt free. “From now on, I shall wander afar,” he told Ziwei. “Strive in your Daoist practice. Though two kalpas still separate us, I shall await you in the stone chamber of Kunlun.” With that, he departed. (Daoists call it a *chen*, Buddhists an *effort*, and the worldly call it an *age*.)*

*From* New Records of the Elixir Terrace*: Zuo Ci, styled Yuanfang, was skilled in transformations. When Cao Cao sought him, Zuo refused. Enraged, Cao ordered his capture, commanding pursuers to kill him on sight. Days later, someone claimed to have seen Zuo and beheaded him, presenting the head to Cao. Overjoyed, Cao examined it—only to find it was a bundle of eastern reeds.*

The left serpent coiled in retreat; the tiger of Chen roared in fury.
*From* Biographies of Eminent Daoists*: Zuo Yuanze dwelled in a cliffside chamber. To its left stood ten great bamboos, and before it, a winding pond bore dozens of emerald lotuses. Dozens of patterned birds, resembling swans and mandarin ducks, swam among them.*

Drawn by its charm, he thus spent the night in the chamber. As night fell, something coiled around his body. Upon waking, he merely sat with eyes closed, and only at dawn did it release him. Inspecting his coarse hemp robe, he found only traces of foul, viscous drool.

That evening, he sat again in the chamber, pacing with a net to await it. Sure enough, a creature emerged from the pond—several *zhang* in length, its eyes casting a radiance like twisted glass.
