With a sharp rebuke from the cliffside, it slowly recoiled and slithered back into the pool. He then warned: "Let later disciples take heed: never dwell alone in caves or grottoes."

Moreover, Master Zhengyi, surnamed Chen and named Baochi, recited the *Great Cavern Scripture* with such resonance that rare birds and strange beasts would often attend him in guard. Each morning at the Laozi shrine and on the eight seasonal festivals when he cast dragon tablets, a white tiger would docilely accompany him, leading his retinue to and fro. If anyone harbored ill intent, the tiger would roar and surge forward, striking the leftmost locust tree in the courtyard—so terrifying the malefactor that they would flee in fear. Thus, the tree was called the "Tiger-Testing Wood."

The Duke of Fang warded off lingering rats; his loyalty and forbearance earned him the name "Cat."
*From* Anecdotes of Immortals*: Duke Fang of Tang studied under Li Babai and obtained his divine elixir. His entire household then ascended to heaven with their dwelling, even the dogs departing with them. Only the rats fell from the sky, their intestines spilling out, and for a month, they would replace their entrails thrice. To this day, the mountain below harbors "dragging-gut rats," which Minister Shu Guangwei called the "Tang rats."

*From* Forest of Records*: Guo Shu, styled Zhongshu, served as a *Zhouyi* Academician during the Guangshun era of the Later Zhou. Demoted to Registrar of Qian Prefecture, he left office upon completing his term and never returned to service, instead roaming freely among Qi, Yong, Jing, and Luo. He drank to excess, addressing all he met—noble or base—with the same casual speech. When he encountered scenic mountains or rivers, he would fast entirely, taking no food.

In the scorching heat of midsummer, his body remained free of sweat. In the depths of winter’s bitter cold, he would break the river ice to bathe, and the surrounding ice would melt. Later, he died suddenly and was buried by the roadside. When his grave was reopened for reburial, his coffin was found empty, as if he had shed his body like a cicada’s husk.

Zhao Xi saved Hui; Dong Feng revived Xie.
*True Reports*: Zhao Xi, during the Han dynasty, served as Inspector of Youzhou. He aided the poor and rescued Wang Hui and dozens of others from extermination by the river. His own person ascended to Crimson Mound, while his descendants all dwelled in grotto-heavens.
*Traditions of Divine Transcendents*: Dong Feng, styled Junyi, was in the south when Du Xie, the Inspector of Jiaozhou, died of a poisonous illness after three days. Feng arrived and placed three round pills into his mouth. After the time it takes to eat a meal, Xie opened his eyes, moved his limbs, and regained his former complexion. By midday, he could sit up, and thus his life was restored. When asked how this came to be, he said: "At first, I saw a red-robed clerk come to summon me, saying, ‘The True Lord Dong has commanded it.’ So I was sent back."

The Duke of Du’s temple image; the Prince of Cao’s hunt.
*Extensive Records*: When Du Naigong was young, he once went to Zhaoying County and played in the wild with other children. A Daoist called out to him alone, stroking his palm and saying, "Young master, apply yourself to study and do not play with these children." Pointing to a temple, he said, "I live here. Will you visit me?" After the Daoist left, Du went to the temple and found a dilapidated village shrine with only one hall still standing, housing an image of the Elder Lord. At first, the Daoist’s face had appeared half purple-black, but now, upon closer inspection, Du saw that it resembled the man he had met earlier—half his face had simply been darkened by rainwater.

*Records of Origins and Transformations*: When the Prince of Cao was demoted to Hengzhou, there was a mountain recluse named Zhang, a master of *yan* arts. The prince once went hunting and captured ten deer, but as the encirclement closed, the deer vanished without a trace. Summoning the mountain man, he asked what had happened. The man replied, "This is the work of a *yan* adept who concealed them." He then fetched water and performed a *yan* incantation. Soon, a Daoist appeared in the water, barely an inch tall, carrying a sack and staff. The prince asked the mountain man, "Can we pursue him?" "Yes," he replied. The prince ordered the pursuit, and the Daoist came, laughing. When asked where the deer were, he said, "I saw them die, so I pitied and concealed them. They are now by the mountainside." The prince sent men to look, and indeed, the deer were hidden by a small hill, motionless. The prince laughed and let them go.

The boy turned the boat; the old man carried the thorn-bush.
*Investigations into the Supernatural*: In the two caves of Gongshan, there was a well-like opening. At the end of the Xiantong era, a Daoist named Zheng descended over a hundred *zhang* by rope, where he saw a light. Following it, he found the path blocked by water, but across the divide grew flowers and trees, and two Daoists were playing chess. A boy poled a boat toward him and asked if he wished to cross. Zheng replied, "I must return." The boy turned the boat and left, and Zheng ascended by rope. The next day, the well’s mouth was sealed with stone, and no one has entered since.

*Records of Hidden Wonders*: Outside the Sword Gate, Hou Tong found four yellow stones, each the size of a *dou*, which turned to gold when he took them. With this wealth, he purchased over a hundred beautiful concubines, grand estates, and vast fertile fields. Suddenly, an old man carrying a thorn-bush approached and said, "I have come to collect your debt. Return my gold—do you not remember?" He took all of Hou’s possessions and cast them into the thorn-bush, which did not seem cramped. In an instant, the old man vanished. Years later, Hou saw the old man walking with a companion. When questioned, the old man only smiled and said nothing. When pressed, he vanished again.

Ziyang ate peach-bark; Tian Luan consumed cypress-leaves.
*True Reports*: Huang Ziyang of Wei was versed in the wonders of long life from youth. He entered Boluo Mountain to study the Dao for over ninety years, eating only peach bark and drinking yellow water from stone. Later, he met Sima Jizhu and thus attained transcendence.

*Extensive Records*: Tian Luan entered Mount Hua and encountered an extraordinary man who pointed to a cypress tree, saying, "This is the elixir of long life. Why seek it afar?" Luan returned home and consumed cypress leaves for several years, feeling his body grow lighter. One night, he dreamed of immortals holding tablets leading him into a grotto, where the assembled transcendents said, "The Cypress-Eating Immortal has arrived," and inscribed his name in gold characters on the Jade Register of the Upper Clarity. They told him, "For now, remain among men. When a position is available, you will be summoned." He then awoke.

Later, he concealed himself in Mount Songyang, retaining a youthful appearance at one hundred twenty-three years of age.

*The Records of the Immortals of the Three Caverns, Scroll Nine* ends here.

---

*The Records of the Immortals of the Three Caverns, Scroll Ten*
Compiled by Orthodox Unity Daoist Chen Baoguang

Guo had no four walls; Liu endured two hardships.

*The Record of True Realms*: Guo, styled Wenju, once obtained the *Divine Tiger’s Inner Perfection Purple Prime Elixir Scripture* in a stone chamber on Mount Huayin. Amid the decline of the Jin dynasty, he carried thatch to Mount Dadi in Yuhang, where he felled trees and leaned them against the forest to fashion a shelter—without four walls. Clad in kudzu fiber and a deerskin cap, he cultivated beans and wheat, gathering bracken to barter for salt and fermented milk. Any surplus food he gave to the poor. In the Qianfu era, he was enfeoffed as the Perfected Lord of Lingyao, and many traces of him remain in the mountains.

*The Records of Hidden Worthies of the Jin*: Liu Linzhi, styled Zijì, cherished simplicity and humility from youth, cultivating emptiness and few desires. He paid no heed to formal conduct, and none knew of him. Fond of wandering mountains and marshes, his heart was set on pursuing the reclusive. Once, while gathering herbs on Mount Heng, he ventured deep and forgot to return. He came upon a stream where, on its southern bank, stood two stone enclosures—one sealed, the other open—too deep and wide to cross. As he sought to turn back, he lost his way and, encountering a woodcutter, asked for the path, barely making it home. Some said the enclosures held immortals’ elixirs and all manner of wondrous things. Linzhi wished to search again but never found the place.

Xiaocheng tied his sash and naturally donned a silk headscarf.

*The New Record of the Cinnabar Terrace*: Liang Chen, styled Xiaocheng, delighted in the Dao of immortals. At seventeen, he became a Daoist, observing fasts, reciting scriptures, and broadly performing meritorious deeds to relieve all manner of suffering. He trod lightly upon the earth, fearing to harm living things. When birds or beasts crossed his path, he would step aside to avoid them. He treated others with humble reverence, ever serene and empty. One day, he told his disciple Wang Zinian: “My good friends await me at the southern ford. I must go to them; you should know this.” He tied his sash and set out southward. Zinian followed, perceiving only dense clouds swirling, their mist veiling the forests and valleys. After a long while, Chen suddenly leapt into the air and soared upon the clouds, vanishing in an instant. Only the distant sound of flutes and drums lingered.

*The Primer of Our Dynasty*: Zhao Ziran, a native of Taiping Prefecture, dreamed of a man who presented him with a plain headscarf and robe. The man’s beard and hair were streaked with white, and he claimed the surname Yin. Leading Zhao up a mountain, he said: “You bear the qi of the Dao.” He taught him the method of avoiding grains and produced a sprig of green cypress, bidding him eat it. In the dream, Zhao consumed it, and upon waking, he no longer needed food.

The green-scarved fine guest, the white-robed old man.

*The Biographies of Divine Immortals*: One day, Master Chen Xiyi said to his disciple Jia Sheng: “A fine guest will arrive today; go quickly and announce him.” Soon after, a man in a brown robe and green scarf appeared at the gate. Jia hurried to report, but the man had already departed. Jia pursued him and saw an old man clad in deerskin. He asked: “Has the old man ahead gone far?” The old man replied: “This is the divine immortal Li Babai—when he moves, it is eight hundred li.” Then the deerskin-clad old man vanished as well. The Master said: “That old man was Master Baibi of the Grand Clarity, who had attained the Dao. Now that he is gone, and the deerskin-clad one has also departed, I cannot remain long.” He then returned to the True.

*The Traditions of Emperor Wu of Han*: Emperor Wu journeyed to Mount Tai, where he encountered a white-robed old man whose ears rose to the top of his head, his hair hanging to his shoulders. A radiance several feet long crowned his head. The Emperor, astonished, questioned him. The old man said: “I have heard that on Qin Mountain grows a calamus, one *cun* long with nine nodes. Consuming it grants longevity.” Then he vanished. The Emperor said: “This was the spirit of the mountain.”

At that time, Wang Xing, having heard of this, consumed the calamus and attained immortality. One *dou* of Deng’s elixir, a thousand strands of myriad herbs.

*The Continued Biographies of Immortals*: Songyang in Chuzhou was the place where Celestial Master Zhang cultivated the True. Deng Qushe, admiring the retreat of those who came before, built a thatched hut to dwell in and practiced austerities day and night. To the south of the mountain stood a massive boulder, upon which he often sat. A divine person appeared and told him: “The Celestial Master’s cinnabar elixir and sword lie beneath this stone. You may retrieve them.” The Master said: “This stone was set by Heaven; no human effort can move it.” The divine person replied: “Persist in your cultivation without slackening, and the elixir and sword may yet be obtained.” The Master followed his words, and before three years had passed, the divine person presented him with one *dou* of elixir and one sword. Later, the Master administered the elixir to heal the sick, and upon achieving his work, ascended in broad daylight.

There was also the Old Man Who Sold Medicines, whose name was unknown. He always carried a large gourd to sell remedies. When people sought medicine for their ailments, he gave it to them whether they could pay or not, and it was invariably efficacious. Once, someone jokingly asked him: “Do you have the Great Return Elixir?” He replied: “I have one pill, but its price is a thousand bolts of silk.” Everyone laughed at him, thinking him mad. Later, in Chang’an, as he sold his medicines, he shook his gourd—it was already empty, save for a single pill resting in his palm. He sighed: “A hundred years of selling medicine, and not one person has bought it. How lamentable! Today, I shall take it myself.” The moment the pill entered his mouth, five-colored clouds arose beneath his feet, and he soared into the sky.

The Bronze Plaque Marked the Deer, the Golden Basin Concealed the Magpie.

*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* records: During Emperor Minghuang’s hunt in Xianyang, a great deer was captured. As the imperial kitchen prepared to butcher it, Zhang Guo petitioned: “This is a transcendent stag, already a thousand years old. In the fifth year of *Yuanshou* during Emperor Wu of Han’s reign, I once accompanied the hunt in Shanglin Park where this very deer was caught. It was released then, marked with a bronze plaque on its left antler.” The emperor ordered verification, and indeed, a bronze plaque of about two inches was found, though its inscriptions were worn and faint.

Another account tells of Xuanyuan Ji, whom Emperor Xuanzong once summoned to inquire whether longevity could be governed. Ji replied: “Sever sensual indulgence, moderate rich flavors, harmonize grief and joy as one, and bestow virtue without partiality. Thus, you will naturally align with Heaven and Earth in power, match the sun and moon in brilliance—even the Way of Yao, Shun, Yu, and Tang may be attained, let alone long life and enduring vision.” Upon his withdrawal, the emperor tested him by concealing a white magpie beneath a golden basin. As Ji rested in the palace quarters, he remarked to the eunuch: “How could the emperor again make this old man divine the hidden?” The eunuch did not grasp his meaning. The emperor promptly summoned him back. As Ji reached the jade steps, he declared: “The white magpie beneath the basin should be released.” The emperor laughed heartily.

Li Ming Refined the Elixir, Bozhen Gathered Herbs.

*The Records of Mount Mao* state: Li Ming, a high official who fled the world and refused office, secluded himself on Yugang Mountain in Juqu. There, he refined an elixir to completion and ascended to the Dark Capital, appointed to the rank of Grand Minister. To this day, his old altar and well remain.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* recount: Jiang Bozhen entered Meng Mountain to practice the Dao and search for herbs when he suddenly encountered an immortal. The immortal instructed Bozhen to stand upright at noon, but his shadow leaned askew. The immortal said: “You know the value of the Dao and devote yourself earnestly to its study, yet you do not realize that imbalance is a flaw. If you wish to correct your heart, always place your hands on your shoulders at sunrise, aligning the sun with your heart. When you feel warmth in your abdomen, your heart will be upright—practice this often for the best results.”

The Ge Clan’s Dragon-Silk Canopy, Nüwa’s Cloud Curtain.

*The Records of Marvelous Ancestors* tell of a woodcutter’s wife from Jiufi Mountain, née Ge, who fell ill for several days. Rising, she addressed her parents-in-law: “Not only has my illness abated, but I shall also attain transcendence.” Soon after, she departed through the door, riding a cloud away. Days later, she returned, describing the celestial palaces: “The towers and pavilions are carved from green jade, their windows and doors adorned with pearls and woven with dragon-silk curtains, their five hues shimmering in the luminous void. There, the incense of immortality burns without end.”

“I have come but briefly to see you.” Soon, a five-colored chariot arrived, drawn by two dragons and driven by mighty warriors, with maidens attending. The woman mounted it and drifted slowly away.

*The Beginnings of Things* states: The Lady of Nüwa crafted cloud curtains and refined the five-colored stones to mend the heavens.

Liu An’s chickens and dogs, Jing Zhi’s tortoises and cranes.

*The Continuation of the Biographies of Transcendents* records: Liu An was the grandson of Emperor Gaozu of Han. Fond of divine transcendence, he refined the Great Elixir, and once it was complete, he departed. At the time, people spread the tale: when Liu An was about to leave, there remained some elixir in a vessel, which he placed in the courtyard. Chickens and dogs licked and pecked at it, and all ascended to heaven. Thus, it is said: “Cocks crow in the heavens, dogs bark amid the clouds.”

*The Biographies of the Transcendents* tells of Xiao Jing Zhi, who fasted to study the Dao. One day, he dug in the earth and found something resembling a human hand, which he ate at once. He encountered a stranger who told him: “You have ingested the numinous medicine of transcendents. What you ate was *rouzhi*—the Fleshly Mushroom. Those who consume it attain a lifespan equal to tortoises and cranes. You should withdraw into the mountains to await the Supreme Dao.” Thereupon, he vanished into seclusion.

De Xiu’s thunderclaps, Wang Xing’s cloud chariots.

*The Traditions of Divine Transcendents* records: Cao De Xiu claimed to have come from Green Islet Mountain in the Eastern Sea, traveling through Jiangxi. For over thirty years, those who saw him found his appearance unchanged. The sick who sought his talismanic remedies were all cured without exception. There was a maiden of twenty who was betrothed when suddenly she fell prey to a malevolent spirit; every treatment only worsened her condition. Her father visited De Xiu and described her illness in full. De Xiu said: “Your home lies near a mountain stream with a deep pool. In spring, your daughter strolled by the brook and was espied by a flood-dragon, which seized her essence and spirit, drawing them into its cavern. Take this talisman and cast it into the pool—soon you will see proof.” After the talisman was thrown, the waters suddenly churned and roared with thunderous echoes. Presently, a creature over twenty feet long surfaced, its form like a black serpent, its head as large as a great ladle—already cloven dead. The maiden’s illness was cured forthwith.

*The Divine Transcendents of the Wang Clan* tells of Wang Xing, a man of Shu who once served as magistrate of Pujiang. Enamored of the numinous peaks in his jurisdiction, he resigned his post and withdrew into the mountains for nine years. One day, he saw within a grotto blossoms of jade unfurling and golden toads leaping. Entering, he found an elixir of transcendence, which he consumed. Immediately, he ascended in a cloud chariot. To this day, the grotto is called “Magistrate’s Governance,” marking the origin of the name.

Floating Beard and White Leopard, Thunder Duke and Yellow Serpent.
*The Traditions of Divine Transcendents* recounts: The Perfected Master Shi Cun, styled Master Floating Beard, studied under Master Yellow Reed and obtained *The Esoteric Writ of the Three Sovereigns*, a scripture for commanding tigers and leopards. He concealed himself in Stone Chamber Mountain of Mount Heng, riding a white leopard whenever he ventured forth. In the *Yuan Kang* era of the Jin, he ascended in broad daylight.

*The Extensive Records of the Strange* states: Wu Sheng Zhi once saw the Thunder Duke pursuing a yellow serpent along a riverbank. Casting a stone at it, he heard a resonant clang. The Thunder Duke flew away, leaving behind a bronze sword inscribed: “Third Flood-Dragon-Slaying Sword of the Perfected Lord Xu Jing Yang.”

Unrivaled and the Gift of Fruit, Sun Zhong and the Offering of Melons.
*The Green Jade Pendant* relates: Li Wu Jing traveled to the capital to seek office. At Zhuqian Garrison, he encountered beggars quarreling on the road. An old woman said: “I spent my life begging to gather this small sum, yet this man borrowed it and never repaid.” Wu Jing took out the exact amount and gave it to the beggar, who thanked him: “I truly owed him the money. You, a stranger passing by, not only repaid it but also resolved our dispute. How can I repay your virtue? My home is in Longhe—look for the green curtain at Bamboo Fence Alley. Will you visit?” Wu Jing found this strange and went at once. Entering the door, he saw several beggars huddled around a fire, their faces brightening at his arrival. They invited him to sit and poured a modest drink. Wu Jing remained suspicious and did not drink, merely wetting his lips. Though it was bitter winter, the platter held summer fruits. He took three small imperial peaches and tucked them into his sleeve to bring home. The beggar sent him off with a poem:
*“The gentleman’s many doubts breed many errors,*
*The world’s lack of faith breeds no sincerity.*
*My home’s path is smooth as whetstone—*
*Why would you, sir, refuse to walk it?”*
When Wu Jing returned to his lodging and examined the peaches, they were made of purple gold. Later, he had them carved into wine vessels. At seventy, his complexion remained rosy—could this be the power of merely wetting his lips with that wine?

*The Records of the Netherworld* tells: In his youth, Sun Zhong was poor. When his melons ripened, three men came to beg for them. Zhong offered them melons, saying: “We are the Lords of Destiny.” Then they transformed into white cranes and flew away.

Hanging Frost of Lingyang, Flowing Rosy Clouds of Manqing.

*The Accounts of Immortals* states: Lingyang Zichun consumed morning radiance and summer *hangdan*. These are the profound yellow qi of heaven and earth at midnight; when the rosy clouds first emerge, the blue-yellow qi is the morning radiance.

*The Master Who Embraces Simplicity* records: Xiang Manqing, a native of Puji in Hedong, entered the mountains to cultivate the Dao. One day, an immortal came to welcome him and took him to the heavens, where he beheld the Purple Palace, Golden Grove, and jade tables. The immortal offered him a cup of *liuxia*, and thereafter he knew neither hunger nor thirst. Suddenly longing for home, he was reprimanded by the Celestial Emperor and sent back to Hedong, where he was called the "Banished Immortal."

Purple Clouds of Minghuang, Crimson Snow of Yuanyuan.
*The Records of the Xuan Chamber* tells: Emperor Minghuang of Tang dreamed of over ten immortals descending upon rosy clouds, arrayed in his courtyard, each holding musical instruments and playing them. Their melodies were so clear and transcendent they seemed not of this world. When the music ceased, one immortal stepped forward and said: "Does Your Majesty recognize this music? It is the immortal tune *Purple Clouds*, which I now transmit to you as the true beginning of Tang’s imperial sound." Minghuang was overjoyed and immediately had it taught. Upon waking, he played it on his jade flute, mastering its rhythms entirely.

*The Supplement to Immortal Biographies* records: Shen Yuanzhi, of unknown origin, was summoned to the Shangdu Kaiyuan Temple during the Kaiyuan era, where the emperor’s favor grew ever deeper. Minghuang would discuss the Dao with him for hours on end. Once, he ordered Lady Zhao Yunrong to attend Yuanzhi with tea and medicine, treating him with utmost reverence. During a private moment, she begged for a small dose of his elixir to prolong her life. Yuanzhi replied: "I have nothing to withhold, but your time in this world is short." When she pressed him further, he gave her a single pellet of *Crimson Snow* and said: "Even in death, this elixir will preserve your body. Only ensure your coffin is large, your tomb spacious, your mouth filled with pearls and jade, and your grave well-ventilated. Then your soul will not drift into emptiness, nor your spirit sink into darkness, and after a hundred years, you may revive. This is the Way of Refining Form in the Great Yin." Later, Yunrong accompanied the emperor to the eastern capital and fell ill at the Orchid Palace. She recounted this to the Noble Consort as he had foretold.

Daoyuan Observes Lanterns, Zhiwei Gazes at the Moon.
*The Supplement to Immortal Biographies* records: The Heavenly Master Ye Fashan, styled Daoyuan, during the Kaiyuan era’s first full moon night, accompanied Minghuang as he moved his retinue to the Shangyang Palace to view the lanterns. The court artisan Mao Shunxin had constructed over thirty resplendent pavilions, adorned with gold, kingfisher feathers, pearls, and jade, towering one hundred and fifty feet high. The slightest breeze set them chiming in harmony. Lanterns shaped like dragons, phoenixes, leopards, and unicorns seemed to leap and bound, defying human craft. The emperor was delighted, but the master said: "Though these lanterns are unmatched, those in the western capital tonight are equally splendid. I have just come from there." The emperor, astonished, asked: "If I wished to go there now, could it be done?" The master replied: "It is simple." He then instructed the emperor to close his eyes. In an instant, they arrived. After admiring the spectacle for some time, the emperor asked to return. Again, he closed his eyes, and in a moment, they were back beneath the pavilion—yet the songs and dances had not yet ended. Later, in Binzhou, the emperor pierced a wine cup with an iron ruyi. The next day, he sent a eunuch on a pretext to retrieve it, confirming the tale was no lie.

*The Minor Records of Sanshui* tells: The Daoist Zhao Zhiwei had attained the Way. One Mid-Autumn, as overcast skies persisted, the crowd lamented the ruined festival. Zhiwei said: "Prepare wine and delicacies, and let us ascend Tianzhu Peak to admire the moon." As they stepped outside, the sky cleared. Upon reaching the summit, the moon shone as bright as day. They descended only at moonset, but upon returning to the mountain’s base, they found the same bitter wind, rain, and gloom as before.

Lie Yukou’s Heart Split Open, the Dao Lord’s Tongue Cut.
*The Cloudy Han Anecdotes* records: The tomb of Lie Yukou lies in the outskirts of Zheng. A Hu man, poor in his youth, worked as a tinker and washer, earning the nickname "Hu the Tinker." Whenever he had fine wine, tea, or fruit, he would offer them at Yukou’s shrine, praying for wisdom. One night, he dreamed a man slit his belly with a knife and placed a scroll within his heart. Upon waking, he could compose poetry. Once, he presented a poem to Magistrate Han, which read:
*"Suddenly hearing Mei Fu come to call,*
*Laughing, he dons his lotus robe and steps from his thatched hall.*
*The children, unused to carriages and horses,*
*Scramble to hide deep in the reeds."*

*The Extensive Records of Marvels* tells: In Kuizhou, there was a Daoist named Wang Hongming whose tongue was so long he could not pronounce words clearly. He said: "I recite the *Dao De Jing*." One night, he dreamed the Dao Lord trimmed his tongue. Upon waking, his speech was flawless.

The Ash Bag Feigns Madness, Ma Nuo Stands Peerless.
*The Biographies of Divine Immortals* records: In Shu, there was a Daoist who feigned madness and was commonly called the "Ash Bag," a disciple of Heavenly Master Zhai. Zhai often warned his followers not to underestimate this man, saying: "I am not his equal." Once, in the midst of a heavy snowfall, the Ash Bag, clad in hempen robes, entered Mount Qingcheng. At dusk, he sought lodging at a monastery, but the monk said: "This poor monk has only one robe—how can I accommodate you in such cold?" The Ash Bag replied: "One robe is enough." At midnight, the wind and snow grew fiercer. Fearing he had frozen to death, the monk went to check and found him several feet from his robe, his body steaming like a steamer, sweat pouring as he slept uncovered. Before dawn, he left without a word. Once, he suffered from mouth sores for months, appearing on the verge of death. The villagers, who revered him as a deity, prepared a vegetarian feast. After the meal, he suddenly said to the crowd: "Look inside my mouth—what do you see?" He opened his mouth wide as a winnowing basket, revealing all five organs. The onlookers were stunned. Later, no one knew his fate.

*The Unofficial History of Jin* records: Ma Nuo’s real name is unknown. During Shi Jilong’s reign in Wei, he begged in the streets, always wearing coarse hempen clothes—hence the people called him "Ma Nuo." His words were extraordinary, his manner like a madman’s. When he received rice or grain, he would scatter it rather than eat. He displayed many divine marvels and was a close friend of the eminent monk Fotucheng.

Su’s Donkey Worms Flow, Wang’s Corpse Springs a Fountain.
*The Biographies of Divine Immortals* records: Ji Zixun once rode a donkey cart with several scholars to Xuzhou. Passing through Rongyang, they stopped at an inn. Suddenly, the donkey he was driving collapsed and died, maggots streaming from its body. The innkeeper rushed to inform him, but Zixun merely said: "So it is."

As he sat calmly finishing his meal, he slowly took out his staff and tapped the donkey. At the sound, the beast sprang up, walking as before.

*The Teahouse Guest’s Tales* records: There was a man called Wang the Wanderer, whose name and hometown are lost. He would carry a bamboo pole with a basket, leading a brindled dog as he went about knocking on doors in Yingzhou, seeking herbs. In the year *Tianxi wuwu* (1018), he traveled to Mount Qingcheng and returned to Yingzhou. The magistrate, Shi Zhongran, summoned him for conversation. Wang said: "I am a rustic, long dwelling in the city, and now I yearn to return home. I have a request to entrust to you." The magistrate did not understand his meaning. One day, Wang shouldered a load of thatch and went alone to Guoning Temple, where he sat down at the temple gate and died. The village elders, hearing the news, prepared a bier by the roadside. When Magistrate Shi learned of it, he said: "This must be what he meant before." He sent an officer to cremate the body, but when they opened the coffin, his face appeared as in life, his limbs soft, as if in deep slumber. Soon, a clear spring welled up beneath the corpse, lifting it to the surface. They then dressed him in fresh clothes, bathed him, and prepared him for burial.

Fang Yuanbian’s eloquence, Dao Hua’s dullness.
*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* records: Wen Qiu Fangyuan, styled Dafang, was precociously intelligent from childhood and well-versed in the classics and histories. Emperor Zhaozong repeatedly summoned him, but he declined, accepting only the imperial bestowal of robes and the title *Xuantong Xiansheng* ("Master of Mystic Unity"). When Luo Yin visited to receive teachings, the Master would close his eyes and instruct him, saying: "Yin, your talent is high but your insight is shallow—you must compose yourself to awaken to things." Thus, Luo Yin ultimately preserved his well-being until the end. One day, the Master suddenly bathed, burned incense, sat upright, and returned to the True. His complexion remained unchanged, and an extraordinary fragrance lingered for three days. At the time, Qian Wusu dreamed of the Master riding a crane to bid farewell. The next day, news of his passing arrived. When they opened his tomb, only an empty coffin remained.

Another figure, Hou Daohua, often behaved like a madman, and many mocked him, yet he never showed anger. He loved reading alchemical classics, histories, and philosophical texts. When asked why he recited such works, he replied: "There are no dull-witted immortals in heaven." The ruins of Yang Xiong’s grave, the righteous burial of Zhou Chang.

*Supplement to the Biographies of Immortals* records: Yang Xiong, styled Ziyun, was a stammerer but excelled in composition. When Wang Mang usurped the throne, Yang heard that an official in charge of trials was coming to arrest him. Fearing he could not escape, he was then collating texts in the Tianlu Pavilion. He threw himself from the railing and died. In the *Tianfeng* era (14–19 CE), he returned to Shu due to illness and died at home. During the *Qianfu* era (874–879), the scholar Zhao Yu fell ill while traveling in Jiazhou and rested at the Kaiyuan Temple. As he recovered, he saw in the temple hall a young man in tattered clothes, his umbrella worn and patched, yet his appearance spotless. The youth sat beside Zhao, who remarked: "This temple’s grand construction must have required immense state resources—why is there a ruined grave pressed so close behind the hall? I once asked the locals, but none knew." The youth smiled and said: "This is the grave of a descendant of the Marquis of Liu, grandson of Piqiang, and ancestor of the Celestial Master. He served as Prefect of Nan’an, died in office, and was buried here." He then recounted events from the two Han dynasties, Wei, and Jin as if he had witnessed them firsthand. When Zhao asked his name, he replied: "I am Ziyun, surnamed Yang." Zhao tried to follow him, but the youth strode out the gate and vanished. To this day, people occasionally claim to see him.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* records: Zhou Chang diligently practiced sudden virtue, his merit no less than that of the enlightened. In a year of severe drought, tens of thousands perished as travelers, yet Chang gathered over ten thousand skeletons, establishing a public burial ground. When he made seasonal offerings, timely rains would fall. Now he serves in the Grotto as Attendant of the Radiant Dawn, where natural thunder resounds and ritual music is embraced by rosy clouds.

*The Seven Lots from the Cloudy Thatch* states: Ma Xiang, styled Ziran, bore the appearance of a madman yet could cure illness. When someone sought his aid, Xiang used no medicine—only striking the afflicted area with his bamboo staff or pointing at it. Blowing on the staff’s tip produced a thunderous roar, and the ailment would vanish. Often wandering through temples, grottoes, and cliffs, he left behind many poems. One, inscribed upon ascending Hangzhou’s Mount Qinwang, reads:
*Where to seek the Great One at the dawn of division?*
*Only the reckoning remains, shifting mortal hearts.*
*Sun and moon of the Nine Heavens alter dawn and dusk;*
*Rivers and mountains of ten thousand li transform past and present.*
*Wind stirs the water’s light, veiling distant charms;*
*Rain swells the mountain mist, drowning towering forests.*
*Qin’s First Emperor devised his Mount Li scheme—*
*Yet the boundless rivers and seas grow ever deeper.*

*The Lives of Eminent Daoists* recounts: The Daoist Zhang Fale dwelled west of Geng Valley, embracing the Origin and guarding the One for over thirty years. Clouds arose from his roof beams, and rosy clouds enfolded his windows—he named his abode Cloud Dwelling Temple. After long cultivation, his Way was perfected, and fierce tigers submitted to serve at his side. Later, he achieved liberation through the corpse, shedding his form like a cicada.

Li Yu consumed jade, Wang Jie refined gold.

*The Record of Responses and Retributions* tells: During the Later Wei, Li Yu obtained an ancient method for nurturing jade. He journeyed to Lantian, personally overseeing the mining, and unearthed over a hundred pieces resembling rings, discs, and various vessels. Yu then pulverized seventy of them into powder and ingested it. When gravely ill, he told his wife: "When I die, my body will surely show something extraordinary. Do not hasten the burial, that later generations may know the wonders of jade-nourishment." It was mid-Seventh Month, and Chang’an sweltered with deadly heat. Yu’s body lay unburied for four nights, yet his complexion remained unchanged. His wife, née Chang, placed two jade beads in his mouth, but it stayed shut. She said: "You claimed jade-nourishment held divine efficacy—why not accept the beads?" At her words, his mouth opened to receive them, and he exhaled.

The body emitted no foul qi. When lifted for enshrouding, the corpse did not slump or collapse.

*The Yan Tan of Mianshui* records: Wang Jie of Jiangzhou, in his youth, traded along the Jiang and Huai rivers. During the Xianping era, he encountered a man at an inn in Nankang dressed in Daoist robes, whose bearing was most imposing. The man bestowed upon Wang a method for transmuting yellow gold and entrusted him with a divine sword, cautioning, "Do not recklessly reveal this unless you meet a true sovereign." Later, Wang feigned madness, shouting in the markets of Shangrao, and was exiled to Lingnan. He escaped and returned to the capital, striking the *Dengwen* drum to petition the throne. Emperor Zhenzong of Song summoned him for an audience and, pleased, renamed him Zhongzheng. Lodging at the home of the eunuch Liu Chenggui, it was often heard that Zhongzheng spoke with others in a voice like a child’s, saying, "I am the Lord of Life. I have silently offered alchemical gold and silver to aid the state." The world called him "Master Wang the Gold-Burner."

He divined for the singing girl; Qiu Fu healed with ghostly needles.

*The History of the Southern Dynasties* states: He Chang, styled Bozu, was skilled in Daoist cultivation and the arts of divination. Once, he encountered a singing girl who had died, and upon casting her hexagrams, declared, "This is no death—she has been summoned by the Celestial Emperor to sing." He placed a clod of earth upon her heart, and after a moment, she revived.

*The Record of Responses* records: Xu Wenbo of Song, a native of Donghai, was the great-grandson of Pu Yang’s governor, Xi. Fond of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi, he lived in reclusion on Mount Qinwang when a Daoist passed by seeking drink. The Daoist left behind a gourd ladle, saying, "Your descendants should guard the world with the Dao and will attain the rank of *ershishi*." When Xi opened it, he found a scroll of Bian Que’s *Mirror Classic*. He studied it diligently and became renowned throughout the land. Later, Qiu Fu further refined his methods, rising to the post of magistrate of Sheyang. One night, he heard a ghost’s piteous groans and asked who it was. The ghost replied, "I am a man of Dongyang who died of lumbar pain. Even as a ghost, the suffering is unbearable—pray, cure me." Qiu Fu asked, "How should I proceed?" The ghost said, "Fashion a straw figure and press the points with needles." Qiu Fu followed his instructions, applying moxibustion and acupuncture, then offered sacrifices and buried the effigy. The next day, a man appeared to express his gratitude before vanishing. The world marveled at his communion with spirits.

Lu Du measured the deer’s steps; Gui Nian distinguished the birds’ cries.

*The Xianji Collection* records: Lu Du, a master of Daoist arts, vowed in his youth while stranded by the Huai River, "If I escape death, I shall never kill a living thing again." Suddenly, two creatures appeared, swimming against the current to ferry him across. Later, he dwelled in reclusion on Three-Visits Mountain in Xichang, Luling. Birds and beasts followed him, and one night, a deer rammed his wall. Lu said, "You’ve damaged my wall." The deer responded at once and left. Before his house lay a pond where he kept fish, which he called by name—they came in turn to drink and eat before departing.

*The Hanlin Repository of Renowned Discourse* records: Bai Guinian, grandson of Bai Juyi, once encountered Li Taibai on Mount Song. Taibai beckoned him and said, "Since my release from the waters, I have wandered freely among mountains and rivers. Longing for my homeland, I returned west to the peaks of Song. The Celestial Emperor submitted a memorial on my behalf, and I was summoned to oversee the celestial archives—here I have remained for a hundred years. Recently, passing through Tong Pass, I composed a lyric:

*Once feasted in Peach Blossom Spring’s deep grotto,
A song of phoenix dance and simurgh’s flight—
Still I recall the moment of parting,
As the bright moon fell, flowers thick with mist,
Like a dream, like a dream,
Tearfully seeing me out the door.*

Then he produced a scroll and bestowed it upon Guinian, saying, 'Study this, and you may discern the speech of birds and beasts between heaven and earth. Cultivate further hidden virtue, and you may become an earthbound immortal.' Crane-like, Guinian stretched forth his neck; the old man steadied his heart.

*The Extensive Records of Marvels* states: During the Tianxi era, Guo Shangdian worked as a laborer at the Zhou Bridge in the Eastern Capital, washing vessels in a teahouse. A man in a blue headscarf and hempen robe, his bearing majestic, appeared—Guo suspected him to be the transcendent Lü. He rushed forward and prostrated himself, saying, 'To meet you, Master, I would gladly serve as your lowliest attendant.' The master replied, 'If you truly wish to follow me, receive my sword.' Guo assented eagerly, craning his neck in anticipation. The master drew his blade to strike, but Guo cried out—and the master vanished. Later, Guo underwent *shijie* (corpse-liberation); when his coffin was opened, only tattered rags remained.

*The Extensive Records* further notes: At the end of the Tang, an old man carried a gourd to sell medicine in Yizhou. Whatever coins he earned, he distributed to the poor, often saying, 'If one wishes others to be free of illness, one must first rectify the heart. Without chaotic desires, reckless thoughts, cravings, or delusions, the heart remains sound—and though the six internal organs may ail, they are not difficult to heal.' This was the old man’s constant admonition. One day, he bathed in the Jin River, reached into his bag, took a cinnabar pill, and swallowed it. Then he transformed into a white crane and flew away.

The Golden Palace Emperor Lord and the Jade Immortal Holy Mother.

*The Pearl Bag of the Three Caverns* states: The Golden Palace Emperor Lord’s chief minister, the Azure Youth, rides a jade-green aurora, ninefold clouds streaming beneath his feathered canopy, ascending to the Supreme Numinous Capital to present the celestial registers of the Three Heavens.

*The Transmission of the Jade Immortal* records: The Holy Mother was born in the era of the Flame Emperor. Tracing her origins, she hailed from Wuyang Prefecture. Possessing unparalleled beauty, her parents betrothed her to a family of Langye. Hearing a neighbor say, 'The Langye clan are bewitching men,' she wept and refused the match. Boarding a small boat, she drifted freely upon the great river, letting the wind carry her where it would. She arrived at Immortal Capital Mountain, located within the kingdom of Koryo. Atop the mountain stood Jade Immortal Peak; within it lay Jade Immortal Grotto; below flowed Jade Immortal Stream. The Holy Mother moored there, steadfast in resolve and unyielding in virtue. Half a year later, she encountered the Holy Mother of the Flower Maidens, who orally transmitted to her the Dao of flying spirits into the crucible, the method of governing the central source’s spirits, and the art of nurturing the spirit with elixir fire. Having mastered these, she attained the Dao. The title 'Jade Immortal' was bestowed upon her in honor of the mountain and grotto. Before the Jade Immortal Shrine lies a square pond, where water from the Jade Immortal Stream is stored. Later generations cast paper into it to divine fortune or misfortune.

Zhang Zhong’s carriage of peace; Dong Jing’s walled hermitage.

*The Unofficial History of Jin* records: The Daoist Zhang Zhong, at the beginning of the Yongjia era, secluded himself on Mount Tai. He ingested *qi* and ate lingzhi, dwelling in an earthen cavern, his disciples likewise living in dugouts. His teachings relied on embodiment, not words. Though the court repeatedly summoned him, he declined all gifts. The emperor said, 'Would you deign to serve as the Grand Tutor?' Zhang replied, 'Once I fled the world to sing with birds and beasts. Now, with age, my strength wanes, and I am unfit to render service. I beg to return to my old mountain.' The emperor granted his request and sent him back in a carriage of peace. He was posthumously titled Master of Peaceful Dao.

Moreover, Dong Jing would often come to Luoyang, wandering with unbound hair, chanting and singing at leisure. He once lodged within a shrine, begged in the marketplace, and wove a net to cover himself. If given anything, he would refuse gold or silk. At one time, the prefect approached the shrine and said to him, "Now is an age of Yao and Shun—why do you, who hold the Dao, wander lost in the realm of men?" He replied, "All things are base; only man is noble. In motion, the Nine Provinces seem too narrow; in stillness, a walled hut is vast enough." Then he vanished, and none knew his whereabouts.

Chongsu refined his essence; Daoquan endured hardship.

*The Record of True Realms* states: The Hall of Refined Thought was the dwelling of Master Chongsu, Zheng Yuanzhang. The master would fast and sit upright, allowing not even the slightest distraction—just as the *Nanhua* says, "When the mind is undivided, it congeals into spirit." Thus he moved the immortals of heaven, who would descend with lingzhi and plow the fields on evenings when clouds dispersed and the moon shone clear. Only his disciples within the inner chambers could hear his discourses; the world never did. The Hall of Refined Thought stood within the Dongxiao Palace in Hangzhou.

*The Biographies of Divine Immortals* records: The Perfected One Yin Daoquan secluded himself on Mount Heng, where an Upper Perfected descended and said to him, "Those who ascend in broad daylight must possess both the talent and the fulfillment of the Dao. You have received one part of this and thus hold the hope of soaring aloft—this is what your toil has earned, and what your past life ordained." Thereupon he bestowed upon him the *Chart of the True Forms of the Five Peaks*. Taking the mountains’ orientations, the sources of their springs, and the locations of their hidden metals and treasures, he wove them into a single chart and declared, "If you cultivate yourself and attain resonance, you will know that the descendants of Wen Shi and the lineage of Taihe have ever produced divine immortals." With these words, he departed. Daoquan ascended in the Yongjia era of the Jin.

A poor scholar embraced a dragon; Zhichuan expelled tigers.

*The Idle Tales of Rustics* recounts: At Baisha stood the Temple of Lord Qin Shan. Each spring in the third month, the people of Shu would gather there to plow. One day, a man in tattered robes, his face gaunt, also came to the deserted site. The crowd scorned him. As he walked along the riverbank, he sat upon a rock and remarked to those nearby, "There is a sleeping dragon in these waters." No one heeded him. He then stripped off his clothes and entered the water, emerging with a dragon in his arms. The stench was foul, its eyes tightly shut, yet claws, teeth, scales, and horns were all complete. Clouds swirled, winds rose, and waters surged—all fled in terror, and the poor scholar vanished in an instant.

*The Biographies of Divine Immortals* states: Ge Hong, styled Zhichuan, once tended cattle. When tigers repeatedly threatened them, he inscribed talismans and performed an exorcism. A man appeared, calling himself the Lord of High Mountain, and said to Hong, "The harm of tigers and wolves shall now be eradicated."

Chen Baoguang of the Orthodox Unity Dao, compiler.

*Idle Tales of Rustics* records: Minister Wang Chuhui was generous in receiving scholars of humble origins. One day, a Daoist inscribed upon a bamboo leaf in large characters: "Daoist Zhu Taozhi respectfully requests an audience." The minister received him, offered wine with ease, and their discourse flowed with grace and dignity. Chuhui said, "I have long cherished the Dao and wished to build a Daoist abbey on Mount Qingcheng to pursue tranquility." The Daoist replied, "Not yet." He then took two seeds from his pouch, planted them, and covered them with a basin. After a moment, he lifted the basin to reveal flowers already grown, instantly reaching four or five feet in height, blooming in layers. The Daoist said, "This is the immortal’s banner-flower." Later, the minister indeed established his authority in two commanderies.

*Extensive Records* states: Xie Yuanqing encountered an immortal who showed him a branch with cinnabar stalks and jade leaves, its gentle breeze producing harmonious notes like the five tones—this was the *langxu* wood.

The charcoal-woman of Xu Xun, the wooden immortal of Lu Ban.
*The Records of West Mountain* recounts: Xu the Perfected Lord Xun had several hundred disciples. One day, wishing to test them, he transformed charcoal into a woman and sent her among his students. Only ten remained unstained by her allure—these were the very ones who later ascended as perfected lords. Today, the Charcoal-Woman Market and Charcoal-Woman Lane remain within Jianchang County.

*Miscellany of Youyang* records: Lu Ban, a man of Dunhuang whose era is unknown, possessed craftsmanship rivaling creation itself. In Bingzhou, he built a floating pagoda and fashioned a wooden kite. Each time he struck the wedge thrice, he would mount it and return home. Before long, his wife conceived, and her parents questioned her. She explained the circumstances. Later, her father obtained the kite, struck the wedge over ten times, and flew to Wu. The people of Wu deemed it sorcery and killed him. Lu Ban then crafted another wooden kite, retrieved his father’s corpse, and, resenting the Wu people for his father’s death, carved a wooden immortal south of Suzhou City. The figure raised its hand, pointing southeast, and Wu suffered a great drought for three years. When they learned it was Lu Ban’s doing, they prepared offerings and oxen to apologize. Lu Ban severed the statue’s hands, and that month, Wu was drenched in rain. In the early years of the state, the people of Wu still prayed to the wooden immortal.

The precious casket of Fa Shan, the jade coffin of Wang Qiao.
*Collected Accounts of the Strange* states: Ye Fashan, styled Daoyuan, once cultivated his spirit and refined the Dao on West Mountain in Hongzhou. One day, three divine beings from Kuocang descended, transmitting the Supreme Lord’s decree: "You shall assist our Emperor Ruizong and the sagely Emperor of Kaiyuan; you must not conceal yourself among the mountains and crags, neglecting your appointed duty." With that, they departed. At the time, neither emperor had yet ascended, yet their temple names and reign titles were already foretold. Later, he was indeed summoned to the capital.

Later, he aided in the pacification of Empress Wei and the enthronement of Prince Xiang as Emperor Ruizong. When Emperor Minghuang succeeded to the throne, Master Ye served beside these sagely rulers in the capital, foretelling and reporting every omen of fortune or misfortune, every shift in affairs. Once, Tubo envoys presented a treasure, its casket sealed with the words: *"Let Your Majesty open it personally; let no other know the secret."* The court fell silent, but Ye Fashan declared: *"This is an ill-omened casket—let the Tubo envoy open it himself."* The emperor followed his counsel. When the envoy did so, a crossbow bolt sprang from the casket, striking him dead—just as Fashan had foretold.

*The Divine Immortals of the Wang Clan* records: Wang Qiao, magistrate of Ye County during the reign of Emperor Ming of the Later Han, one day beheld a jade coffin descending from heaven. He said: *"Does the Heavenly Emperor summon me?"* He bathed, entered the coffin, and was buried east of the city, where the earth formed his tomb of its own accord. That night, the cattle and horses of the county sweated and panted in exhaustion, though none knew the cause. Later, the people built a shrine to him, called the Temple of Lord Ye.

The numinous phoenix of the Queen Mother of the West; the radiant wife, Caihuan.

*The Biographies of Recluses of the Tang* states: The Daoist Wang Yuanzhi was the son of Tan Xuan, Prefect of Yangzhou during the Liang. His mother, Lady Ding, once dozed at midday and dreamed of a divine phoenix alighting upon her body, whereupon she conceived. She also heard a child’s wail from within her womb. The monk Baoji said to Tan Xuan: *"The child you bear shall become the patriarch of divine immortals."* When Emperor Yang was still Prince of Jin, he dispatched envoys to summon Yuanzhi. Upon their meeting, Yuanzhi’s beard and hair turned white in an instant, and the prince, alarmed, sent him away.

*The Supplement to the Biographies of Immortals* records: Wen Xiao lodged at the Abbey of the Perfected Lord Xu in Hongzhou. On the fifteenth day of the eighth month—the day of the Perfected Lord’s ascension—men and women gathered in throngs, linking sleeves to dance and sing in fulfillment of vows. Suddenly, Wen noticed a courtesan whose song subtly wove his name and hers into verses of divine immortality:
*"If you would accompany me to Lu’s immortal altar,
Then Wen Xiao shall ride the radiant Caihuan.
With embroidered robes and beaded curtains at our disposal,
The jade terrace shall fear no frost or snow."*
Struck by her words, Wen followed her after the song into a grove of towering pines. There, her dwelling was solemn and grand, attended by guards in formation, with desks and ledgers like those of an official bureau. Some records pertained to judgments, many concerning those drowned in rivers and lakes. Wen pressed her repeatedly for answers, until she said: *"This must not be lightly revealed—I would bring calamity upon you."* Soon after, a messenger in yellow robes arrived, declaring: *"For privately revealing heavenly secrets to satisfy desire, Wu Caihuan is demoted to a mortal’s wife for twelve years."* She then returned with Wen to their humble home in Jinling. Later, they rode a tiger together into Yuewang Mountain, and none knew their fate thereafter.

Liu Zhao’s azure radiance; Mu Jing’s lament at Yellow Bamboo.

*The Supplement to the Biographies of Immortals* states: Liu Xiang, at the end of Emperor Cheng’s reign, was collating books in the Tianlu Pavilion when an old man in yellow robes, leaning on a staff of green bamboo, knocked at the door and entered. Asked his name, he replied: *"I am the essence of the Great One. The Heavenly Emperor has heard that the descendant of Metal (Liu) possesses vast learning and has descended to observe you."* He then produced a scroll from his breast—containing books of celestial charts and terrestrial maps—and bestowed it upon Liu before departing.

*The Chronicle of King Mu* records: The Son of Heaven journeyed south to Yellow Terrace Hill to view the hunting grounds of Xia Qi in the level marshes. A bitter cold brought rain and snow, and he composed three verses to mourn his people:
*"My ancestor at Yellow Bamboo,
Mouths round and shivering with cold.
The Emperor tends the nine paths,
Lamenting my dukes and ministers,
The hundred lords and chief ministers—
Exhorting my myriad people:
Day and night, forget not!"*

The bright pouch of Red Pine; the *White Cloud Register of Immortals*.

*The Records of Qi Xie* states: Deng Shao entered Mount Hua on the first day of the eighth month and saw a youth holding a five-colored pouch, collecting dew from mulberry leaves. The youth said: *"Master Red Pine takes this to brighten his eyes."* This is the origin of the "bright pouch" made on the eighth day of the eighth month.

*The Biographies of Immortals* records: Liu Baiyun of Jiangdu was a man of abundant hidden virtue. He encountered Yue Zichang, who said to him: *"You possess the celestial register of immortality and the bones of one destined for transcendence—why do you wander in the dust of the mortal world?"* He then bestowed upon Liu the *Register* and instructed him: *"First master transformation, then you may receive the Dao."* Liu followed his teachings, achieving myriad transformations and traveling seven hundred *li* in a day. When he met Yue Zichang again, he ingested the elixir for a thousand days and ascended to immortality.

Hou Kai’s dust-bound sameness; Youqi’s blending with the common.

*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* states: Hou Kai, styled Faxian, at fourteen became a disciple of Master Zheng Yi. The master said: *"If you bind your heart and steel your resolve, unwavering in the Dao, you shall not easily attain it unless you dwell in mountain seclusion."* Hou replied: *"The Dao resides in the square inch of the heart—why must one retreat to mountains?"* The master said: *"I know this well, yet divine immortals often lodge in wilds and marshes. Only after achieving perfection do they emerge to mingle with the dust of the world."*

Also, Xue Youqi passed the imperial examinations during the Kaiyuan era but boldly withdrew, refusing office. He entered Mount Heming to seek the abode of the Han Celestial Master, cultivating the Dao for nearly twelve years, his vital energy ever more abundant. In the early Tianbao era, he wandered to Mount Heng, selecting a site for secluded transcendence. His mind roamed freely in the realm of self-attainment, unbound and at ease, yet he could harmonize his light with the common and break his edges to fit the square. Of the Three Caverns’ scriptures and teachings, there was none he did not master. Thus, recluses and free spirits turned to him for guidance. He once presented *The Subtle Discourse on the Origin* and a commentary on the *Scripture of Salvation for the Multitudes*, which circulated in the world.

Wang’s mulberry fields; Magu’s submerged lands.

*The Records of the Xuan Chamber* states: Master Wang, a man of the Dao, concealed his traces by the Wu River, and none recognized him. Shi Huizhi of Hongnong, having heard his name, sought him out.

As dusk fell, the Master traced a line in the courtyard with his staff—suddenly, thunder roared and shook the earth, mountains and valleys layered upon one another, and a vast lake stretched to the horizon. The Master said, "The hills and lands swiftly shift, giving way to the transformation of mulberry fields." The seated guests trembled in fear, but the Master reassured them, "This was but for amusement." With a sweep of his broom, the courtyard returned to its former stillness.

*The Biographies of Divine Transcendents* records: When Magu descended to Cai Jing’s home, she entered to see Wang Fangping and bowed to him. Magu said, "Since we last met, I have witnessed the Eastern Sea turn to mulberry fields three times. Recently, Penglai’s waters have grown shallower than before—could it soon revert to hills and land?" Fangping smiled and replied, "The sages all say that even the seas will one day stir with dust."

Jade altars hum with wind-chimes; jasper terraces glisten with pure dew.

*The Records of Spirit Investigation* states: In the markets of Jianye, a diviner suddenly inscribed on the wall of the Purple Tenuity Palace:
*"Yesterday I passed the Purple Tenuity on my way to court,
The jade altar’s wind-chimes, apricot blossoms sparse.
A peach of jade bore me tidings—
Why ask why I no longer return to the mortal world?"*
From that day, he vanished without a trace, and all said he had ascended.

*The Unofficial Histories* recounts: In the early Kaicheng era of the Tang, the scholar Xu Lai, while traveling in Hezhong, suddenly fell gravely ill and lost consciousness. On the third day, he abruptly rose, seized a brush, and wrote upon the wall:
*"At dawn I entered the jasper terrace, the dew’s breath pure,
Seated there was only Xu Feiqiong.
My dust-laden heart yet clings to worldly ties—
Ten leagues down the mountain, the moon shines in vain."*
Having written this, he fell back into slumber. At daybreak, he startled awake and altered the second line to read: *"Heavenly winds blew down the sound of pacing the void."* After finishing, he sat motionless as if drunk, then awoke and spoke no more. After a long while, he gradually said, "In my dream, I arrived at the jasper terrace, where over three hundred female transcendents gathered. One among them claimed to be Xu Feiqiong and bade me compose a poem. When I finished, she instructed me to revise it, saying, 'I do not wish the people of the world to know of my existence.' After I completed it, she praised me highly and had all the transcendents compose matching verses. She said, 'You will eventually come here. For now, return. If someone guides you, you may yet find your way back.'"

Li He’s record of the jade tower; Dongfang Shuo’s inscription on the urn.

*The Garden of Calligraphy* records: As Li He lay dying, a figure in vermilion robes arrived in a chariot drawn by a red yak, bearing a tablet inscribed with characters resembling ancient seal script or the thunder-riven script of antiquity. The figure announced, "Li He is summoned." Unable to read the text, Li He descended from his couch and kowtowed, pleading, "My mother is old and ill—I do not wish to go." The vermilion-robed figure replied, "The Emperor has completed a white jade tower and summons you to compose its record. The joys of heaven are not to be lamented." Moments later, Li He’s breath ceased. In his childhood, he had addressed his mother as "Ami."

*The Record of Forgotten Lore* states: In the time of the Yellow Emperor, there was a vessel of agate; by the era of Yao, it still endured, filled with sweet dew that brimmed without ever diminishing. Called "precious dew," it was bestowed upon the assembled ministers. By the reign of Shun, the dew gradually waned, its abundance or scarcity mirroring the purity or corruption of the age—when times were pure, the dew overflowed; when degenerate, it vanished. The First Emperor of Qin, dredging the Miluo’s flow, unearthed a crimson jade urn with a capacity of eight *dou*. Placed within Shun’s temple, Dongfang Shuo recognized it and inscribed upon it: *"Auspicious clouds arise from the Dew Altar, propitious winds stir in the Moon Pavilion. Behold the Three Gourds as though a foot in height, gaze upon the Eight Vastnesses like a coiled sash. Li ascends the Cinnabar Terrace, Zǐwēi the Crimson City."*

*The Six Quires* records: The Perfected of Purple Yang, Zhou Jitong, entered the depths of Mount Meng, where he encountered a gatekeeper of the hermitage. Bowing twice, he begged for the secret of immortality. The gatekeeper replied, "Your name is inscribed in the Cinnabar Terrace’s jade chamber—why fear you will not ascend as an immortal?"

*The Biographies of Divine Transcendents* tells of the Heavenly Master Sima, whose name was Chéngzhēn and style Zǐwēi. The female transcend Nü Zhen Xie Naturally sailed across the sea to Penglai in search of a master. Arriving at a mountain, she met a Daoist who said, "Sima Chéngzhēn of Tiantai, whose name is recorded in the Cinnabar Terrace and whose person dwells in the Crimson City, is a true and worthy teacher." Naturally returned and sought him out, receiving the Way and attaining transcendence. He had over seventy disciples. One day, he declared, "From the eastern slopes of Jade Cloud Peak, I have often gazed toward Penglai, where true immortals descend in their chariots. Now I am summoned by the Green Lad Lord and must depart." Soon after, he shed his mortal form. By imperial decree, he was posthumously honored as Grand Master for Thorough Counsel with Silver and Cyan Brilliance, and enfeoffed as the Perfected Lord of Orthodox Unity. The emperor personally composed his stele inscription, and his collected works circulated in the world.

*"Coiling serpents bow in submission, soaring cranes take flight."*

*The New Records of the Cinnabar Terrace* recounts: Peng Zong, styled Faxian, at the age of twenty became a disciple of the Perfected Du Chong. Deeply favored and instructed, he savored the flavor of the Dao and refined his essence to commune with spirits. Within the mountains, venomous serpents and fierce tigers abounded, but Zong would subdue them with his *qi*, causing them to coil and crouch motionless, unable to stir even when touched. Only when he released them would they depart.

*The Divine Transcendent Biographies of the Wang Clan* states: The daughter of Prime Minister Wang Hui, from childhood, yearned for the Dao. She chanted scriptures and played the zither, often saying, "The Grotto Palaces have summoned me to fill a post among the immortals." She inscribed a poem: *"Endlessly I climb mountains and ford streams, while immortals descend to hear my zither’s song. This heart holds no love for the mortal world—only the twin cranes flying toward heaven’s edge."* That very night, she passed away in an instant. At dawn, a pair of cranes soared above the garden trees, celestial music and rare fragrance filling the fields. When her form was placed in the coffin, only her empty garments remained.

*"Hollow and numinous melons, sweet snow of Xián Isle."*

*The Record of Forgotten Lore* further relates: During the Later Han, Lady Yin, a favored consort of Emperor Ming, dreamed of eating an exceedingly sweet melon. The emperor dispatched envoys to seek it from distant lands. At that time, Dunhuang presented a rare melon seed, while the kingdom of Dānshān offered a giant peach-pit gourd named *Qiónglóng*, three feet in length and twisted in form, its taste as sweet as malt sugar. A Daoist had once brought this gourd from Penglai, calling it the "Hollow and Numinous Melon," which bore fruit but once in four kalpas. The Queen Mother of the West had left it in this land, and though ages had passed, its seeds yet remained. It was also said that the giant peach blossomed beneath the frost and ripened only in the height of summer, and was likewise the food of immortals.

Again, King Mu of Zhou journeyed east to the Valley of Great Steeds, pointing toward the Palace of Spring Clouds, where he gathered the essentials of immortality from masters of all quarters. The Queen Mother of the West arrived in a chariot drawn by emerald phoenixes, preceded by striped tigers and leopards, and followed by chimeras and purple elk. She trailed slippers of cinnabar jade and spread mats of azure reeds. Yellow-tube reeds and jade platters were set beneath a canopy of jade, where they feasted in grand assembly. As wine, they offered the limpid, gem-like essence of *qióngyīng*, and also served the "Crimson Ripples of Cave Abyss" and the "Sweet Snow of Xián Isle."

*"Bówēi’s golden essence, Rénběn’s jade flakes."*

*The New Records of the Cinnabar Terrace* states: Zhuang Bowēi, from youth, was devoted to the Dao. Daily at sunset, he would face the northwest, close his eyes, clench his fists, and visualize Mount Kunlun. After twenty-one years of this practice, he ingested elixirs and entered the mountains to study the Way, yet maintained this discipline for another decade. One day, as he closed his eyes, he suddenly beheld Kunlun. Persisting in his visualization, he was visited by an immortal who bestowed upon him the method of the "Golden Essence," by which he attained the Dao. Such was the power of his concentrated spirit—had he not followed this path, the mystery would have remained beyond reach.

*The Miscellaneous Morsels from Youyang* tells of Zheng Renben, who, while traveling in the mountains with his cousin, lost their way. They encountered a man resting his head upon a bundle. Upon inquiry, he replied, "Do you know of the Seven Treasures City? Eighty-two thousand households are constantly repairing it, and I am one of them." He opened the bundle to reveal several axes and adzes, along with two packets of jade-flake rice. Dividing it with Zheng, he said, "Eat this, and you shall remain free of illness for life."

*"Lǐ embodies the Dao and its virtue; Yán weighs its merits and flaws."*

*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* records: The Daoist Li Hanguang of Jinling, at the age of thirteen, was deeply devoted to the study of the Dao. Though alone in a dim chamber, he conducted himself as though in the presence of a sovereign or father. Those who saw him could not help but compose their countenance and demeanor. Emperor Xuanzong summoned him to inquire about governance and transformation. Li replied, *"The* Dao and Its Virtue *is the teacher of kings. In ancient times, when Emperor Wen of Han practiced it, the people were guided toward benevolence and longevity."* The emperor then asked about the golden tripod. Li answered, "The Dao is impartial; those who lightly ascend are the selfish within the impartial. Though their selfishness may occasionally manifest, it is but the Sage’s way of preserving the teaching. To seek life while indulging desire is like chasing the wind—is it not difficult?" The emperor greatly praised him.

Moreover, Yan Da reached enlightenment, styled Daotong. From the time his topknot was first bound, he harbored aspirations beyond the mundane world. During the Jiande era of Emperor Wu of Zhou, the emperor summoned the Dharma Master to the private palace hall. By then, the floating and superficial among the Buddhist clergy had already been purged, and a decree was issued to the ministers and nobles to debate the abolition of the Daoist tradition. The emperor asked the Dharma Master which was superior, the Dao or the Buddha. He replied, "The host is superior, the guest inferior." The emperor asked, "How does one distinguish host from guest?" He answered, "The Buddha came from the West—is he not a guest? The Dao emerged from the Central Plains—is it not the host?" The emperor said, "If the guest returns to the West, does the host not see him off?" He replied, "If the guest departs, it benefits the lands of the Hu; if the host remains, it does no harm to the Central Hua. Those who leave are not pursued, and those who stay preserve themselves—why see them off?" The emperor praised his response.

Coins summoned to flight, lamps extinguished at a gesture. *The New Record of the Cinnabar Terrace* states: Master Ge the Immortal once took coins and had someone cast them into a well. Yan went to the well, called for the coins with a vessel, and onlookers saw each coin fly out of the well and into his container. *Further Accounts of Immortals* records: Song Zhibai became a Daoist, his brows and eyes bright as daylight, his speech elegant and refined. In summer, he wore cotton; in winter, his footprints appeared in snow, yet a pace and a half around his body, steam-like qi arose, and the snow did not congeal. With a point, he could extinguish a lamp; with a gesture, he could strike a person as if blown by a draft through a crack. Sometimes he ate five catties of roasted meat, a basin of garlic shoots, and two *dou* of wine. Wherever he stayed, he would use gold and silk to acquire a pair of braids and a beautiful woman, then take them with him on his travels. People believed this restored his brain and replenished his primordial *qi*. Later, in Nancheng County of Fuzhou, he ascended to heaven in broad daylight, his court robes still hanging, his talismanic writs suspended.

From the *New Records of the Elixir Terrace*: When Tao the Recluse was appointed Gentleman in Attendance at the Court of Imperial Sacrifices, he felt considerable discontent. In a letter to his elder cousin, he wrote: "In my youth, I resolved to sever worldly ambitions, vowing that by forty I would serve as a Gentleman of the Ministry of Personnel, then take office as magistrate of some fine, antiquity-rich county in eastern Zhe, where I might briefly enjoy its mountains and rivers before casting off my official’s cap to pursue loftier paths. All this I once spoke of as easily as pointing to my palm. Yet now, at thirty-six, I am only just appointed Gentleman in Attendance—better to depart sooner than endure such toil and humiliation. I intend to remove my court robes and hang them at the Gate of the Divine Tiger, don a deer-felt cap, and stride directly out through the Eastern Pavilion."

Later, bidding farewell to Wang Yan, he mentioned this plan. Yan cautioned, "His Majesty governs with stern discipline and permits no eccentric conduct. Should you act against his wishes, it may thwart your noble aspirations—what then?" After a long silence, the Master replied, "I act for myself, not for reputation. If such concerns arise, why persist?" Thus, he never attended court, submitting only a memorial of resignation.

From the *True Sayings*: The next morning, Attendant Gentleman Xia Fu, styled Zizhi, a native of Chenliu, consumed a diet of wood-ear mushrooms and refined mica. In his youth, when summoned by the imperial court, he hung his official writ on a mulberry tree and departed, so steadfast was his resolve for lofty reclusion.

When the Kingdom of Yuezhi presented a rare beast, Ma Cun engaged in archery games.

From the *Biographies of the Immortals*: Emperor Wu of Han visited Anding and the Kingdom of Yuezhi. He dispatched envoys bearing four ounces of silent incense, each piece as large as a sparrow’s egg and black as mulberry bark, along with a fierce beast, its form resembling a leopard cat with yellow fur. The Emperor asked, "Such a small creature—why call it fierce?" The envoy replied, "That which commands awe among the hundred birds does not hide its power in size. Thus, the divine *lin* is king of the great elephant, and the phoenix is ancestor of the roc—neither depends on bulk or stature. Our kingdom lies thirty million *li* from here. We have long observed the east wind entering the pitch-pipes and azure clouds crossing the musical tones, signs that a ruler fond of the Dao would arise in the Middle Kingdom. For this reason, we presented these two offerings. Who could have foreseen Your Majesty would fail to recognize their truth?" The Emperor, resenting the envoy’s presumption, sought to punish him. By the next day, both the envoy and the fierce beast had vanished.

From the *Extensive Records*: A villager from Mayang Village spotted a wild boar and shot it. The arrow led him to a stone chamber, where an old man said, "This is no true boar—leave at once." A youth escorted him out. The villager asked, "Who is that old man?" The youth replied, "He is the River Earl, commanded by the Celestial Emperor to lecture on the *Book of Changes* to the immortals." The villager then asked the youth, "And who are you?" The youth answered, "I am Wang Fusi. Failing to master the *Book of Changes*, I was punished to guard this gate." The youth sealed the door with a stone. When the villager looked around, all was desolate—he knew not where he stood.

Yang Jun questioned the dragon; Ge Gong borrowed the fish.

From the *True Reports*: Yang Xi dreamed he ascended a high mountain, surrounded on all sides by vast waters. He saw a white dragon, its body spanning several *zhang*, flying eastward with radiance that lit up the sky. A woman in white entered its mouth, then emerged three times before ceasing. She stood to Yang Xi’s right, while to his left appeared an old man in embroidered robes and a lotus crown, leaning on a nine-jointed red staff. Both gazed at the white dragon. Yang Xi asked, "What manner of woman enters the dragon’s mouth?" The old man replied, "She is the Grand Primordial Jade Maiden, Xiao Zifu, who draws dragon *qi* to refine her form." Yang Xi asked, "Who are you, and why have you come to this place?" The old man answered, "I am Luo Guangxiu, the Immortal Lord of Penglai. This Penglai Mountain is my domain, and I came to meet you as its presiding lord." Yang Xi asked, "May one ride this dragon?" The old man replied, "This dragon is ridden by the Perfected: Zhang Youshi, Shi Qing’an, Xu Yufu, and Ding Zhangning." Yang Xi asked, "How can four people ride a single dragon?" The old man said, "This is a dawn-attending dragon, swift as a light chariot in the mortal world."

From the *Biographies of Divine Immortals*: As Immortal Lord Ge traveled, he encountered a fishmonger on the road. He said to the vendor, "May I borrow this fish to visit the River Earl?" The vendor replied, "The fish is already dead." Lord Ge inscribed a talisman and placed it in the fish’s mouth, then cast it into the water. The fish leapt up at once.

Shuang Qiuzu ascended lightly; Zi Zhen rose to the void.

From the *Biographies of Eminent Daoists*: The Daoist Shuang Qiuzu dwelled in White Horse Grotto, reciting the *Scripture of the Yellow Court*. When his practice reached fulfillment, he shut himself in his chamber for seven days. His disciples, alarmed, opened the door—only to find him gone, having ascended lightly into the heavens. He left behind his immortal robe on a treetop and his bamboo mat in a niche; even a century later, both remained unspoiled.

Another Daoist, He Zizhen, was learned and lofty in aspiration. He cultivated the Dao on Mount Song. One day, clouds and cranes filled the sky with music. Zizhen ascended into the void and departed. The scholar Chen Tao, witnessing this with the people of Luoyang, composed a poem:

*"Zi Jin’s simurgh soars o’er ancient Luo’s stream,
The golden peach ripens anew for Lord He’s flight.
Triple Clarity’s music plays ‘neath Song Mountain’s height,
Five-colored clouds gather before the imperial park’s gleam.
Crimson crests dance low to greet the crimson staff’s might,
Green sleeves sing on, halting the fragrant plow’s dream.
Who now will weep over white letters in sorrow’s seam?
The Supreme Ultimate’s light spans a hundred million years’ light."*

Liu Xu’s hidden virtue; Han Chong’s benevolent governance.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* states: Liu Xiang’s family was immensely wealthy, yet he aided the destitute and impoverished, practicing hidden virtue. Repeatedly promoted, he became governor of Songliu, where he diminished his own share to provide for others. There, Master Ma Huang appeared to him, saying: "Your benevolence moves Heaven and Earth; your virtue stirs gods and spirits. The Supreme Honored One commends your sincerity and has sent me to guide you toward long life. I am an immortal official—will you follow me?" Xiang accompanied him and was taught the method of ingesting the blossoms of the Five Stars. Now he resides in Huayang Grotto as the Right Supervisor of Principles.

Also, Han Chong, a native of Piling, encountered the divine adept Wang Weiyuan, who transmitted to him the *Flowing Pearl and Cinnabar Prime* method. He instructed: "Practice this Way, and you may serve in office. When your merit is complete, nothing will hinder your ascent as an immortal." Chong first served as magistrate of Wanling, where he governed with the Seven Governances to comfort the people, and locusts never alighted within his borders. Later promoted to governor, he cherished the people as if they were wounded, and his transformative governance spread far and wide. He again met Weiyuan, who taught him the method of reclusion and bodily dissolution. Thereupon, he entered Great Mount Huo to transcend the world. Now he serves in Huayang as the Left Supervisor of Principles.

Xiao Wen mended shoes; he bore a shoulder-mirror on his back.
