*The Traditions of Divine Transcendents* records: Xiao Wen spent over a decade mending shoes in the marketplace, yet none knew him as an immortal—only that he did not age. Those who admired him sought his Way and its safeguards, but none succeeded, save for Mother Liang, who obtained his method of kindling fire. One day, he ascended Triple Brightness Mountain to bid her farewell, then rose amid columns of great flame.

*The Arrayed Traditions of Transcendents* states: There was a mirror-polisher who always carried a shoulder-mirror in the marketplace. No one knew his name, and all addressed him only as "Shoulder-Bearer." At times, he sold medicines, and those who consumed them were cured. Gu He held the canopy; Chunyu Dian grasped the handle.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* states: Gu He was a man of Wu. Orphaned in youth, he possessed steadfast resolve and served the Jin as Palace Aide to the Censor-in-Chief, later rising to Vice Director of the Imperial Secretariat. In the first year of the Yonghe era, he underwent liberation by means of a simulated corpse, whereupon the Most High welcomed him to serve as Canopy-Bearer, and he now resides in the Grotto of Flourishing Yang.

*The Arrayed Traditions of Transcendents* records: Chunyu was a native of Shangyu. From childhood he loved the Dao, and in his maturity he mastered the arts of divination. He withdrew to dwell in seclusion on Mount Tianmu, where he encountered the transcendent Huichezi, who bestowed upon him the *Scripture of the Cinnabar Elixir*. Upon completion of his work, he now serves in the grotto as Handle-Bearer, presiding over the testing of those of the Dao.

Han Kang concealed his name; Dai Meng altered his surname.

*The Unofficial History of the Later Han* states: Han Kang, styled Boxiu, often gathered herbs on famous mountains and sold medicines along the roads of Chang’an. For over thirty years, he never altered his prices. Once, a woman came to purchase medicine, and Kang held firm to his price. The woman grew angry and said, “Are you not Han Kang Boxiu? How can you not vary your price?” Kang sighed and said, “I sought only to avoid fame, yet now even women know me. What use is selling medicine?” He then fled into the mountains of Baling. Though the imperial carriage repeatedly summoned him, he never came.

*The Traditions of Eminent Daoists* records: The Daoist Dai Meng was originally surnamed Yan and named Ji, a man of the late Han. Believing that those who nurtured life should conceal their names and hide the hour of their birth, he changed his surname to Dai and feigned service at the court of Emperor Wu. From youth, Meng loved the Dao and served his mother with filial devotion. After the mourning period for his mother ended, he entered Mount Hua to ingest wood essences. There he met the Perfected Lord Pei, who transmitted to him the *Scripture of the Jade Girdle and Golden Pendants*, whereupon he gained the ability to move lightly through the famous mountains, covering seven hundred *li* in a day.

Yellow talismans cured epidemics; Su Xiang revived the dead.

*The Comprehensive Survey of Divine Marvels* states: In Chang’an there was a man named Huang, who once acquired a servant. The servant carried his burdens and followed him for nearly one or two years. Huang’s family was poor and distressed, and the couple lamented their plight. The servant overheard them and asked, “How much does the master require?” They replied, “Five hundred thousand cash.” The servant said, “I have a small skill that can obtain it.”

He then purchased fine paper, brushes, inkstones, clay jars, and fermented beans, each one item. The next morning, he took them to the marketplace. There, the servant layered hundreds of sheets of paper, held a brush, and announced to the crowd: “Today I shall inscribe a talisman on the topmost sheet, allowing the ink to seep through all the layers. Next year, a great epidemic will strike Chang’an, and this talisman will cure it. Each talisman will cost fifty gold pieces.” The following day, he indeed obtained five hundred thousand cash. Then, practicing qi circulation, he exhaled upon the grass to kindle a fire, its flames illuminating one another. Covering his head with the clay jar, he sat within the fire and vanished without a trace.

The next year, a fiery plague indeed ravaged Chang’an, but those who possessed the talisman were spared.

*The Record of Subtle Marvels* states: There was a man named Su Dege who excelled at compounding *Returning Soul Incense*. However, those who had been buried for eighty-one years or more could not be revived. At one time, the Astronomer’s Registrar Xu Bi wept as he entreated him, saying, “My parents and great-grandparents all wish to pay their respects once more.” Su assented, then drew from his怀 a packet resembling white sandalwood incense and placed a pinch in the censer. The smoke curled upward in delicate tendrils, its fragrance surpassing even borneol. Su softly intoned: “Xu Zhao desires to see his forebears; may this incense smoke serve as their guide.” After the time it takes to eat a meal, a sudden gust swept the curtains, and his ancestors—parents and great-grandparents—all appeared. Xu wept as he bowed. Gazing intently, he saw that their robes and headdresses were exactly as in life. They said, “Today’s reunion is truly rare.” After they had drunk, they slowly departed beyond the curtains, dissolving into mist. Later, Su Dege also vanished without a trace.

The Jade Cup Maiden, an immortal of Golden Flower Mountain.

*The Record of Hidden Wonders* states: A scholar named Cui encountered a maiden at the mouth of Luogu Valley in Shuzhou and married her. Cui’s mother said, “This new bride is bewitchingly beautiful; she must be a fox spirit who will harm you.” The maiden replied, “I originally hoped to serve you for a lifetime, but since your honored mother treats me as a fox enchantress, I shall depart tomorrow.” The next day, she entered the mountains and disappeared. Later, a foreign monk said, “The wife you took was the immortal Jade Cup Maiden. Had she stayed for a year, your entire family would have become immortals.” Scholar Cui could only sigh in regret.

*The Jade Canon of the Great Cavern* states: In the Heaven of the Grand Beginning, there is a Pool of Flowing Jade. Within the pool stands a jade tree, its lotus blossom spanning ten *zhang*, the pool itself a thousand *li* wide, its waters fragrant and sweet. The Golden Flower Immortal dwells within the lotus, drinking the pool’s waters to clarify the five viscera and cause purple clouds to bloom upon the face. Zhang mistakenly consumed forbidden food and was thus forbidden to eat meat.

*The Extensive Record of Marvels* states: Zhang Tao, Dragon Diagram Scholar, was the son of Zhang Kui, Straight Academician of the Bureau of Military Affairs. When his father served as Palace Attendant, he daily attended court audiences and rented a residence in the capital, where he usually kept his gates closed. One day, someone knocked urgently, shouting, “Little master, why won’t you let me out?” Zhang rose to look and saw an elderly Daoist. Suspecting the man was mad or drunk, he paid him no further mind, and after a long while, the Daoist departed. That very night, his wife gave birth to Tao. In the first year of Jingyou, he passed the imperial examination. Later, he once mistakenly ate dog meat and dreamed that a yellow-robed messenger summoned him to a palace. There, a Daoist asked, “Why did you consume forbidden food?” Zhang defended himself, saying, “I did not dare to eat it intentionally; it was an accident.” The Daoist replied, “If that is so, then remain here for now. I shall speak on your behalf.” After a short while, he emerged and called to Zhang, “Come and express your gratitude.” He led him before a hall and announced, “Zhang Tao has mistakenly consumed forbidden food.” Zhang bowed twice in thanks and awoke, his body drenched in sweat. Scholar Zhang possessed a refined spirit and noble bearing, his heart open and magnanimous—was he not one of the immortals’ court, temporarily banished to the mortal realm?

*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists*: The mother of Daoist Ying Yijie dreamed of a shooting star entering her window. Startled awake, she saw light filling the room and consequently conceived. After his birth, he disliked meat and strong flavors. He was entrusted with the *Zhengyi* and *Zixu* registers, and his mastery of their practices was profound. He once told his disciples: “I uphold the teachings and laws, yet I cannot eradicate all hidden flaws. The Dao moves without opposing others, and right action arises naturally. Yet how far apart are the Jade Capital and Golden Gates from the Yellow Springs and Fengdu? It is but the mind’s own manifestation. Strive on, all of you.” “When Wang Zijin peered into the well, Scholar Yan knocked at the gate.”

*Records of Forgotten Tales*: In the first year of the *Dashǐ* era, the year Wei Emperor became Prince of Chenliu, there was in the kingdom of Pin-Si a cinnabar-stone well, not hewn by human hands. Its depths reached the subterranean springs, and its waters perpetually boiled and surged. When immortals wished to drink, they drew the water with long silk cords. The people of that land were all immensely strong, subsisting not on the five grains. At noon, they cast no shadow, and they drank the essence of clouds and mist. Their garments were woven from feathers, and their hair, thick as strands of hemp, was as tough as sinew. When stretched, it measured nearly ten feet, yet when released, it coiled back like a silkworm’s thread. They twisted it into ropes to draw water from the cinnabar well, in which dwelled white frogs with two wings, flitting to and fro above the well. The immortals ate them.

Wang Zijin approached the well and peered in. A blue sparrow carried a jade ladle in its beak and offered it to him. As Zijin took it, clouds arose and snow swirled. He waved his sleeve, and the clouds and snow stilled. The white frogs transformed into a pair of turtledoves, which vanished into the clouds.

*Records of Lively Conversations*: Yan Shize served as *Shangyi Fengyu* during Emperor Muzong’s reign. While entering the mountains to gather herbs, he came upon a thatched hut enveloped in vines and mist. There, a man lay upon a rock. Yan greeted him, and the man replied: “Since the An Lushan rebellion, I have lived here in seclusion, unaware of the passing years. I keep no fire and live without smoke. Yet you have come from afar, and I have nothing to ease your hunger.” He took from his paper pouch something resembling lentils, drew one out, and boiled it in spring water. After a long while, the fragrance filled the air, and it was ready. He bade Yan eat, and upon tasting it, Yan felt fully satiated. The man said: “That you have come here is due to your predestined affinity. In time, you will rise to the rank of *Fangbo*. If you can cast off worldly splendor, you may yet attain long life.”

*Records of the Multitude of Immortals from the Three Caverns*, Scroll Eleven—concluded.
*Records of the Multitude of Immortals from the Three Caverns*, Scroll Twelve.

Chen Baoguang of the Orthodox Unity Dao, compiler.

When He Hou poured wine, Daozi flicked ink.

*The Comprehensive Records of Immortals*: He Hou was a recluse on Mount Cangwu during the reign of Emperor Yao. When Emperor Shun made his southern inspection and lodged at He Hou’s home, the Five Elders of the Great Emperor came to pay their respects, saying, “The time for your ascension has come.” The next day, the Five Emperors descended to welcome Shun, who ascended to heaven in broad daylight. The Five Emperors gave He Hou a vessel of elixir, instructing him to pour it into wine. His entire household of over three hundred drank without exhausting it. When the remaining wine was sprinkled upon the house, the entire dwelling ascended, and he was appointed as the Perfected One of the Great Ultimate. Today, on Mount Jiu Nong, there stands a temple to He Hou beside the Temple of Shun.

*The Lesser Records of Immortals*: Wu Daozi received the divine transmission of immortals, and his paintings were miraculously lifelike. When Emperor Taizong of Tang heard of this, he summoned him to the palace. There, a freshly plastered wall several *xun* in length was prepared for him to paint landscapes. Daozi ordered curtains to be draped over the wall, then splashed ink upon it. After covering it again, he waited a long while before saying, “May Your Majesty now behold the painting.” The mountains, forests, grasses, trees, human dwellings, and birds—all were perfectly rendered. The emperor gazed at it for a long time before noticing a small cave beneath the rocks. Daozi pointed and said, “This cave is home to many immortals. If you knock, there will be a response.” He then struck the cave with his hand, and the rocky door opened, revealing a youth standing beside it. Daozi said, “There are many fine treasures within. Would Your Majesty like to see them?” He leapt into the cave and beckoned to the emperor, but the emperor dared not enter. The cave door then closed again, and Daozi vanished without a trace.

Zang Qiong as the Wine Star, Zhang Lu as the Rice Bandit.

*Unofficial Histories*: When Minister Zhang Qiong Jianxiong governed Xichuan, he ordered his attendants to seek out those skilled in Daoist arts. Among them was a tavern keeper whose wine surpassed all others, yet who cared little for profit and extended credit generously. Four men in gauze hats and straw sandals would come to drink, consuming several *dou* each time. Though they accumulated debts of over ten *shi* of grain, they would repay it all at once before departing in high spirits, laughing and jesting. Someone reported this to Minister Zhang, who then secretly ordered surveillance. For over a month, the four did not return. Then, one day, they reappeared. Zhang, in plain clothes, stealthily followed them. As he approached, the four suddenly leapt up, bowed twice, and upon rising, vanished without a trace. At the time, Emperor Xuanzong was fond of the Dao, so Zhang reported the matter. The emperor summoned him and asked, “Those were the Wine Stars of Venus,” Zhang replied.

*The Biographies of the Celestial Masters*: Zhang Lu, styled Gongqi, was the governor of Hanzhong and Nanzheng. Whenever he performed rituals to heal the sick, they would swiftly recover. For each healing, he required a *hu* of rice as offering, amassing vast quantities. When the Prince of Wei assumed regency, he derided Zhang as the “Rice Bandit” and dispatched generals to suppress him. His disciples, seeing the approaching army, rushed in alarm to inform their master. Zhang drew a line on the ground with his hand tablet, and the river’s currents surged violently, preventing the troops from crossing. He then forced them to use naval forces. Zhang again traced the air with his tablet, and nine towering peaks rose into the heavens, blocking their advance. Hearing of this, the Prince of Wei sent envoys to apologize and bestowed upon Zhang the titles of Inspector of Liang and Yi Provinces, General Who Pacifies the South, and Marquis of Guanzhong. Later, he ascended in flight.

Returning to truth, revealing scriptures—Bo Chou expounds the *Yi*.

From *Miscellaneous Records of Xiangshan*:

In the bingchen year of the Xining reign, Jia rebels raided Yong Prefecture, and Scholar Tang Zizheng of the Bureau of Edicts, along with his entire household, perished in the massacre. Tang was a native of Guizhou. During the Zhiping era, he traveled to the capital for the imperial examinations. Midway in Quanzhou, he sought to hire a servant. Wandering into Yuanzhou, he encountered a former bondsman he had once employed—one who carried heavy burdens with the vigor of a strong bird. Even when Tang whipped his horse to a gallop, the servant remained a hundred paces ahead. Fearing the man might flee, Tang dismissed him.

That very day, the servant journeyed from Quanzhou to Tangzhou—a distance of nearly two thousand seven hundred *li*—and by midday had already arrived. He left a letter with the post-station attendant, instructing: "When Scholar Tang of Guizhou arrives, deliver this to him." Tang himself arrived over a month later. As he dismounted at the station, the attendant approached and asked, "Are you not Scholar Tang?" Then produced the letter, sealed by *Guizhenzi*. Tang broke the seal to find a single poem:

*At Yuanshan we met, then parted for Quanzhou,*
*Unmet, the Master’s Dao remains unfulfilled.*
*Most seek wealth and rank with all their heart,*
*Yet in the end, none may learn the immortal’s art.*
*Dispense the elixirs in your basket without cease,*
*Guard the cinnabar in your crucible, transmit it not in vain.*
*When the Horned Dragon’s feast arrives,*
*Seek the yellow-cliffed retreat in the forest springs.*

When asked to describe the sender’s appearance, Tang recognized the dismissed servant from Quanzhou. The year of his death, bingchen, precisely matched the "Horned Dragon" foretold in the poem.

From *Supplement to the Biographies of Transcendents*:

Yang Bochou was fond of studying the *Yi*. He withdrew to Mount Hua, where He Tuo once asked him about his learning in the *Yi*. Yang replied: "In the Golden Heaven Grotto beneath Mount Taihua, I received the *Yi* taught by Fuxi himself—identical to the primordial Dao, its principles exhausting all mysteries. How could the common discourses of worldly scholars fathom the aims of immortals?" The trigrams of *Ge* ascend and descend; the winds of *Zheng* blow north and south.

From *New Records of the Cinnabar Terrace*:

Once, while traveling by boat, Master Ge the Immortal was asked by his disciples about the ten-odd talismans in his chest: "What powers do these talismans possess? May we witness them?" The Master replied: "Divine talismans can accomplish anything." At the disciples’ request, he cast one into the water. The current surged violently, and the talisman drifted downstream. "What do you make of this?" he asked. A guest replied, "If any of us threw one in, it would do the same." He cast a second talisman, which defied the current and swam upstream. "And this?" The guest exclaimed, "Extraordinary!" He cast a third talisman into the water, where it hovered motionless, neither rising nor sinking. Suddenly, the upstream talisman descended, the downstream one ascended, and all three converged at the center, gathering together without drifting away.

From *The Biography of Zheng Hong*:

*The Records of Kuaiji* states: South of Shedi Mountain lies White Crane Mountain, where cranes retrieve arrows for immortals. The Han Grand Commandant Zheng Hong, while gathering firewood, found a lost arrow. Soon after, someone came searching for it. Zheng returned it, and the man asked what he desired. Recognizing him as a transcendent, Zheng replied, "I often struggle to carry firewood along the Ruoye River. I wish for a south wind at dawn and a north wind at dusk." Later, this came to pass. To this day, the winds of the Ruoye River are still called "Lord Zheng’s Winds."

Dai Yang was of humble appearance; Li A was impoverished.

From *The History of the Jin*:

Dai Yang, styled Guoliu, was a native of Changcheng in Wuxing. At the age of twelve, he fell ill and died. After five days, he revived, recounting that celestial envoys had appointed him as the Wine Cellar Scribe, bestowing upon him talismans and registers. He was led by banner-bearing attendants to ascend Penglai, Kunlun, Jishi, Taihuang, Da, Lu, Heng, and other sacred mountains. Upon being sent back, he encountered an old man who told him: "In the future, you will attain the Dao and be recognized by a nobleman." In adulthood, he mastered the art of wind angles, divination, and astrology, excelling in prognostication without relying on wind or observation. He was devoted to the Dao, though of short and plain stature.

From *The Biographies of Divine Transcendents*:

Li A, a native of Shu, often begged in Chengdu, yet whatever he received he would redistribute to the poor. He would depart at night and return by dawn, and none knew where he lodged. Later, he once told others: "I have been summoned to Kunlun and must depart." Thereafter, he was never seen again.

Liu Kuan, the Venerable, was the Summer Radiance Duke.

From *Declarations of the Perfected*:

During the Later Han, Liu Kuan served as Grand Commandant under Emperor Ling. Once, while seated with guests, he sent a servant to purchase wine. The servant returned after a long delay, thoroughly drunk. The guests could not bear it, and Liu Kuan’s horse said: "Beast." Shortly after, Liu Kuan sent someone to check on the servant, fearing he might secretly take his own life. Turning to his attendants, he said: "This man was called a beast—what greater humiliation could there be? That is why I feared for his life." His wife, wishing to test his forbearance, waited until he was dressed in court robes for an audience. She had a maidservant carry a bowl of meat broth, which was then spilled onto his ceremonial attire. The maid quickly gathered the soiled garments, but Liu Kuan’s expression remained unchanged. He merely said, unhurried: "Did the broth scald your hand?" Such was his nature and demeanor that he was hailed throughout the realm as the Venerable.

From *The Unfolding of Hidden Subtleties*:

Xia Qi, King Wen, the Duke of Shao, and Wu Jizha—Xia Qi was the Radiance Duke. These Four Radiance Dukes shall later ascend the ranks of transcendence, presiding over the affairs of spirits in the four directions.

Li Ying’s chamber of flood dragons, Sun Simiao’s palace of dragons.

From *Records of the Tree of Forgetfulness*:

Li Ying encountered a divine woman who bestowed upon him a bolt of plain silk, saying it was woven in the chamber of flood dragons. Later, he met a Hu merchant who offered three million in exchange for it, explaining: "This was woven from the fine beard beneath a dragon’s chin, and it takes thirty minor kalpas to complete a single weft."

From *Sequel to the Biographies of Transcendents*:

Sun Simiao once saw a man killing a snake. He removed his robe to ransom it, applied medicine to its wound, and released it into the grass. Months later, he was suddenly summoned to a walled city resembling a royal residence. There, a man in crimson robes thanked him, saying: "My younger brother was saved by you." Sun secretly asked those around him where he was, and they replied: "The Water Palace of Jingyang." After being detained for a feast, he was asked what he desired. Sun replied: "I dwell in the mountains and delight in the Dao, so I have no wants." The host then presented him with thirty prescriptions from the Dragon Palace, saying: "These remedies can save the world and succor the people." Upon returning, Sun tested the prescriptions, healing countless lives. He compiled the *Essential Prescriptions Worth a Thousand in Gold*, incorporating the Dragon Palace remedies within. Emperor Gaozong of Tang, having heard of his fame, offered him the post of Remonstrance Advisor, which he declined. Later, his body vanished, leaving only his clothes—he is now known as the Perfected Sun.

Ge Qi summoned rain, Zhao Bing called forth wind.

From *The Biographies of Divine Transcendents*:

The Yellow Reed Master, surnamed Ge and named Qi, healed the sick. Even those from a thousand *li* away would send their names for treatment, and all were cured. He lived to two hundred and eighty years of age. He could restrain water, causing it to flow backward, and lift a thousand *jin* with ease. He could run as swiftly as a galloping horse, and above his head there was always a five-colored qi, rising over a *zhang* in height. During severe droughts, he would descend into deep pools, summon the dragons, and urge them to ascend to the heavens, bringing rain for days. One day, he mounted a dragon and departed, bidding farewell to his kin—never to return.

From *Comprehensive Records of Transcendents*:

Zhao Bing, styled Gong’a, was a native of Shuyang. Once, while traveling afar, he encountered an old friend. Bing poured water from a rushing stream and turned it into wine, then carved mulberry bark into platters, and all drank and feasted to their hearts’ content. Another time, arriving at a river he wished to cross, he sought a boat from the shore, but the boatman ignored him. Bing then spread his cloak upon the water and sat, summoning the wind to churn the currents as he crossed—his garments remained utterly dry. Those who witnessed this marveled at his divine prowess.

Ruan Qiu sold scallions; Wen Bin fed on chrysanthemums.

From *Biographies of Transcendents*:

Huang Ruan Qiu was a Daoist of Mount Mu, clad in furs with unbound hair and ears six or seven *cun* long. His mouth was toothless, yet he could walk four or five hundred *li* in a day. He would often rest upon the mountains, planting scallions and selling herbs to sustain himself. For over a hundred years, none recognized him—until Zhu Huang pointed him out, revealing him as a divine being. But when they sought him again, he had vanished.

Wen Bin, a native of Taiqiu, made his living selling straw sandals. One day, he abandoned his wife and entered the mountains, subsisting on chrysanthemums and never emerging. His wife, grown old, followed him into the mountains and found Bin looking younger than before. He refused to descend, saying, “You too are suited to the Dao.” He then instructed her to consume chrysanthemums and cultivate *qi*, and together, husband and wife attained the Way.

Xie Fu dwelled in Lesser Wei; Li Zhi lodged in the Neck Star.

From *Unofficial History of the Jin*:

Xie Fu, styled Qingxu, was a native of Kuaiji. He entered Mount Taibai for over a decade. The General-in-Chief, Huan Wen, summoned him to serve as his secretary, and the imperial court offered him the post of Academician—both of which he declined. Earlier, when the moon trespassed against Lesser Wei (also called the Recluse Star), astrologers interpreted this as a sign that a hermit would be affected. Dai Kui of Qiao Commandery was a man of great talent, and some feared for him. Soon after, Fu died, prompting the literati of Kuaiji to mock the people of Wu, saying: “The lofty scholars of Wu—so eager for death, yet death eludes them.”

From *Pure Tales from the Jade Flask*:

Li Zhi, while serving in the southern court, once composed *The Rhapsody of the Neck Star*. In its preface, he wrote: “In my youth, I was often ill, so frail I could scarcely bear my robes. In the year *gengyin*, I dreamed I wandered into a Daoist palace, resplendent with gold and jade. Within a grand hall stood a precious platform, and upon it coiled a golden dragon, its azure beard and golden mane radiating light across heaven and earth. Beside it stood a green-haired Daoist, his eyes flashing like lightning. He said to me: ‘This is the Palace of the Neck Star. The great celestial wheel never halts—hasten to pay homage. You shall serve this dragon, and your accumulated ailments will vanish.’ As I prepared to bow to the dragon, I first bowed to the Supreme Dao.”

When Emperor Taizong ascended the throne, Emperor Zhenzong was named Crown Prince. The emperor commanded Li Zhi and Li Kang to serve as his tutors, admonishing Zhenzong: “These two ministers are venerable scholars of profound virtue—treat them not lightly. Choose upright men to guide you, and the foundation of our lineage and state shall be secure.” Zhenzong reverently heeded his father’s instruction, bowing to them before they spoke—a sign that aligned with the omen of the Neck Star.

A turtle drawn on tile; a deer bent by grass.

From *Miscellaneous Morsels from Youyang*:

Wang Qiong possessed the arts of the Dao. He took a tile shard, drew a turtle’s shell upon it, and held it in his sleeve. After a moment, he removed it and placed it on the ground—it had become a living turtle, crawling about the courtyard. After several days, it reverted to tile.

From *Records of the Western Mountains*:

Perfected Lord Huang, named Renxian and styled Ziting, once received a summons from the Jade Emperor to ascend to heaven. His two younger brothers, however, remained at their hunting grounds. Ziting hastened to them and said: “We are creatures of wandering nature, unfit for immortality. Yet if our entire family could ascend together, we would not refuse. Perhaps our allotted share of deer has not been fulfilled—this is why we cling to delusion.” Ziting, seeing their resolve, entrusted them to the guardianship of earthbound transcendents and taught them the arts of cultivation.

He then broke a blade of grass and transformed it into a deer, thereby stilling their deluded minds. Later, his two younger brothers concealed themselves in the Western Mountains.

**Observing Fragrance’s liberation from the net, and Xu Ying’s release to the East:**

From *True Records*:
Wang Guanxiang was the daughter of the Numinous King and the younger sister of Qiao. Having received from Qiao the Dao of flying liberation from the net, she entered the mountains with him. After thirty-nine years of cultivation, her Dao was perfected, and she was invested as the Purple Azure Palace Consort, charged with overseeing the Eastern Palace.

From *New Records of the Alchemical Terrace*:
Xu Ying was the elder brother of the Prefectural Historian. Resolutely withdrawing to the mountain forests, he devoted himself to savoring the Dao. Encountering Wang Shilong, he received the Dao of release to the East, practiced the method of reversed circulation, and ingested jade nectar to refine his brain essence. Within two or three years, his face radiated brilliance, his complexion reverted to youth, and he regained his vitality. Only his endowment of qi was meager, preventing him from attaining the highest rank of transcendence. The Director of Destinies, Jingwu, commended him and memorialized the Upper Palace, transferring his name to the Eastern Isle and establishing him as an earthbound transcendent.

**Zhou driving away malevolent spirits, and Liu commanding ghosts and gods:**

From *Records of the Western Mountains*:
Perfected Lord Zhou, whose personal name was Guang and style Huichang, entered Shu and obtained the arts of driving away malevolent spirits and expelling demons, which he used to relieve suffering. Hearing that Perfected Lord Xu in Jingyang was healing illnesses with talismans and incantations, drawing patients from far and near, he journeyed from Yuntai Mountain in Shu to Jingyang to seek an audience, wishing to become his disciple. Lord Xu accepted him, and Zhou mastered all the essential mysteries. Later, he ascended with Lord Xu.

From *Traditions of Divine Transcendents*:
Liu Gen, during the reign of Emperor Wu of Han, abandoned his official post to study the Dao. Entering a stone chamber in Mount Song, he was questioned by Wang Zhen about the origins of his pursuit of transcendence. Liu replied: “I once entered the mountains to refine my spirit, exploring every method. Later, on Mount Huayin, I saw a person riding a carriage drawn by a white deer, accompanied by over ten attendants and jade maidens bearing colored banners. I prostrated myself twice and begged for a single teaching. The divine person asked: ‘Have you heard of Han Zhong?’ I answered: ‘Indeed, I have.’ The divine person said: ‘I am he.’”

Thus he transmitted to him the essentials of the Dao. The Dao encompasses those who ascend to heaven treading upon clouds, those who wander among the Five Peaks, those who achieve immortality, and those who undergo liberation by means of the corpse. The highest elixirs include the Nine-Turned Cyclically Transformed Cinnabar and the Golden Liquid of the Great One—ingesting these grants immediate ascent to heaven, without the need to accumulate days and months. Next are substances like mica and realgar, which, though they do not immediately allow one to ride clouds and mount dragons, can still command ghosts and spirits, enable transformations, and confer longevity. Below these are the various herbal medicines that cure the hundred diseases, replenish deficiency, preserve youth, and allow one to abstain from grains while replenishing qi, yet they cannot make one immortal.

Li Zhen attained transcendence near and far, while Zhang Hao achieved perfection. From *Traditions of Eminent Daoists*: Li Zhen’s family was exceedingly poor. One day, a Daoist named Zhang Qiwu visited Li, seeking lodging. Li treated him with great generosity. Zhang would often become drunk, sometimes cursing Jin, and would vomit and soil the furnishings. The servants all despised him, yet Li never took offense. Zhang then said to Li: “I have received such generous regard from you. Now, as I depart, would you accompany me for a few miles?” Li went with him. Zhang said: “I have wandered among men for fifty years and have never encountered one as benevolent and kind as you.” Thereupon, he transmitted to Li the arts of transmuting base metals into gold and silver. Li declined, saying his fate was too meager to dare accept such teachings. Zhang, lost in thought, sighed: “Your Dao is beyond what I can attain.” Then, drawing out his hairpin and wielding it as a sword, he traced a line upon the ground, which split open at his touch. He said: “From here, we part.” With that, he cast himself into the earth and vanished. Li marveled at this.

Zhang Hao, during the Yongchu era of the Han, was once summoned by imperial decree to serve as a Daoist among the recluses. At twenty years of age, he was selected for this role. One day, Feng Heng suddenly arrived. Zhang, upon seeing him, prostrated himself in reverence and sought instruction in what he had yet to comprehend. Feng examined his heart and sent him to cross a deep abyss, where he encountered sharks and whales pressing close, yet his countenance did not change. He was tempted with beauty and tested with wealth, yet his heart remained unmoved. Feng said: “He is teachable.” Thereupon, he bestowed upon him the *Azure Waist Purple Script*, the *Golden Root Superior Canon*, and half a tael of divine elixir, admonishing him: “Act, and you shall attain it; neglect, and you shall lose it.” Zhang prostrated himself and received the command. He then entered Mount Chicheng, ingested the elixir, and practiced the Dao. By the Taichu era of Wei, he had achieved perfection.

The noble lady An and the extraordinary guest Sun. From *Declarations of the Perfected*: In the second year of the Xingning era, the Lady of Purple Tenuity descended with Lady An to the chamber of Yang the Perfected. The Lady of Purple Tenuity said: “Today, a noble guest has come to visit.” Lady An, a divine maiden, was the daughter of Lady Li. Formerly, she had gone to Mount Gui to study the Supreme Clarity Dao, whereupon she attained perfection, received the scriptures of the Most High, and was appointed as a Perfected Consort of the Nine Flowers. She was granted the surname An and allowed to wander in the Grand Clarity. *The Master Who Embraces Simplicity* states: Sun Deng was an extraordinary person. He had no family and dwelled in mountain caves and earthen hollows. In winter, he wore only a single garment, and in the bitterest cold, he would let down his hair to cover his body. *Declarations of the Perfected* also records: Sun Deng could produce the eight sounds with a single string—truly an extraordinary scholar.

The Daoist wields a broom of millet, while the master dons a cloth headscarf. From *Records of the Thatched Pavilion*: Yong Fazhi once venerated a stone statue of Lord Lao, reciting the *Tianpeng Mantra* without cease. One night, he dreamed of a divine person taking a broom of millet from before the stone statue and giving it to him, saying: “Whenever there is one who suffers, sweep them with this broom.” Upon waking, he did as instructed. Thereafter, whenever the sick came to him, he would brush them with the broom, and they would be cured instantly. The people of the time praised him, saying: “The Daoist Yong sweeps the blind so they may see, brushes the lame so they may walk.” The afflicted gathered like clouds.

From *New Records of the Elixir Terrace*: Xuanyuan Ji dwelled on Mount Luofu, styling himself the Master of Luofu. It was said he had lived for several hundred years. Whenever he entered the mountains to gather herbs, dragons and tigers would follow in his retinue. He could divide his form and transform his shadow, appearing wherever he willed. Whenever he went out, he carried a cloth headscarf. If he encountered the sick, he would brush them with the cloth, and they would be cured instantly. Later, his whereabouts became unknown.

Liu and Ruan of Tiantai, Yuan and Liu of Hepu.

From *Records of Divine Transcendents*: Liu Chen and Ruan Zhao once journeyed to Mount Tiantai to gather herbs. Losing their way, they crossed a stream and saw two women of surpassing beauty, who invited them to their home. There they were served sweet wine, sesame rice, and dried mountain goat—all of exquisite taste. The two men lodged in the mountains for half a year. When they returned home, their village had fallen to ruin, and seven hundred years had passed.

From *Tales of Marvels*: During the Yuanhe reign, Yuan Che and Liu Shi of Mount Heng sought to cross the sea by boat from Hepu. Suddenly, a hurricane swept them into the vast ocean, leaving them adrift and directionless. Soon the wind stilled as they reached a desolate island. The two men disembarked, whereupon they encountered two maidens who had transformed from birds. They knelt and pleaded for mercy, begging to return to the mortal world. Taking pity, the maidens led them to pay homage to the Lady of Southern Qi. When they announced their names, the Lady smiled and said, “Once there were Liu and Ruan; now there are Yuan and Liu. Is this not fate?” She commanded the maidens to escort them, gifting them a jade flask inscribed with a poem:

*You came from a single-leaf boat,*
*You depart toward the Hundred-Flower Bridge.*
*If mortals should knock on the jade flask,*
*The mandarin ducks will speak plainly.*

Suddenly, a bridge hundreds of paces long appeared, its railings adorned with rare blossoms. The two men crossed it and arrived at Hepu, but when they turned back, the bridge had vanished. On their way back to Mount Heng, they tapped the jade flask mid-journey, and a pair of azure mandarin ducks spoke: “When you hunger or thirst, walk forward and you shall find sustenance.” Soon after, they encountered a lavish feast laid out by the roadside, which they ate to sate their hunger. Later, they met a scholar named Master Taiji, to whom they showed the flask. The master said, “This is my jade-liquid flask, lost long ago.” Thereafter, the two men followed the scholar to seclude themselves on Zhurong Peak, and it is believed they attained the Dao.

Eyebrows and eyes of Shaojun, nose and mouth of Zirong.
From *Records of Divine Transcendents*: Li Shaojun was a man of Qi who heard that Emperor Wu of Han favored divine transcendents. Shaojun offered the emperor an esoteric method, saying, “Cinnabar can be refined into gold, and consuming it allows one to ascend to heaven.” At the time, the emperor noticed a bronze vessel in his chamber, and Shaojun declared, “This belonged to Duke Wei of Qi.” When the emperor verified the inscription, he realized Shaojun was indeed several hundred years old. His skin gleamed, and his brows, eyes, mouth, and teeth remained as youthful as a child’s.

From *New Records of the Elixir Terrace*: Zhao Qu, styled Zirong, once suffered from leprosy so severe he was near death. Disgusted with himself, he entered the mountains, offering his body to tigers and wolves, never to return. Suddenly, he encountered an extraordinary figure who taught him a method of ingesting medicinal substances. His illness vanished, his body grew light and strong, and at one hundred seventy years of age, he retained a youthful countenance. When he ventured outside, two beautiful women would appear between his nose and mouth, and he could hear the sounds of zither and lute. He lived among mortals for over three hundred years, yet his complexion remained like a child’s.

True Perfection attains the celestial origin; one may dwell and divine by the Dipper.
From *Biographies of Transcendents*: Li Duoduo was the younger sister of the divine transcendent Li Tuo. Following her brother in cultivation, she received from him the essentials of attaining the celestial origin. After a hundred years of practice, she appeared no older than twenty. The Supreme Lord descended to bestow upon her the Way of Ascension. In Shu, there remains a place called Duoduo’s Abode.

From *Biographies of Eminent Daoists*: The Daoist Ren Keju, of unknown origin, was forty years old, simple and earnest, carrying a sack in service to the Daoist Xiang Daorong. Moved by his devotion, Daorong entrusted him with the *Mirror of the Celestial Origin* and the *Secrets of the Numinous Treasure*, cautioning him: “Only after eighteen years may you reveal these to others. Their verification of fortune and misfortune must not be sought for glory or profit, nor transmitted recklessly. Those who obtain this Way become divine transcendents; those who leak it meet untimely ends.” Thereafter, Keju gradually began to speak of others’ fortunes. When performing rituals of exorcism or offering, he would first instruct each person to fast and face a wall, where he arranged an image of the Dipper’s bowl. Seated before it, the fortunes and misfortunes of all would become vividly clear.

Li Mi sprinkles garlic; Shu Mao plants leeks.
From *Household Records of the Ye Marquis*: In his youth, Li Mi was so light he could stand atop a screen. A strange figure remarked, “This child will ascend at fifteen.” His parents, alarmed, once detected an otherworldly fragrance in the air and sprinkled garlic juice to prevent him from flying away. As he grew, he practiced grain avoidance and Daoist breathing exercises, his bones crackling like jade. People called him “the boy with gleaming bones.” He once composed a song:

*Heaven covers me, earth bears me,*
*Did heaven and earth intend my birth or not?*
*Else why would I fast and ascend the celestial thoroughfare,*
*Or wander the imperial capital, crying out?*
*How could I remain unexalted and not depart,*
*A mere towering man, empty and proud?*

From *Declarations of the Perfected*: During the Qin dynasty, Jiang Shumao, the Marquis of Baling, came to reside at the foot of Mount Juqu. He cultivated five fruits and five pungent vegetables, selling them to purchase cinnabar for his use. To this day, leeks and chives grow wild in the mountains—perhaps remnants of his planting. He was enfeoffed as a marquis during the reign of King Xiao of Qin, and the place is now called Jiangba.

The Dragon Lord’s Orange Shrine; the Fisherman’s Apricot Altar.
From *Supplement to Biographies of Transcendents*: Liu Yi hailed from the Jiang and Xiang regions. During the Yifeng reign, having failed the imperial examinations, he was returning home when an old friend residing in Jingyang came to bid him farewell. Six or seven *li* from their meeting place, he saw a beautiful woman herding sheep in the wilderness. Finding her intriguing, he inquired about her plight. She replied, “I am the youngest daughter of the Dragon Lord of Dongting. Married to the lesser dragon of the Jing River, I was mistreated by my husband and despised by my in-laws. My father-in-law favored his son and banished me to this place.” She entrusted Liu Yi with a letter, instructing him to deliver it north of Dongting Lake, where a massive orange tree stood—known as the Orange Shrine, worshipped by the locals. Upon arrival, he was to strike the tree three to five times with an object, and the letter would reach its destination. Liu Yi followed her instructions. A warrior emerged from the waves and led him beneath the water, where the palace halls resembled those of an earthly king. There, Liu Yi was detained for days of feasting and revelry.

Upon his return, he was bestowed with rare and wondrous treasures beyond naming or comprehension.

From the *True Scripture of Southern Florescence*:
Confucius wandered amidst the black-canopied grove and rested upon the Apricot Platform. His disciples read aloud while he plucked the zither and sang. Before the melody reached its midpoint, an old fisherman descended from his boat and approached. His temples and brows were streaked with white; his hair hung loose, and his sleeves were tucked up. Striding across the plain, he climbed the bank, left hand resting on his knee, right hand cradling his chin, listening until the song’s end.

Zhang drank from the stream; Boyang consumed cinnabar.

From *Supplement to the Biographies of Transcendents*:
Zhang Xie, a native of Wuling, was devoted to learning from childhood. He often meditated upon the Peach Blossom Abbey, yearning to encounter divine immortals and thereby transcend the world. His shorn temples and hidden aspirations remained unspoken. During the Qianfu era, Zheng Kua governed Wuling. Moved by Zhang’s piety in reciting scriptures, he ordered an investigation into his devout practices. Before the ritual’s completion, celestial music arose amid five-colored clouds. After a long while, a chalcedony vial descended from the sky. Zhang received it—within was but water, which he drank to the last drop. Its sweetness was unparalleled. Suddenly, he leapt upward and ascended into heaven.

From *Biographies of Divine Transcendents*:
Wei Boyang entered the mountains to concoct the elixir of immortality, accompanied by three disciples. When the elixir was complete, he knew their hearts were not yet fully resolved. Testing them, he said: “Though the elixir is done, it must be proven with a dog. If the dog flies, it may be consumed; if it dies, it must not be taken.” He fed the elixir to the dog, which died instantly. Boyang declared: “We have forsaken the world, abandoned our families, and entered the mountains. If we fail to attain the Dao of immortality, returning would bring shame. Whether in death or life, I shall consume it.” The moment the elixir touched his lips, he too fell dead. The two remaining disciples exchanged glances and said: “We made the elixir to seek longevity, yet consuming it brings death. What is the use?” They departed to prepare coffins. After they left, Boyang revived, taking with him the disciple who had consumed the elixir—surnamed Yu—and the white dog. Encountering a woodcutter entering the mountains, he penned a letter to his hometown and entrusted it to the two disciples. Only then did they awaken, filled with remorse.

The muleteer pushed open the gate; Hu Zong unlatched the barrier.

*The Traditions of Divine Transcendents* records: Master Huang of Mount Mao was a Daoist of the highest conduct and learning. As he expounded the teachings to a vast assembly, a man suddenly forced his way through the crowd and shouted, “Daoist slave! The day is scorching—why gather this mob? Why not retreat into the deep mountains to cultivate the Dao? How dare you prattle on!” The master made no reply, and after a long while, his countenance softened. He said, “Is it not for funds to repair the temple? Bring forth all your broken cauldrons and miscellaneous iron.” The master followed his instruction. He then ordered a furnace dug into the earth, where the iron was melted in roaring flames. Taking a small measure of medicine, he stirred it in, and after a moment, removing the fire, the molten metal had turned to silver. The master bowed in gratitude, then departed with a smile, vanishing without a trace. Later, someone saw him in the capital, a whip tucked into his belt as he drove a mule, moving as swiftly as the wind. Some called him the “Muleteer.”

*The Comprehensive Mirror of Divine Marvels* states: Hu Yongzong, the Daoist superintendent of the Taiping Abbey in Jiangzhou, had lost his sight and resigned his post. He often had attendants guide him through the abbey. One day, as they reached the gate in heavy snow, someone remarked that a pauper sat by the steps, a piece of malt candy held in his mouth, selling ink at the price of one *jin* per inch. Few buyers came. Yongzong asked the reason, and the man replied, “The snow is too heavy to enter the city, so I rest here—but no one pays me any mind.” Moved with pity, Yongzong gave him fifty *jin* of silver, and this became a daily practice. One day, the man knocked at the gate to take his leave, but the barrier remained bolted. Speaking through the door, he said, “I gift you an inch of ink—keep it for yourself and use it as you will. If you have any left, grind and drink it, and your suffering will end.” Yongzong thanked him and asked his name. The man replied, “My name is on the signboard where I sell ink.” He placed the ink down and departed, his voice fading into the distance. When Yongzong unlatched the gate, it was too late. He ground the ink and drank it, and at once, his eyes regained their clarity. Some later noted that the character for “ink” (*mo*), when split by a vertical stroke, forms the character *lü* (吕), suspecting the man was Lü Dongbin.

Shen Bin’s stone outer coffin and Yuan’s bronze inner coffin are recorded thus: *Miscellaneous Records of the Jia Clan* states that Shen Bin, a gentleman of the sixth rank, was devoted to the Dao from youth. As he neared death, he instructed his sons, “The hall where I reside marks an auspicious site—bury me there.” His descendants dared not disobey. When they prepared the grave, they uncovered a stone outer coffin, spacious and broad, along with three exquisitely carved blue-stone lotus lamps. An inscription read: *“In the second year of Kaicheng, though opened, it was not sealed. The lacquered lamps remain unlit, awaiting Shen Bin’s arrival.”* Later, he was seen dwelling in the Heavenly Treasure Grotto of West Mountain.

*The Memorial Hall Record of Lord Yuan* states: Lord Yuan was a man of the Later Han. According to the *Northern Qi Imperial Digest of Literature*, when Yangxian County was first established, Yuan Yi of Kuaiji possessed divine foresight. He became its magistrate, foretelling floods and droughts. He claimed that upon death, he would become a god, and sometimes slept for days on end, dreaming of feasting with spirits. One day, without illness, he suddenly died. After his burial, a storm darkened the skies, and his coffin vanished. That night, villagers heard the sound of thousands on Jingnan Mountain. At dawn, they found the coffin there and rushed to report it to the county. By the time officials and commoners arrived, the coffin had been divinely concealed, leaving only a stone altar and stone chamber. The mountain was renamed Lord’s Mountain, and today it is called Bronze Coffin Mountain, for it is said that when Lord Yuan died, Heaven sent down a bronze coffin—much like the jade coffin that descended for Wang Qiao when he was magistrate of Ye.

A purple cloud riding the wind, a yellow plum falling into a well: *Records of Marvels* tells of the twenty-fourth year of the Kaiyuan era of the Tang, when the imperial court resided in the Eastern Capital. Li Kuo was appointed prefect of Henan. On that day, a great wind arose, and a female Daoist rode the gale to the Jade True Abbey, alighting upon the bell tower. A throng gathered to witness her. Word reached the prefect, who, a man of blunt disposition, was enraged by the crowd. He stripped her and flogged her, yet she neither pleaded nor showed injury, her complexion unchanged. Kuo was astonished and began to treat her with reverence. He reported the matter to the throne, and she was summoned to the inner palace. When asked her origin, she revealed herself as a female Daoist from the Purple Cloud Abbey in Puzhou. She had abstained from grain for so long that she grew light, and the wind had carried her here. Later, another great wind arose, and she flew away, never to return.

*The Extensive Records of the Strange* recounts: In Huangmei County, a female Daoist named Zhang Lianqiao was eight or nine years old. One day, while fetching water with a jar, she saw a lotus blossom the size of a small plate rising from the well. When she reached for it, it receded; when she withdrew, it emerged again. This happened several times before she fell into the well. Her family, alarmed by her prolonged absence, searched and found her standing atop the well water. Thereafter, she ate nothing, and her parents allowed her to leave home and become a Daoist. At eighteen, as she sat alone in the abbey one day, she saw coins raining from the sky, followed by yellow medicine. She swallowed two pellets and felt her spirit and vitality double. Lu Cong summoned thunder, Wang Xiang divided his shadow: *Further Accounts of Transcendents* tells of Ye Qianyao, styled Lu Cong, who possessed profound Daoist arts. During a drought, the people begged him to pray for rain. He burned incense and made his petition, and in an instant, rain fell. When someone asked him to command thunder, he scraped the ground with his foot, and a rumbling arose from beneath the earth. One day in the city, he suddenly shouted and brandished his arms to display his power. When questioned, he replied, “I saw a fire breaking out in such-and-such a place and went to extinguish it.” Investigation confirmed his words.

*The Traditions of Transcendents* states: Wang Xiang was born with exceptional intelligence. Every day, he gazed at the high peaks of Mount Zhongnan and told his parents, “When I grow up, I will dwell upon this mountain.” People held him in high regard. Upon coming of age, he had no desire for office, devoting himself to sitting in forgetfulness and mirroring the void. He encountered the transcendent Master Meng, who taught him Daoist arts, enabling him to transform his body and divide his shadow.

A single river of clouds from Xie, ten thousand acres of waves from Wang: *Collected Accounts of Transcendents* records that Xie Ziran of Guozhou abstained from grain and often spoke of Daoist matters in lofty and extraordinary terms.

When Prefect Han Yi arrived in the commandery, he suspected her claims were false and summoned her to the prefectural seat, where he confined her for months. When he finally ordered the doors opened, she emerged with her complexion and form unchanged. Han Yi then had his daughter formally become her disciple. Later, on Mount Dafang, he established an altar and invited the Daoist priest Cheng Taixu to prepare the *Record of the Three Caverns*. He also relocated Ziran to reside within the city walls.

In the ninth year of Zhengyuan, Prefect Li Jian assumed office. Ziran told him, “Dwelling in the city is not suitable; I wish to live among springs and rocks.” Li Jian then built a chamber for her on Mount Jinquan. One day, she visited the prefectural seat to bid Li Jian farewell, then ascended to heaven in broad daylight on Mount Jinquan. Thousands of men and women witnessed it, and in an instant, five-colored clouds stretched across the entire valley.

*The Ink Guest’s Waving Rhinoceros* records: Wang Pingfu, during the *guichou* year of Xining, was on night duty at the Chongwen Library when he dreamed someone invited him to the sea. There, he saw a magnificent central palace where music played—pipes, flutes, and drums—with many attendants. The palace was inscribed *Palace of Lingzhi*. Pingfu wished to go with them, but someone beside the palace called across the water, “The time has not yet come. For now, return; one day, we shall welcome you.” At that moment, he awoke with a start, just as the palace bell tolled. He composed a poem:

*A thousand acres of waves, leaves flying on the wind,*
*Pipes and flutes in the palace named Lingzhi.*
*Wielding the brush, not as one of this mortal world—*
*At the toll of the Chang’an bell, the dream breaks.*

Iron fan of Xiuchuan, golden cakes of Guanfu.

*The Idle Tales of Rustics* states: Peng Xiao, a Gentleman of the Ministry of Rites, styled himself Xiuchuan and took the sobriquet True Unity Master. He often told others, “I am a descendant of Qian Keng. Though my family has produced those who attained the Dao, I, though clad in red and purple and enjoying official emoluments, have never slackened in my cultivation. I shall become a lofty figure of this age and never descend to the realm of lesser ghosts.” When he governed Jintang County, he would ride a white ox to and from Changli Mountain, as if seeking a place of true communion. Often, white cranes flew and cried around him. Xiao annotated the *Scripture of Yin Conformity* and explicated the *Triple Concordance*. Whenever he inscribed talismans—calling them “iron fans”—those who consumed them would recover from illness.

*The Collected Accounts of Transcendents* records: Huang Guanfu was poor and often burned willow leaves as incense, subsisting on plants and eschewing the five grains. When her family pressed her to marry, she suddenly told her parents, “There is something extraordinary in the well before our gate.” They went to look, and the water surged violently. She then leapt into the well and did not emerge for a long time. When they dragged her out, they found an ancient statue of the Heavenly Worthy, its appearance identical to hers. The water immediately stilled. They placed the wooden statue by the roadside, and Guanfu returned home weeping. Her mother came to see her, and though she longed for her daughter, suddenly colorful clouds and celestial music descended into their courtyard, accompanied by a grand procession. Three women appeared and said to her parents, “Your daughter was originally an immortal of the Upper Clarity. For a minor transgression, she was banished to the mortal realm. Now that her term is complete, she returns to heaven. Do not grieve.” They added, “This year, pestilence will claim many lives. Move your family to Yizhou to avoid the coming calamity.” They left behind several golden cakes before ascending to heaven.

*Records of the Multitudes of Immortals of the Three Caverns*, Scroll Twelve, concludes.

*Records of the Multitudes of Immortals of the Three Caverns*, Scroll Thirteen.
Compiled by Orthodox Unity Daoist Chen Baoguang.

At Sha Park, she flexed her graceful wings; in Huayang, she nurtured her pinions.

*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* records: West of Yizhou City stood a Daoist abbey, where none but dedicated practitioners would reside. Xu Zuoqing once called himself a Daoist of Mount Qingcheng, visiting three or four times a year. On the Double Ninth Festival during the Tianbao era, Emperor Minghuang hunted at Sha Park and saw a lone crane soaring through the clouds. He shot it down with an arrow, but the crane, though struck, descended slowly, its wings still agile as it flew southeast. That very day, Zuoqing arrived with the arrow in hand, his countenance troubled. He told others, “While traveling in the mountains, I was unexpectedly struck by this object, but I am unharmed now. This arrow is not of mortal make. By next year, its owner will come here, and I shall return it.” He left it on the rear wall and inscribed the date: the ninth day of the ninth month in the thirteenth year. When Minghuang fled to Shu, he visited the abbey and, seeing the arrow, ordered it brought to him. It was indeed his own imperial arrow. Astonished, he inquired further, and the Daoist answered truthfully. Thus, he realized that the crane he had shot in Sha Park the previous year was none other than Xu Zuoqing. Upon examining the inscription, he also understood how Zuoqing had flown from Sha Park to this very place. Today, there stands Flying Immortal Ridge, said to be where Zuoqing alighted with the arrow, and below it lies the abbey of Flying Immortal.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* states: Jiang Shumao, once the Marquis of Baling, abandoned his post to cultivate the Dao in Mount Juqu. In a letter to his colleagues, he wrote, “I once studied the Way at Guigu, attained it at Shaoshi, nurtured my wings in Huayang, and awaited my ascension at Yicheng. Now, riding the whirlwind’s chariot, I rest in Juqu, awakening to the swift passage of time. Feng Chang turns yellow to return to the primordial; Shi Jing guards the white within the black.”

*The Declarations of the Perfected* further records: Feng Chang, styled Yanshou, mastered the arts of yin-yang and divination by fifteen. Moved by the teachings of Perfected Deng, he could reverse yellow to red and his face took on a jade-like luster. Upon achieving the Dao, he became the Perfected One of the Western Peak. As for Chen Shijing, he guarded the Way of the Mysterious White, sitting outdoors each dawn to visualize the black qi of the Mud Pill, the white qi of the heart, and the yellow qi of the navel—three qi arising like clouds to envelop his body, transforming into fire that burned through him inside and out. He practiced this daily, circulating qi twenty times before ceasing. As the saying goes: “Know the white, guard the black, and death cannot claim you; know the black, guard the white, and all evil vanishes.” Shijing later attained immortality.

Fei Yun spiraled in radiant ascent; Qi Xia blazed in splendor.

*The Continued Biographies of Immortals* tells of Fei Yuanjing, who was serene and indifferent from childhood. When she vowed to enter the Daoist path, her parents said, “A daughter must have a home.” They forced her to marry Li Yan, and for months, the couple lived as respectful guests. One day, she told her husband, “The immortals forbid me to be your wife. Let me sever ties and dwell alone.” That midnight, voices of laughter were heard in her chamber, and her husband, suspicious, secretly observed—only to find the room filled with light. At dawn, she bade him farewell and departed, enveloped in swirling clouds and mist, as immortal maidens played celestial music to accompany her ascent.

The Qi family’s Xiaoyao was devoted to the Way, reciting scriptures without cease. When she ascended, her parents compelled her to marry Ji Xun, but she secluded herself in a chamber, fasting and meditating in stillness, beyond mortal comprehension. One night, a thunderous crack split the roof, and only her robes remained within. Looking up, they saw clouds and radiance blazing as Xiaoyao ascended.

The Upright Rustic and the Luminous Recluse.

*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* records: Master Wu Jun, styled Zhengjie, arrived in the capital during the early Tianbao era and was registered among the Daoist clergy. He entered Mount Song to study under Master Pan Zheng and master its teachings. Minghuang, hearing of his reputation, summoned him for discourse and was greatly pleased. When the emperor later inquired about the Dao, Wu replied, “Of all teachings on the Way, none surpass the *Five Thousand Words of Laozi*.”

The emperor further inquired about the methods of divine transcendents for refinement and alchemy. Wu replied, “Such are the affairs of rustics—pursued over years and months, not matters a sovereign should fix his mind upon.” Whenever Wu expounded, he spoke of the teachings of names and the affairs of the world, subtly admonishing the Son of Heaven with veiled words. The emperor held him in high regard.

The Daoist Li Hanguang was skilled in seal and clerical scripts, and some claimed he surpassed his father. Upon hearing this, Wu never wrote another character in his life. Later, when studying under Master Zhengyi, he received the treasured cloud-seal scriptures, which the master entrusted to him without reserve, saying, “Li Hanguang is a true guest of the Jade Clarity.”

Hidden in pillars, distant as Luo; entering diagrams, willows take form.

*The Unofficial Histories* records: The Daoist Luo Gongyuan was once summoned by Minghuang, who asked him about the arts of invisibility. Luo replied, “Your Majesty has already been inscribed in the jade registers and golden tablets of the Nine Clarities—how could one of such weight for the altars of soil and grain lightly indulge in petty arts?” Enraged, the emperor reviled him, and Luo fled into a palace pillar, repeatedly exposing the emperor’s faults. The emperor grew even angrier and split the pillar to pursue him. Yet when he looked, Luo had entered a jade tablet, which the emperor shattered into a dozen pieces—each bearing Luo’s form. The emperor apologized, and Luo returned to his original state. Though pressed relentlessly, Luo eventually taught the emperor some arts but refused to reveal their full defense.

The emperor tested Luo by commanding him to conceal himself, yet Luo’s sash and belt remained visible, and at times his shadow flickered forth. Enraged, the emperor ordered his execution. Later, the eunuch Fu Xianyu returned from Shu and encountered Luo, who engaged him in conversation. From his sleeve, Luo produced a letter and a bundle of *dang gui* from Shu to be delivered. Xianyu presented these to the emperor, who grew deeply remorseful. At the close of the Tianbao era, the emperor indeed fled to western Shu—thus the *dang gui* was no false token.

From the *Youyang Miscellany*:
In the Zhengyuan era, the painter Ning Cai depicted the *Gathering in the Bamboo Grove* with such skill that when the scholars Guo Xuan and Liu Cheng sat as guests, they often clashed in displays of *qi*. Suddenly, Liu turned to the painting and said, “Today I shall offer you a modest feat: without applying the five colors, I will make its brilliance surpass all others. How would that be?” Guo scoffed in disbelief. Liu replied, “I shall enter the painting itself to refine it.” With that, he leapt up, vanished into the scroll, and was gone. The guests were aghast. The painting hung upon the wall, and though they searched thoroughly, they found nothing. After a long while, a voice spoke: “Master Guo, do you believe now?” The sound seemed to issue from within the painting. Moments later, Liu’s eyes flashed open as he plummeted from the scroll. Pointing to the image of Ruan Ji, he said, “My efforts have only just reached this.” The onlookers examined the painting and saw that Ruan Ji’s likeness alone had transformed—his lips appeared as if caught mid-whistle. Ning Cai, beholding this, no longer recognized his own work. Convinced Liu had attained the Dao, he and Guo expressed their gratitude, and within days, Liu vanished without a trace.

Zixu studied the ancients; Taojun was learned in the classics.

From the *Records of the True Realm*:
Master Ji, the Celestial Master, bore the name Qiwu and the style Zixu. He was well-versed in ancient learning and the classics. At one time, the Buddhist monk Fanzhen inquired about the origins of the Daoist and Buddhist teachings. The Master distinguished their differences, saying: “The Dao has no before or after—its depths defy all discourse. Laozi embodies the immortal Dao of longevity, belonging to the Left Office of the Most High. Thus Laozi was born from the left armpit, raising his hand to the left, which is why the left is revered in our methods. Our teachings prescribe the wearing of yellow, for yellow is yang. Those who attain the Dao hold life and death in their own hands, moving freely without restraint. Though the great kalpas may end, our Dao will never perish. The Crown Prince, however, embodies the Dao of rebirth and the Dharma of extinction, belonging to the Right Office of the Most High. Thus the Prince was born from the right armpit, raising his hand to the right, which is why the right is revered in their methods. Their teachings prescribe the wearing of black, for black is yin. When one’s allotted span is exhausted, death follows. Though these two paths diverge, they converge in the Most High—yet none but the perfected may fathom their mysteries.” Those who heard him were filled with admiration.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* states: Taoist Master Tao, styled Wengzhong, began his career as a district officer, assisting in the propagation of classical learning. In his later years, he served as Prefect of Jiaozhou, where he encountered Shu Guoyouping and ingested the Ninefold Essences. He refined his qi to fortify the stellar guardians within his heart. Through continued cultivation, he attained the Dao and now dwells in the grotto-heavens. Fire-seal Master Teng and Lamp-stand Lord Xu.

*The Wine-Jar Dialogues of Mianshui* records: When Attendant Censor Teng Zongliang was stationed as Prefect of Liyang, he heard of a reclusive adept living deep in the mountains. Seeking him out through rugged and remote paths, Teng found the man just awakening from sleep. The adept rose, bowed, and invited Teng to sit beside him. Their conversation was profoundly reasoned. Teng inquired how long the adept had lived in the mountains. The man replied that he had fled the ravages of war and lived there alone; he knew spring by the blossoming of flowers, autumn by the falling of leaves, winter and summer by the extremes of cold and heat.

After some time, the adept turned to Teng and said, “The day is drawing late, and you must be hungry. There are mountain yams and wild chestnuts to sustain you.” He then kindled a fire from pine needles, roasted the yams and chestnuts, and offered them to Teng. After eating, he asked, “Does gathering firewood with your hands not trouble you? Would you like me to give you a fire-bell?” The adept smiled and said, “Living long in the mountains, I have grown content with simplicity. I do not wish my heart to be enslaved by things—such a bell would be of no use.”

As dusk fell, Teng departed with a sense of melancholy. Later, when he sent someone to inquire again, the adept had vanished without a trace.

*The Record of the Western Mountains*: Master Xu the Perfected, together with Lord Wu, returned to Yuzhang and thereupon went back to Mount Xiaoyao. Day after day, he expounded the Dao to his disciples and admonished the villagers, so that all turned toward goodness and shunned transgression, and benevolence and filial piety flourished. Once, a man came to the mountain to sell an iron lampstand, and the Perfected Master purchased it. That night, using it as a lamp to illuminate a chipped and peeling spot, he examined it closely and saw that it was in fact gold. He promptly sought out the man and returned it to him. When Bo Ci’s illness was cured, he paid homage with propriety and felt his body grow light.

*The Declarations of the Perfected*: Fan Bo Ci suffered from a perverse and taxing illness that lingered for years. Though he spent his family’s wealth in search of a cure, the malady persisted. Thereupon, he resolved to enter the Dao and abandon the secular world, devoting himself to quiet sitting and self-cultivation. After fifty days, his illness was cured. He then entered Mount Tianmu, where for nineteen years he ingested pure essences and concentrated his thoughts. Moved by his sincerity, perfected immortals descended and bestowed upon him an elixir. After consuming it, he ascended to heaven in broad daylight and was appointed as the Perfected of Primordial Unity. On Mount Heng, there was a Daoist practitioner named Zhang Lizheng who, upon first encountering Lord Wang of Xicheng, received from him an elixir formula. After ingesting it, his eyes grew bright and his body light, and he could walk five hundred *li* in a day. The August Lord of Shuanghua dispatched a messenger to welcome him, and he ascended on clouds in broad daylight. Emperor Wu of Han had four excesses; the Yellow Emperor mastered seven flavors.

From *Miscellaneous Records of Immortals’ Biographies*: The envoy of Yuezhi said to Emperor Wu, “Excessive seeing breeds arrogance, excessive speech invites calamity, excessive thought fosters lust and treachery, and excessive adornment leads to extravagance. No ruler who indulges in these four excesses has ever brought order to the realm.”

From *The Inner Transmission of the Yellow Emperor*: The Queen Mother bestowed upon the Emperor the sevenfold refinement of *mei*. The Emperor asked, “What are the seven *mei*?” The Queen Mother replied, “When the eyes are *mei*, they do not see clearly; when the ears are *mei*, they do not hear keenly; when the mouth is *mei*, it lacks discernment; when the nose is *mei*, it does not breathe freely; when the hands are *mei*, they lack steadiness; when the feet are *mei*, they do not walk rightly; when the heart is *mei*, it is not true. But if the heart is not disordered, it is true; if the eyes are not *mei*, they see clearly; if the ears are not *mei*, they hear keenly; if the mouth is not *mei*, it is discerning; if the nose is not *mei*, it breathes freely; if the hands are not *mei*, they are steady; if the feet are not *mei*, they walk rightly. Thus, the essence of the seven *mei* lies in one. If that one falters even slightly, the other six become obstructed, and the whole body falls into disorder, nearing death.”

Huang Jue saw off a guest; an immortal breeze attended the gathering. From Liu Gongfu’s *Poetry Talks*: Huang Jue was skilled in poetry. Once, he saw off a guest beyond the capital gate, but upon arrival, the guest had already departed too far to catch up. At an inn, he saw a Daoist priest beside him and invited him to share the wine and dishes he had brought. After they finished, the Daoist lifted a vessel, scooped water, and wrote the character *lü* (吕). Huang Jue then realized he was Lü Dongbin. The Daoist said, “Next year, you will meet me in Jiangnan.” Huang Jue indeed obtained an official post in Jiangnan. When the time came, he saw the Daoist take from his robe seven large coins, followed by three small ones, and say, “This number cannot be increased.” He also gave Huang Jue a medicine of several *cun* in length, instructing, “On New Year’s Day, grind it with wine and consume it to prevent illness for a year.” Huang Jue followed his words. By age seventy, the medicine was nearly exhausted, and he wrote a poem: *“The almanac’s days are few, / Counting on my fingers, next year I’ll be seventy-three.”* He indeed passed away that year.

From *Miscellaneous Records of Immortals’ Biographies*: Liu Xianfeng was a Daoist of Jiulong who entered Mount Bailu and was granted esoteric defenses by a divine person. At Dasnow Temple on Mount Pengkou, a great assembly was held. Xianfeng and over ten Daoist companions attended but paid no formal respects. As soon as they departed, a commotion erupted among the assembly, with cries of alarm: beyond the fence, over thirty tigers roamed freely, causing such panic that the crowd could not eat. The monks, realizing it was Xianfeng’s doing, had everyone bow in apology with incense. The tigers then dispersed.

Xie Yuanqing’s *unicorn fat*; Jie Xiang’s *fin sashimi*. From *Further Biographies of Immortals*: Xie Yuanqing encountered divine immortals who served him *phoenix-crown millet*, *dragon-essence rice*, *unicorn fat*, *striped-glass marrow*, *Black Continent white nectar*, *Void Cavern sacred melons*, *Fusang cinnabar stalks*, and *Qing River patterned algae*. They also offered *jade-rice wine*, *cassia brain*, and *rue blossoms*, played the *eight jade chimes*, the *cloud-cluster sheng*, and the *cavern-yin qing*, performed the *Primal Harmony* song, and danced the *revolving phoenix* and *soaring simurgh*. From *Extensive Records*: Jie Xiang, styled Yuanze, discussed with the King of Wu which sashimi was finest. Xiang said, “The *zi* fish from the sea is supreme.” He requested a square pit be dug before the palace hall and filled with water. Xiang cast his line into the pit and, in the time it takes to eat a meal, caught a *zi* fish and prepared sashimi.

Empress Zhang’s broken nail; Guard-One’s shattered lump. From *Records of Responses*: Empress Zhang, mother of Emperor Wu of Chen, once received a small tortoise from a Daoist, its shell radiant with five colors. The Daoist said, “In three years, there will be a sign.” When the time came, the Empress gave birth, and purple light filled the chamber. The tortoise had vanished. The Empress was beautiful from youth, with fingernails five *cun* long, their color a blend of red and white. Whenever she observed mourning rites for a relative, one nail would break first. From *Divine Immortals of the Wang Clan*: Wang Shouyi, in the early Zhenguan era, styled himself “Wang the Layman of Mount Zhongnan” and sold medicine in Luoyang. A wealthy man, Liu Xin, had a son born with a lump of flesh above his brow. Shouyi gave him a pill from his gourd, and in an instant, the lump burst open, revealing a small, brilliantly colored five-colored snake over a *zhang* long. Shouyi shouted at it, and the snake leapt up, shrouding the sky in mist. Shouyi mounted the snake and departed.

Wang Ni’s flying steps; Xu Que’s ascent. From *Divine Immortals of the Wang Clan*: Wang Ni was the teacher of Nie Que and attained the Dao between the eras of Fuxi and Shennong. The Yellow Emperor met him and received the essentials of the Dao. He lived through the reigns of Shaohao and Zhuanxu, often wandering among mortals and practicing the *flying steps*. He was still seen in the time of Yao and Shun, then ascended to heaven one day later. From *Elegant Talks of Prefectural Pavilions*: The Perfected Xu Que, in the late Tang, visited the Zhaoxian Abbey on Mount Nanyue and inscribed a song on the wall: *“The great furnace refines human nature and fate, / Tools differ, but all destinies are fixed. / Demons and specters vie in supernatural arts, / Yet only harm the wicked, not the upright. / A babe just learning to walk / Knows only the sun and moon rise east and west. / But by the might of the myriad spirits, / The moon moves south, the sun north. / Jade is jade, stone is stone— / Buried deep in mud, they never change. / Deng Tong starved to death, Yan Guang lived in poverty— / Are emperors truly powerless? / A man of ambition, unrecognized, should not force his way, / Clinging to dragons and phoenixes wastes the spirit.”* Several days after writing this, he ascended. Zichang’s virtue aligned; Tunan’s Dao perfected.

**The True Sayings**: The Supreme Perfected One told the Prefectural Governor: “I have seen Lu Zichang of Nanyang—a man of pure simplicity, untaught and uninitiated, yet aligned with Heaven and resigned to fate. Though he knew nothing of the methods of cultivating life and committed no evil, his virtue harmonized with the natural order. Though he did not prolong his years or transcend the world, at death he ascended to the Hall of Blessings, refined his spirit, and received qi. His name was recorded in the Emperor’s register, and now he serves as a Gentleman for Cultivating Literature. Such was his inborn destiny, a blessing flowing from the accumulated virtue of his ancestors. Had he grasped the principles of nurturing life and practiced the Supreme One’s secrets, his pursuit of the Dao would have met with unbroken success.”

**Records of Eminent Daoists**: Chen Tunan, styled *Tunan*, failed the imperial examinations and roamed mountains and rivers for over twenty years. One night in his courtyard, he saw a golden-armored spirit holding a sword, who said: “Your Dao is perfected. You shall have a place of return and completion.” The Master asked: “What is this place of return and completion?” The spirit replied: “It is where all treasures gather in autumn. Shall I not withdraw to the West?” He then moved to dwell on Mount Hua.

*Pure Wind* notes: “Xue Ji admonished like a comet’s warning.”

**Miscellaneous Records of the National Histories**: The Tang Grand Astrologer Li Chunfeng was revising the new calendar when he predicted a solar eclipse on the day of the new moon’s conjunction.
