The omen was inauspicious, and Emperor Taizong was displeased. “If the sun does not darken,” he said, “what will you do?” Chunfeng replied: “If it does not, I will accept death.” On the appointed day, the Emperor sent a messenger to the courtyard to tell Chunfeng: “I grant you leave to bid farewell to your wife and children.” Chunfeng replied that it was still early by a quarter-hour and pointed to the shadow on the sundial: “When it reaches here, the eclipse will occur.” As he foretold, the sun darkened, not deviating by a hair’s breadth.

Chunfeng once memorialized: “The seven stars of the Northern Dipper will transform into humans. Tomorrow, they will drink wine in the Western Market. You should send someone to await them.” The Emperor followed his advice. A messenger was dispatched, and seven Brahmin monks entered through the Golden Light Gate, ascending to the upper floor of a wineshop in the Western Market. The messenger mounted the stairs and announced: “I have come to invite the masters to the palace.” The foreign monks glanced at one another and laughed: “That little boy Li Chunfeng speaks of us!” After drinking, they descended, but the monks had vanished.

**Supplement to the Biographies of Immortals**: Xue Ji, a native of Fenyin in Hebei, later resided in Weizhou. He left the secular world to become a Daoist and was versed in astronomy and calendrical science. When Emperor Taizong was preparing to perform the Feng and Shan sacrifices, a comet appeared, and Xue Ji admonished him, halting the rites. His prognostications of auspicious and inauspicious events always aligned with Li Chunfeng’s. Later, he died without illness, and wondrous fragrances, clouds, cranes, and celestial music filled the air—seen and heard by all at the foot of the mountain and within the abbey. When his coffin was interred, it was found to be empty.

*Master Hua of the Great Empyrean* wandered far in the Upper Clarity.

**Declarations of the Perfected**: Lü Zihua of Shanyang was a disciple of the Dark Lord. He ingested the liquid of rainbow cinnabar but had not yet studied the *Inner Canon*. He came to the Supreme Lord to receive the *Hidden Writ of the Great Empyrean* and recited it, delighting in secluded chambers and square terraces, yet never aspiring to the ranks of immortality.

Xu Yuan’You, styled *Far Wanderer*, was the elder brother of the Prefectural Governor. A man of pure emptiness and Daoist inclination, he dwelled in the wilderness and encountered an extraordinary person who taught him the method of reversing the flow. He ingested jade liquid and refined the essence of the brain, his face glowing with radiance, his youth restored. In the Yonghe era of the Jin, he withdrew into reclusion and never returned. Later, he dwelled on the central peak of Mount Dadi. When his elixir was completed, jade lads and white deer descended to welcome him. The Southern Ling Abbey now marks his former abode.

**Gongfang licked sores; Zhang Cang sucked milk.**

**Biographies of Divine Transcendents**: Li Babai was a man of Shu, eight hundred years old—hence his name. Sometimes he concealed himself in the mountains, sometimes he appeared in the marketplace. Tang Gongfang was sincere but had not met a enlightened master. Li wished to test him and posed as a traveling merchant. Gongfang, unaware that Babai was an immortal, treated him with greater care than his other guests, cherishing him deeply. Later, Babai feigned a grave illness, and Gongfang summoned physicians and prepared medicines worth tens of thousands, undeterred by the cost. His concern was written on his face.

Babai’s condition worsened, his body covered in foul, festering sores. He said: “If you lick my sores, they will heal.” Gongfang did so. Babai said: “Your licking cannot cure me. If your wife licks them, they will heal.” Gongfang then had his wife lick them. Babai said: “Now, bathe me in thirty *hu* of fine wine, and I will recover.” Gongfang prepared the wine and bathed him, and his body became smooth as congealed fat, without a trace of sores. Babai then told him: “I am an immortal. Because of your sincerity, I tested you. You are teachable.” He gave Gongfang a volume of elixir scriptures. Gongfang entered Mount Yuntai, compounded the elixir, and ascended to heaven in broad daylight.

**The Master Who Embraces Simplicity**: During the Han, Chancellor Zhang Cang happened to drink a woman’s milk and lived to one hundred eighty years. This was but a minor method, yet he tripled the lifespan of ordinary men. How much more might one achieve by mastering all the profound and wondrous arts?

**Sand seas and stone medicines; Tangchang’s jade elixir.**

**Records of the Forgotten**: The Yellow Emperor sent Feng Hou to carry books and Chang Bo to bear a sword. At dawn, they wandered the sands of Huan; by dusk, they returned to Yinpu, covering ten thousand *li* in a single breath. The sands of Huan bore a stone leaf with a single stem and a hundred leaves, blooming once every thousand years. The place was also called Shalan, where the sands surged like waves. Within dwelled divine dragons, fish, and giant turtles, all capable of flight. The immortal Ning Feng ate a flying fish and died, but revived after two hundred years. Thus, Master Ning’s *Ode to the Sand Sea* says:
*“The azure leaf gleams, unfolding through a thousand years,
A century’s brief death—eating the flying fish.”*

**Miscellaneous Conversations**: At the Tangchang Abbey in Chang’an, a jade elixir flower drew a constant stream of carriages and horses. One day, a girl of seventeen or eighteen appeared, clad in embroidered robes with trailing sleeves, her countenance graceful and lovely. She was attended by two female Daoists and three young servants, all with topknots, their beauty unmatched. An extraordinary fragrance suffused the air.

She stood for a long while, then instructed a young servant to gather several branches of the flower, saying: "My appointment at Jade Peak awaits—now we may depart." She raised the reins, and in an instant, they were already halfway to the heavens. Only then did they realize this was the journey of divine immortals.

The Daoist shook out his robes; the general raised his whisk.

**Comprehensive Records of Marvels**: A Daoist named Fang, whose full name was lost, secluded himself in the western mountains of Yiyang. In Cizhou stood the Temple of the Protector of the Nation, Spirit of Efficacious Response. Each year in the second and third months, people from across the land presented rare birds and strange beasts, exquisite crafts and wondrous weapons, delicacies and exotic fruits—nothing was wanting. Crowds gathered at the temple’s base, touring, feasting, and reveling until early summer, when the villagers dispersed. Without fail, Daoist Fang arrived every year, always gifting his acquaintances with nine-times-steamed yellow essence. One year, he suddenly did not appear, and all assumed he had moved to another mountain—or perhaps had perished.

In the City God’s Temple of the Spring Capital, behind the deity’s throne, lay a corpse, its body buried beneath over an inch of dust. When officials prepared to examine it, the figure suddenly shook out its robes and rose—it was Daoist Fang. He rejoined his companions for a bout of unrestrained drinking, lingering for over a month before departing. From then on, he never returned.

**Declarations of the Perfected**: The Lord of Life Preservation addressed Yang the Perfected, saying: "Xu was able to entrust his form to the hidden transformation and received the *Night Release* method from Zhang Zhennan." It is said that this Xu was Xu Yuan, whose master served as General of Zhennan. After his corpse-release burial in Linyi, forty-four years later, during the Wei era, a great wind arose, splitting open his wooden coffin. His body appeared as if alive, and when he lifted the tail of his robe to cover his face, he burst into laughter. The divine messengers then welcomed him to Mao Mountain.

The Heavenly King questioned Xu.

**Records of Mount Mao**: Three envoys of the Three Heavens arrived on purple clouds, bearing jade tablets and clad in embroidered robes, bearing the decree to summon the Great Lord Mao to his post at Red City. Today, Mount Mao’s Embroidered Robe Pavilion marks the site where he received the Nine Bestowments.

**Declarations of the Perfected**: The immortal Wang Zideng held the position of Eastern Minister of the Lesser Heavenly Realm. Knowing of Attendant Xu’s reverent solemnity, Zideng inquired of the Eastern Minister: "Where is this man now?" The Eastern Minister replied: "He is from my hometown. Within, his true nature is upright; without, he blends with worldly affairs—truly a man of fine talent. Now he cultivates the supreme Dao."

Plucking flowers to drink wine, striking bamboo to harmonize songs.

**Elegant Talks from the Prefectural Pavilion**: Li Mengfu, during the early Kaiping era of the Liang, served under General Zhong Fu in Hongzhou. Daily, he drank with commoners, composing wild verses in unrestrained abandon. In all seasons, he wore flowers in his hair, dangling a fish from his fishing rod as he strolled through the market, singing the *Fisherman’s Chant* to sell his lyrics. Those fond of such things vied to purchase them, and with the money earned, he would enter a tavern. His verses numbered over a thousand, circulating throughout the lands south of the Yangtze. A couple of examples:

*The temple bell’s toll drifts across distant shoals,
A half-moon’s waning light sinks before the hills.
Slowly I pole my oar, turning back to the bay—
Waves churn the dawn’s brocade into scattered gold.*

And:

*Fisher-brothers, fisher-comrades, joyous in your coming,
The old woman’s shrine sits by the river’s bend.
Coconut-shell ladles, cups of knotted wood,
Boiled perch piled high upon the table.*

Zhong Fu, deeming him mad and a corrupting influence, sought to punish him. In prison, Mengfu silently composed over ten poems, including:

*Plucking flowers to drink wine—what harm in that?
Woodcutters’ songs, fishermen’s chants—no hindrance to the times.*

Zhong relented, sparing him punishment, and sent him away. His whereabouts afterward were unknown.

**Biographies of Divine Immortals**: The Bamboo-Striker, whose surname and given name were unknown, frequented a wine shop in Chengdu. In his hand, he held a bamboo node, striking it to produce a resonant *clang*, singing in harmony with the sound. The words of his songs all aligned with the Dao’s intent. For over a decade, he did this, until one day, he approached a medicine vendor in the eastern market and said to Huang’s son: "I know you, elder, are fond of the Dao. Now I wish to entrust you with a sincere request—would you accept?" Huang replied: "I would hear what you require."

He said: "I am a mendicant scholar, dwelling beneath the Seven Li Pavilion Bridge. Now gravely ill, I shall soon die. On the day of my death, I ask that you burn my body with fire—but take care never to touch my heart."

The next day, Huang arrived at the bridge and found him. The Bamboo-Striker gratefully expressed his thanks, then passed away as his words ended. Huang prepared burial garments and a coffin, interring him before cremating the remains outside the city. At once, an extraordinary fragrance filled the air, thick and lingering, while crows gathered and cried until evening. Only his heart remained unburned, still as large as a dou measure.

As dusk fell, Huang’s son prepared to return home. In haste, he struck the heart with his staff—suddenly, a thunderous explosion erupted, startling men and horses. From the flames emerged a figure over a foot tall: the Bamboo-Striker himself, striking his bamboo node with resonant clarity. The sound soared into the clouds as he vanished from sight.

**A painted hawk, two sighing geese.**

*Biographies of Eminent Daoists*: The Daoist Li Guizhen, active during the late Tang, once visited the Xinguo Abbey in Hongzhou. There, he saw the merit statues in the Three Officials Hall—exquisitely crafted in layered silk by imperial decree of Emperor Minghuang. Yet the caretakers had neglected their upkeep, allowing sparrows and pigeons to soil them with droppings. Guizhen then painted a hawk on the wall, and from that day forward, no birds dared perch there. Later, some witnessed Guizhen ascending to immortality on Mount Luofu.

*Unofficial Records of the Jin*: Dong Yang, styled Zhongtong, was a native of Junyi in Chenliu. During the Taishi era, he traveled to Luoyang but sought neither office nor fame. In the Yongjia period, the ground in the eastern outskirts of Luoyang, near Buguang Alley, collapsed, and two geese emerged. The gray one took flight, while the white one could not. Upon hearing this, Dong sighed: "This was the site of the Di Springs, where the Zhou once held their covenant. Now two geese appear—the gray one symbolizes the barbarians, the white our own state. How can I speak of this fully?" Turning to Xie Kun and Ruan Fu, he said: "The *Book of Changes* says, ‘To know the incipient movements is divine.’ You two should conceal yourselves well." Then, with his wife, he shouldered their belongings and entered Shu. No one knew their ultimate fate.

**Deng Cheng’s serpent sword, Tao Kan’s dragon shuttle.**

*Supplement to the Biographies of Immortals*: Deng Cheng, a native of Linchuan in Fuzhou, came from a family devoted to the Dao. Whenever he burned incense, he would vow to aid the state and uphold the teachings, pursuing the Way on Mount Magu. His kin, hearing of his aspirations, questioned him: "To serve the state requires civil or martial talent. You withdraw to become a Daoist—how will you fulfill such a vow?" He smiled and replied: "This resolve is not something the worldly can comprehend."

One day, as he returned from the mountains to his private residence, he encountered a massive serpent blocking the path midway. Scooping up water, he sighed at it and said: "If you were a dragon, you would soar with the waters—do not obstruct the way." Suddenly, the serpent transformed into a sword, which he took home. Later, a Daoist priest saw it and said: "This is a divine sword." He then taught Deng the method of wielding it.

Emperor Minghuang, deeply devoted to the Dao and its mysteries, widely sought those who could expand its teachings. He summoned Deng to the capital, and one day, during a moment of leisure, said to him: "The fortress of Shibao has become a persistent border concern." Deng replied: "Though I am but a humble rustic, I have received undeserved favor. How dare I not offer my service to ease Your Majesty’s worries?" Thereupon, he lit seven lamps in the shape of the Dipper, burned incense, and prayed in silence. One lamp flew away like a shooting star, and the fortress of Shibao was reduced to ashes.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* states: Tao Kan was the Marquis of Xihe. Once, while fishing, he caught a shuttle, which he hung on the wall. A storm arose, and the shuttle turned into a crimson dragon, leaping out through the roof. This account appears in Liu Qin’s *Garden of Marvels*.

**Jade Spring’s treasure steed, the hibiscus-white mule.**
*The Blue-Green Locks*: Chancellor Liu traveled to the capital to take the imperial examinations. Passing through the town of Single-Log Bridge, an old clerk presented him with a poem: *"This year, ride a poor mule on your way; / One day, you shall return on a treasure steed."* It also said: "You are none other than the Jade Spring Daoist Lord of Mount Luofu."

*Ouyang’s Poetic Discourse*: After Shi Manqing’s death, he appeared in a vision to someone, saying: "Now I am an immortal, master of the Hibiscus City, riding a white mule that flies like the wind." Later, he descended to a scholar’s home and left a poem: *"The orioles’ songs do not fade with spring’s passing; / Flower shadows ever follow the sun’s slow tread."*

**Three Friends of Mist and Vine, Six Recluses of Bamboo Stream.**
*The Declarations of the Perfected*: Zheng Ao, styled Yunshi, sat for the imperial examinations but did not pass. At the end of the Tang dynasty, he cast off his robes and departed, becoming a Daoist on Mount Shaoshi. Two azure youths, a crane, and a zither accompanied him in his wanderings. He befriended Li Zhen, a powerful minister of the Liang court. When Li sought to appoint him to office, Zheng refused. Later, when Li was exiled to the south for his crimes, Zheng walked a thousand *li* to visit him, further elevating his reputation among those who heard of it.

Zheng learned that on Mount Hua, the resin of the five-seeded pine, buried in the earth for a thousand years, transformed into a medicine capable of expelling the Three Corpses. He moved to Mount Hua and, with Daoist Li Daosheng and Luo Yin, formed the "Three Friends of Mist and Vine," known to the world as the Three Eminent Scholars.

In the Tang dynasty, Li Bai excelled in strategic arts and swordsmanship, embracing the life of a knight-errant. He valued generosity and disdained wealth, sojourning in Rencheng, where he joined the local scholars Kong Chaofu, Han Zhun, Pei Zheng, Zhang Shuming, and Tao Mian in residing on Mount Zulai. There, they drank deeply and sang wildly each day, earning the name "Six Recluses of Bamboo Stream."

**Wang Tan cast off his hairpin, Wei Jie returned his sash.**
*New Records of the Cinnabar Terrace*: Wang Tan, styled Yangbo, was a native of Taiyuan. His spirit shone with exceptional brilliance, his inner workings profound and subtle. His numinous insight was all-encompassing, his essence pure and luminous, his radiance ascending the celestial paths, his sincerity congealing before the jade steps. Thus, he dwelled in the jade forest’s emerald shade, where the immortal waves stilled. Truly, he could lift his head to pluck the finest blossoms and crane his neck to enter the realm of purity.

Initially, during Empress Lü’s regency, he held the position of Palace Attendant. When Emperor Wen ascended the throne, Wang cast off his hairpin at the phoenix gates. The Son of Heaven honored the worthy with Daoist rites and called him a "man of leisure."

*The Biographies of Divine Immortals*: Master Wei Jie, during Emperor Xiaozhuang of the Later Wei, served as Prefect of Yangxia. He studied under the Daoist Master Zhao Tong, then returned his official sash to the court and received the sacred texts of the Three Caverns, along with secret divine formulas. Later, he chose to reside on the southern slopes of Mount Hua, where people called him the "Master of Huayang."

**Yan Hall of Ze Min, the bedchamber of Du Chong.**
*Famous Discourses of the Hanlin Academy*: Zhang Zemin died during the Yuanfeng era but later revived. He recounted arriving at a place resembling a realm of jasper and jade, where a hall bore the inscription *Hall of Pure Feasting for the Five Ministers*. He asked the gatekeeper if he might enter, and the keeper replied: "This is where divine immortals gather. You may not enter." Zhang inquired about the names of the five ministers, and the keeper said: "Lü Yijian, Li Di, Liu Chong, Pang Ji, and Fu Bi."

Suddenly, a voice called from within, and a rider emerged, his appearance resembling that of Minister Kui. He summoned Zhang for a conversation. Zhang asked: "Have you now departed the world to become an immortal?" Liu replied: "I am now a local earth deity, akin to a prefect or magistrate among men. My merits and conduct are not yet sufficient—how dare I aspire to immortality? I was overseeing construction, which has just been completed. The windows, halls, and steps are all illuminated by the radiance of kings. I have already commissioned Wang Yuanzhi to compose a record, with Shao Biao providing the seal script."

Zhang asked: "Minister Fu is well—why did this hall appear before him?" Liu replied: "It has been three years now."

*The New Record of the Alchemical Terrace*:

Master Du Chong studied under Master Wen Shi for over a decade. Moved by his devotion, the Perfected One Zhan descended into his bedchamber and bestowed upon him an elixir formula, saying: "The Earth Bureau has reported your merits, and the Old Lord has dispatched me to transmit to you the scriptures of immortality." Chong reverently cultivated and refined the teachings, and his practice bore verification. He could command tigers and leopards, summon and employ spirits, penetrating the hidden and illuminating the obscure—his depths were unfathomable.

Gan Ji healed illnesses, but when his methods became excessive, he fell gravely ill.

*The Records of Jiangbiao*:

During the reign of Sun Ce of Wu, there was a Daoist named Gan Ji who studied Daoist texts and crafted talismanic water to heal the sick. The people of Wu and Kuaiji held him in high regard. Once, when Sun Ce was hosting guests at the commandery tower, Gan Ji hurried through the gate below. Two-thirds of the generals and guests descended the stairs to bow to him, and though they tried to stop them, they could not. Sun Ce immediately had him arrested. His mother said: "Master Gan has aided the army with blessings and healed the generals and soldiers—you must not kill him." Sun Ce did not heed her. Soon after, Gan Ji died, and when they went to bury him, his body was nowhere to be found.

*The Records of the True Realm*:

Master Zhu Junxu, styled Faman, resided at the Jade Purity Abbey. There, he untangled worldly entanglements, upheld the integrity of the Perfect Realization, and secluded himself in a quiet courtyard, lowering the curtains of his empty chamber. His bearing was grand and refined, his composure as serene as the radiance of heaven; his nature was profound and still, his reputation as resounding as thunder across the lands south of the Yangtze.

The original site of the Jade Purity Abbey in the gorge was cramped and bustling, while the ancient altar of Heavenly Pillar Peak lay in a distant, tranquil realm. Thus, he brushed off his robes and departed without lingering, staff in hand, journeying onward. Over the course of several years, though his undertakings remained unfinished, he suddenly fell mildly ill. One morning, he rose with resolve, ordered water for bathing, donned his cap and robes, lit incense, and sat upright. Addressing his disciples, he said: "For me, the span between Peng and Shang, life and death, has long been equal. Yet the subtleties of the Dao are ever still—those who are moved by it attain communion; the workings of the spirit are ever obscure—those who align with it achieve enlightenment. I have spoken of this, and you have understood. As for the scriptures, precepts, and practices, you must strive to uphold them." With these words, he passed away in an instant.

*Records of the Immortals of the Three Caverns*, Scroll Thirteen—concluded.
*Records of the Immortals of the Three Caverns*, Scroll Fourteen.

Chen Baoguang, Daoist of the Orthodox Unity lineage, compiled and arranged:

*Minor Realms of Clarity and Emptiness; Great Mao of Cold Blossom.*

From *The Divine Immortals of the Wang Clan*:
Wang Bao, styled Zideng, wore a deer-hide cap and hempen robe, wandering through renowned mountains. His refined devotion moved the vast heavens. One midnight, he suddenly heard the sounds of men and horses in the forest, the clamor of flutes and drums. In an instant, a thousand chariots and ten thousand riders emerged, floating in the void. A divine being, borne on a brush of tripled clouds and holding a tiger tally, halted his carriage and declared: "You are recorded in the Supreme Clarity’s register, your name inscribed in golden script upon the Scroll of Flourishing Blossoms. Enrolled in the Clarity and Emptiness, you ascend to the Minor Realms. You shall also oversee the treasury of sacred texts, assuming the office of Heavenly King."

From *Records of Mount Mao*:
Sun Hanhua was the granddaughter of the King of Wu. She cultivated the Dao upon Mount Mao, and upon its attainment, ascended in emptiness, leaving the mountain to be named Mount Hua Lao in her honor.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* states:
Hanhua was a disciple of the immortal Du Qi. Practicing the White-and-Primordial method, she attained youthful countenance. Now she dwells amidst the Great Mao peaks, occasionally appearing to those who encounter her.

*Sweet Dew of Wang Xi; Divine Glue of Tian Sheng.*

From *The Secret Records of the Immortals*:
The Perfected Wang Xi, during a great plague, entered Mount Xi to gather herbs, distributing them to save countless lives. One day, he encountered a Daoist who said: "You possess the bones of the transcendent and have accumulated vast virtue." The Daoist then taught him the arts of nurturing wind and drinking dew. One day, sweet dew descended upon the bamboo and trees beside his dwelling. Wang Xi drank it and ascended to heaven.

From *Supplement to the Biographies of Immortals*:
Tian Xian lived in seclusion at Poting. In his youth, he taught at a village school. The daughter of Prefect Qi of Rao, married to a man named Li, conceived within months. Li departed for the imperial examinations in Chang’an. As his wife was about to give birth, she was harmed by a ghost and died. Upon failing the exams, Li returned to Rao. In the wilderness, he encountered his wife, who recounted her fate and said: "Go to Poting and beseech Master Tian—perhaps I may live again." Li followed her words and found the master at the village school, pleading for mercy. The master replied: "But her dwelling has already collapsed." He then emerged from the schoolhouse and called to the earthly officials, asking: "The prefect’s daughter, slain by a ghost in childbirth—her dwelling is ruined. What is to be done?" An official replied: "Only if her hun and po souls are reunited and bound with divine glue will she revive." The wife then lived, and the master vanished without a trace.

*Prophetic Verses of Xia Zhou; Characters of Zhong Shu.*

From *Biographies of Eminent Daoists*:
Li Xiazhou was a man of the Dao. During the Kaiyuan era, he was summoned into the imperial precincts and remained there for a long time. At the end of the Tianbao era, he vanished one day, leaving only prophetic verses on the wall of his dwelling concerning An Lushan’s usurpation and the emperor’s flight to Shu—none could decipher them at first, but all later proved true. The final stanza read:
*"In Yan’s market, all the people depart;
At Han Pass, the horses never return.
Should you meet the ghost beneath the mountain,
Tie a silken sash upon the jade ring."*

"In Yan’s market, all the people depart" refers to the rebellion raised with the troops of You and Ji. "At Han Pass, the horses never return" alludes to the defeat at Tong Pass, where the cavalry of Geshu Han did not come back. "Should you meet the ghost beneath the mountain" contains the character *gui* (ghost), pointing to Mawei Station. "Tie a silken sash upon the jade ring" refers to Lady Yang, whose childhood name was Jade Ring; at Mawei, Gao Lishi strangled her with a silk scarf. All his foresights were of this nature.

From *Pure Discourses from the Jade Flask*:
Guo Zhongshu was unrestrained and unruly, often clashing with others.

In the early years of the Jianlong reign, Nie Chongyi was appointed to the academy, a master of the Confucian tradition in the Central Plains. Once, when Prince Zhao of Han paid him a visit, Guo Zhongshu, drunk and reckless, mocked his surname with a verse:

*"When newly exalted, you’re but a pile of wealth,
Climbing the dragon, you’re deaf by stealth.
Though you have three ears,
Alas, you’re not wise—just hears."*

Chongyi retorted instantly, using the characters *zhong* (loyalty) and *shu* (forbearance) to turn the jest back on him:

*"Do not mock one with three ears,
Far better than harboring two hearts."*

Guo was deeply shamed, and this incident ultimately led to his downfall. Later, accused of slandering the court and misappropriating official goods, he was exiled to Dengzhou. He died en route, and his body was buried by the roadside. Days later, when his kin came to rebury him, they unearthed the grave only to find what seemed a cicada’s molted husk—hardly a thing of this world.

*"The Hollow King feigns death beyond the horse’s field,
A Daoist trick of shedding skin, a ghostly yield."*

There was a Daoist immortal named Wang Lao, whose age none knew. He claimed only the surname Wang, with no trace of his origins. He would appear to die in one place, only to reappear alive in another, often compared to a cicada’s molt. The people called him the Hollow King.

From *Records of Jianghuai’s Strange Folk*:
Sima Jiao, also known as Sima Yi, could walk a thousand *li* in a day with swift strides. His temper was fierce, and none dared approach him. He had the ability to feign death, his body swelling and rotting until it stank, only to revive moments later. Once, he stayed at an inn in Xuancheng, where he summoned the innkeeper to drink with him. After humiliating the man, he bound him in restraints. They grappled until Sima suddenly collapsed—dead, his body cold and pallid. The entire town gathered to gawk. The locals seized the innkeeper, intending to hand him over to the authorities. Evening fell, so they decided to wait until dawn. That night, they heard clamor from the inn: *"Master Sima is gone!"* Only then did they realize he had faked his death.

*"Niu scatters grain for the cuckoo’s call,
Guo strews beans to weave his thrall."*

From *Biographies of Eminent Daoists*:
The Daoist Niu Wenhou was sharp-witted and erudite, well-versed in ancient and modern learning, and often exhorted others to virtue. Each winter’s chill, he would scatter grain upon the earth so that birds and insects might have sustenance. His hidden merits and quiet kindness extended to all—great deeds for humanity, small acts for creatures. Through cultivating himself and accumulating virtue, his practice deepened over time.

From *Biographies of Fang and Wei*:
Guo Pu visited the home of Hu Mengkang, Prefect of Lujiang, intending to depart. Fond of the prefect’s maidservant but unable to obtain her, he took three *sheng* of small beans and scattered them around the house. The next morning, the master saw thousands of red-clad figures encircling his home. As he drew near, they vanished. Disturbed, he asked Guo to divine the cause. Guo said: *"Your household should not keep this maid. Sell her twenty *li* southeast of here, and do not haggle over the price—then this omen will cease."* The master complied. Guo secretly sent someone to buy the maid, then cast a talisman into the well. The thousands of red-clad figures appeared bound, one by one leaping into the well. The master rejoiced.

*"Wang’s flying memorials ascend to the throne,
Zhang’s swift petitions replace the sealed scroll."*

From *Biographies of Divine Immortals of the Wang Clan*:
During the turmoil of the Jin dynasty, Wang Zuan pitied the suffering of the people. Each night, he sent flying memorials to the Heavenly Court. Soon, the Supreme Lord descended from the void and said to him: *"Your compassion for the people moves me. I have heard your pleas."* He then commanded an attendant to bestow upon Wang the *Three-Five Fasting Precepts*, saying: *"Practice these diligently, and true immortality may be yours."*

From *Extensive Records*:
Zhang Zhi, a native of Daojiang in Pengzhou, encountered the Daoist Jiang Zhenbian, who taught him the art of commanding the Six Ding spirits. During the Dali era, when the Military Commissioner of Xichuan, Cui Bichang, had an urgent matter to memorialize, he dispatched a rider on horseback. Three days after the courier’s departure, Cui found the original draft still among his documents—the sealed memorial in the case was merely a rough copy. Realizing the courier could not be recalled, he was distraught, not knowing what to do. Learning of Zhang’s skills, he summoned him and explained the situation. Zhang said: *"This memorial can be replaced—no need for worry."* He lit a censer, placed the draft upon the rising incense smoke, and it vanished. Moments later, the sealed memorial fell before him. When the courier returned, he was questioned but knew nothing of the change. The memorial was presented with its seal intact, and Cui was deeply astonished.

*"Shihuang’s dragon needles heal,
Cui Wei’s serpent moxa seals."*

From *Biographies of the Immortals*:
Ma Shihuang was the Yellow Emperor’s equine physician, skilled in diagnosing life and death in horses and curing them without fail. One day, a dragon descended before him, ears drooping and mouth agape. Shihuang said: *"This dragon is ill—it knows I can heal it."* He took his needles and pierced its lips. The dragon then departed. Years later, dragons repeatedly appeared to carry Shihuang away.

From *Extensive Records*:
During the Zhengyuan era, Cui Wei encountered an old woman who called herself Bao Gu. She gave him a small bundle of mugwort, saying: *"For warts and tumors, one moxa treatment suffices."* Then she vanished, leaving Cui perplexed. One evening, he fell into a dry well with no means of escape. Nearby, a white serpent, several *zhang* long, coiled in the shadows. Cui noticed a wart on its lip. A wildfire’s embers drifted into the well, and Cui, recalling Bao Gu’s words, used the fire to moxa the wart. It fell away at his touch. Realizing the serpent was a dragon, he mounted its back. Its body glowed like a torch, ascending into a cave where a blue-robed youth greeted him: *"I am the Jade Capital Lad. I have brought Master Cui here."* Cui asked: *"Who is Bao Gu?"* The youth replied: *"She is Bao Gu, daughter of Bao Guang, wife of Ge Hong. She often practices moxibustion in Nanhai."* Cui, astonished, then asked: *"Who is the Jade Capital Lad?"* The youth said: *"An Qisheng often rides this dragon to the Jade Capital—hence the name."*

*"He begged for Mirror Lake’s grace,
Ge sought the Grotto of Sentences’ embrace."*

From *Biographies of Eminent Daoists*:
He Zhizhang, styled Jiju, was a native of Yongxing in Yuezhou. His nature was open and unconstrained...

At first, he passed the imperial examination and was repeatedly promoted, eventually appointed as Guest of the Emperor and granted the title of Secretariat Director. In his later years, his conduct grew ever more unrestrained and eccentric. At the beginning of the Tianbao era, he fell ill and dreamed of wandering the imperial palace. After several days, he awoke and petitioned to become a Daoist and return to his hometown. The imperial edict permitted his residence to be converted into the Thousand Autumns Abbey. He further requested that an official lake be designated as a release pond for living creatures, and the edict granted him a bend of Mirror Lake in Kechuan. As he departed, the Emperor bestowed a poem to honor his journey, and people likened him to the Two Shu.

From *Biographies of Divine Immortals*:
Ge Hong, styled Zhichuan, originally bore the surname Zhuge. His distant ancestor, having campaigned in the Jiang-Han region, halted at Jurong in Danyang and sighed: *"I alone remain here—what ‘Zhu’ is there?"* Thus, he removed the *Zhu* from his name. The rise of the Ge surname began from this. Ge Hong exhaustively studied classical texts, with a particular fondness for the arts of divine transcendence. His relatives and friends recommended him for his talent and bearing, deeming him fit to oversee the national histories, and he was selected as Gentleman Cavalier Attendant. Ge Hong firmly declined the appointment, citing his advanced age and desire to compound the elixir. Hearing that Jiaozhi produced cinnabar, he sought the post of magistrate of Julou and set out with his nephews and sons.

Lord Gong fended off immortal wine,
His method preceded the divine lamp.

From *Gleanings of the Forgotten*:
Gong Fang of the Tang was a native of Xingyuan Prefecture. In Xingyuan stood Dou Mountain Abbey. From the plain rose a solitary peak, its four faces sheer cliffs, its summit flat as the bottom of a dipper—hence its name. Clad in vines and towering pines, its scenery was wondrous. Once, Gong Fang drank the immortal wine of Li Babai, and his entire household ascended to the heavens from their dwelling. Thus, later generations inscribed these lines:

*With rosy cups raised, drunk and blissful,
Unknowing, the whole household dwelt in crimson skies.
Only the homestead recalled the chickens and dogs left behind—
Who would believe the path to heaven so distant?*

*From the vast Three Clarities, worldly dreams cast aside,
The Eight Radiant Clouds attended the early court.
Only the old grove’s verdant trees remain,
Dew-laden mists sealing the startled autumn wind.*

Today, the ruins of the foundation still stand.

**The New Record of the Elixir Terrace**

Peng Zong, styled Faxian, once studied under the Perfected Master Du Chong. He dwelled in the essence of the Dao, his spirit refined and penetrating. One night as he performed his rituals, several divine lamps floated in the air above his mat, their radiance steady and luminous—all who witnessed it marveled.

He altered his surname and concealed his name to prolong his years.

**The Inner Biography of Master Ma**

Ma Mingsheng, originally surnamed He and styled Junxian, served in his youth as a county clerk capturing bandits. Once, he was wounded by outlaws and nearly died. Suddenly, by the roadside, he saw a woman of sixteen or seventeen, her clothing exquisite and her beauty unmatched. As she passed, Mingsheng knew she was a divine immortal. He kowtowed and begged for healing. The woman gave him a pill the size of a small bean, and upon taking it, he recovered at once. Mingsheng then abandoned his post, changed his name, and followed the goddess to Mount Tai. Recognizing his potential, she had Anqisheng teach him the formula for the Golden Liquid Elixir. Later, Mingsheng ingested the elixir and ascended to heaven.

**Comprehensive Records of the Strange**

When Fan Zhongyan was stationed in Chenzhou, the mother of the prefect fell ill. A Daoist was summoned to present a memorial to the celestial court. Fan laughed and said, "How could a common man deliver a petition to the Emperor on High?" The Daoist held his tablet and prostrated before the altar, remaining motionless all night. When someone touched him, he was stiff as a corpse. At the fifth watch, his limbs stirred slightly. After a long while, he said to the prefect, "Your mother’s lifespan extends six more years. Do not worry over her suffering." The prefect asked, "Why did tonight’s memorial take so long?" The Daoist replied, "Just as I was leaving the Heavenly Gate, I encountered the announcement of next spring’s imperial examination candidates. The onlookers lined the road, causing a delay." Fan asked, "What is the top candidate’s surname?" The Daoist said, "It is Wang. The second character of his given name is smudged with ink, and a note beside it is too faint to read." Later, the prefect’s mother recovered. The following spring, the top candidate was indeed Wang Gongshou, whose name was altered by imperial brush to Gongchen. Only then did Fan believe in the Daoist’s divine communion.

**The Hound of Guigu and the Goose Scripture of Shanyin**

**Supplement to the Biographies of Immortals**

Guigu was a man of the Jin during the reign of Duke Ping. He dwelled in seclusion at Guigu in Songyang and took it as his sobriquet. The master’s surname was Wang and his given name Hua. Su Qin and Zhang Yi studied the arts of horizontal and vertical alliances under him, their cunning and schemes vying for dominance, leaving no room to be transformed by the supreme Dao. As they prepared to depart, the master gave each a single shoe, which transformed into a hound to guide them. By that very day, they arrived in Qin. The master lived among mortals for several centuries before vanishing without a trace.

**Annotations to the Declarations of the Perfected**

Wang Xizhi was the son of Wang Kuang, elder brother of Wang Liao. He possessed an elegant demeanor and excelled in calligraphy. Later, he served as Prefect of Kuaiji. In the eleventh year of the Yonghe era, he left office and never returned to officialdom. He had previously associated with Master Xu and held a deep admiration for the Dao.

**The Jin History** also records: Xizhi loved geese. At the time, a Daoist in Shanyin was fond of raising geese. Xizhi went to observe them and was greatly pleased. He sought to purchase them, but the Daoist said, "Write the *Yellow Court Scripture* for me, and I will present the entire flock as a gift." Xizhi happily complied, completing the writing before carrying the geese home in a basket.

**Pine Seeds of Ban Quan and Lingzhi of Yong Du**

**Biographies of the Immortals**

Ban Quan was a gatherer of herbs on Mount Huai. He favored eating pine seeds, and his body grew fur. He could run as swiftly as if flying, and he lived for three hundred years. Liu Xiang composed an ode for him:

*Ban Quan feeds on pine seeds,
His form at ease, his gaze serene.
His feet tread upon simurghs and phoenixes,
His strides outpace galloping steeds.*

**Supplement to the Biographies of Immortals**

Ge Yongdu often resided on Mount Shangqing in Shu, aspiring to transcend the mortal realm. Beneath a towering pine, a nighttime glow appeared, and he obtained lingzhi shaped like a human figure. After refining and consuming it, he gained the ability to fly and transform. Once his elixir was complete and he ingested it, he ascended to heaven in broad daylight. Thus, Mount Shangqing came to be called Ge’s Grave Mountain.

**The Celestial Master Subdues Demons; The Perfected Lord Conquers the Ox**

**Biographies of Eminent Daoists**

The Celestial Master Zhang Daoling once battled demons in Chengdu, seizing twenty-four hells and transforming them into courts of blessing. He subdued the twenty-eight constellations to channel the vital qi. At the time, a demon chieftain still resided beneath Mount Qingcheng, where humans and ghosts conducted trade—a place called the Ghost Market. When the Celestial Master arrived, the chieftain transformed into a jeweled throne for him to occupy. Blades could not pierce it, nor could fire burn it. Thus, the demon chieftain submitted.

**Records of the Western Mountains**

When the Perfected Lord Xu Xun returned to Yuzhang, he surveyed the city. Suddenly, a handsome youth who called himself Shen paid a visit. His manners were refined, his speech eloquent. Xu wished to detain him briefly, but the youth abruptly departed. The Perfected Lord said to his disciples, "This is no mortal—it is a clam spirit. Could it have heard of my intent to eliminate harm and come to test me? If I do not slay it, it will surely become a great calamity." He ascended a height to observe its destination, where it transformed into a yellow ox playing on the sands of Longsha. The Perfected Lord then cut paper into a black ox and sent it to fight. He instructed his disciple Shi Cen to take a sword to the scene, cautioning him, "Wait until the oxen are locked in fierce combat, then strike the yellow one." Shi followed the command, severing its left thigh. The yellow ox fled into the well of Hengquan outside the city’s west gate, while the black ox reverted to paper.

**The Pipa Bestowed by Wang; Ji Xian Ascends the Onion Peak**

**Secret Leisurely Conversations**

Wang Baoyi served as Military Controller for Gao Congsi of Jingnan. His daughter refused to eat meat or drink blood, and at the age of five, she could recite the *Yellow Court Scripture*. As she grew, she dreamed of climbing a mountain beside flowing water, where she saw palaces of gold and silver. A voice said it was Mount Fangzhang. Dozens of female immortals appeared and declared, "The Maiden Ma has arranged for us to be sisters." They taught her several pipa tunes. Thereafter, she encountered them every few nights. Over a year, she learned over a hundred pieces, the most exquisite being *Solitary Finger Shang*, played with a single finger. Later, the Maiden Ma appeared in a dream and said, "I shall soon summon you." The next day, clouds and cranes with celestial music filled the courtyard, and she passed away suddenly.

*The Lives of Eminent Daoists*: Daoist Xuan Yuanji dwelled on Mount Luofu. Emperor Xuanzong once summoned him to the palace. At the time, the capital had never seen *jing sou* or lychee blossoms. The emperor mentioned this, and in an instant, both flowers appeared—branches and leaves lush and fragrant, as if meticulously arranged.

Daoyuan was skilled in astronomical calculations and memorials written in empty reverence. The original biography states: Qiu Jun, styled Daoyuan, was versed in the numerology of yin-yang and the Supreme Ultimate. In his early years, he wished to visit the Huayang Grotto-Heaven and sought the post of magistrate of Jurong. Upon completing his term, he sent a poem to his Daoist friends on Mount Mao:

*The phoenix’s call invites me to behold your radiant virtue,*
*Yet pine and vine now part from my heart.*
*Moonlight bathes the solitary peak of ten thousand fathoms,*
*While in the ancient halls, no one returns.*

*I long to aid the sagely Tang and Yu in unfolding the Dao,*
*Yet deeply ashamed before Mao and Xu’s counsel to forget worldly schemes.*
*Tomorrow, my light sail departs once more,*
*While purple mushrooms year by year grow fat in vain.*

Jun once told his family, "My lifespan will end at eighty-one." One morning, after rising and washing, he asked for brush and ink and composed a poem on spring grasses. Upon finishing, he sat upright and passed away. That year, he was eighty-one. When his coffin was opened, it was empty—his followers declared it a *shijie* (release by means of a simulated corpse). Chi Shouguang, the prefect, and Minister Teng Gongfu recorded the event. He was buried on Mount Jiuhua. Years later, a yellow-robed messenger arrived in haste at Diyang, bearing a letter from Jun. When his family opened it, the messenger vanished without a trace. The letter read: "I was originally listed among the immortals but was demoted to govern Mount Tai due to errors in my astronomical calculations."

*The Record of Unique Marvels*: During the Zhengyuan era of the Tang, Wang Qiong, magistrate of Danyang, failed his triennial evaluations three times in a row. Deeply distressed, he visited the Daoist Ye Xuzhong on Mount Mao to request a memorial to inquire about his fortune. Xuzhong presented the memorial, which rose with the incense smoke and vanished into the distance. After a meal’s time, it fell back to the ground, its end inscribed in vermilion: *"Having taken a hundred taels of gold, you forfeit three years of official salary. Having wrongfully killed two men, you will face judgment after death."* A year later, Qiong died suddenly without illness.

*Wine may be poured for friends,*
*While fragrant chestnuts are offered in ritual.*

*The Continued Records of Immortals*: Wang Kejiao, a native of Huating in Suzhou Prefecture, made his living by farming and fishing. One day, as he poled his boat along the Song River, he suddenly saw a painted pleasure barge adorned with colorful patterns. Aboard were seven Daoists who called out Kejiao’s name. Startled, Kejiao was led by an attendant with bristling sideburns onto the red deck. Before each of the seven stood a vessel of green jade filled with wine, and the fruits on board gleamed with crystalline clarity. One of them said, "What fine bone structure—yet born into lowly poverty, much of your destiny has already been shattered." Another said, "Give him wine to drink." The attendant poured wine into a goblet, but no matter how many times he tipped the vessel, not a drop emerged. Then one said, "Give him chestnuts." The chestnuts glowed with a greenish light like dates, each about two inches long, their flesh crisp and sweet. A yellow-robed attendant was ordered to escort Kejiao away. As he closed his eyes, he suddenly found himself seated before the Waterfall Temple on Mount Tiantai—half a year had passed since he left home.

*The Seven Signatures of Yunyou*: Yin Wenxiang, styled Daoquan and self-styled Seven-Seven, once attended an official banquet. One day, a performer among the entertainers mocked him. Seven-Seven said to the host, "Shall we make a game of two chestnuts?" The guests all agreed. He passed the chestnuts around, and as each person sniffed them, an extraordinary fragrance filled the air. Only the two who had ridiculed Seven-Seven found their noses stuck fast to the chestnuts, which had turned to stone. They tugged in vain, crying out, "The stench is unbearable!" The two leapt up and began to dance wildly, their hairpins scattering to the ground as they wept in succession, their rouge and powder streaming down. The troupe of performers, swept up in the frenzy, danced in unison, their drumbeats and music falling into perfect rhythm. The song ended, yet the dance continued, and all at the banquet collapsed in helpless laughter. The host pleaded for mercy, and after a while, the stone fell from their noses, and their hairpins, rouge, and powder were restored as before.

The maiden Hua’e, the Perfected Lady of the Primordial.
*The Record of Forgotten Tales*: During the reign of King Zhao of Yan, the kingdom of Guangyan presented two maidens as tribute, one named Xuanbo and the other Zhuoying. The king was devoted to the Dao of immortals, and so the two maidens descended in mortal guise. Their jade-like skin was flawless, their bodies light, and their qi fragrant. The king housed them in chambers of sheer silk and fed them the ointment of *minying*. Both were skilled in dance; wherever they moved, a fragrant breeze arose, and as they whirled and circled, they seemed scarcely able to support themselves. The king brushed them with his tassel, and they danced with bewitching grace, their movements as delicate as phoenixes in flight. When he waved his sleeve, they ceased. Recognizing their divine nature, the king lodged them in the Terrace of Lofty Clouds, where he laid out mats woven with qilin patterns and burned the incense of *tudu*, imported from the kingdom of Boyi.

*The Unofficial History*: On the night of the Mid-Autumn Festival in the early Tianbao era, Luo Gongyuan said to the emperor, "Would Your Majesty accompany me on a journey to the moon?" He took a cassia branch and cast it into the air, where it transformed into a great bridge gleaming like white gold. The emperor walked upon it to the lunar palace, where hundreds of female immortals in plain white robes danced gracefully in the vast courtyard. When he asked what melody they performed, they replied, "The *Rainbow Skirt and Feathered Robe*." Again, in the fourth year of Tianbao, the Perfected Lady Yang was formally invested as the Noble Consort, and thereafter, each day, the *Rainbow Skirt and Feathered Robe* was performed. As the poet Liu Yuxi wrote:
*"The Emperor of Kaiyuan had all he desired,*
*Yet regretted only the fleeting light of those days.*
*Gazing from the Three Hamlets toward the mountain of immortals,*
*He returned to compose the* Rainbow Skirt and Feathered Robe.
*His heart thereafter dwelt by the Jasper Pool,*
*Following the Three Clarities and Eight Sceneries.*
*Suddenly he rode the white clouds to heaven,*
*Leaving only autumn wind and verse behind in the world."*

The Crown Prince drank the elixir; the Long-Hu presented frost.
*The New Record of the Alchemical Terrace*: The monks Shi Daowei and Zhu Falan asked Master Ge the Immortal, "The Dao arises without before or after—it cannot be spoken of or discussed. Yet we wonder: between Laozi and the Crown Prince, who came first?" The master replied, "Profound is your question, for it accords with the true and correct. The Dao is fundamentally without before or after, without left or right, without existence or extinction, without high or low, without noble or base. It is formless and imageless, and so we name it the Dao—it cannot be expressed in words. The Crown Prince was born after Laozi, but in a former life, he accumulated merit and virtue, and so he was granted a cup of the Supreme One’s golden liquid. His body became like a radiant cluster of purple gold, and thus he was called the Golden Immortal."

*The Declarations of the Perfected*: The Great King of the Northern Rong, the Long-Hu, presented Emperor Shun with the frost of white langgan and the ten-times-refined purple essence. Whoever consumed them would attain long life and ascend as an immortal, becoming coeval with heaven and earth. Shun took them, and soon after, he died and was buried in the wilds of Cangwu. For though the dragon played the *Ling’a* and the phoenix drummed the *Yunchi*, he still underwent corpse-liberation and feigned death, wishing to sever the bonds of life and death and show the people that all things have a beginning and an end.

The Recluse Immortal of White Stone; Lu’s dream of yellow millet.
*The Biographies of Divine Immortals*: The Master of White Stone refused to cultivate the methods of ascending to immortality, seeking only not to die while still enjoying the pleasures of the human world. Peng Zu asked him why, and he replied, "In heaven, one must serve many exalted beings—it is more burdensome than life among men." Thus, he was called the Recluse Immortal.

*The Record in the Pillow*: During the Kaiyuan era, the Daoist Master Lü passed through Dengye on his travels. At an inn, he encountered a young man from the prefecture, Lu Sheng, who was also staying there. The innkeeper was steaming yellow millet, and they waited together for it to cook. Lu sighed deeply, and the old man asked why. Lu recounted his hardships in life. The master took a pillow from his bag and gave it to Lu, saying, "Pillow this, and you shall attain glory and contentment as you wish." Lu rested his head upon it and dreamed he entered the pillow’s hollow. There, he soon passed the imperial examinations, rose through the ranks of the court, and served as a general and minister for fifty years. His sons and grandsons all held high office, and his prosperity was unmatched. He submitted a memorial: *"I am over eighty, having served in the Three Ducal Offices. Though I have done little to repay Your Majesty’s profound grace, I now take eternal leave of this sacred age."* That very night, he died. Lu Sheng stretched and awoke to find Master Lü beside him—the yellow millet was still not done.

Lu bowed in gratitude and said, "This is how you, Master, have curbed my desires—how dare I not heed your teaching?" He prostrated himself twice and departed.

Lord Lu achieved liberation through *shijie*, while Yan Hui attained *zuowang*.

*Records of the Strange* states: When Yan Zhenqing was sent to punish Li Xilie, all within and without knew he would not return. His kin and clan saw him off at Changle Slope, where, drunk, he leapt and danced before the front pillar, saying, "Long ago, I met the Daoist Tao Baba, who bestowed upon me the *Bixia Elixir* of the Jade Blade. To this day, I have not declined." He added, "At seventy, misfortune turns to fortune. One day, await me on Mount Luofu—could this not be today’s trial?" When he reached Tai Liang, Xilie ordered him strangled, and he was buried south of the city. After Xilie’s defeat, his family opened the coffin to find his countenance as in life, his body suffused with golden light, his nails grown beyond his hands, and his beard and hair several feet long. He was reburied on the northern mountain of Yanshi. Later, a merchant arrived at Mount Luofu, where two Daoists were playing *weiqi*. One asked, "Who has come here?" The merchant replied, "A humble traveler from Luoyang."

The Daoist smiled and said, "Fortunately, entrust me with a letter to deliver to my family." He sent a boy to fetch paper and brush, then wrote a letter. When it reached the Yan family on the northern mountain, his descendants received the letter and exclaimed in astonishment, "This is the handwriting of our late Grand Preceptor!" They opened the tomb and coffin, only to find it empty.

*The New Record of the Elixir Terrace* states: Yan Hui served as Attendant Gentleman of Bright Spring and Director of the Three Heavens. He once said to Confucius, "I have improved—now I sit in forgetfulness." Confucius, startled, asked, "What do you mean by 'sitting in forgetfulness'?" Yan Hui replied, "I discard my limbs and body, cast off perception and intellect, detach from form and abandon knowledge, merging with the Great Thoroughfare—this is what I call 'sitting in forgetfulness.'" Confucius said, "To merge is to be without preferences; to transform is to be without constancy. Truly, you are worthy! I, Qiu, would follow after you." (See *Zhuangzi*.)

Zhao Gao concealed a sparrow; Tao Dan raised deer.
*The Record of Forgotten Tales* recounts: King Ziying of Qin drowned Zhao Gao in a well, yet after seven days he did not die. They then boiled him in a cauldron, but after seven days the water still did not boil, so they executed him. A prison officer said, "When Gao was first imprisoned, he carried in his bosom a green orb the size of a sparrow’s egg." A Daoist master explained, "Gao’s ancestors had received the elixir method of Han Zhong. In winter, he could stand on solid ice; in summer, beside a furnace—yet felt neither cold nor heat." When Gao died, a green sparrow flew from his corpse into the clouds—a sign of the Ninefold Transmutation.

*The Lives of Jin Recluses* states: Tao Dan, styled Chujing, was the grandson of the Grand Commandant Tao Kan. Orphaned young, he favored the arts of nourishing life, believing immortality attainable. At fifteen or sixteen, he began practicing qi-absorption and grain avoidance, never marrying. Though his family possessed a thousand gold pieces and over a hundred servants, Tao Dan sat all day in solemn stillness, never concerning himself with worldly affairs. He enjoyed reading and was skilled in divination. He built a hut in Linxiang Mountain of Changsha, keeping a white deer as his companion. When visitors sought him, he would cross streams to evade them, so none could approach. When the prefecture nominated him as a Filial and Incorrupt candidate, Tao Dan fled to Mount Pi in Luoxian County, never returning, and none knew his end.

The Queen Mother’s Jasper Pool; the Old Lord’s Jade Table.
*The Liezi* records: King Mu of Zhou ascended the Kunlun peaks to view the palace of the Yellow Emperor, then sealed it to bequeath to posterity. He was received as a guest by the Queen Mother of the West, who feasted him by the Jasper Pool. *The Mu Tianzi Chronicle* adds: On an auspicious *jiǎzǐ* day, the Son of Heaven presented a white jade tablet and a dark jade disk to the Queen Mother, offering a hundred bolts of brocade and three hundred of white silk. The Queen Mother bowed and accepted them, then feasted him by the Jasper Pool. She sang: "White clouds drift in the sky, mountains rise of themselves. The Way stretches far—ask the rivers and hills. May you not die; come again!" The Son of Heaven replied, "When I return to the eastern lands, I shall harmonize and govern the Central States, equalize the myriad people, and then I shall come to see you again."

*The Biographies of Heavenly Masters* states: In the first year of Yongshou, the Old Lord descended upon the capital of Shu. The earth spirit caused a jade table to emerge. The Old Lord ascended it and bestowed upon the Heavenly Master the esoteric scriptures of the Northern and Southern Dippers, commanding him to universally save all beings and succor the common people.

Liang’s wife changed her attire; Yuan’s daughter altered her garments.
*The Biographies of Han Recluses* records: Liang Hong, a man of the same county as Meng, had a daughter who was ugly and dark, strong enough to lift a stone mortar, and refused all marriage proposals. At thirty, her parents asked why. She replied, "I wish for a husband as worthy as Liang Boluan." When Liang Hong heard this, he betrothed her. Upon marrying, she first entered his home adorned in finery. Liang Hong said, "I desire a companion in coarse cloth to dwell with me in the deep mountains. Now you wear silks and powders—is this what I wished?" His wife replied, "I only wished to observe your resolve. I have my own garments for reclusion." She then bound her hair in a simple knot, donned plain cloth, and approached him at work. Liang Hong rejoiced, "This is truly the wife of Liang Hong! She can serve me well." Later, they entered the mountains of Baling together.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* states: Zhang Jizi, also named Fa in his youth, was deceived by Yuan Po’s lofty conduct and married to his daughter. The daughter wore lavish attire, but Jizi paid her no heed. Only after she changed her garments did they become husband and wife. They entered Mount Shi, where they met the Lord of the Mountain Diagram—a minister of King Xiang of Zhou and an immortal—who taught Jizi the method of the Nine Clouds’ Water and the Strong Pillars. Through cultivation, Jizi attained this Dao.

Ziyun nurtured his spirit; Zhaosu tempered his desires.
*The Record of True Realms* states: The Heavenly Master Xiahou, named Ziyun, personally planted orchids and irises in his medicinal garden. He said, "The sages of antiquity used superior medicines to nurture the spirit, middle-grade medicines to cultivate nature, and lower-grade medicines to dispel illness. These can make one’s spirit efficacious, one’s nature luminous, and one’s illnesses vanish." Thus, he often distributed medicines in the market, adapting to each person’s depth of encounter. His poem on the medicinal garden reads: "Green leaves and red blossoms spread wide; the immortal classics I study alone. By chance, I transplant chrysanthemums from Kunlun’s slopes, hoeing through the roots of white clouds."

The day the master ascended, a woodcutter peering into the garden saw him whip a beast—tiger-like yet not a tiger, unrecognizable—into the eastern mountains, swift as the wind.

*The Pure Words from the Jade Flask* records: Wang Zhaosu of Suanzao County was learned in antiquity, pure and upright, his conduct towering above the world. When purchasing goods, he paid the exact price asked. Recommended by Li Mu to Emperor Taizong, he was summoned to the imperial chambers. At seventy-seven, his complexion was like cinnabar, his eyes like polished lacquer. He had lived as a widower, free from desire, for forty years, with no female attendants in his household. Appointed Professor of the Imperial University, he remained in the palace for over a month. When the emperor inquired about governing the world and nurturing life, Zhaosu replied, "To govern the world, nothing surpasses loving the people; to nurture life, nothing surpasses tempering desire—beyond this, there is nothing." The emperor cherished his words and inscribed them on screens and tables. He passed away at eighty-nine.

The claws of Magu were like a crow’s; the body of Fuxi was like a serpent’s.

*The Records of Immortal Assemblies* states: Magu, during the reign of Emperor Xiaoming, descended with the immortal Wang Yuan to the home of Cai Jing. As they approached, the sound of flutes, drums, and horses filled the air in an instant. Magu appeared to be eighteen or nineteen, her hair coiled atop her head with the rest cascading to her waist. Her garments bore patterns unlike any in the world, neither brocade nor silk. Her claws resembled those of a crow, and when Cai Jing saw them, he secretly thought, *"If my back ever aches, it would be wonderful to have such claws to scratch it."* Wang Yuan, already aware of Cai Jing’s thoughts, had him seized and whipped.

*The Records of Forgotten Tales* recounts: Yu carved through the Dragon Gate Mountains, also called the Dragon Gate. Reaching a hollow cavern, its depths stretched for tens of *li*, so dark that progress was impossible. Yu carried fire and pressed onward, encountering a beast like a boar holding a night-shining pearl whose glow was like a torch. A black dog barked ahead of him. After what seemed ten *li*, Yu lost all sense of day and night. When he awoke, the light gradually returned, and the boar and dog had transformed into human forms. Then he saw a divine being with the body of a serpent and the face of a man. Yu spoke to him, and the deity revealed to him the diagram of the Eight Trigrams, arrayed upon a golden plate, with eight spirits attending at his side. Yu said, *"Was it Huaxu who bore you, the sage child?"* The deity replied, *"Huaxu was the goddess of the Nine Rivers who gave birth to me."* He then drew forth a jade tablet, twelve inches long, corresponding to the twelve hours, and entrusted it to Yu to measure heaven and earth. Yu wielded this tablet to regulate the waters and stabilize the land. The serpent-bodied deity was none other than Fuxi.

Cui Chengzi refined the Five Stones; Ge Qi lifted a thousand *jin*.

*The Supplement to the Biographies of Immortals* tells of Cui Chengzi, who cultivated the Way of the Five Stones’ ethereal essence. For ten years, he ingested it in a grotto chamber on Mount Pei, then departed in spirit, concealing his form within the cave. A traveler later saw his remains, yet his bones had not scattered, and a surpassing fragrance lingered. Upon closer inspection, five objects, white as chicken eggs, were hidden there. The traveler took them and left, but suddenly, a tremor of fear struck him—he had lost the Five Stones. The next day, he returned to the cave and found Chengzi seated within, his beard bristling as he declared: *"The Dao is attained through accumulating merit and piling up deeds, extending virtue to all beings. Only when the Earth Bureau recommends you and your name is inscribed in the Emperor Lord’s register can the divine elixir be completed and a true master encountered. You possess not a shred of goodness, yet harbor the intent to steal my ethereal essence and divine herbs, seeking transcendence—is this not difficult?"* Though the matter was precious to the gods, Chengzi, moved by compassion, chose not to punish him, fearing the mountain spirits would not forgive. *"You must leave at once,"* he said. Soon after, the traveler contracted a grave illness. A year later, Chengzi descended to his home at night and taught him the method of washing the heart and repenting his transgressions. His illness was cured.

*The Master Who Embraces Simplicity* records: Ge Qi’s strength could lift a thousand *jin*, and above his head rose a five-colored *qi*, over a *zhang* high. One day, he ascended on a dragon.

"Self-scooping water," as Liu Zhengxing said.

*The Idle Tales of Rustics* recounts: In Lizhou’s commandery, there was a man with unbound hair and bare feet, clad in a short hemp robe. When spoken to, his replies, though unconstrained, often concerned matters of heaven, which most could not comprehend. At night, he would stand and sleep in the spirit temple. In the height of summer or the depths of winter, none knew his whereabouts. The people called him "Heaven’s Self-So." Outside the southern gate of the commandery lay a market, a bustling hub of commerce. One evening, a fire broke out at dawn, burning the houses to the ground. The city’s inhabitants climbed to high places to watch, but Heaven’s Self-So sighed and muttered to himself: *"The people of this region indulge in extravagance and debauchery, knowing nothing of the good Way. Having committed evil for so long, Heaven is about to destroy them."* He then scooped water from a stone basin before the temple and cast it into the air. Soon, a black mist emerged from the temple gate, transforming into a great rain that extinguished the fire. Heaven’s Self-So then vanished without a trace.

*The Biographies of Divine Immortals* tells of Liu Zheng, who possessed the Way of defense. He could make one man act as a thousand, and a thousand as ten thousand. He could also exhale water to summon clouds, gather earth to form mountains, and pierce the ground to create abysses.

Li Lian, the Idle Guest; Guimeng, the Scattered Man.

*The Preface to the *Chart of Pointing to the Origin*** states: Li Lian called himself the "Idle Guest of the Three Immortals’ Gate" and the "Celestial Idler Under Heaven." He once wrote in the *Preface to Pointing to the Origin*: *"To knock upon the gate of mystery, one must rely on a master’s hand. Without encountering a true immortal, the great Dao is difficult to comprehend."* While traveling in Jiangnan, in Nanjing’s Yingtian Prefecture, he met the Hua Yin True Man, Shi Jianwu Xisheng, who wore a green turban, purple shoes, a black belt, and flowing robes. His radiance dazzled the eye, and his presence was awe-inspiring. Yet in conversation, he expounded the heavenly mysteries, elucidating the great Dao in terms both ancient and modern—something rarely seen. He then bestowed upon Li Lian the *Chart of Cultivating the True Origin*, comprising fifteen diagrams, clear and luminous. It could truly be called the secret scripture of a true immortal.

*The Original Biography* records: In the Tang dynasty, Lu Guimeng was lofty and unrestrained in his youth. Though he sat for the imperial examinations, he did not pass even once. He dwelled in Fuli of Songjiang, following Zhang Bo, and disliked associating with common companions. Even when they came to his door, he refused to see them. He did not ride horses but traveled by boat, with a canopy of reeds, carrying bundles of books, a tea stove, a brush rest, and fishing gear. Thus, he was known as the "Scattered Man of Rivers and Lakes."

*Records of the Immortals of the Three Caverns*, Scroll Fourteen
*Records of the Immortals of the Three Caverns*, Scroll Fifteen
Compiled by Chen Baoguang, a Daoist of the Orthodox Unity Tradition.

The River Master’s Dao was revered, and the Primordial Lady ranked highest among immortals.

*The Biographies of Divine Immortals* states: The River Master’s surname and given name are unknown. During the reign of Emperor Wen of Han, he built a thatched hut by the river and constantly recited the *Laozi*. Emperor Wen, who admired the words of *Laozi*, dispatched envoys to inquire about its unsolvable meanings. The Master said: "The Dao is revered, and virtue is noble—these cannot be questioned from afar." The Emperor then rode in his carriage to visit him, dismounted, and bowed deeply to ask about its profound mysteries. The Master replied: "Always take the hearts of the common people as your own heart."

*The Seven Lots from Cloudy Peaks* records: Ren Sheng secluded himself on Mount Song to study. One night, a yellow-robed figure arrived, holding a summons, and said: "Your life has ended." After traveling several dozen *li*, banners, insignia, and canopies stretched endlessly. A woman riding a kingfisher-green brush, attended by dozens of guards, appeared. The yellow-robed figure and Ren Sheng withdrew to hide by a wall. The woman, seeing them from afar, asked who they were. The yellow-robed one truthfully explained. The woman took the summons, examined it, and said: "Now that we have met, I cannot be without compassion." She took up a brush and wrote: "Granted three more years." Ren Sheng bowed in gratitude and asked the messenger who she was. The yellow-robed figure replied: "This is the Three Primordials’ Primordial Lady, the most exalted among immortals." Ren Sheng indeed revived, and three years later, he passed away.

Lin Bo’s house was pierced, and the purple clouds’ stone shattered.

*The Inner Biography of the Heavenly Master* states: Zhang Zixiang, styled Lin Bo, was broadly learned in the Confucian classics and served as a magistrate in Luoyang. He resigned his post and retired with his wife to Mount Longhu, dedicating himself to cultivation. He could expel the elixir from his abdomen, place it on his palm to admire, or toss it into a vessel, where its radiance pierced the house before he swallowed it again. He lived over a hundred years. After his death, when his coffin was buried, it was found to be empty.

*The Biographies of Divine Immortals* records: During the Five Dynasties, the Jiangnan Daoist Tan Zixiao possessed Daoist arts. He could perform rituals to summon stellar constellations, tread the *Yu Step* and *Kui Formation*, and bind and control ghosts and demons. He dwelled in the Secluded Grotto of Mount Lu. At that time, a monk from Ye named Yu Han was constructing a pavilion, but his work was obstructed by an unyielding boulder. Despite employing a hundredfold labor, they could not level it. The master arrived and said, "This is easily done." He pinched a mudra with his fingers, exhaled water upon it, and commanded the workers to strike. The stone shattered at once, and the ground was leveled in a single day.

*The Lady of the Moon steals the elixir; the Jiang River nymphs unfasten their pendants.*
*The Biographies of Divine Immortals* states: Yi possessed an elixir of immortality, but his wife secretly consumed it and fled to the moon, becoming Chang’e.
*The Collected Records of Immortals* recounts: Two daughters of the Jiang River nymphs were strolling along the riverbank, adorned in splendid attire and jeweled with ten *liang* of luminous pearls, each as large as a kingfisher’s egg. Zheng Jiaofu saw them, conversed with them, and begged for their pendants. They unfastened them and gave them to Jiaofu. He placed them in his robe and walked several dozen paces, but both the women and the pendants vanished.
*The Inner Transmission of the Han Odes* says: Long ago, Zheng Jiaofu was traveling to southern Chu. At the foot of the Han River’s high terrace, he encountered two women wearing two pearls, each as large as a sparrowhawk’s egg. Jiaofu said to them, "I desire your pendants." The two women unfastened them and gave them to him. After Jiaofu departed, the women suddenly disappeared, and the pendants were lost as well.

*Wu Yi spreads the feast; Zhu Rong convenes the assembly.*
*The Records of Mount Wu Yi* states: In the second year of Qin Shihuang’s reign, on the fifteenth day of the eighth month, the Lord of Wu Yi gathered over a thousand villagers on the summit of Slow Pavilion Peak. Men and women were seated on mats to the east and west, laid with crimson-cloud cushions and purple-haze quilts. They performed the *Left Immortal’s Song of Guest Clouds*, and as the wine circulated, the master of ceremonies, Peng Jinzhao, sang the *Song of Human-Divine Gathering and Parting*:
*"The meeting of heaven and earth is rare,
As the sun sets beyond the western hills, the evening birds return in flight.
A hundred years pass in an instant—our aspirations unfulfilled,
The heavenly palace so near, yet we cannot follow in sorrow."*
When the song ended, colorful clouds suddenly gathered on all sides. The villagers bid farewell to the immortals and descended the mountain. Looking back, not a single trace remained on the peak.
*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* records: Xue Jichang resided on Mount Heng, delving into the profound mysteries of the Dao. Emperor Minghuang once summoned him to the palace and questioned him on virtue and the Dao. His discourse was exquisitely subtle, and the emperor, delighted, bestowed upon him exceptional favor. One day, he suddenly told his disciples, "Tonight, the Heavenly Assembly convenes on Zhu Rong Peak. I have been summoned and must depart." He then ascended into the void and vanished.

*The white dog of Master Bin; the black boar of Wan You.*
*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* states: Du Guangting, styled Binsheng, once told his disciples, "Last night, I dreamed of ascending to the Celestial Court, where I was appointed overseer of the Min and Emei mountains. Now that the True Palace on Mount Qingcheng is complete, I fear my time in this world is short." Guangting had long kept a white dog named Chuiyun. He once ordered it to be fed sesame oil, its paws wrapped in silk, saying, "I have heard that oil on a dog’s paws allows it to run ten thousand *li* in a day." When the True Palace was finished, he donned his ritual robes, ascended the hall, gathered his disciples to bid them farewell, and passed away. A wondrous fragrance filled the room, and Chuiyun howled and died as well.
*The Extensive Records* recounts: Wan You cultivated the Dao in the uninhabited wilds of southern Qian. Every twenty or thirty years, he would emerge to sell medicine in the city. Wang Jian of Shu welcomed him into the palace and treated him with utmost reverence, inquiring about his diet. Wan You remained silent, saying, "I am no immortal, nor one who consumes elixirs. I merely empty my heart, nurture my *qi*, discard my faults, and examine my conduct." When asked his age, he replied, "I only recall the years of the Night King’s reign in Shu and the era when Can Cong ruled the capital. When summoned, I witnessed the sun and moon racing, flowers blooming and trees withering—I do not reckon the years by the *ganzhi* cycle." Later, he firmly declined to remain and returned to the mountains. Wang Jian wept and entreated him to stay, but Wan You refused to speak of the future. After his departure, words were found inscribed on the wall of his dwelling:
*"Do not let the green boar’s hooves be stirred,
For once disturbed, the raging flames cannot be quenched.
The startled beast must not bear yellow at both ends,
For when its color appears, Heaven will not weep."*
The wise could not fathom its meaning. In the year *yihai*, an army marched east to seize Qin and Feng. *Yihai* was the year of the "green boar," the time of calamitous fire. Three years later, in the year *wuyin*, the earth collapsed and Mount Jian was built. Only then was it understood that *yin* signified the "startled beast," and both the heavenly stem and earthly branch were of the element earth, which is yellow. Thus, the phrase "the startled beast bears yellow at both ends" was not a word amiss.

*Huan He tends the hearth; Liu Hao oversees the kitchen.*
*The Biographies of Divine Immortals* records: Song Huanhe, styled Qingyuan, served Tao Hongjing at the Huayang Lodge on Mount Mao for over a decade. His nature was upright and solemn, his demeanor serene as if devoid of purpose. One day, two azure lads and a white crane descended from the sky and alighted in the courtyard. Hongjing, startled, received them, believing they had come for him. The azure lads said, "The command of the Most High is for Master Huan." Hongjing silently considered his disciples—none bore the surname Huan. Upon inquiry, he found only Huan He, who tended the hearth. Questioned about his practices, Huan replied, "For nine years, I have cultivated the Dao of silent audience. Today’s summons is the result of that." Huan donned celestial robes, mounted the white crane, and ascended to heaven.
*The Supplementary Biographies of Immortals* states: Liu Hao’s family had long revered the Dao. He practiced with utmost devotion and, though over eighty, remained free of illness. The villagers suspected his longevity was due to his mastery of the Dao. One day, he suddenly told his family, "Yesterday, I received a celestial decree. The Most High has ordered me to oversee the kitchen of the Nine Immortals’ Treasure Chamber for a hundred years. Tomorrow, I must depart." On the appointed day, he passed away without illness. A wondrous fragrance filled the courtyard and lingered for days.
*Liu Rong’s powdered turtle; Zhang Guo’s paper donkey.*
*The Biographies of Divine Immortals* records: The Southern Pole Master Liu Rong took powder, coated a cup with it, and molded it into a turtle. He boiled and ate its flesh, then chewed its shell, which reverted to a wine cup.
