He could also hold powder in his mouth to form eggs, spitting out dozens at a time, which he would boil and eat. The yolks of these eggs all contained a small amount of powder. He would then take water and shape it into fine wine, and all who drank it would become intoxicated.

*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* records: Zhang Guo often rode a white donkey that could travel several hundred *li* in a day. When he rested, he would fold the donkey, reducing it to the thickness of paper, and store it in his headscarf box. When he wished to ride again, he would breathe upon it with water, and it would once more become a donkey.

Lu Feng repairs the estate; Xi Shan crafts the carriage.

*The Supplementary Biographies of Divine Immortals* records: Lu Feng was skilled in carpentry and traveled between Qi and Dai. The magistrate of Shouzhang had a secondary estate with over twenty collapsed buildings. He sent an official to summon craftsmen for repairs. The official detained Lu Feng along the way, but Lu Feng invited him to a tavern to drink. Though the estate was over thirty *li* away, by dusk, Lu Feng was already drunk. The magistrate was known for his temper, and the official grew deeply anxious. The next day, they drank again, and the official urged Lu Feng to depart. Lu Feng replied, "I came to repair the estate, and it has already been put right. Why do you rush me so?" The official did not understand his meaning. After a long while, a village boy arrived from the estate and reported that the previous night, as if by wind and rain, the buildings had been restored. The official reported the matter to the magistrate, who summoned Lu Feng to apologize, but Lu Feng merely murmured in assent.

Xi Leshan’s origins are unknown.

During the Dahe era, Chang’an endured over a month of heavy snow, leaving peddlers and common folk with no means to seek food. Leshan braved the snow to visit a cartwright’s household and said, “I am skilled in crafting wheel spokes. I can make a hundred at once, and the labor is worth three hundred *wen*.” The family put him to work. By lamplight, he wielded his axe, laboring through the day and into the night. Before the third watch had passed, the hundred spokes were complete. At dawn, he received thirty thousand *wen* and returned home to relieve the hunger of the poor. When the snow cleared, he departed, and none knew where he had gone.

Liezi rode the wind—one might call it "out-snowing the snow." As the *True Scripture of Southern Florescence* says: "Liezi rode the wind and traveled, light and effortless. Fifteen days passed before he returned. Among those who attain good fortune, few are so frequent. Yet even so, though he avoided walking, he still relied on something. As for one who rides the uprightness of Heaven and Earth, harnesses the transformations of the six qi, and roams the boundless—what does he rely on?" The *Continuation of the Immortals’ Biographies* states: Du Zisheng, styled Keyun, claimed to be from Duling, though none could fathom his age. He could drink three *dou* of wine without becoming drunk and was skilled in writing in the sand. Once, he stood in the snow for two or three days; people thought him frozen to death, but when they brushed him off, he rose slowly, shook off the snow, and walked away as if waking from a drunken slumber. During the Tang, Du Ruxiu served as prefect of Suzhou. Hearing that Keyun was in the city, he invited him to the prefectural hall, bowing and addressing him as "Daoist Elder." The officials were astonished, but Ruxiu explained, "When my late father governed Xichuan, he was close with the Daoist Elder. It has been forty years since we parted, yet his appearance and bearing remain unchanged." Later, he traveled to Hang and Yue, then departed. Kuafu chased the sun; Taibai grasped the moon.

The *Classic of the Great Wilderness* states: "There was a man who held two yellow serpents in his hands, named Kuafu."

The *Liezi* records: "Kuafu, overestimating his strength, sought to chase the sun’s shadow. He pursued it to the edge of Valley’s End, parched with thirst. He rushed to drink from the Wei River, but its waters were insufficient. He then set out northward to drink from the Great Marsh, yet before he arrived, he died of thirst along the way. He cast aside his staff, and where his flesh and fat soaked the earth, the Deng Forest grew—spreading vast for thousands of *li*."

The *Gleanings of Forgotten Lore* recounts: "Later, Du Fu spoke of Li Taibai lodging by the river. It was a high autumn night, the current crystalline, like ten thousand *qing* of cold jade. Taibai, seeing the moon in the water, declared, 'I shall enter the water to grasp the moon!' Soon after, his body was nowhere to be found. Some say the waters dissolved him—such are the affairs of immortals."

Cloud-essence for Yuan Dao, stone-marrow for Wang Lie.

The *Biographies of Eminent Daoists* states: "Ye Fashan, styled Yuandao, was a native of Kuocang and descended from a family of Daoists. His mother, née Liu, dreamed one day of a shooting star entering her mouth; she swallowed it and conceived. After fifteen months, she gave birth. At seven, he fell into the great river and returned only after three years. His parents asked the reason, and he replied, 'The Azure Lad led me to drink cloud-essence and pay homage to the Supreme. Thus, I tarried awhile.'"

The *Accounts of Divine Immortals of the Wang Clan* records: "Wang Lie, styled Changxiu, was a Gentleman of Scattered Leisure. Ji Shuye held him in great esteem and often sought his instruction. Together, they entered the mountains to roam and gather herbs. Later, Lie went alone to the Taihang Mountains, where he suddenly heard a sound like thunder from the northeast. Unsure of its cause, he went to investigate and saw the mountain split and stone cracked, with a blue-green mud flowing forth like marrow. Lie took some and ate it. He then returned with Shuye to see, but the broken mountain had already restored itself. According to the immortal classics, every five hundred years, the mountains of immortals open once, and stone-marrow emerges. Those who obtain and consume it attain a lifespan coeval with heaven. The marrow Lie had earlier obtained was of this kind."

Shen Bin sat three times for the examinations; Dong Wei wove a hundred knots.

From *Elegant Conversations of the Prefectural Pavilion*:

Shen Bin, styled Ziwen, was fond of the immortals. Orphaned in his youth, he made a pact with his western journey: three attempts at the examinations. On his first attempt, he dreamed of an immortal’s song:

*"The jade palace swings wide its gates, and one enters with ease,
Golden peaches ripen full—yet none dare steal a taste.
The phoenix startles at the sacred fan, flapping its wings in fright,
The dragon dreads the golden whip and turns its head in flight."*

On his second attempt, he recalled the immortal’s song:

*"The elm wind sighs, autumn on the ninefold heavens,
The Queen Mother returns from her feast in the jade tower.
Days and months grow long as twin phoenixes slumber,
The mulberry fields shift, and the six giant turtles grieve.
Clouds obscure the flutes and pipes as they drift away,
Stars strike the banners, each streaming its own course.
Of late, poetry and wine have lost their wild abandon,
Riding the dragon, I recall the Way of Supreme Clarity."*

For his third attempt, he submitted his examination scroll, placing Liu Xiang at the top with this poem:

*"Once I sat for the Son of Heaven’s grand examination,
Four reigns of wind and moon have silvered my temples.
I did not follow the founding emperor, shouldering his sword,
But for the cultured emperor, I returned to my books.
Ten years of war’s dust have swept away my old livelihood,
Spring rains drench the city, rotting my humble home.
Why should you begrudge me a single branch?
The cinnamon of immortality still blooms in abundance each year."*

The examiners, upon reading Shen’s poem, that year exceptionally passed both Liu and Shen. Later, Shen traveled south beyond the lakes, where he too became a man of the Dao.

From *Biographies of Reclusive Scholars*:

Dong Wei dwelled in Luoyang at the White Society, fashioning his robes from tattered silk and scraps of cloth, earning the name "Hundred Knots." Xie Xiutong was a scholar of refined conduct.

From *Cloud and Thatch*:

The Daoist master Nie Shidao heard that the Perfected One Mei and Attendant Censor Xiao had secluded themselves on Jade Bamboo Mountain, where many had glimpsed them. Nie journeyed to Jade Bamboo, lodging at the Abbey of Pure Emptiness. Thrice he wandered the Darkwood Hollow, hoping for even a single sighting, and departed with unwavering resolve, venturing deep into the mountains. Suddenly, he saw a man in plain robes and a black gauze hat, who called himself a wayfarer. When Nie asked where he was bound, Nie replied that he sought Mei and Xiao. The wayfarer said: "I hear your spirit stirs with longing for the Dao, and you have traversed famous mountains in search of it—this is no easy devotion. Your past deeds are most serene, and your name is already inscribed in the Jade Register. Though you may not yet ascend in flight, you shall certainly transcend the world. I am Xie Xiutong."

Additionally, when the future Chancellor Yuan Zi was yet obscure, he once climbed Green Han Peak in Fuzhou on a day of leisure. There, a scholar in Confucian garb engaged him in familiar conversation. Yuan remarked: "This place, with its wondrous mountains and springs, must surely be the abode of transcendent immortals." The scholar replied: "There are five or six Daoists who occasionally come here, though none know where they dwell. They shun human attention, but if you bring fine wine, you might catch a glimpse." Later, Yuan obtained excellent wine and returned, staying several nights until the Daoists indeed arrived. The scholar arranged seats and served the wine. Then, he led Yuan out to bow before them. The Daoists exchanged glances in alarm, and one angrily rebuked the scholar: "This man is sincere in his resolve, and moreover, he is a scholar of noble lineage." After a long while, their displeasure eased. Gazing at Yuan, they said: "This man resembles the meditating monk of Western Florescence—he has come and gone for forty-seven years." When they asked Yuan his age, it was precisely forty-seven. Clapping their hands, they declared: "Your blessings and rank are at hand." Later, Yuan indeed became chancellor.

Left Ci was one-eyed; Xu Hua had a crooked head.

From *Records of Divine Immortals of the Three Caverns*:

During the Wei, Left Ci could transform himself at will. Later, he entered Eastern Wu. There, a man named Xu Duo lived in Dantu. When Left Ci visited, Xu was away, and a guest at his gate lied, saying: "Master Xu is not here." Left Ci, knowing the guest mocked him, departed at once. The guest saw an ox walking along the top of a poplar tree; when it climbed the tree, it vanished, but when it descended, the ox reappeared. Upon the tree, even the wheel hubs sprouted thorns one or two feet long, which could not be cut nor pushed aside. Terrified, the guest rushed to tell Xu Duo: "There was a one-eyed old man whom I deceived, saying you were not home. After he left, the cart and ox became like this!" Xu said: "That was Left Ci. How could you deceive him?" The guests scattered to pursue Left Ci, kowtowing and begging forgiveness. Left Ci’s anger subsided, and he departed, whereupon the cart and ox returned to normal.

From *Extensive Records of Marvels*:

In Chengdu, there was a painter surnamed Xu, skilled in capturing likenesses. One day, a poor man in tattered, haggard clothes came seeking a portrait. Xu laughed: "Your appearance is so robust—why seek a portrait?" The man opened his cloth bag, took out a yellow Daoist robe, a deerskin cap, and a white jade hairpin. Donning the cap and changing his clothes, he sat upright and rubbed his face—his youthful complexion returned. He tugged his beard, and it turned black at his touch, revealing a handsome man. Xu exclaimed: "I did not know a divine immortal had descended!" The Daoist said: "Paint my image and place it in your shop. If anyone seeks to buy it, charge only a thousand coins." Later, someone recognized him: "This is the Perfected One Zhu of Lingquan." Seekers flocked to Xu, who demanded two thousand coins per portrait. In a dream, the Daoist said: "Your blessings are limited—you must not greedily disregard my words, lest you shorten your lifespan." He struck Xu’s cheek. When Xu awoke, his head was crooked, and he became known as "Xu the Crooked Head."

Jie Zitui and the yellow sparrow; Feng Junda and the green ox.

From *Biographies of Divine Immortals*:

Jie Zitui was a man of Jin who lived in obscurity. Zhao Chengzi studied the Dao with him. One morning, a yellow sparrow perched at his gate. Duke Wen of Jin found it strange and lived abroad with Jie for over a decade, enduring hardship without complaint. When they returned to Jieshan, a man named Bozi often came to call Jie: "It is time to leave." Jie then followed Bozi in his wanderings. Later, Duke Wen sent envoys with jade and silk to summon him, offering rites of respect, but Jie did not go.

From *Biographies of Eminent Daoists*:

Feng Heng, styled Junda, often rode a green ox, earning the name "Green Ox Daoist." When people fell ill—whether he knew them or not—he would offer medicine from the bamboo tube at his waist, or apply needles, curing them instantly. When Emperor Wu of Wei asked for the great principles of nurturing life, Feng said: "Let the body labor but not to excess; let the diet be meager but not to the point of depletion. Avoid rich and heavy foods, moderate sour and salty flavors, quiet your thoughts, temper joy and anger, forsake frivolous pursuits, be cautious in the bedchamber. In spring and summer, release and disperse; in autumn and winter, store and conceal. Then you will approach the Dao."

The imperial park presented jujubes; the marketplace of Heyin sold pomegranates.

From *The Biography of Dongfang Shuo*:
During Emperor Wu’s reign, the Shanglin Park presented jujubes. The Emperor struck the railing of the Weiyang Palace’s front hall with his staff and called out to Shuo: "Chi! Come, come! Sir, do you know what is in the basket?" Shuo replied: "Forty-nine jujubes from the Shanglin Park." The Emperor asked: "How did you know?" Shuo said: "The one who called me was the Emperor; striking the railing twice—two great forests; Shuo comes, come come—jujubes; *chi chi*—forty-nine." The Emperor laughed heartily and bestowed ten bolts of silk upon him.

From *Biographies of Eminent Daoists*:
The Daoist Zhang Yuanhua, whose esoteric practices were unknown, one day bid farewell to his fellow villagers in their dreams, saying: "I shall journey afar for a time." That very night, he indeed achieved transcendence. At dawn, those who harbored doubts and came to visit found that all, young and old, had shared the same dream. After his burial, a hole large enough to admit an arm appeared in his tomb. Those who understood said: "This is the cicada’s molt." Soon after, a merchant from Heyin arrived at Rufen, selling pomegranates along the way. He encountered a Daoist who said: "I am the temple master of your grave, Zhang. I entrust you with a letter." He then purchased several dozen pomegranates and presented them to the guest at the Northern Dipper Hall. The merchant agreed.

Upon arrival, his disciples said: "This letter bears the master’s own brush." They led the guest to the portrait hall, where he remarked: "The one I saw earlier differs not in the slightest from this."

Carrying a zither and shouldering a medicine gourd, he drifted as a home upon the waters.

*Accounts of Eminent Daoists*:
The Daoist Li Zhenyin dwelled in reclusion on Mount Hua. In the west of Qizhou there lived a man named Wang You, whose wealth numbered in the tens of thousands. He often maintained a guesthouse to welcome travelers from all directions, where fine delicacies and choice wines were ever at the ready. Li arrived bearing a zither and a medicine gourd, and Wang invited him to stay in the guesthouse.

Li said: "I have heard that those who delight in music all have a master to guide them; those who scheme and maneuver harbor ambitions of persuasion; those who study military strategy must possess a warrior’s heart. Yet I carry but a single zither and a single medicine gourd—could there be no purpose in this? To carry a zither is to know that the ancient tones of the qin may restore simplicity and curb excess. To shoulder a medicine gourd is to know that people suffer many ailments, and I would ease their pain and grant them enduring peace. Moreover, my zither does not transform only myself—it transforms others. My medicine does not preserve only myself—it preserves others. Though you are able to aid others with your abundance, this differs from those who amass wealth through oppression and plunder. Yet compared to the noble and wealthy of antiquity who welcomed scholars, you still fall short. The key lies in distinguishing the worthy from the unworthy, and thus your person and reputation may remain unblemished."

Li then called for wine and drank alone until dawn, whereupon he abruptly took his leave. Wang secretly followed and saw Li transform into a great stag, bounding westward into the unknown.

*The Tang History Continued*:
Zhang Zhihe, styled Zitong, visited Yan Zhenqing, who served as prefect of Huzhou. Yan, finding Zhang’s boat shabby, offered to replace it, but Zhang replied: "I wish to make my home upon the waters, drifting where the clouds and waves may take me."

Li Deyu praised Zhang Zhihe as one "reclusive yet renowned, prominent yet unburdened, neither impoverished nor prosperous, strict in ritual and reverent in conduct."

*Records of the Multitude of Immortals from the Three Caverns*, Scroll Fifteen—concluded.
*Records of the Multitude of Immortals from the Three Caverns*, Scroll Sixteen.

*The Orthodox Unity Daoist* Lai Baoguang compiled:

Zhang Zhihe’s water play, Xia Tong’s paired plowing.

*Accounts of Immortals’ Exploits*:
Zhang Zhihe was a native of Shanyin in Kuaiji. When Yan Zhenqing traveled east to Pingwang Post, Zhihe, flushed with wine, performed a water play: he spread a mat upon the waves, drank alone, and chanted in song. The mat drifted to and fro, while clouds and cranes followed above. Yan’s retinue and guests all marveled in astonishment. Soon after, Zhihe waved his hand upon the water in farewell to Yan Zhenqing and ascended, departing.

*Records of Jin Recluses*:
Xia Tong, styled Zhongyu, was a native of Yongxing in Kuaiji. Orphaned in youth and poor, he supported his parents with filial devotion. Often foraging for food, he would journey by starlight and return at night, sometimes gathering shellfish by the sea to sustain them. Though eloquent in discourse, his clan urged him to serve in office, saying: "Your clarity and uprightness suit you to be an administrative aide. By engaging with the prefectural court, you would surely rise to prominence. Why endure hardship in the mountains and forests, spending your life by the seashore?" Tong flushed with anger and replied: "Do you truly take me for such a man? Were I born in an age of peace, I would debate the path of withdrawal with Yuan and Kai. In a corrupt era, I would share in the filth with Qu Yuan. Between purity and defilement, when the time is right, I shall plow alongside Ju and Nie. How could I debase myself and bend my will to serve in the prefectural court?" Ji Ping was revived, and Yin Yao reborn.

*Tales from the Hall of Audience*:
In Shangdang, there lived a transcendent named Cheng, who possessed talismans. When Liu Wu served as military commissioner of Ze and You, a man of Linluo County named Xiao Jiping, though wealthy, suddenly died one day. Cheng, who had once received Jiping’s generous kindness, rushed to see him upon hearing of his death and told his son: "Your father has not truly died—he was summoned by a mountain spirit to treat him. He may yet live." Thereupon, he inscribed a vermilion talisman and cast it into the air. After the time it takes to eat a meal, Jiping revived, saying: "At first, a man in green robes appeared, saying, ‘The spirit of Mount Huo has summoned you.’ After traveling some fifty *li*, I encountered a red-robed man with a staff, descending from the sky in fury. He said to me, ‘Cheng the Demon-Slayer summons you—go quickly!’ The green-robed man fled in fear. The red-robed man took my sleeve and brought me back, and I awoke." His family was astonished and asked Cheng who this "Demon-Slayer" was. Cheng replied: "I studied under a master and took refuge in the *Dragon-Tiger Record of Demon-Slaying*." He then untied the talisman pouch at his waist to show them. Later, he traveled to Min and Yue, and his whereabouts became unknown.

*The Supplementary Biographies of Transcendents* records: Dan Yinyao, a Daoist of Dongting, dwelt on Mount Jiao. He studied the Way of Refining the Form through the Great Yin. After dying in a cliffside grotto, he appeared in a dream to his disciple, saying: "Examine my corpse and let no other disturb it. In six years, I shall revive—come to welcome me with garments." The disciple looked and found the body putrid, infested with worms, and decayed, save for the five viscera, which remained unchanged. He sealed and guarded it as instructed. When the six years had passed, he returned to find the body whole and seated upright. The disciple prepared a bath and fresh robes to receive him. His hair and beard had grown black, coarse, and straight, like the mane of a beast. Sixteen years later, he died again in the same manner, only to revive after another seven years. This occurred three times in all, and with each cycle, his appearance grew more robust. Emperor Yang of the Sui summoned him to the Eastern Capital, but he earnestly begged to return to the mountains, and the emperor soon granted his wish.

"Shoulder Wu’s Three Abidings, Mo Di’s Five Phases."

*The Record of the Assembly of Perfected Ones on Mount West* states: Shi Jianwu, styled Xisheng, once composed the *Inscription on the Three Abidings*, which reads: The *Great Change* says, "Essence and qi become things; the wandering soul brings transformation." Among all the myriad forms, nothing is preserved before the primordial qi. If the primordial qi abides, then the form abides; if the form abides, then the spirit abides. When these three abide, my life rests with me, not with Heaven. This principle is perfectly clear, yet those who dwell in the mysteries do not believe it.

*The Biographies of Divine Transcendents* records: Mozi, named Di, served as a grandee in Song. At eighty-two, he feigned madness, saying: "The affairs of the world are now clear to me. Glory and rank cannot be long preserved. I shall abandon the vulgar stream to follow the Way of Red Pine." He entered Mount Di in Zhou to refine his spirit. Suddenly, a man appeared before him, and Mozi asked: "Are you the numinous qi of the mountain, or a transcendent who has attained the Way? I pray you tarry a while and instruct me in the Daoist teachings." The divine man replied: "I knew you were devoted to the Way, so I came to await you." Thereupon, he bestowed upon Mozi the *Plain Writ of the Unfinished Circle and Square*. Mozi bowed in receipt, and after practicing its methods, he achieved their efficacy. He compiled their essentials into the *Record of the Five Phases*. Later, he attained earthly transcendence and concealed himself to escape the Warring States.

"Xing Gong’s Cinnabar Crucible, Zhou Guan’s Medicine Cauldron."

*The Selected Tales* recounts: The summit of Mount Jiuyi lies beyond the reach of human footsteps. Legend tells of a transcendent who once dwelt there. Recently, a young hunter named Cao Xiong, son of the local military inspector, pursued game deep into the mountains and ascended to the peak. There, he saw two or three thatched huts. Within one, a man sat leaning on a table. Xiong bowed twice and asked who he was. The man replied: "I am the Transcendent Xing. I have dwelt here since the reign of Emperor Xizong of Tang." He then invited Xiong to discuss worldly affairs. Xiong observed his dwelling: it contained only a study and a cinnabar crucible. On the table lay several poems. Xiong requested copies and obtained over ten, of which one is recorded here:

*A decree from the August Emperor descends to the immortals’ home,*
*Soon the stars shall align, and a guest shall stir the elixir.*
*On the wall hangs a three-foot sword of wind and cloud;*
*Before the bed stands a furnace of dragon and tiger, one crucible of sand.*
*Riding a thousand-year crane over the isles of the sea,*
*Sitting amidst the flowers of the four seasons in the Gourd Palace.*
*For love of the* Yin Fu *I inquire into the primal meaning,*
*Then follow the colored banners into the misty rosy clouds.*

When Xiong later returned to revisit the site, it took him several months to arrive. Though the dwelling remained, the transcendent had vanished.

*The Cold Studio Night Talks* records: Zhou Guan, of unknown origin, styled himself "Wooden Mamu." During the Zhiping era, he often wandered the Western Mountains and later arrived in Yuanzhou, where he encountered a young scholar named Li in the marketplace. Struck by Li’s refined bearing, Zhou wished to take him back to the forest retreat. Yet Li indulged in wine and sensual pleasures, showing no inclination toward self-cultivation. Zhou pointed to a medicine cauldron and composed a verse to admonish him:

*"Heaven ordains the dull to become a cauldron,*
*Even with three legs, how can it walk?*
*Though ears exist, it heeds no Dao,*
*Only clings to the world’s fiery pit."*

Soon after, Zhou died in the Western Mountains. As he was about to transform, someone asked his age. He replied:

*"At eighty, a wine immortal in the Western Hills,*
*Straw sandals worn through, hempen robe threadbare.*
*If you ask my age by the sexagenary cycle, ask not—*
*The radiance of the Supreme Ultimate knows no years."*

Later, someone saw Zhou near the Zhou Bridge in the capital. He entrusted a letter for Li in Yuanzhou, saying: *"Next year on the Mid-Autumn night, I shall pay you a visit."* When the appointed time arrived, Zhou indeed appeared at Li’s home. Finding Li away on business, he inscribed the door in white earth and departed, leaving these words:

*"This Mid-Autumn night, I come to keep last year’s vow.*
*Yet the three-legged cauldron is not seen—*
*In a snap of the fingers, all is empty."*

Li subsequently fell from his horse and broke a leg.

A brocade curtain descended from the sky, and the Grand Duke coaxed a crow.

*The Records of the Western Mountains* state: On the first day of the eighth month in the second year of Ningkang during the reign of Emperor Xiaowu, Master Xu the Perfected rose at dawn to find auspicious clouds descending from the heavens. Immortals alighted in his courtyard, whereupon a celestial decree was proclaimed: *"The August Emperor bestows upon the disciple of the Dao, Xu Xun, a purple feathered robe, a jade banner with a precious scepter, an elixir of jade nectar, and a pill of golden cinnabar—each one measure. The decree is to be received and obeyed."* On the fifteenth day of that month, at the *gen* hour, celestial music resounded from the heavens. A procession of azure lads, colorful banners, a dragon-drawn carriage, and a feathered canopy arrived to welcome Master Xu, his family of forty-two, and even their chickens and dogs, all ascending upon clouds. Moments later, a brocade curtain fluttered down from the sky, and a hawk’s perch fell in the southeast of his estate.

*The Accounts of Divine Immortals’ Encounters* recount: When Guo Ziyi first joined the army in the desert frontier, he was sent to hasten the delivery of military provisions. Some ten *li* from Yinzhou, dusk fell, and a sudden sandstorm plunged everything into darkness. Unable to proceed, he took shelter in an empty roadside

Later, Guo Ziyi achieved great merit and rose to eminent rank, being appointed Grand Commandant, Director of the Secretariat, and Grand Preceptor. He lived to ninety before passing away.

Jade tally and golden key—heaven’s favor in precious tokens.

*The Extensive Records* states: Wang Jia, having passed the filial piety examination, was appointed as a judicial officer in Zizhou. At the time, Du Xian served as a judicial officer in Bozhou, and the two, being colleagues, grew quite close. When they were dispatched together to Luoyang, they crossed the Qiantang River and ascended Luosha Mountain to observe the tide. Wang Jia remarked to Du Xian, "The Great Yu was truly a sage. When he governed the waters, he placed golden caskets and jade tallies to pacify the rivers and streams. This city of Hangzhou lacks such a stabilizing force—it will soon fall into ruin."

Du Xian asked, "How do you know this?"

Wang Jia replied, "Beneath this very stone lies the answer. Let us see for ourselves." He then instructed Du Xian to close his eyes and grasp his hand, bidding him leap down. When Du Xian opened his eyes, he found himself at the riverbed, where an empty space like a hall stood. There was a stone casket over a *zhang* tall, its lock gleaming. Wang Jia opened the lock, lifted the lid, and guided Du Xian’s hand inside. Within lay a golden casket, three *chi* in height, sealed with a golden lock. Wang Jia said, "The jade tally lies within, but only those with destiny may behold it." He then led Du Xian’s hand out, and in an instant, they stood upon the shore once more.

Wang Jia then told Du Xian, "You are destined for the rank of prime minister. Take care to preserve yourself." He proceeded to recount in detail Du Xian’s future official appointments, career, and even his lifespan. Later, Du Xian’s promotions unfolded exactly as foretold.

*The Miscellaneous Records of Leisure* recounts: During the Chongning era, Peng Tianchong, a candidate for the imperial examination from Nankang Prefecture, had never studied the Dao. Suddenly, he claimed that celestial beings descended to his home and bestowed upon him a heavenly scripture. One day, after bathing, he announced to others, "I am ascending to heaven." He shut himself indoors and was not seen again. Forty-two days later, he unexpectedly crossed the river and returned, saying, "When I first departed, I boarded a golden boat carried by clouds, as if floating upon silk. Gradually, I ascended to heaven, where I met a Daoist who said, 'In a past life, you were the magistrate of Pengze and rendered service to the people, thus your name is recorded in the immortal registers.' He then pointed to a white vapor and said, 'This is the sorrowful *qi* of your parents longing for you. Why not return? After ninety years, you may come back.' He gave me a golden key and a silver key, instructing me to sail downstream to Caizhou, sell the golden key for money, and return home. As I neared the river to cross, my funds ran out, so I sold the silver key to buy a boat. By the time I reached home, my money was spent."

His parents were overjoyed. Later, when he was arranged to marry, no further anomalies occurred.

Foolish sheep below the city, ailing cranes in the mountains.

*The Goose-Damaged Collection* records: In Luoyang, there was a cave into which a man fell in despair. Inside, he beheld palaces and figures unlike those of the mortal world. There were also great sheep with pearls in their beards; people ate them without knowing their origin. When he asked Zhang Hua about this, Hua replied, "This is one of the Nine Halls of Earthly Immortals. The great sheep are foolish dragons."

*The Cloud Thatched Hut* states: Before attaining high office, the Tang minister Li Shi was deeply devoted to the Dao. Once, while wandering Mount Song, he heard moaning from the overgrown grass. Upon investigation, he found an ailing crane, which spoke in human words: "I was once an immortal, but fate has afflicted me. A woodcutter injured me, and one of my legs is nearly broken. I require several *ge* of blood from a person of three lifetimes’ purity to heal."

Li Shi immediately removed his robe, preparing to draw blood, but the crane said, "There are few such people in the world, and you are not yet wholly pure." It then gave him an eyelash, saying, "Take this to the eastern capital. Simply gaze through it, and you will know the truth." On the road, Li Shi looked at himself and saw the head of a horse. Upon reaching Luoyang, he encountered many people, none of whom were whole—some had the heads of dogs, others of donkeys or horses. By chance, on a bridge, he saw an old man riding a donkey. Gazing through the eyelash, he saw the man was truly human. Li Shi bowed and recounted the crane’s plight. The old man, Huang, dismounted at once, bared his arm, and drew blood. Li Shi collected it in a small bottle and returned to the crane. He moistened the wound with the blood, bound it with torn cloth, and the crane expressed its gratitude: "You will become a prime minister in a prosperous age. Later, you too shall ascend lightly. Our reunion will not be far—take care not to waver in your resolve."

Li Shi bowed in reverence, and the crane soared into the sky.

The light of the wing’s shadow discerns right from wrong; the rise of clarity brings suffering and joy.

*The Biographies of Daoist Scholars* states: Meng Jingyi, styled Fuming, was a descendant of Mencius. By nature, he was profoundly filial. When Qi Jingling Wang widely propagated Buddhist scriptures and gathered many monks, Meng Jingyi debated with them on the rightness and wrongness of the two teachings. He dissected each matter with flawless reasoning, his words flowing without hindrance. Even the eloquence of Lan Sheng resisting Ying or the persuasive power of Li Gong subduing Po could not surpass him.

*The Supplement to the Biographies of Immortals* records: Chen Xingming traveled among famous mountains, where he encountered a divine person who told him, "Most who cultivate the Dao cannot persevere for long, thus few witness its fruition. Your accumulated merit is commendable. If you harbor no thoughts of retreat, why fear not being listed in the jade registers? Yet, what comes before is suffering, and what follows is joy. Suffering has its end, but joy is boundless. Dwelling in secluded valleys, keeping company with birds and beasts, enduring hunger and thirst, bearing the hardships of cold and heat, forsaking worldly glory, parting from loved ones—this may be called suffering. But to live as long as heaven and earth, to transform without limit, to ride the void and command the clouds, to sit and grow wings—this is joy. Should you not strive diligently in your cultivation?"

Chen Xingming bowed and said, "I shall forever bear your sacred words in mind and devote myself wholly to the Dao, never daring to be negligent." After eighteen years of cultivation, on the first day of the third month in the first year of the Jin Daishi era, he ascended to heaven from Mount Heng.

Returning to truth, tamed rabbits; Yan He feeding oxen.

*The Record of Mount Heng’s Abbey* states: During the Dazhong era of our dynasty, a man named Song Guizhen abandoned his official post to embrace the Dao. He built a hut beside the mountain, ate *zhi* herbs, and abstained from grains for over thirty years. At dawn and dusk, as he chanted scriptures, white snakes and white rabbits would gather tamely, as if listening. Prefect Zhao Xu named his hut the "Hall of Broad Coldness" and once left a poem as a gift.

*The Zhuangzi* states: The Lord of Lu, having heard that Yan He was a man who had attained the Dao, dispatched an envoy with gifts to precede his visit. Yan He dwelt in a humble lane, clad in plain cloth and tending oxen himself. When the Lord of Lu’s envoy arrived, Yan He received him in person. The envoy asked, “Is this the home of Yan He?” Yan He replied, “This is indeed He’s home.” The envoy presented the gifts, but Yan He said, “I fear the messenger may have erred and would incur blame. It would be better to verify.” When the envoy returned to make further inquiry, Yan He could no longer be found.

Teeming fruit rides the tiger; Tai Bai mounts the child.

*The Extensive Records* recount: Wang Shuang was a servant to Zhang Maoshi of Nanyang. One day, he took leave of his master, saying, “I am deeply grateful for your kindness and wish to repay it. My home lies nearby, and its scenery is worth seeing. Would you care to visit?” Maoshi replied, “I would, but I do not wish my family to know. Could we slip away unnoticed?” Shuang said, “That is easily done.” He cut a bamboo stalk, inscribed a talisman upon it, and instructed, “Hold this as you enter your chamber, feign illness, and secretly place it among your belongings. You will then be able to slip away unnoticed.” They traveled south together for several *li* until they came upon a yellow-capped attendant holding a green *qilin*, accompanied by two striped tigers, waiting by the roadside. Maoshi, startled, wished to turn back, but Shuang said, “Have no fear. Press onward.” Shuang then mounted the *qilin*, while Maoshi rode a tiger. Ascending the palm of an immortal, they crossed ravines and scaled peaks, yet felt no roughness of the terrain. At the foot of a mountain, they beheld sights of otherworldly beauty—pavilions, pines, and rocks unlike anything in the mortal realm. Hundreds of purple-robed scribes greeted them by the roadside. Upon entering, dozens of maidens in azure robes, each of extraordinary beauty, bore instruments and led them in, bowing as they went. They were feasted in the central hall, where songs of *luan* birds and dances of phoenixes, along with music of all kinds, filled the air—sounds unheard of in the human world. Their spirits soared in lofty detachment, and they no longer gave thought to worldly affairs. There, they were instructed in the supreme Dao and presented with a hundred *yi* of gold to take home. When Maoshi returned, his family was gathered in mourning, for they believed he had been dead for seven days. Yet his heart remained warm, so they had not yet encoffined him. Thereafter, Maoshi abandoned his official post to wander famous mountains, and none knew his ultimate fate.

*The Record of Extraordinary Persons* states: In the early Yuanhe era, a man at sea witnessed Li Taibai conversing and laughing with a Daoist on a high mountain. After some time, the Daoist mounted a crimson beast amid emerald mist and departed. Taibai leapt up, strode vigorously after him, and caught up to ride eastward together.

The lofty scholar Shan Juan; the immortal official Ma Zhou.

*The Lives of Lofty Scholars* records: Shan Juan was a scholar of high virtue in the time of Shun. When Shun wished to yield the throne to him, Juan said, “In winter, I wear furs; in summer, coarse cloth. In spring, I plow enough to exert my strength; in autumn, I harvest enough to sustain myself. I rise with the sun and rest at its setting, roaming freely between heaven and earth, my heart content. Why would I desire the realm?” He refused the offer and entered the depths of the mountains, and none knew his whereabouts thereafter.

*The Biographies of Immortals* states: In his youth, Ma Zhou was often beset by drunkenness. One day, while leaving the city, he encountered an old man who bowed and sat with him beneath the trees. The old man said, “The Supreme One has commanded you to assist the sagely grandson. Why do you drown in wine, bringing hunger and hardship upon yourself? Your five spirits have already scattered, and you will die soon. Why not awaken?” He added, “You were originally the White Spirit Officer of Mount Hua, an immortal official. Now you are summoned to take up your post.” Zhou followed him and indeed saw his official residence, where his name and rank were recorded. He entered through the gate to find furnaces, fires, and vessels all prepared. Suddenly, five figures stood before him, saying, “Earlier, while you were drunk, we fled. Now, we ask you to close your eyes and reenter the divine chamber.” The next day, he visited Tian Gang, who said, “You have met with destiny. Your position will reach that of chief minister. Strive to fulfill it.” Zhou indeed rose to the rank of grand councilor for several years. Then, true immortals descended to his chamber and said, “Your service to the state is complete. The Great One summons you. You may no longer remain.” He then departed this life.

Bo Yuan gazes into the unseen; Liang Chen wanders in dreams.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* records: On Mount Huo, Deng Boyuan and Wang Yuanfu, both students of the Dao, received from divine beings the method of "swallowing the solar cinnabar radiance." After thirty-four years of practice, Boyuan could see in darkness and write by night. When his Dao was perfected, the Supreme Emperor dispatched a feathered chariot to ascend with Yuanfu in broad daylight.

*The Lives of Eminent Daoists* states: The Daoist Liang Chen, styled Kaocheng, was once observed by a physiognomist who remarked, "This youth’s eyes stream with white light, and his countenance gathers true qi—he is no ordinary man." Later, he indeed delighted in the Dao of immortality. In the early Xianxi era, he studied under Master Zheng at Louguan Abbey. Often, he dreamed of wandering with immortals—now ascending famous mountains, now drinking stone marrow—through which he gained profound insight. Many revered and followed him, but Chen, weary of their devotion, retreated deep into the Kun Valley. His eyes could perceive objects beneath the earth, and his ears could hear sounds from miles away. One day, he suddenly told his disciples, "A companion awaits me on the southern peak; I must go now." Donning his cap and robes, he departed, enveloped in swirling clouds. His form vanished, though the faint strains of celestial music lingered in the air. This occurred in the first year of the Taixing era, the year *wuyin*.

Bershan foretells fortune and misfortune; Shouxin guards against calamity and blessings.
*The Biographies of Divine Immortals* recounts: Bershanfu, a native of Yongzhou, dwelt on Mount Hua. He consumed yellow essence for over two hundred years. Whenever he visited a household, he discerned the good and evil of its ancestors as if witnessing them firsthand, and his predictions of fortune and misfortune always proved true.

*The Eastern Studio Jottings* notes: In Qinzhou, an elder named Xu Shouxin, known as Second Elder, daily swept the halls and courtyards with a single broom. He never spoke to others; if questioned, he would flee without reply. Yet if he uttered even a single word, it foretold fortune or misfortune. When Minister Lü Xiqing, having completed his mourning, prepared to journey to the capital, he detoured to visit the elder. Bowing, he asked for guidance, but the elder fled in alarm. Lü pursued him, and the elder suddenly turned back, saying, "Guard well, guard well." Lü assumed this meant guarding his wealth and rank. Upon returning to court, he was appointed governor of Jianzhou. When Xu Xi and Shen Gua had newly suffered defeat, Lü pleaded to decline the post and requested to accompany the two chief ministers to the imperial audience. Enraged, the emperor demoted him to governor of Shanzhou—an office corresponding to "single guard." During the Chongguan era, the elder was treated with great favor in spring audiences and granted the title *Master of Serene Harmony*. He was later given residence in the Wanshou Palace of Xianyuan. Chen Xun trades in fresh goods; Qizhen dwells in seclusion and devours meat.

*The Southern Wild Records* states: Chen Xun was skilled in astronomy and excelled in refined recitation. He once chanted:
*"One cauldron, yin and yang, civil and martial fire,
Ten years of cold and heat beneath the demon’s hide.
I tell the eastward-flowing stream: let your temples gray,
In the corner, I’ll cling to my iron raincoat still."*

And again:
*"Heaven and earth have seen the tender shoots of my work,
When dragon and tiger mature, seals and sashes grow sparse.
Late in life, he severed worldly ambitions,
Devoting himself to cultivation, hidden on West Mountain to grow herbs."*

During the Kaibao era, he once entered the city with an alchemist to sell elixirs. If he received praise, he would demand adornments; if he approached a furnace, he would drink as if no one were present. He sang:
*"Lan Caihe, worldly affairs swirl in endless confusion—
How much better to sell medicine, buy wine, and drink,
Then return to empty Kunlun, clapping hands in song!"*

Some suspected he and the alchemist were husband and wife; others claimed he had attained immortality.

*The Primer of Our Dynasty* records: Helan Qizhen, of unknown origin, was a Daoist who claimed to be over a hundred years old. Skilled in qi-absorption, he feared neither cold nor heat, often going without food, though at times he indulged in wine. He wandered the markets, consuming several pounds of rancid meat. Later summoned to court, Emperor Zhenzong presented him with a poem.

Erzhu floats on stone; Bao Jiao embraces the tree.

*The Supplement to the History of the Five Dynasties* records: Master Erzhu’s merits and conduct were most profound. A transcendent being gave him a single pill of medicine, saying, “When you see floating stone, then take it, and the Way of immortality will be achieved.” The master followed these instructions, casting a stone into the water each time he encountered one. Later, while preparing to cross the river at Mount Xia, an official named Shi awaited him with a boat. When asked his surname, the man replied, “Shi.” Asked to which region he belonged, he answered, “Fuzhou.” The master suddenly understood, swallowed the pill, and instantly ascended lightly into the air.

*The Biographies of Recluses* states: Bao Jiao, of unknown origin, ate no grains and wore no silk or hemp. Dwelling deep in the mountains, he consumed wild fruits and clad himself in bark. Someone once asked him, “Wild fruits and bark are also products of heaven—how do they differ from grains and silk?” Bao Jiao then ate nothing more and died embracing the withered branches of a tree.

Yan Dong’s single gourd, Dao Hui’s hundred *hu* of virtue.
*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* records: The Daoist Yan Dong, of unknown origin, arrived in Jinling during the Qi dynasty’s Jianyuan era and lodged with the Daoist Li Jingyou. Yan appeared dull-witted, but when engaged in discussion, his insights were profound. He always carried a single gourd with him. After five or six years in Jinling, one day he announced his departure, took up his brush, and annotated the *Scripture of Salvation for Humanity*, his pen never pausing until the work was complete by day’s end. Later, he entered the mountains of Liyang and vanished without a trace.

*The Collected Works of Xianji* states: Kong Daohui maintained his resolve and refused office. His father, You, was a man of such perfect conduct that it moved the spirits. He lived in reclusion on Mount Siming and once saw several hundred *hu* of coins in a valley, regarding them no differently than tiles or stones. Woodcutters who scrambled to take them found that once in hand, the coins turned to sand and pebbles. A deer wounded by an arrow fled to You, who tended its wound until it healed before letting it go.

The Immortal Lord’s tangerine well, the Divine Maiden’s bamboo altar.
*The Bangjiang Collection* records: The Immortal Lord Su, one day summoned by the Most High to assume a post among the perfected, took leave of his mother, saying, “Next year, this prefecture will suffer an epidemic. Take a cup of water from the well in our courtyard and a sprig of tangerine leaf to relieve the people’s suffering—it will prove most efficacious and also provide a modest means of support.” With these words, he stepped outside, where a host of immortals surrounded him with banners, regalia, and feathered canopies arrayed on either side. Slowly, he ascended into the heavens. The following year, a great plague indeed struck, and his mother used the well water and tangerine leaves to heal the afflicted, none of whom failed to recover at once. The tangerine well remains to this day.

*The Records of Assembled Immortals* states: The Cloud Radiance Lady, named Yaoji, was the daughter of the Queen Mother of the West and could soar and transform at will. When Yu the Great encountered her, he suspected she was some strange apparition rather than a true immortal and asked Tonglü about her. Tonglü replied, “The root of heaven and earth is the Dao; those who wield the Dao’s function are sages. Among the ranks of sages are perfected beings and immortals. Some perceive the qi and attain realization without cultivation—Lord Wood and the Metal Mother are such beings. The Cloud Radiance Lady is the Metal Mother’s daughter.” Thereafter, Yu paid her homage and received from her the methods of water control and channel dredging, as well as texts for summoning spirits and commanding ghosts. The Divine Maiden then transformed into stone; the Goddess Stone on Mount Wu is her metamorphosis. There is also a divine altar there, beside which grows bamboo that droops like a hairpin. If any flying object lands upon the altar, the bamboo sweeps it away with the wind, keeping the altar spotless throughout the year.

Liu Shang’s medicine pouch, Letian’s alchemical furnace.
*The Biographies of Divine Immortals* records: Liu Shang lamented how swiftly time passed and how his sinews and bones gradually weakened. Racing about from dawn to dusk, he wearied himself in vain—what benefit did fleeting worldly office bring to his own person? Thus, he feigned illness, resigned his post, and entered the Dao. While traveling in Guangling, he encountered a Daoist selling medicine in the city streets. The Daoist fixed his gaze upon Liu with great intensity, then set down his medicines, took up his carrying pole, and ascended to a tavern, where they drank together. The Daoist presented Liu with a small medicine pouch and playfully recited:
*“For no reason, you came to Yangzhou,
We met and climbed the winehouse tower.
This pouch of medicine I bestow as parting gift—
What more could you seek for a thousand years?”*
Then he departed. Liu opened the pouch to find a paper-wrapped gourd containing nine sesame-seed-sized pills. Following the instructions, he took them and immediately felt his spirit invigorated and hunger vanish. Later, he went to Yixing, enamored of the scenery around Lake Tai, and became a fisherman-recluse in the mountains. Many saw him and said, “That is Master Liu, the former gentleman of the court.” His *Eighteen Stanzas for the Barbarian Lute* circulated in the world.

*The Jottings from Chouchi* records: Letian built a thatched hut on Mount Lu and attempted to refine the elixir, but his furnace and crucible failed. The next day, an appointment as prefect of Zhongzhou arrived—he then understood that worldly affairs do not coexist.

Zhang Shuo’s feathered robe, Yuan Xian’s hempen cap.
*The Biographies of Immortals* records: Zhang Dun of Dongting encountered the immortal maiden Du Lanxiang, who descended into his chamber and taught him the Way of bodily ascension and transformation. She left behind a jade tablet as a token of his attainment of perfection. Commanding a jade maiden to present him with a feathered robe, crimson slippers, and a dark cap, she said, “These are the garments of the highest immortals, not found even in the grotto-heavens.” Zhang then ascended in broad daylight.

*The True Scripture of the Southern Florescence* records: Yuan Xian lived in Lu, his door a wicker gate and his window a clay jar. Zigong arrived in a grand carriage drawn by fine horses, which could not pass through the narrow lane. He went to see Yuan Xian, who greeted him at the gate wearing a hempen cap and wooden clogs, leaning on a staff. Zigong said, “Alas! What ails you, Master?” Yuan Xian replied, “I have heard that to lack wealth is called poverty; to study yet be unable to practice is called sickness. I am poor, not sick.” Zigong hesitated, his face flushing with shame. Yuan Xian smiled and said, “To act in pursuit of worldly favor, to befriend for mutual advantage, to study for the sake of others, to teach for one’s own sake, to indulge in the depravities of righteousness, to adorn oneself with carriages and horses—these are things I cannot bear to do.”

Roaming the mountains and abstaining from grains, Zhongdu resisting the cold.
*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* records: Sun Youyue, styled Yingda, once consumed magic mushrooms and abstained from grains. He also specialized in taking the *Grain Immortal Pills*, his complexion and spirit ever youthful, growing younger with age.

*The Divine Immortals of the Wang Clan* records: Wang Zhongdu encountered the Perfected One of Venus, who bestowed upon him the Rainbow Elixir. Thereby he mastered the extremes of cold and heat. Having lived for over two hundred years, in winter he wore but a single layer of clothing and rode a four-horse carriage to accompany the Emperor at Kunming Pond, circling the waters at a gallop. Though the Emperor donned a sable robe, he still felt the chill, while Zhongdu’s countenance showed no change, his back steaming with rising qi. Yet in the height of summer, when exposed to the blazing sun and surrounded by ten braziers, he uttered no complaint of heat, nor did his body perspire. Later, he too ascended as an immortal.

Sun Bo ignited fire; Tan Qiao entered water.
*The Biographies of Divine Immortals* states: Sun Bo, a man of Hedong, possessed refined talent and skill in composition. He wrote a hundred poems and recited hundreds of thousands of words of scripture. In his later years, he studied the Dao, mastering the extensions of the *Mozi*. He could cause grasses, trees, metals, and stones to burst into flame, illuminating several *li* around, and could likewise transform his own body into fire. Once, while traveling, he encountered an imperial envoy with a retinue of several hundred, yet none were burned. Those who were ill pointed at him and were cured. Later, he compounded the Divine Elixir and attained immortality.

*The Continued Biographies of Divine Immortals* states: Tan Qiao, styled Shengshu, was broadly learned and possessed a prodigious memory. He traveled through famous mountains, practiced grain avoidance to cultivate qi, and took delight in wine. Later, he entered Mount Nanyue to refine the elixir and achieved success—entering water without getting wet, entering fire without being scorched, and mastering transformation and concealment of form. He practiced intestinal cleansing like Ding Ci and tooth-tapping like Bao Zhu.

*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* records: Every mid-spring, Tu Dingci would have people take purgative medicines. At that time, he would go to a stream and sit beneath a small waterfall, allowing the water to enter his seven orifices—this he called "intestinal cleansing." One day, he sat in meditation and transformed. His family gathered around, weeping, but he revived and said, "It is difficult to bury one who departs while seated." After speaking, he transformed again. For several nights, his complexion remained as if alive. When they proceeded to the burial vault, his coffin was exceedingly light. Those who recognized the signs understood it as a case of *shijie* (liberation by means of a simulated corpse).

*The True Sayings* states: Bao Zhu had no knowledge of Daoist methods or techniques. At the age of forty, he developed facial wind-qi, causing his mouth and eyes to become misaligned. His upper and lower teeth would clatter together audibly day and night without cease. He lived to be one hundred twenty-seven years old. Later, he encountered cold weather and crossed a great river, falling into the Longevity River and drowning. The Northern Emperor repeatedly dispatched killing ghosts, the Day-Roaming Spirits, and the Earth-Calamity Envoys to take him, yet in the end, none dared approach.

The ghost officials inquired into the reason, and the Celestial Slayer replied: "This man employed manifold *fangyan* (esoteric methods) to restrain us. He would constantly knock his teeth and sound the Heavenly Drum to alert the myriad spirits within his body, preventing them from scattering. Thus, ghostly *qi* dared not enter, and so we had no opportunity to seize him. Had Bao Zhu not crossed the river by water, he might have lived beyond a thousand years without fail. As it happened, he encountered extreme cold and walked upon the ice, his jaws clenched so tightly that he could not knock his teeth. Thereupon, the ghosts took advantage of his drowning in the river. Yet even then, the movement of his teeth from the cessation of his wind-disease still served to repel death and ward off the slaying ghosts—how much more so for a Daoist who truly knocks his teeth, sounds the Heavenly Drum, and gathers the spirits!

*Divine Glue* mends the bowstring; *Spirit Mushroom* revives the dead.

*The Supplementary Records of Immortals’ Biographies* states: Emperor Wu of Han, while touring the Northern Sea and offering sacrifices at Mount Da, received four ounces of *Divine Glue* from the Queen Mother of the West via her envoys. The Emperor deposited it in the outer treasury, unaware of its wondrous properties. One day, while hunting tigers in the Hua Lin Park, his crossbow’s trigger mechanism snapped. An attendant requested a portion of the glue, moistened it in his mouth, and used it to mend the bowstring. Once repaired, the Emperor ordered several warriors to pull the string, yet it held fast all day without breaking. The Emperor marveled at this.

The glue originates from Fenglin Isle, where it is concocted from the beaks of phoenixes and the horns of unicorns, hence its name, *Gathering-String Glue*.

*The Extensive Records of the Strange* recounts: Xu Fu, styled Junfang, was of unknown origin. During the reign of the First Emperor of Qin, many wrongful deaths occurred along the roads of the imperial park. Crows would carry grass in their beaks to cover the faces of the dead, whereupon the corpses would immediately revive.

The court officials reported this to the First Emperor, who immediately dispatched envoys to inquire of Master Guigu about the grass. The Master replied: "This is the immortal herb from Mount Zu on the Eastern Sea’s Ancestral Isle, growing in fields of *qiong* jade. It is also called *Yangshen Zhi* (Spirit-Nourishing Mushroom), its leaves resembling wild rice. A single plant can revive a thousand people." The Emperor then sent Xu Fu with three thousand boys and girls, each aboard towering ships, to sail into the sea in search of Ancestral Isle and its immortal herb. They never returned.

Xu transformed from a woman into a man, as did Qiu Zeng.

*The Jin History* records: Xu Deng, a native of Min, was originally a woman who transformed into a man. Skilled in Daoist arts, he could immobilize people with a command, halt flowing water, and make tigers and wolves crouch to the ground. He befriended Zhao Bing as a fellow practitioner beyond the mundane world, and together they perfected their Way, ascending as immortals.

*The Scripture of the Original Acts of the Numinous Treasure* states: In the Northern Chamber lived a diligent and virtuous man named Wang Fudu, who had a daughter named A Qiu Zeng. At sixteen, while bathing in fragrant waters within a secluded chamber, she saw a golden light illuminating her in an unusual manner. She emerged from the pure room and, gazing from a height, beheld the radiant splendor of the Perfected and Divine Immortals. Qiu Zeng then bowed and invoked their names, saying: "Having now beheld the Heavenly Worthy, I wish to transform my body into that of a man." In an instant, her form changed into that of a man. The Primordial Heavenly Worthy of the Origin then commanded the Venerable Deity of the Southern Pole to be Qiu Zeng’s master, bestowing upon him the true scriptures and assigning golden boys and jade maidens to his service. The Dao spoke to Qiu Zeng: "Having gained the Venerable One of the Southern Pole as your master, is this not exalted? When your merits are complete and your virtue perfected, you shall naturally attain the rank of a Perfected, transcend the Three Realms, swiftly traverse the Nine Clouds, and attend the feasts of the Most High."

Cai Jing passed through a dog’s burrow; Song Qing emerged from a chicken coop.

*The Biographies of Divine Immortals of the Wang Clan* records: The Perfected Lord Wang Yuan, styled Fangping, was traveling through Mount Guacang when he passed the home of Cai Jing, a follower of the Dao. Recognizing that Cai’s bone structure was suited to the Way, the Lord came to deliver him, saying: "You are destined to be delivered from the world and fill a post among the immortals. However, you lack understanding of the Dao, and your *qi* is scant while your flesh is abundant. You can only achieve *shijie* (corpse liberation), a method swift as passing through a dog’s burrow." With these words, he departed. Suddenly, Cai’s body grew as hot as fire, and he sought water to douse himself. His family poured water upon him as if quenching a parched thing, yet within three days, he wasted away to skin and bones. He then entered his chamber, covered himself with a quilt, and his body vanished.

Beneath the quilt, the body remained intact, head and feet complete—like the shed husk of a cicada.

From the *Dongwei Annals*: Li Shouzhong, serving as Imperial Messenger, was dispatched to the southern lands. Upon reaching the borders of Qiongzhou, he encountered an old man who called himself Yang Xiajv, aged eighty-one. Yang invited Li to his home, where Li met his father, Ri Shulian, aged one hundred twenty-two, and his grandfather, Song Qing, aged one hundred ninety-five. During their conversation, Li noticed a small child peering out from beneath a chicken coop. Song Qing explained: "This is our ancestor of nine generations past. For several lifetimes, he has neither spoken nor eaten. We do not know his age, but on the first and fifteenth of each month, we lift him down, and the descendants line up to bow before him."

Liang Xu’s piercing gaze, Li Yuan’s harmonious breath.
From the *Baopuzi*: The Daoist Liang Xu, of unknown origin, heard that chewing betel nut could reverse aging and restore youth, and that it allowed one to see ghosts clearly at night. At seventy, he began consuming it and grew ever more vigorous. By one hundred forty, he could run alongside galloping horses. Later, he withdrew into the Green Cloud Mountain.
From the *Xianzhuan Shiyi*: Li Ziyuan dwelled in a stone chamber on Mount Lishang, studying alchemical scriptures and dedicating himself to the Dao. Suddenly, an immortal descended riding a ram and taught him the secret of *qi*-harmonizing breath. After years of practice, he ascended to heaven in broad daylight. The mountain where he lived is now called the Tenth Transformation. Below flows the Mian River, and the rocks of the mountain still bear the hoofprints of divine rams.

Xun Huan rode a crane, Ruan Qiong shattered a turtle.
From the *Suiyi Ji*: Xun Huan was visiting the Yellow Crane Tower in Jiangxia when he saw something drifting from the southwest—a visitor astride a crane, clad in feathered robes and rainbow garments. Host and guest conversed joyfully. Then, bidding farewell, the visitor mounted the crane, soared into the sky, and vanished from sight.
From the *Yuren Xianhua*: The Perfected Master Zhao was skilled in sending flying talismans to heal people. A girl in the household of Ruan Qiong had been bewitched by a spirit. Her parents summoned physicians and diviners, but none could identify the culprit. Ruan then begged the master for help. The master said: "There is no need for me to go in person. Simply affix my talisman to your door or window, and you will see proof." Ruan placed the talisman on the door. That night, at the first watch, a sound like a heavy blow rang out. He lit a candle and looked—there lay a massive turtle, writhing on the ground. Soon it died, and the talisman vanished. Ruan crushed the turtle’s head and cast it into a ditch. The girl recovered shortly after.

A gallant knight d

Field Xuan cherished a stone fragment, while Yang Qing treasured a bamboo sliver.

From the *Accounts of Divine Transcendents*: Field Xuan lived in reclusion on Mount Heming when he encountered a white-robed divine person who gave him a stone fragment, saying, "Swallow this, and you will never hunger." Xuan consumed it, thereafter attaining the Dao and entering the mountains, never to emerge again.

From the *Sequel to the Accounts of Divine Transcendents*: Yang Qing passed the imperial examinations and was appointed magistrate of Le’an in Taizhou. He abandoned his post and entered the mountains, where he met a crimson-robed figure who called himself Lingying. Lingying led him into a grotto, where he obtained lingzhi and consumed it. "You possess the destiny of a transcendent," Lingying said. He then introduced Yang to Lord Mao, who declared, "Though you bear the bones of a transcendent, you have yet to ascend. You must cultivate on earth." With that, he sent Yang forth. Thereafter, Yang abstained from grain, his body grew light, and when he shook his joints, they emitted the sound of bamboo slats striking a board. He could drink three *sheng* of wine and travel three hundred *li* in a day.

Gang styled himself the Gate of Heaven, while Ba called himself the Valley of Ghosts.

From the *Accounts of Divine Transcendents of the Wang Clan*: Wang Gang styled himself the Master of the Celestial Gate. Well-versed in the methods of nourishing life, he composed a scripture stating: "Yang arises in *yin* (the third earthly branch), the essence of pure Wood; Yin arises in *shen* (the ninth earthly branch), the essence of pure Metal. When Wood is cast into Metal, nothing remains unharmed. Women who use rouge and powder follow the whiteness of Metal. Thus, the Perfected attends to the mysteries, discerning the waxing and waning of forces: I move as the Azure Dragon, while they move as the White Tiger. Before them lies the Vermilion Bird, behind me the Dark Warrior—this is the Way of Undying."

The Master of the Celestial Gate practiced this Way and lived to one hundred eighty years, his complexion like that of a child; he has now ascended to the Dark Continent to become a Perfected One.

Moreover, Wang Fu studied the Dao under Lord Lao, entered Stone Beam Mountain to ingest medicinal herbs, and his countenance remained like that of a child. Later, he moved to Ghost-Nurturing Mountain and styled himself the Master of the Searching Valley. At that time, Su Qin and Zhang Yi sought his instruction. The Master said: "Hearing the Dao is easy; cultivating the Dao is difficult." The two studied for three years before taking their leave. The Master said: "You have labored with four-horse carriages, and your achievements and fame now blaze forth. Have you not seen the trees by the river? Chariots and horses break their branches, waves and rapids erode their roots—such is the fate of those who dwell there. Have you not seen the plants of Mount Song and the pines of Huayin? Their leaves and trunks rise into the azure clouds, untouched by axes or saws; black foxes and red leopards shelter beneath them, patterned crows and vermilion phoenixes roost upon their ridges—such is the fate of those who dwell there. Alas! You two have scorned the enduring longevity of the catalpa and pine, prizing instead the fleeting splendor of a single day. How lamentable!"

Nanchang resigned from office; Yuanyu fled from salary.

From the *True Sayings*: Lord Nanchang of You formerly served as the Spirit Pass Marquis of the Southern Vermilion Yang Celestial Gate under the Northern Emperor. Later, he was transferred to the position of Director of the High and Bright Bureau. In the past, he was dismissed from office for his involvement in the affair of Liu Qingsun; now, he is finally to be reinstated.

From the *Biographies of Recluses of the Jin*: Guo Ma, styled Yuanyu, possessed an extraordinary resolve from a young age. He secluded himself in the Reed Valley of Linsong, carving out a stone grotto to dwell in and consuming *qiao* fruit to lighten his body. Zhang Tianxi dispatched the emissary Meng Gongming bearing credentials, a carriage lined with rushes, and fragrant herbs to summon him with full honors. When Gongming arrived at the mountain, Ma pointed to a soaring swan-goose and said: "How could such a bird be caged?" He then fled deep into the wilderness, refusing all advancement. Gongming detained his disciples, whereupon Ma sighed: "I fled from salary not to evade punishment. How could I, in my seclusion and practice of righteousness, bring harm to my disciples?" He then emerged and accepted the summons.

The mountain elder inscribed talismans; the Daoist drew talismanic diagrams.

From *Tales of the Marvelous*:

Gao Yu wove a boat and anchored at Bright Pool. One night, he peered at the water’s surface and saw three large lotus leaves afloat, upon which three beautiful women sat. They spoke among themselves: "Bright Pool has no bottom, and Tangerine Isle drifts—this is no empty saying." Each then declared her preference: one would study the Buddhist teachings, another the Dao, and the third the Confucian classics. The next day, they divined what food they would receive: "Each according to her inclination."

At dawn, a monk indeed rowed to the center of the current—only to drown. Soon after, a Daoist and a scholar drowned as well, all three perishing in quick succession. Presently, a boat arrived bearing an official who announced himself as the Scribe of Qiyang Mountain. Gao Yu, bewildered by these events, recounted them to the scribe, who grew furious: "How dare you meddle thus?" He took a cinnabar brush, inscribed a talisman, and ordered his disciple to carry it into the depths of the pool. The disciple walked as though on level ground until he reached a great stone cavern, where three white silkworms rested upon an outer boulder. At the sight of the talisman, they transformed into three women in white robes and pleaded: "We beseech the Celestial Master—grant us three days to relocate." The scribe raged: "You must depart by tomorrow, or I will dispatch the Six Ding spirits with swords to slay you."

The next day, black clouds churned with wind and rain, and three great fish surged forth in the current, vanishing downstream.

*The Records of Spirits Searching*: Xu Mao, a man of Wu, was fond of alchemical transmutation. One day, he encountered a Daoist who hung a painted fan upon the wall. There was a crucible, and a boy stood atop it. The Daoist called to the boy, who knelt before the crucible. The painted fan swayed repeatedly, and the flames of the crucible blazed brightly. After a short while, the elixir was complete. The Daoist said: "The alchemical arts of transmutation are governed by the reckoning of Heaven and Earth; they cannot be sought without accumulating merit and piling up good deeds." He then told Xu Mao: "Fifty years hence, you shall seek me on Mount Mao." Then he vanished without a trace.

Wu Gang hews the moon; Cai Dan weeds the lingzhi. *Miscellaneous Morsels from Youyang*: An old tradition holds that within the moon stands a cassia tree, a hundred *zhang* tall. Beneath it, the marks of an axe heal as soon as they are made. The man’s surname was Wu, his given name Gang, and he had erred while studying the Dao. Some say the cassia in the moon is but the shadow of the earth, and the empty spaces are the reflection of water—there is nothing there in truth, and this explanation is rather closer to reason. *The Master Who Embraces Simplicity*: Cai Dan loved the Dao and abandoned his home to enter the mountains. Unable to endure the hardship, he returned and deceived his family, saying: "I have become an earthbound immortal, low in rank, herding dragons for the Lord Lao." Later, while playing a game of divine chess, he lost a multicolored, striped dragon and was thus demoted to Kunlun Mountain to weed lingzhi.

Junping wields his divining brush; Wang lacks shamans and physicians. *Supplement to the Biographies of Immortals*: Yan Zun, styled Junping, was a native of Chengdu in Shu Commandery. He was deeply devoted to the teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi, widely read in the classics, and made his living through divination. When he spoke to a man’s son, his words were grounded in filial piety; to a man’s younger brother, in fraternal duty; to a man’s minister, in loyalty. Yan was skilled in composition and, following the principles of Laozi and Zhuangzi, wrote a work in ten scrolls with a hundred chapters, titled *The Pointing Finger*. Later, his entire household ascended to Heaven, and even his house and pavilions flew away with them. *Biographies of Lofty Scholars*: Wang Zhi of Anqiu, styled Zhongdu, was a native of Changling. In his youth, he studied the *Laozi*, was tranquil and content, and sought no advancement. At the time, he was known as the Venerable Man of Anqiu. When Emperor Cheng heard of his reputation and wished to see him, Wang Zhi declined and refused the audience, instead serving as a shaman-physician among the people.

Jieyu bears the fruit of trees; Qiugong chews pine resin. *Biographies of Immortals*: Jieyu was a man of Chu who delighted in nurturing life. He ate the fruits of grasses and trees and wandered Mount Emei. For generations, people saw him, and he lived for several hundred years. Liu Xiang composed a eulogy for him: "Jieyu delights in the Dao, nurturing his nature in hidden radiance. He satirized Confucius, discoursing on the decline of the phoenix. Embracing the primordial to harmonize, preserving his heart in subtlety. He strides the sacred peaks, chanting long upon Emei." Also, Qiugong was a man of unknown origin who, during the time of Tang, worked as a carpenter. Though he had already lived three hundred years, his complexion grew ever more robust. People knew of his longevity and all revered him as a teacher. They saw him eat nothing but pine resin.

Cangshen heals the crane; Lingxu rides the tortoise. *Records of Strange Matters*: Qiang Shen was utterly filial in serving his mother. When a crane was struck by an arrow, he took it in, nursed it back to health, and released it once its wound had healed. Later, the crane returned with its mate, bearing a luminous moon-pearl in its beak by night as thanks. *Biographies of Eminent Daoists*: Yu Lingxu dwelled on Mount Heng for over a decade. He encountered a divine person who taught him the method of the Returning Wind. After practicing it, he could sit and perceive all affairs under Heaven as though viewing his own palm. Yet he concealed his abilities, refusing to astonish the common folk, and no one knew of his powers. He kept a great tortoise, which he called Yuan Dian, four or five *chi* in width. Those who saw it were terrified. One day, his master rode this tortoise into the Nine Nong Mountains and did not emerge. Woodcutters sometimes glimpsed him there.

Congshan borrows a horse; Zhu Chong returns the calf. *Biographies of Eminent Daoists*: Liu Congshan, styled Shuntian, studied under the Daoist Wang Taihe at the Jianlong Abbey during the Xiangfu era. In the third year of the Zhihe reign, when Emperor Renzong fell ill, he summoned Congshan to the Great Celebration Hall to present a memorial. The emperor ordered his ministers to bow in his stead upon its completion. The ministers asked: "Has the memorial reached its destination?" Congshan replied: "The memorial has been delivered." By then, the emperor’s celestial retinue had already departed for some time, but suddenly his spirit cleared, and he sent word asking: "What deity is General Ge?" Congshan answered: "Beneath the Three Heavens’ gates stands a deity named General Ge." The emperor then ordered that an altar be swiftly prepared for offerings. The next day, his health was restored, and he told his attendants: "Last night, I arrived at the gates of Heaven, where a certain General Ge said the emperor was not yet fated to come. He urgently lent me a horse to send me back, and so I awoke." *Unofficial History of the Jin*: Zhu Chong, styled Jurong, was known for his perfect conduct from a young age. He was serene, restrained in desire, and fond of learning, though poor. He often tilled the fields and tended crops. Once, a neighbor lost a calf and, mistaking Zhu’s calf for his own, took it home. Later, he found his calf beneath the trees and, deeply ashamed, returned the borrowed calf to Zhu, who refused to accept it. Whenever an imperial edict arrived, he would flee into the deep mountains. His contemporaries regarded him as a latter-day Liang Hong or Guan Zhong.

Dong the Daoist draws his bed; Wang Yao builds his prison. *Supplement to the Biographies of Immortals*: The immortal Dong the Daoist begged for wine and danced as he sang, wandering the world. In the Dazhong era, he often stayed in Hezhong Prefecture. When merchants found him drunk and sleeping by their doors, their wares would sell out at once. People revered him as a divine immortal. Sometimes he feigned madness, singing: "Lotus threads weave a myriad layers of silk; the immortal’s heart is full of cares." When he encountered heavy snow, he would draw a bed in the snow with his staff and lie upon it. If asked whether he was cold, he would reply: "The pines and cedars of the deep mountains fear not the endless snow." His words flowed like verse, and most were of this kind. *Divine Immortals of the Wang Clan*: Wang Yao, styled Boliao, was a native of Poyang. He was quite skilled in healing illnesses, and none he treated failed to recover. Yet he performed no sacrifices, used no talismanic water or acupuncture, and did not prescribe medicine. When treating the sick, he would simply spread an eight-*chi* cloth upon the ground and sit. He neither ate nor drank, and after a short while, the patient would recover and rise, whereupon he would depart.

Where malevolent spirits wrought harm, Yao would trace a prison upon the earth, summoning them forth—all appeared in their true forms within the bounds, be they fox, hare, or serpent. He would then behead them or burn them to ashes, and the afflicted would instantly recover.

Wang Guo loathed the dust of the world, while Yuanjian cut himself off from the common.

*Divine Immortals of the Wang Clan*:
Wang Guo, a worthy man of Chu, despised the filth of worldly winds and the stench of fame and profit. He withdrew into the mountain forests, cultivating stillness and humility in all his deeds. One day, he ascended upon a cloud and departed.

*Record of the True Realm*:
During the Tang, the Ritual Master White, named Yuanjian (his courtesy name unknown), was a native of Chengdu in Western Shu. In the year the Illustrious Emperor fled to Shu, he received ordination under a special dispensation and resided at the Upper Emperor’s Abbey. His resolve was to sever all ties with the mundane, wandering freely and soaring afar. Light as the wind, he drifted through the years, traversing rivers and lands as suited him, following the footsteps of recluses of old. At last, he settled at the Abbey of Heaven’s Pillar in Yuhang, where he vanished in the Yuanhe era.

Ziming’s tiles turned to gold; Li’s stones became jade.

*The Transmission of the Celestial Masters*:
Zhang Ci, styled Ziming, was the sixteenth-generation descendant of the Celestial Master.
