Inheriting the true methods of the Perfected, he annually transmitted the esoteric registers of all ranks during the Three Primes. Possessing Daoist arts, he could transmute tiles into gold—some cast into water would ignite flames, others revert to tiles. Later, when he departed this world, celestial music resounded in the void.

*Idle Tales of Rustics*:
The Perfected Lord Li Tuo of Changli Mountain in Hanzhou, whose given name is lost to record, had refined "water jade" and the Nine-Florate Elixir in these mountains since the early Western Zhou. Three journeys and returns spanned over eight hundred years, earning him the name "Li Eight-Hundred." Once the elixir was perfected, he could coat stones to turn them into jade and transform grains of sand into pearls. Even now, after rains, one may still gather five-colored true pearls there. During the Jianwu era of the Later Han, he ingested the elixir and ascended upon a dragon. The site of his alchemical refinement remains, its stone walls suffused by the elixir’s qi, all transformed into hues of gold and jade, radiant beyond compare. A rectangular impression, over a foot long, resembles a human footprint. Later, a Buddhist temple was erected there, and the monks falsely claimed it to be the footprint of Mahākāśyapa. Though the tale has endured through the ages, it was in truth the site where the Perfected Li Tuo refined "water jade."

Yuanhua subdued ghosts; the Immortal Elder flogged shamans.

*The Transmission of Eminent Daoists*:
The Daoist Zhang Yuanhua, whose origins are unknown, was once summoned to a tavern by a guest. When Yuanhua declined wine and meat, citing his sacred registers, the guest grew irate. Yuanhua scrutinized him and discerned his inhuman nature. "Pray, return briefly," he said, "and await me." Drawing his sword, he lured the creature beyond the city walls, commanded it to sit, and slew it—revealing a ghost with crimson hair and a blue face, no larger than a child of five or six. He carried its head to show the people, declaring, "This ghost dared deceive me, so I executed it to rid the people of harm."

*The New Record of the Elixir Terrace*:
The Immortal Elder Ge Yuanxing passed through Wukang, where the host lay gravely ill. A female shaman was summoned to invoke spirits, who demanded the elder drink wine. When he refused, the spirit spoke insolently. "How dare you?" the elder said. He then commanded the Five Marshals to seize and drag the spirit out, flogging its back. Though no one was visible, the sound of lashes filled the air, and blood flowed from the unseen form. By the time they reached the central courtyard, the spirit was seen embracing a wooden post, stripped of its garments. The host’s illness was cured.

The First Emperor drove stones; the Jade Maiden played pitch-pot.

*Records of the Bizarre*:
The First Emperor of Qin constructed a stone bridge across the sea to observe the sunrise. When the laborers moved the stones too slowly, a divine being whipped them until they bled. To this day, the bridge’s stones retain a reddish hue.

*The Record of the True Realm* further states:
Beside the road to the Dongxiao Abbey in Lin’an, atop a stone cliff, an inscription records: The First Emperor, resentful that mountains blocked the Eastern Sea, commanded ghostly soldiers to relocate this peak. As the mountain began to shift, a transcendent suddenly appeared, rebuking the ghosts and pressing his body against the mountain to halt its movement. To this day, imprints of his shoulders, hairpin, and cap remain deeply etched into the cliff.

*The Biographies of the Immortals*:
Shu Wangfu played pitch-pot with a Jade Maiden. Each toss encompassed twelve hundred isles. Should a throw miss, Heaven would emit a sharp, hissing sigh—like laughter.

King Mu’s Eight Steeds and the Magistrate’s Twin Apparitions.
According to the *Primary Account*: King Mu of Zhou, enamored of the Dao of transcendents, harnessed his eight steeds to ascend Kunlun Mountain, where he partook of the fruit of the royal tree. After paying homage to the Queen Mother of the West and receiving the secret of ascending to Heaven, he later cast off his mortal form to demonstrate to the people that all things must end.

*The Divine Immortals of the Wang Clan* records:
Wang Qiao, magistrate of Ye County during the reign of Emperor Ming of Han, was guarded by divine attendants. On the first and fifteenth of each month, he would journey to the capital to pay court to the emperor. Perplexed by the frequency of his visits yet the absence of any carriage or escort, the emperor secretly ordered the Grand Scribe to observe him. The scribe reported that as Wang approached, a pair of hares would arrive from the southeast. When the hares appeared, they were caught in a net—only to reveal themselves as a pair of black crows, which were in fact the shoes bestowed upon him by the emperor four years prior as a gift for the Minister of Works.

The Grand Void Receives the Seal; Daoquan Bears the Talisman.
*The Supplement to the Biographies of Immortals* states:
Cheng Taixu, a native of Xichong in Guozhou, devoted himself to stillness and solitude, dwelling on Mount Min in the south. He fasted and sat in oblivion until one evening, when a violent wind uprooted trees, thunder crashed, and lightning split the sky amid torrential rain. In the courtyard, a hollow in the earth bubbled with boiling water. Stirring it with his staff, he retrieved two jade seals with double knobs, which proved remarkably efficacious. Each year, people from far and near sought his aid, and those who received talismans from him would bring them to his door to be stamped with the seal. The recipients flourished, their wealth and fortune increasing, and with it, they relieved the poor and aided the needy, to universal acclaim.

Another figure, Yin Daoquan, cultivated the Dao of *Dongzhen* on Mount Heng, refining his spirit and attaining the power of penetrating vision. He bore the talismans of the Five Emperors and the Six Jia, as well as the *Left and Right Spirit-Flight* talismans. Heavenly immortals descended to him and declared: "Those who ascend in broad daylight must possess both talent and the Way. Emperor Wu of Han, Liu Che, was moved by descending immortals who bestowed upon him the *True Forms of the Five Peaks* and the *Twelve Matters of Spirit-Flight*, yet he only attained the Dao of *shijie*—casting off his corpse—without achieving the full transformation of body and bones. You have received but one of these, yet your aspiration for transcendence is clear. This is the fruit of your diligent cultivation, as well as the destiny allotted to you in past lives."

When asked about the *Twelve Matters of Spirit-Flight*, the immortals replied: "The *Spirit-Flight* was originally bestowed by the Metal Mother to enable Emperor Wu to pacify the Five Peaks and bless the myriad people. Yet he ultimately failed to perfect the supreme transformation of non-action, instead indulging in militarism and exhausting his armies, spilling blood and slaughtering lives. He thus forfeited his own fortune and could not, like the Yellow Emperor or Yu the Great, ride dragons, shed his form, and vanish into the light. This was his loss."

Zhou Fu the Pavilion Keeper; Ding Du the Master of the Seal.
*The True Sayings* records:
The current Pavilion Keeper of the Southern Gate is Zhou Fu. At the Post Pavilion, there are two pavilion keepers, while the Fourfold Gate of the Luminous Mirror has four Gatekeepers of the Four Directions. The Gate of Heaven alone has nearly eight Gatekeepers of the Four Directions.

*The Comprehensive Record of Marvels* states:
During the Qingli era, a court official set out before dawn to attend the morning audience. On the main thoroughfare, he saw over thirty beautiful women, resplendent in fine attire, riding two by two as if leading a procession. Soon after, Ding Guanwen Du, surrounded by his retinue, followed on horseback. The official exclaimed in surprise: "Ding is usually so frugal—why does he have so many concubines?" A man riding at the rear replied: "Not so. These are immortal women come to welcome the Master of the Hibiscus Pavilion." At the time, Ding was on leave from court, and shortly afterward, news of his death arrived.

The Old Man of the Southern Pole; the Maiden of the Western River.

*The True Dao Annotations* states:
In the *Primal Record of the Seven Sages*, the Red Lord descended to deliver teachings, transforming his appearance to that of a śramaṇa, and together with his six disciples, revealed their names and identities. It is also said: in the primordial qi, he is the Primal Lord; in the Primal Palace, he is the Primal Marshal; at the Southern Asterism, he is the Old Man of the Southern Pole; in the Great Void, he is the Perfected of the Great Void; on Mount Nanyue, he is Master Redpine. He is the teacher of the Four Perfected of the Celestial Thearch and the companion of the Great One.

*The Biographies of Female Immortals* records:
The Maiden of the Western River was the niece of Boshanfu, a divine immortal. Through diligent cultivation and ingestion of elixirs, she lived for over two hundred years, her appearance growing ever more youthful. Seeing her nephew afflicted by illness in his youth, she gave him medicine. Though he was already seventy, he gradually regained his youth, his complexion like that of an infant. During the Han dynasty, an emissary passing through the Western River region saw a woman outside the city walls striking an old man with white hair, who knelt to receive the blows. The emissary, astonished, inquired about this. She replied: "This is my son. Long ago, my uncle Boshanfu attained the Dao of divine immortals. Taking pity on my frequent illnesses, he gave me divine medicine, and I gradually regained my youth. This son of mine refused to take the medicine I offered, and so has grown decrepit with age. I am angry with him, and thus punish him." The emissary asked their ages. The woman answered: "I am two hundred and thirty years old."

Silver flowers rained from the heavens; stalactites were tapped for their essence.

*The New Record of the Elixir Terrace* states:
Yao Tan, styled Yuantai, was once honored by Duke Xiang of Jin, who knelt and faced north to call him teacher. He savored the Dao in the cool breeze for many years. One day, his disciples went out and saw silver flowers falling from the sky in profusion, scattering upon the earth. After a long while, they vanished. That day, the Primal Lord of the Great Simplicity dispatched immortals to welcome him, bestowing upon him scriptures and appointing him Perfected of the Mystic Continent, overseeing the White Water Palace.

*The Biographies of the Immortals* records:
Koushu was a scribe of Zhou. Skilled in circulating qi and refining his form, he boiled stalactites to ingest their essence, calling it "stone bell milk," and lived for several hundred years. He came and went within Mount Taishi, where an outer stone bed served as his pillow. When the summer winds arose, Liu Qingyun would ascend.

*The Unofficial History of Jin* records:
Xia Tong, styled Zhongyu, was a native of Kuaiji. During the Shangsi Festival, when all the nobles and officials of Luozhong, down to the common gentry, gathered at the floating bridge, with men and women thronging the roads and carriages and robes illuminating the way, Tong sat in his boat, paying no heed. The Grand Commandant Jia Chong found this strange and questioned him. Tong slowly replied, "I am Xia Zhongyu of Kuaiji." Chong said, "In ancient times, even Yao sang, and Shun sang. Could you sing a song and let others harmonize with you?" Tong replied, "In the assemblies of former lords, the grace of the myriad states spread like clouds, and the sagely transformation still lingers in the people’s songs of gratitude." Thereupon, he composed a song of longing. Then, striking his foot against the boat and lifting his voice in a resonant, impassioned melody, a great wind arose in response. When he shouted, thunder and lightning gathered in darkness; when he let out a long whistle, sand and dust swirled into the air. The nobles turned to one another and said, "Had we not traveled to Luoyang, how could we have seen such a man?" Tong later returned to Kuaiji, and his ultimate fate remains unknown.

Master Wu’s *Discourse on the Mystic Principles* states:
Someone asked, "In ancient times, there were divine immortals. Why are they not seen today?" The answer: "The pure and the turbid flow in separate streams, and the subtle workings of the true differ in their realms. How could they be seen?" It also says, "Lingwei returned briefly after a thousand years, and Hunyuan has descended repeatedly even to this day. How can you say they are no longer seen?" Again, it asks, "Who are the immortals of today?" The answer: "Since the Tang dynasty, they may be briefly described: Liu Qingyun ascended in the land of Shu, Wei Shilong soared in Songyang, Dao merged with the cicada’s molting in Taiyi, and Dongyuan’s bones took flight in distant lands. The imperial transformation reverses aging, and Qi Yi returns to the true."

*The Master Who Embraces Simplicity* records:
Huanghua, known as Lingzi, attained the method of returning to the origin and reversing aging. His scripture states: "This method can repel weapons, guard one’s household, protect children and bless descendants. Those who see it rejoice; those who do not long for it. It aligns with the hearts of the divine and wins the favor of the people." He lived among men for over five hundred years, yet his complexion grew ever more youthful.

*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* records:
The Daoist Zhang Qizhen, styled Qi Yi, was a native of Qiantang. At the time, King Zhongyi revered the Daoist arts and, every third and eighth day of the lunar month, held purification fasts, entrusting Qizhen with their oversight. During the Taiping Xingguo era, Emperor Taizong established the Taiyi Palace and summoned the purest scholars from across the land to reside there. Qizhen was selected and summoned to the imperial palace, where he again found favor. Later, the emperor, noting errors in the Daoist scriptures, ordered scholars from both streets to correct them, and Qizhen once more took part. One day, as he lay down to rest, he vaguely saw a crimson-robed official arrive with a tally, saying, "By command, Zhang is to swiftly purify himself of filth and proceed to that place to take up his duties." At dawn, he summoned his disciples and told them, "I am about to depart. Remember this well." Then, in tranquility, he returned to the true.

Meng refined the hun and po; Elder Wang cultivated the spirit.

In the *Declarations of the Perfected*: Shan Shiyuan received the teachings of Master Meng. At dusk, he would first recite the *Scripture of the Yellow Court* once, then retire, allowing his hun and po to refine themselves. By practicing this Way for twenty-one years, he too attained immortality—this is the method of reciting it ten thousand times, though even three or four recitations suffice.

The Lady of Northern Peak, Jiang, recited this scripture, which also preserved health and granted immunity to illness—this is the Way of not dying.

In the *Records of Divine Immortals of the Wang Clan*: Elder Wang’s origins were unknown. He befriended Feng Junda and sought the Dao among the famous mountains. There, a divine being told him, "Your spirit moves heaven. The Most High has sent me to transmit to you the secret of transcending the world. Yet the Dao of immortality is not distant—it is near, drawn from your own body. Without thought, without care, neither exhaling nor inhaling, the true unity fills the within, and you shall attain long life and ascend in flight.

Thoughts and cares flit about, wearying your form. The Most High abides in unbroken continuity, effortless in its application—this is the true Dao."

With these words, the divine being ascended to heaven.

To save a friend with talismans, to gather kin in honor of ancestors.

From the *Supplementary Records of Immortals*: Mu Jiangfu was devoted to the Dao and unmoved by fame or profit. He befriended Tiao of the Eastern Ford of Chang’an, and whenever he visited, they would drink wine and discourse on the Dao for days and nights on end. One day, Tiao suddenly died, throwing his household into panic. They sent for Jiangfu, who arrived at night and, upon hearing the news, smiled and said, "He can be saved." He then removed his robe and lay beside Tiao beneath the covers, cautioning the family not to cry out in alarm but to respond when called. Blowing out the candle, he slept. At midnight, he relit the candle to find Tiao sitting up, saying, "Just now, a messenger in yellow robes took me away, but soon I heard an order: 'The Great One has sent a swift envoy to bring him back.'" Tiao then recovered as before. Jiangfu vanished without a trace.

Elsewhere, Huang Fengxian of Mengyang once entered Geqi Mountain, where a Daoist taught him arts of transformation. A wealthy man of the same county, surnamed Song, gave his daughter to him in marriage. Song’s relatives were numerous, and their visits for feasts and greetings lasted over a month without end. One day, the relatives jested, "We wonder what sort of banquet Huang will prepare for his in-laws." Fengxian promptly replied, "Tomorrow, I shall modestly prepare wine and dishes—may all of you attend." That day, over eighty guests from the neighborhood arrived, visiting his home daily. Though Fengxian had made no prior arrangements, his courtyard suddenly appeared immaculate, the tables laden with exquisite delicacies, and the decorations—paintings and ornaments—unlike anything found in western Shu. All was wondrous beyond compare. The guests were awestruck as music played softly, and they drank to intoxication. Those who tried to leave found two serpents coiled around the pillars at the door, and in terror, none dared depart. Later, when the truth became known, Fengxian, ill at ease with his newfound fame, moved his family to Taihua Mountain in Dongchuan.

The maiden absorbs qi, the perfected rides the clouds.

From the *True Sayings*: Weizi was the daughter of Zhang Qing. While in the *Yi Qian* office, she often ingested misty qi, claiming it was the essence of mountains, marshes, water, and fire, the vital breath of metals and stones. With prolonged practice, she could dissolve her form into emptiness and merge her body with the clouds.

From the *Records of Divine Immortals of the Wang Clan*: Wang Daozhen, a man of the Han, attained the Dao and dwelled on the eastern ancient terrace of Guigu Mountain. A white cloud often emerged from the terrace, appearing from afar like a towering pavilion. Daozhen would ride this cloud to play atop the mountain, returning at dusk. The white cloud would then withdraw into the terrace—this was the Guigu Mountain north of Qinghan in Jingzhou. Some mistakenly call it the Yang Terrace, but this is incorrect.

Yin loses his parents’ care, Wu lacks their sweet nourishment.

From the *Biographies of Eminent Daoists*: The Daoist Yin Tong, styled Lingjian, was well-versed in classics and history. He often lamented the impermanence of worldly things and would gaze at the heavens in sighs. His parents, disbelieving, pressed him to marry and pursue an official career. Kneeling, he pleaded, "I have heard that Zhang the Perfected ascended to heaven, and Zhen Nan followed in his footsteps. The Mao Lords attained the Dao, and the prefect submitted in sincerity. How much more, as descendants of the Great Ancestor, the Perfected of the Supreme Ultimate, should we not carry on this illustrious legacy? I beg to follow my calling." His parents relented, and he departed in a gust of wind. By the time he lost his parents, his filial duties were already fulfilled, yet his resolve to preserve the One and guard the truth only grew stronger with each passing day.

Emperor Taiwu of Wei, having heard of his reputation, held him in particular reverence.

*Ancient and Modern Poetic Talks* records: Wu Renbi traveled to Luofu Mountain, where he studied the Lao-Zhuang teachings under Master Zhang and grasped their essential meaning. Upon taking leave to return home, he planned to journey to the capital to sit for the examinations. The master said, "Observing your qi and destiny, you may remain here—I shall teach you the Dao of longevity." Renbi declined, citing his aged mother’s need for care, vowing to withdraw from worldly pursuits once he had achieved renown, for it would not be too late to study then. That same year, he passed the imperial examinations and traveled to Zhe to pay respects to Qian Wusuo, who received him with exceptional courtesy and invited him to join his staff. Renbi declined, expressing his refusal in verse:

*My tattered sable ill befits the lotus pavilion,*
*My frail form still shames the tortoiseshell pin.*
*Beneath the mountain moon, the Han’s glow spans ten leagues—*
*How can I bear, from this moment, to betray my longing for home?*

Wusuo ordered him to compose the *Record of Luocheng*, but Renbi refused. Enraged, Wusuo banished him to the river, and the people of Wu mourned his fate.

In the early Jianlong era, Ning Xu and others conducted a jiao ritual at Luofu Mountain. After the ceremony, they explored the mountain’s caves and grottoes. Upon reaching the summit, they saw a stone gate where an elderly scholar, clad in creeping fig and dodder, sat guarding the entrance. Xu inquired about the place, and the scholar replied, "This is the abode of the Master of Luofu." When asked who he was, the scholar answered, "I am Master Wu, named Qibi." With these words, the door closed, and nothing more was seen.

Wang Kuo’s wine was rich, and Yunsheng’s oranges were sweet.

*The Immortal Biographies of the Wang Clan* states: During the Xiantong era, Wang Kuo of Jingzhou embarked on a boat voyage across Dongting Lake. A fierce wind arose, forcing him to moor at the foot of Jun Mountain. He disembarked with several companions, and suddenly, the scent of wine filled the air. When he asked his companions, none detected it. Nearby, a cave appeared by the path. Kuo entered and, after a few steps, found a depression in the cave where the wine’s fragrance was intoxicating. He cupped his hands to drink and became pleasantly inebriated. From then on, he felt invigorated and free of illness, gradually losing his taste for grains. He entered the famous mountains to study the Dao. Later, in the *Scriptures of the Immortals*, he read: *"On Jun Mountain lies celestial wine; those who drink it ascend as immortals."* The wine he encountered was this very nectar.

*The Record of Extraordinary Persons* recounts: Chen Yunsheng entered Longhu Mountain. During the Tianyou era, he was seen on Magu Mountain—seventy years had passed since he left home, yet his appearance remained unchanged. The prefect welcomed him to the prefectural seat, where they sat together at night. Yunsheng remarked, "The oranges of Fengcheng are sweet; I have been craving them." Shortly after, he presented a hundred oranges.

Deng Yu observed the crows, and Shangqiu herded the swine.

*The Original Biography* states: Deng Yu secluded himself on Mount Heng, abstaining from grains for over thirty years. At night, he recited the *Great Grotto Scripture*, and in response, the Celestial Lady Wei of the Southern Marchmount descended to address him: "You possess the destiny of an immortal; I have come especially to visit you." One day, he suddenly saw three azure crows, crane-like in size, dancing, flying, and calling—after some time, they departed. Observing them, Yu said to his disciples, "The azure crows have come; the imperial audience is near." Thereupon, he dissolved his form and departed.

*The Biographies of Divine Immortals* recounts: Shangqiu Zixu delighted in herding swine. He often played an iron flute, and though seventy years old, he had no wife or children, yet his complexion showed no signs of aging. The villagers marveled at his extraordinary nature. When asked the essentials of his Way, he would only say: "Eat water-plantain and drink water—naturally, one does not hunger." Thus, he lived for over three hundred years.

Fu Wen savored yellow melons; Zhan Gong, white plums.

*The Biographies of Divine Immortals* states: Fu Xian’s origins were unknown. He often stayed in Ju, frequenting the shrines along the seashore. Suddenly, three immortals appeared in a shrine, playing a game of dice with melons as stakes. They hired him to carry several dozen yellow and white melons, instructing him to close his eyes. When he awoke, he found himself on Fangzhang Mountain, south of Penglai.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* records: In ancient times, during the reign of Gao Xin, there was an immortal named Zhan Shanggong who planted plums on the Hidden Dragon Ground of Mount Mao, filling the land with them. Zhan Gong now serves as the Right Guardian of the Nine Palaces. He often tells others that when he once lived below Huayang, he ate white plums of exquisite flavor. Though it seems but a short time since, three thousand years have already passed.

*Records of the Immortals of the Three Caverns*, Scroll Seventeen—concluded.

*Records of the Immortals of the Three Caverns*, Scroll Eighteen
Compiled by Daoist Chen Baoguang of the Orthodox Unity Tradition.

Lu dug for sustenance, while the Qu family buried coins.

*The Extensive Records* states: There was a man of Mount Lu who possessed Daoist arts. One day, he passed through the borders of Fuzhou and moored his boat at the estate of the scholar Lu. Someone remarked that the man of Mount Lu was no ordinary person, so Lu paid him a visit to seek an auspicious response. The man of Mount Lu said: "Do not travel this year, for calamity will strike at any moment. Behind the hall where you live lies a jar of coins, covered by a plank. These are not yours. The rightful owner is now only three years old. Beware of spending even a single coin, or misfortune will follow. Can you heed my warning?" Lu bowed in gratitude.

Once the man of Mount Lu departed and the ripples on the water had yet to settle, Lu laughed to his wife: "If the man of Mount Lu speaks thus, what more could I desire?" He ordered his servants to dig several feet into the ground, and indeed, they found a plank. Beneath it lay a large jar filled with scattered coins. Overjoyed, Lu and his wife gathered them, threading the coins with grass fibers. As they neared ten thousand, their children suddenly suffered unbearable headaches. Lu exclaimed: "Could the man of Mount Lu’s words be coming true?" He rushed on horseback to catch up, apologizing for breaking the precept. The man of Mount Lu grew angry: "You have invited misfortune upon your kin. Weigh the value of profit against the cost yourself." He poled his boat away without a backward glance. Lu galloped home, performed a ritual, and his children recovered.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* says: On Liangchang Mountain during the Han dynasty, there lived a family named Qu at its base. They were immensely wealthy, possessing hundreds of millions in wealth. They buried bronze vessels there, which remain to this day. There are also coins, located on a small hill to the northwest.

Xian’an of Purple Radiance, Bo’er of Red Lotus.

The *Original Biography* states: The Southern Wei Lady, named Hua Cun, styled Xian’an, received a visitation from the Perfected of the Great Ultimate in her chamber on the winter solstice at midnight. They set out wine and delicacies, presenting her with purple radiance of primordial clouds.

*The Supplement to Immortal Biographies* records: Zhang Bo’er cultivated the Dao on Stone Drum Mountain in Shu during the Western Jin. His devoted practice moved the immortals, who suddenly descended and said to him: "This mountain bears the herb of eternal life, the night-shining mushroom. Consuming one allows one to ascend to heaven in broad daylight. The herb grows by the water’s edge; at night, it appears as a red lotus." After years of arduous search, he found and ate it, gaining the ability to fly and see through all things. He then rode away on a red tiger, and none knew his whereabouts.

The Parrot of Gui Shun and the Cuckoo of Wen Xiang.

*The Return of the Uncanny* records: Liu Gui Shun, sailing from Baling, was caught in a storm and landed on Jun Mountain. After walking several li, thousands of parrots called out his name, saying: "Liu the Twelfth, buffeted by wind, you’ve reached this wondrous realm—what was misfortune has become beauty." Suddenly, two Daoists appeared and said: "You’ve been struck by the *gang* wind; why not hasten back?" They handed him a foot of silk and instructed: "Cover your eyes with this, and you shall depart at once." Gui Shun suddenly flew back to his boat.

*The Continuation of Immortal Biographies* records: Shang Wen Xiang styled himself "Seven-Sevens." When Zhou Bao governed the Western Zhe region, he treated Wen Xiang with great favor. Bao said to him: "The azaleas of Crane Forest Temple are renowned throughout the world. I once heard you sing while drunk: *‘Who brews the wine for distant journeys? / Who makes the flowers bloom in an instant?’* Can you make these flowers bloom for the Double Ninth Festival?" Wen Xiang replied: "Very well." The day before, he lodged at Crane Forest Temple. At midnight, a woman appeared and said: "I am commanded by the heavens above to oversee these flowers. They are not long for this mortal realm. Today, I open them for the Daoist." On the Double Ninth, the flowers indeed bloomed in radiant splendor. Later, amid the ravages of war, they returned to the celestial gardens.

The Three Flutes of Junqing and the Single String of Tai Zhen.

*The Extensive Records of the Uncanny* records: Lü Junqing moored at Jun Mountain on a moonlit night, drinking wine and playing the flute. Suddenly, a fishing boat drew alongside. An old man held a flute as thick as clasped hands and said: "This is the music of heaven; it cannot be played." He then produced a second flute, the size of those used in the world, and said: "This is the music of the grotto-heavens." Finally, he showed a third, as slender as a brush handle, and said: "This is the flute of mortals." He played the smallest one once or twice, and waves surged violently. After three or five notes, boats and oars danced upon the water. Terrified, Lü begged him to stop. The old man ceased playing and chanted:
*"An old man of the Xiang reads the Yellow and the Old,
Hand grasping purple reins, seated on emerald grass.
When spring arrives, he forgets the depths of the Xiang’s waters;
At dusk, he loses the road to Baling."*

*The Master Who Embraces Simplicity* records: Tai Zhen was the daughter of the Queen Mother of the West. She and the Lady of the Eastern Peak frequented Mount Tai, dwelling in deep seclusion where mortals could not tread. A thousand *zhang* above the earth, her abode held jade tables and golden treasures. Each time she plucked a single string of her zither, a hundred birds would gather, soaring and dancing to her music.

The Shattered Seal of Master Cui and the Opened Casket of Ling Zi.

*The Records of the Hall of Manifestation* records: Ten *li* east of the commandery, deep in the mountains, stood a Daoist abbey where the priest Yan resided. A stone coffer, three feet in length, was sealed with sturdy locks. It was said that when Master Yin ascended, he entrusted this casket to his disciples, warning them never to open it, lest great disaster befall them. When Prefect Cui arrived in the commandery and heard of this, he ordered the seal broken. Opening the coffer, he found only talismans and registers. Suddenly, Cui died violently. After three days, he revived and said: "I was seized by the officials of the underworld. Why did I open the Realized One’s stone coffer? Now the Celestial Emperor has decreed that my lifespan be shortened and five of my official appointments revoked. I have only two years left." Indeed, Cui died two years later.

*The New Records of the Alchemical Terrace* records: Fan Ling Zi was devoted to the Dao from his youth. After many years, he encountered Sima Jizhu, who was entering the mountains of Chang. For seven years, they dwelled in a stone chamber. In the northeast corner was a stone window, and each time Jizhu departed, he would say: "Do not open it." This warning was repeated many times. One day, Ling Zi could no longer resist and opened it. Below, he saw his family—parents, siblings—so near yet untouchable. Overcome with sorrow, he wept. When Jizhu returned, he sent Ling Zi home. Later, Jizhu entrusted him with a bronze casket, again cautioning him not to open it. Ling Zi once more disobeyed, and as before, he saw his family. Jizhu sent him away, and he never attained the Dao.

The Peerless Grace of Lü Hua and the Unmatched Beauty of Shao Yuan.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* records: E Lü Hua claimed to be from the Southern Mountains, though none knew what immortal she was. A woman of twenty or so, clad in blue, her countenance was of peerless grace. On the night of the tenth day of the eleventh month in the third year of the *Shengping* era, she descended to the home of Yang Quan. She presented him with a poem, a fire-washed cloth handkerchief, a gold hairpin, and a jade armlet. When asked about her identity, she said: "I am Luo Yu, a woman of the Dao from Nine Farmers Mountain. I have already lived nine hundred years."

*The Extensive Records* records: Cui Shao Yuan was the youngest daughter of Cui Gong, Prefect of Fenzhou during the Tang. Her mother dreamed of a divine figure clad in crimson, riding a green dragon and holding a purple coffer, which he presented amid azure clouds. Fourteen months later, she gave birth to Shao Yuan. At birth, an extraordinary fragrance filled the air. Her hair covered her eyes, and her ears reached her cheeks. Her beauty was unparalleled. She was once the Left Scribe of the Jade Emperor in the Heaven of No Desire.

The traces of Master Qiu’s crane linger, while Fangping sheds his form like a cicada’s molt.

*The Biographies of Divine Transcendents* records: Master Fuqiu once accompanied Wang Qiao on a journey to Wangwu Mountain, where they rested their cranes along the way. Wangwu Mountain bears a Terrace of Resting Cranes, upon which crane tracks remain to this day. There is also a secluded abode of Master Fuqiu, and the southern peak is now called Nanling.

*The Arrayed Biographies of Transcendents* states: The Daoist Wang Yuanzhi, styled Fangping, was recommended as a worthy and upright scholar and was repeatedly promoted to the position of Gentleman Attendant. Proficient in astronomy, he withdrew to live in the mountains and forests. When the Han emperor repeatedly summoned him, he refused to appear. The prefectural governor was ordered to escort him to the capital, yet Fangping sealed his lips and spoke not a word. He told his disciples: "My allotted years are nearly spent; tomorrow I shall depart." When the appointed time came, he dissolved without a trace, like a cicada shedding its husk.

Ji Jing refined his essence, while Qian Keng held his breath to still his qi.

*The Secret Formulae for Cultivating Perfection* states: The Daoist Ji Jing, at the age of one hundred and seventy-eight, remained robust and vigorous. Each morning, he would swallow the Jade Spring to polish his teeth. The Jade Spring refers to the saliva in the mouth. Upon waking but before rising, he would rinse his mouth until it was full of saliva and then swallow it. He would polish his teeth thirty-four times—this was called "refining the essence."

*The Biographies of Divine Transcendents* records: Qian Keng was none other than Peng Zu. Skilled in guiding and extending qi, whenever he fell ill, he would hold his breath to attack the afflicted area. His qi would course through his body, reaching even the tips of his fingers, and soon his body would be harmonized. He once said: "The superior adept transforms without external aid; the middling adept employs different coverings; the inferior adept relies on medicinal herbs. Yet even if one consumes a hundred medicines, it is not as good as solitary fasting." Later generations compiled his methods of guiding and absorbing qi into *The Scripture of Peng Zu*, which circulates in the world.

He Fang’s pulse-sight, Yuan Chao the dwarf.

*The Records of Origins and Transformations* states: At the end of the Tang, the scholar He Fang once purchased an ancient scroll. As he read it, he found a coiled strand of hair four inches in circumference within its pages, forming an endless loop. At the broken ends, more than a sheng of water dripped forth. When burned, it emitted the scent of hair. Fang often spoke of this to a Daoist, who exclaimed: "Alas! You are indeed of common bone—encountering this, you cannot ascend in feathered transformation. It is fate." According to the *Scriptures of the Transcendents*, if a white fish consumes the character for "divine transcendence" three times, it transforms into this object, called *maiwang* ("pulse-sight"). At night, one places the loop toward the star at the center of the sky, and the star’s envoy will descend at once, allowing one to seek the elixir of returning cinnabar. If this water is mixed and ingested, one’s bones will be exchanged, and one will ascend. Fang then examined the ancient scroll and found several worm-eaten passages. Upon deciphering their meaning, he realized they were all characters of the divine transcendents, and he prostrated himself in reverence.

*The Extensive Records* states: In the second year of Guangde of the Tang, the rebel Yuan Chao of Linhai County invaded Yongjia. His fleet was blown thousands of li away by the wind until they spied a distant mountain, verdant and towering, with walls and ramparts gleaming in five colors. They steered toward it and docked, finding the gates unbarred and no one within. In the chambers, they saw only over twenty Hu saddles, along with a great quantity of Shu brocade and golden vessels. As the rebels saw no one, they began to seize the items. Suddenly, a woman emerged from the golden city and said: "Are you not Yuan Chao’s followers? How have you come here? What use do these objects have for you, that you so recklessly take them?" She turned to the saddles and said: "Do you think these are for dogs? They are not—they are for dragons. You should return them at once." The rebels each returned the items they had taken. They then asked where they were, and she replied: "This is the place where the Compassionate Immortal of Mirror Lake cultivates the Dao."

Tao Kan’s cranes mourned; the Dao aligned, and locusts vanished.

*The Collected Writings of the Worthy* states: When Tao Kan was observing the mourning period for his mother, two visitors came to offer condolences. They departed without weeping and transformed into divine cranes, soaring into the heavens. The people of the time found this extraordinary.

*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* records: The Daoist Liu Daohe encountered a divine person who said to him: "I have heard that you are fond of the Dao and that your resolve and integrity are unyielding." He then bestowed upon him the talismans of the Alliance of Might for summoning and subduing spirits. Daohe received and swallowed them, and from then on, his Daoist arts never failed to take effect. When Emperor Gaozong was about to perform the Feng sacrifice at Mount Tai, the rain did not cease. The emperor ordered Daohe to perform exorcisms and prayers, and the skies soon cleared. He was favored with rewards, which he distributed to the poor and needy. When Luoyang suffered from a plague of flying locusts, Daohe presented his talismans to the officials, instructing them to distribute them throughout the region. The locusts vanished at once.

Qi Hui returned to his chamber; Huixu built a bridge upon the waves.

*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* states: The Daoist Qi Hui studied under Master Su and obtained the methods of the *One and the Cavern* and the *Art of the Dipper*. After practicing them for many years, he attained profound mastery. He once read in the scriptures of the transcendents that those who wished to become immortal guests should enter Mount Taibai. Thus, he selected his disciples who were devoted to the Dao and set out together. Upon arrival, they returned, and within his chamber, the laughter and conversation of divine transcendents could often be heard.

*The Extensive Records* states: Huixu was a monk of Guoqing Temple on Mount Tiantai. One day, while traveling with companions, they came to a stone bridge. Huixu crossed it and ascended a sheer cliff, where he saw an old man who asked: "It is said in the world that those who cross this bridge will see arhats. Where are the arhats?" The old man replied: "This place is a blessed land of divine transcendents, a lower palace of the Celestial Emperor, the Golden Court of the Land of Immortality. Here, the Lord of the Upper Perfected, Tongxiang, presides over thirty thousand ranked transcendents and three hundred perfected beings. Once a year, the Most High descends to this hall to assess the merits and practices of those who study the Dao across the world. This is no abode for arhats." The monk asked: "Can one study to become a transcendent?" The old man said: "Accumulate merit and refine your conduct, and you may ascend to heaven in broad daylight." He then led Huixu a few steps forward, and suddenly the old man vanished. In an instant, Huixu found himself before Guoqing Temple. Thereafter, he changed into Daoist robes and devoted himself to the study and refinement of elixirs. On Mount Zhongnan, he encountered the old man again and obtained an elixir. After consuming it, he ascended into the heavens. Some say the old man was none other than Zhang Guolao.

Nie Yu met Peng and Cai; Xie and Mei encountered Xiao.

*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* records: The Daoist Nie Shidao resided at Zhaozhen Abbey in Nanyi. One day, he entered the mountains in search of the Perfected Cai. As dusk fell, he saw a woodcutter and asked: "Where are you going?" Upon hearing that the Perfected Cai was secluded there, Nie expressed his wish to pay homage. The woodcutter said: "It is too late to reach him now. There is a household ahead where you may stay the night." Nie proceeded and saw a thatched cottage. A farmer there asked: "Where are you going?" Nie replied: "To seek the Perfected Cai." The farmer said: "The woodcutter you just met is the Perfected Cai." Nie sighed: "To meet a divine sage and not recognize him—is this fate?" The farmer offered him lodging, and Nie retired for the night. When dawn broke, the host had not yet risen, so Nie set out on his way. He encountered an old man who invited him to sit upon a rock. Asking where he was going, Nie recounted his purpose. The old man said: "The farmer you stayed with last night is the Perfected Cai’s son." Then he added: "Your Dao-qi is abundant, but your immortal bones are not yet fully formed." He plucked a blade of grass and gave it to Nie to eat. When Nie looked up, the old man had vanished. Upon returning, Nie told his fellow Daoists, who said: "In one journey into the mountains, you met three immortals. The old man was the Perfected Peng. You saw both the Perfected Cai and his son, as well as the Perfected Shan—surely this marks the height of cultivating the true."

*The Continued Biographies of Immortals* states: Xie Xiutong journeyed to Mount Jie, where a divine being told him: "The Jade Bamboo Mountain of Lu is a detached palace of the Controller of Destinies. You should go there to refine yourself." Xiutong followed this advice. One day, deep in Hanyuan, he suddenly passed a grand estate and saw a youth emerge. The youth asked: "Are you not Xie Xiutong? The Perfected Xiao and Mei have awaited you for a long time." He then led Xiutong to meet the two perfected beings. Xiutong bowed and said: "A mere ant in the dust of the world, I have long yearned for the Dao. Now my withered bones are revived, and I have gained hope of attaining the primordial assembly." The perfected beings then bestowed upon him pine needles and five ears of fine grain to eat. Later, Xiutong attained the Dao through *shijie* (release from the corpse). He remained in the world, unharmed, though Chang Zong suffered from illness.

*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* records: Tao Tianhuo was a native of Nan’an who lived by the sea. One day, the sea waters suddenly surged, and his family fled in panic to escape the disaster. Tianhuo had just been born, and his mother, carrying him, could not keep up. The whole family wept for him. When the waters receded and they returned, they found the child unharmed, nestled in the fork of a mulberry tree. Thus, he was named Tianhuo ("Heaven-Alive"). As he grew, he displayed keen insight and an abundance of true qi. During the reigns of Emperors Xian, Mu, Gong, and Wen of the Tang, he served as an Inner Offering Daoist. At that time, none among the high ministers and great officials did not revere and admire him. Later, he returned to the seashore and never emerged again.

*The Garden of Discourses* states: Chang Zong was ill, and Laozi asked him: "Your illness is severe. Have you no final teachings to leave your disciples?" Zong said: "When passing one’s hometown, one alights from the carriage—do you understand?" Laozi replied: "Is this not to say one should not forget one’s origins?" Zong said: "Ah, yes. When passing a tall tree, one quickens one’s pace—do you understand?" Laozi said: "Is this not to say one should respect the elderly?" Zong said: "Yes. Open your mouth—is my tongue still there?" Laozi said: "Yes." Zong asked: "Are my teeth still there?" Laozi said: "Gone." Zong said: "The tongue endures through softness, while the teeth perish through hardness. This is it. The affairs of the world are all encompassed herein." Zhang’s servant turned gold into an inkstone; Xie’s immortal wielded an iron brush.

*The Extensive Records* states: Jiang Sheng of Wu Commandery lived in reclusion on Mount Siming, where he devoted himself to refining elixirs. After many years, he had yet to succeed. One day, among the poor laborers he hired, he found a servant named Zhang Quansu, who spoke of divine elixirs. Jiang rebuked him: "You are a mere servant—do not speak recklessly." Zhang then took out a gourd and removed a single pellet, saying to Jiang: "This elixir can transmute earth into gold. May I borrow your inkstone to demonstrate?" Jiang did not believe him. That night, Zhang suddenly died. Before his body could be encoffined, it vanished, leaving only his clothes and belt behind. The inkstone, however, had turned to purple gold.

*The Gleanings of Forgotten Lore* states: He Xiangu resided in Lingling County of Yongzhou. When Teng Zijing was serving as prefect of Yueyang, one evening a great thunderstorm struck. After it cleared, three characters remained on the cliff of the western peak of Huarong: "Xie Xian Huo" ("Xie the Immortal’s Fire"). The script was strange and wondrous. The prefect ordered the characters copied and inquired about their meaning, but no one knew. Someone said that He Xiangu of Lingling might know, so he sent an envoy to ask her. The immortal said: "This is a ghost from the Thunder Department. He and his brother both enjoy writing with iron brushes. The man is three feet tall." The prefect sent someone to verify this at the western peak, and it was indeed so.

Zhang Kuan observed the stars; Shan Sheng swallowed the sun.

*The Six Quilts* records: During the reign of Emperor Wu of Han, a woman was bathing in the Wei River, her breasts seven feet long. The emperor, astonished by her strangeness, sent someone to inquire. She replied: "The driver of the seventh carriage knows my origin." At the time, the Shu native Zhang Kuan was serving as a palace attendant. He answered: "The celestial star governs sacrifices. If rites are performed without purity, the Woman Star appears."

*The Scripture of Perfected Warrior* states: Long ago, Empress Shansheng dreamed of swallowing sunlight and gave birth to Perfected Warrior. Born divine and spirited, he grew fierce and valiant. Refusing the throne, he devoted himself to cultivation, aiding the Jade Emperor and vowing to sever all demons under heaven and protect all beings. Day and night within the royal palace, he made this oath, and his father could not restrain him. He abandoned his home, bid farewell to his parents, and entered the Wudang Mountains to cultivate the Dao for forty-two years. His merit complete and fruit ripe, he ascended to heaven in broad daylight.

Qu Yuan was exiled; Jia Yi was demoted.

*The Record of Forgotten Tales* recounts: Qu Yuan, exiled for his loyalty, withdrew to the Yuan and Xiang rivers. Clad in blossoms and feeding on herbs, he mingled with birds and beasts, shunning worldly affairs. He gathered *xiangshi* fruit to harmonize his osmanthus bones, nurturing his spirit and heart. Driven by the king’s persecution, he threw himself into the waters of Qingling. The people of Chu, longing for him, called him the Water Immortal. His spirit wandered the celestial caverns, his essence descending upon the banks of the Xiang. The people of Chu built a shrine for him, which endured until the end of the Han. Within those mountains lay a numinous grotto. Those who entered to gather herbs found themselves in a realm of radiant skies and rosy clouds, fragrant flowers and shadowed willows, vermilion towers and jade pavilions—palaces unlike any in the world. Maidens in rainbow skirts, their faces fair as ice and beauty unmatched, offered them nectar and bestowed the elixir of cinnabar. Hunger and thirst vanished. Upon returning to their villages, they found neither home nor kin. Seeking out their ninth-generation descendants, they were told: "Our distant ancestor entered Dongting Mountain to gather herbs and never returned. Three hundred years have passed."

*The Declarations of the Perfected* states: Jia Yi, formerly an attendant in the Western Bright Capital, was demoted for mishandling matters under Ma Rong and assigned to guard Mount Tai. The Lord of Mount Tai soon requested him as a marshal, and the appointment was approved.

The stone likeness of the Young Lord; the golden hairpin of the Supreme Perfected.

*The Record of Forgotten Tales* records: Emperor Wu of Han summoned Li Shaojun and said: "I long for Lady Li. Can I see her?" Shaojun replied: "You may see her from afar, but she cannot share your curtained chamber." In the Eastern Sea lies the *qianying* stone, blue in hue and light as a feather. In bitter cold, the stone grows warm; in scorching heat, it chills. Carved into an image, it is no different from a living person. If this stone likeness is sent, the true person will come." The emperor later obtained the stone and ordered artisans to carve it into the form of the lady. When finished, it was placed behind a sheer gauze curtain, appearing as if alive. The emperor rejoiced greatly.

*The Supplementary Biographies of Transcendents* states: Yang Youtong, originally named Shiwu, was taught in his youth the arts of summoning and defense by a Daoist. After Emperor Ming’s flight to Shu and the incident at Mawei, the emperor could not cease thinking of his consort. He summoned Shiwu to his traveling court and asked about her. Shiwu replied: "Though she may be in heaven, earth, the darkest depths, or among ghosts and spirits, I can seek her everywhere." The emperor commanded him to search thoroughly, yet no trace was found. Later, in the western ruins of the Southern Palace on Penglai Mountain, where a host of immortals dwelled, the Supreme Primordial Female Immortal Zhang Taizhen was none other than the consort. Shiwu went to see her. She said: "I am a handmaiden of the Supreme Palace. The emperor is the Perfected Person of the Vermilion Palace of the Great Yang. By karmic destiny, his worldly attachments were strong, and he was banished among men. After one cycle of twelve years, you shall meet again." She then took half of the gold hairpin and inlaid box from the Kaiyuan era, along with a jade hairpin, as tokens. "When the emperor sees these, he will surely recall them." With these words, she wept and departed. Shiwu presented the objects to the emperor, who was moved to silent tears for a long time.

Yuanzi observed the precepts; Jiwei maintained long fasts.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* states: In his youth, Yuanzi was fond of the Dao and diligently observed its precepts and disciplines. With wholehearted devotion, he practiced austerities, taking only vegetables at midday. He refined his form and preserved his essence, refusing all external distractions. Though summoned by provincial and prefectural authorities, he declined every appointment. At times, he wandered into the mountains and forests, shunning the dust of the world. His aspirations were to emulate Master Wangzi Qiao on Mount Weicen and to follow Lord Lingyang on the steps of Buyuan. Thus, he changed his name to Yuanzi and styled himself Yanqi.

*The Esoteric Instructions on Ascending to Perfection* records: Jiwei once maintained a long fast for three years, offering single-minded devotion in his quest. Thereby, he was able to absorb the radiant qi of the sun and moon, and his body became suffused with divine light.

Zengzi mended shoes; Madame He wove sandals.
*The True Scripture of Southern Florescence* recounts: When Zengzi dwelled in Wei, his garments were threadbare, his complexion swollen and sallow, his hands and feet calloused. For three days, he did not kindle a fire; for ten years, he did not sew new clothes. When he straightened his cap, its cords snapped; when he grasped his lapels, his elbows showed; when he slipped on his shoes, his heels protruded. Yet he dragged his feet and sang the *Odes of Shang*, his voice filling heaven and earth as though issuing from metal or stone. The Son of Heaven could not make him a minister, nor could the feudal lords befriend him. Thus, those who cultivate the will forget the body; those who cultivate the body forget profit; and those who attain the Dao forget the heart.

*The Extensive Records* tells of Madame He the Second of Guangzhou, who made her living by weaving. At the age of twenty, she lived with her mother and had never practiced the arts of immortality. One day, she suddenly declared, "I am weary of staying here; I wish to follow the Dao." The next day, she flew away to Luofu Mountain, where she would come and go, often visiting the northern slopes of Xiaosi Mountain. In Xunzhou, four hundred li from the Southern Sea, stood a yangmei tree several dozen arm-spans in girth. Madame He would gather its fruit and return in time for her fasts. During the Kaiyuan era, the prefect dispatched envoys to summon her. On the way, one envoy, captivated by her beauty, had not yet spoken when she suddenly said, "If the envoy harbors such thoughts, I cannot stay." With these words, she rose into the air and vanished, leaving no trace of her whereabouts.

A soldier embraced a stone; an old man by the river met a locust tree.
*The Record of Seen and Heard* relates: Beneath Yellow Crane Tower in Ezhou lay a luminous stone called Stone Radiance. The stone was said to mark the cave of an immortal. An elderly soldier guarding the pass would rise each morning to bow before the cave. One evening, when the moon was as bright as a painting, he saw three Daoist masters emerge from the cave, laughing and reciting poetry. As they prepared to return inside, the soldier followed them. The Daoists asked, "Who are you?" The soldier explained his presence and begged for wealth and honor. The Daoists replied, "Within this cave, ask the stone to embrace a piece and take it away." The soldier did so, and upon examining the stone by daylight, found it had turned to gold.

The *Traditions* record: There was a man named Jiang Zhi who was skilled at playing the flute and could produce the sound of a dragon’s roar. Later, he traveled to Quexiang and, at the Pan Dou Inn, fell asleep drunk beneath a great locust tree by the roadside. That night, he saw a figure several dozen feet tall who said, "A young master from the Jing Mountain Lodge has come to visit his elder brother." Soon, he heard someone descend from the locust tree and engage in conversation. At dawn, Jiang went to the Jing Mountain Lodge and saw a locust tree ten arm-spans in girth in the courtyard. Suspecting it was a numinous being, he said, "I seek the Dao but have not met a teacher. If the spirit of this tree is efficacious, I beg for your instruction." The spirit replied, "Go into Jing Mountain and seek Immortal Aunt Bao. You will surely attain transcendence." Jiang entered the mountain and indeed met a divine teacher, who said, "You bear the physiognomy of Qin Gao. I now bestow upon you a jade flute. If you play it for three years, you will summon a dragon from the grottoes to bring you the Water Elixir. Swallow it, and you will become a water immortal." Three years later, while playing the flute in the tower of Yueyang Temple, a dragon transformed into a man and presented him with the elixir. After consuming it, Jiang’s appearance reverted to that of a youth, and he could enter water without getting wet.

Ancient men forgot official ambitions; Han ministers upheld their integrity.
*The Traditions of Divine Immortals* tells of Gu Yuanzhi, who died after drinking wine. Three days later, he revived and said he had wandered through the Land of Harmonious Spirits, where rare flowers and precious fruits never withered, and the fields were filled with giant gourds containing the five grains, sweeter and more fragrant than the millet and rice of the Middle Kingdom. The climate was perpetually mild and pleasant, like the second or third month of spring. The people of that land spent their days carrying baskets to gather herbs, singing joyfully, and dispersing at dusk. After reviving, Yuanzhi distanced himself from worldly affairs, entirely forgetting his official ambitions. He roamed mountains and rivers, styling himself the Master Who Knows Harmony. Later, his whereabouts became unknown.

*The Traditions of Divine Encounters* records: During the Tang dynasty, the chancellor Han Kuang governed the Jiangnan West Circuit. Proud and overbearing, he harbored rebellious intentions. A traveler named Li Shun moored his boat at Jingkou one night when his lamp went out. Suddenly, he was blown to the foot of a mountain. Disembarking to seek help, he walked five or six li and came upon a magnificent palace. A man in a black headscarf and casual attire addressed him, saying, "I wish to entrust you with a letter for Minister Han of Jinling. Do not refuse the effort." When Li asked where he was, the man replied, "This is Guang Sang Mountain in the Eastern Sea, where Master Kong, the Perfected Official, governs after attaining the Dao. Minister Han is none other than Zhong You. His nature is stubborn and self-reliant, so the Master fears he may disrupt the penal system and has sent this letter to admonish him." Li took the letter and returned to his boat, which soon arrived at its original mooring. He delivered the letter to Han, who opened it to find nine ancient characters he could not recognize. Suspecting sorcery, he detained Li Shun. Then, a guest with arched eyebrows and ancient attire arrived, claiming expertise in ancient scripts. Han showed him the letter, and the guest said, "This is a letter from Confucius, written in the tadpole script of the Xia dynasty. It reads: 'Admonish Han Kuang to uphold his ministerial integrity and not act recklessly.'" Han was astonished. From then on, he remained silent and restrained, preserving his reputation to the end.

Wind arises from Xun; snow is avoided by Ge.

From *Records of the Uncanny*:

Jinzhou Prefect Xiao Zhizhong planned to hunt on the La Day festival. A woodcutter on Mount Huo saw a tall figure, and soon tigers, rhinoceroses, deer, boars, foxes, and hares gathered around. The tall figure said, "I am an emissary of Yuanming, sent by the Northern Emperor. On the La Day, Prefect Xiao will hunt you. Some of you are fated to die by hawk, others by arrow." An old elk knelt and begged for salvation. The emissary said, "Yan Si of East Valley is skilled in strategy." The beasts followed him.

The woodcutter trailed them to a thatched hut, where a Daoist in a yellow cap sat. The old elk pleaded piteously. The Daoist said, "If I command Teng Liu to send snow and Xun Er to raise winds, Prefect Xiao will not hunt." The woodcutter returned home. Before dawn, wind and snow filled the day, and Prefect Xiao did not venture out.

From *Extensive Records*:

Cui Xizhen was adept at painting and devoted to self-cultivation. One snowy day, he saw an old man in a straw raincoat sheltering under his gate. Cui invited him inside. As they stood before a screen, the old man glanced around and vanished without a trace. Behind the screen hung a painting of a pine tree with an immortal and a thatched hut beneath. Perplexed, Cui consulted Master Li Hanguang, who said, "This was painted by the third son of Immortal Ge. It suggests that those who attain the Dao live as long as pines and bridges."

Embracing the One, he roams free; Lu Hong bows like a chime-stone.

The *Stele of Immortal Child Wu* states: "Embracing the One, he roams free, scorning wealth and nobility in times of peace. Luxuriant cinnamon trees cannot conceal the moon’s radiance; sheer green peaks cannot hinder the passage of white clouds. Words are insufficient—the Dao lies within." The rest of the inscription is omitted.

Another poem reads:
The sagely lord seeks those beyond the world,
All summoned to the Golden Gates to glimpse the empty truth.
I wish not to display my name,
But to befriend Tian Yuan, who embodies the Dao.

Long ago, the Immortal Child presented this text to Master Zhu Ziying of Mount Mao. After receiving it, the messenger vanished, and Zhu suspected the Immortal Child himself had come. The Immortal Child’s surname was Wu, given name Baoyi, and people called him Wu the Immortal Child.

From *Miscellaneous Tales of the Northern Dream*:

Emperor Ming of Tang summoned the hermit Lu Hong of Mount Song. Only after three summons did Lu arrive. When he was received in audience, he did not bow but merely bent like a chime-stone. Asked why, Lu replied, "The *Daodejing* says, 'Ritual is the thinning of loyalty and trust—how can one rely on it?' This mountain-dwelling subject, Hong, dares to present himself with loyalty and trust." The emperor marveled at him.

The aged Pan’s feast, the jade immortal’s bitter herbs.

From *Records of Primordial Transformations*:

During the Yuanhe era of the Tang, after a storm at Shaolin Temple on Mount Song, an old man leaned on a staff and knocked at the gate seeking shelter. The temple attendants, having already barred the door, directed him to an empty hut outside, which had neither bed nor mat. After the second watch, monks saw strange lights outside the temple and went to investigate. Inside the hut, they found lavish curtains and a feast spread out, with the old man drinking and eating at ease. At dawn, the old man rose, rinsed his mouth, and gathered the bedding, curtains, and screens into a gourd. The hut was once again empty. When asked his name, he said he was surnamed Pan and was traveling north from Mount Heng to Taiyuan. Later, some claimed to have seen him again.

From *Green Jade Fragments*:

Zhang Shining, an assistant instructor in Taiyuan Prefecture, fell gravely ill and was near death. He chanted:
*"The jade emperor’s palace with banners of kingfisher plumes,
Purple clouds and towering pavilions—home of the immortals.
Why ask if worldly things scatter like blossoms?
Turning back, Wu Ling’s flowers now lie fallen and spent."*

After he died, his spirit descended upon his sister, saying: *"I am registered as the Eighteenth Jade Immortal of the Tenth Grotto in Heaven. While attending a feast at Jasper Pool, I reviewed the ledger of earthly immortals’ merits and deeds. Catching the scent of idle tea leaves, I lingered and failed to arrive on time, thus incurring the penalty for tardiness. The Queen Mother of the West petitioned on my behalf, noting that someone had asked about sharing wine. I was to be exiled to repay this debt, drifting through mortal life. Now, I return to my celestial register."* He then sang:
*"Rest, rest, rest—
When rest is stolen, best to rest,
Joy fills the house without end."*

Of the Daoist lineage: the red carp of Immortal Master Fu, the white fish of Gongyuan.

From *Biographies of Eminent Daoists*:

Immortal Master Fu Zongzong dwelled in seclusion on Ziyang Mountain. Emperor Ming of Tang summoned him to court. At the time, the Li River in Lizhou was prone to drowning, and ferrymen pleaded for relief. The master cast a talisman into the river. The next day, two red carp were found dead on the shoals, their bellies inscribed with cinnabar characters:
*"Red carp, red carp,
Born in river waters,
Failing to avoid the immortal’s decree,
Rightly meet your end."*
The prefect memorialized the event, and an imperial edict ordered a shrine built by the river.

Furthermore, Luo Gongyuan had lived for several centuries during the Tang, shifting between old and young, so none recognized him. Once, when the prefect hosted a feast for officials in the prefectural gardens, the gentry and commoners gathered in throngs. Suddenly, a man in white appeared, and a young boy shouted at him: *"You dare leave your post without permission!"* The man in white departed. The officials seized the boy and reported him to the prefect. When asked his name, the boy replied: *"Luo Gongyuan. I just saw the Dragon King and drove him away on your behalf."* The prefect did not believe him and asked: *"Can you reveal your true form?"* The boy answered: *"That is no difficulty."*

He then bored a hole and channeled water into it, whereupon a white fish leapt forth with the current. A thread of blue smoke arose, and in an instant, black mist stretched across the sky, thunder and lightning crashed, and rain fell—transforming into a white dragon that soared away. Red whiskers fell, and purple radiance gave rise to new beard growth.

From *Traditions of Divine Transcendents*:
The Red-Whiskered Master, a native of Feng, was said to have been the fish warden during the reign of Duke Mu of Qin. He consumed pine nuts and asparagus root, and whenever his teeth fell out, they regrew; when his hair shed, it returned. Later, he ascended Mount Wu in the south, and after seventy years, none knew his whereabouts.

From *Miscellaneous Morsels from Youyang*:
The presiding Daoist nun of the Eastern Mound Sage Mother Temple, Kang Zixia, claimed that in her youth, she dreamed of being summoned to a place where she was told: *"A celestial decree commands you to assume the role of General and inspect the Southern Marchmount."* Thereupon, she was clad in golden armor and set upon a steed, leading over a thousand attendants as butterflies swarmed toward the south. Suddenly, her whiskers sprouted, and the Marchmount Spirit prostrated himself before her horse. In her dream, she issued commands as though in waking life, traversing every peak of the marchmount and every valley of the Man and Han peoples.

In a daze he returned, awakened by the quail’s cry—thereupon, dozens of whiskers sprouted upon his face.

The Bao brothers, heaped kindling for mother-in-law and daughter-in-law.
From *Declarations of the Perfected*:
The Lord of Life and Destiny said: "The female adept Bao Guan’s seventh-generation ancestors, Li Zhan and Zhang Lü, once kept an inn at Weiqiao. They accumulated hidden virtue, loved the Dao, and longed for transcendence, so their blessings extended to Guan. They were made to change their generations, refine their forms, and be reborn as brothers. Though their ancestral roots differed, their lapsed virtue was the same, so they were destined to be born into the same clan. Now they dwell as immortals in the Grotto-Heavens."

From *Extensive Records of the Strange*:
The imperial chess master Wang Jixin followed Emperor Minghuang in his western flight to Shu. One night, they lodged at a house by a deep stream where a mother-in-law and daughter-in-law attended to their needs. As dusk fell, the household shut its doors. That night, Jixin heard a voice say to the daughter-in-law: *"This fine evening offers no diversion—shall we play a game of encirclement chess?"* Though no lamp burned in the hall and the two women remained in their separate east and west chambers, the daughter-in-law said: *"I place a piece at east five, south nine."* The mother-in-law replied: *"I place a piece at east five, south twelve."* The daughter-in-law then said: *"I place a piece at west nine, south ten."* The mother-in-law said: *"The night has reached the fourth watch."* After thirty-six moves, the mother-in-law declared: *"You have been pushed north—my victory is but five boards."* At dawn, Jixin asked the old woman for instruction. She glanced at the daughter-in-law and said: *"This man may be taught the standard formations."* The daughter-in-law then demonstrated the methods of attack and defense, capture and rescue, reinforcement and resistance—though briefly. The old woman said: *"This alone will render you peerless among men."* Jixin walked away a few steps, then turned back—already the house had vanished.

Zhou Bao’s reburial, Qi Sheng’s thatched hut.
From *Investigations into the Divine*:
Zhou Bao, Military Commissioner of Zhexi, was repairing the city walls when, at the Crane Grove Gate, he uncovered an ancient tomb. The coffin was nearly rotted, yet when opened, a woman lay within, her face as though alive, her garments unspoiled. The foreman reported this to Bao, who inspected it himself and said: *"She must have consumed an elixir and awaited the proper time for her transformation—now the hour of dissolution has come."* He ordered her reburied with full honors, providing a carriage and musicians for the procession. Bao and his officials watched from a height as the cortege proceeded. After several *li*, a purple cloud enveloped the hearse. All saw a woman emerge from the carriage, seated upon the purple cloud as it rose slowly into the sky. After a long while, it vanished. When the coffin was opened again, it was empty.

From *Biographies of the Immortals*:
In his youth, Qi Longming found a dragon hatchling in the earth, shaped like a palace guardian. He built a thatched hut to raise it. When the dragon grew large, it destroyed the hut and departed, its whereabouts unknown. Some fifty years later, Longming suddenly appeared, riding a dragon, and said: *"This year, five hundred *li* from here, there will be a flood."* People took it for an omen, but when the time came, a great deluge indeed occurred, drowning tens of thousands.

*Record of the Immortals of the Three Caverns*, Scroll Eighteen, ends.
*(The character "畫" was originally written as "畫"; amended according to the *Collected Essentials* edition.)*

*Record of the Immortals of the Three Caverns*, Scroll Nineteen
Compiled by Orthodox Unity Daoist Chen Baoguang

The Virtue-Resting Fish Offering and Ziqian’s Dried Meat Sacrifice

*Sequel to the Biographies of the Immortals*:
Cao Dexiu often told people: *"If your household suffers illness, come seek my talismans and medicines. I ask no payment of silk or coin—only a platter of river fish prepared as *kuai*,* with a flagon of fine wine to feast me, and the illness will heal of itself."* The villagers revered him as a divine being. One day, he announced: *"I shall now depart for the Heavenly Treasure Grotto. Yet next year, cattle plague will rage fiercely. I leave my name for you to transmit this teaching: when the cattle plague comes, prepare *kuai* as an offering and inscribe my name on a slip to affix to the oxen’s horns—then they shall suffer no harm."* Later, when a great cattle plague indeed struck, all oxen in the district bearing his inscribed slips were spared, while those without perished.

*Records of Mount Wuyi*:
The Supreme Numinous Lord, Perfected Immortal Hua, dispatched his disciple named Shuqi, who rode the clouds astride a crane, to wander this mountain. The people often called him the Crane-Riding Immortal. In former times, a certain Prince Wei named Ziqian, along with Zhang Zhan, Sun Chuo, and others, cultivated the Dao here. When a severe drought occurred, Prince Wei set out wine and dried meat to sacrifice to the immortals and pray for rain. Moved by this, the Crane-Riding Immortal descended from the clouds upon a white horse, and timely rains fell. Zhang Zhan and the others, having witnessed this, composed a quatrain in silent admiration:
*Upon Mount Wuyi, the Lord of Wuyi,*
*White horse and trailing reins enter purple clouds.*
*Mid-air, only the three libations of wine are heard—*
*O’er Dragon Pool’s waves, the rain scatters and scatters.*
The immortal was greatly pleased by the poem.

Su Lin’s breath cultivation, the ancient worthies’ ingestion of elixirs.

*The Records of Divine Transcendents* states: Su Lin encountered Master Juanzi, who instructed him, saying, "If you wish to become a Perfected One upon the earth, you must first ingest elixirs and expel the Three Corpses, slaying and extinguishing the grain-worms. Those who consume elixirs without expelling the Three Corpses—though they may cease eating grains—will find the worms still alive. Merely abstaining from the Five Grains while engaging in breathing exercises and laborious practices will only cause the worms to multiply, making immortality unattainable." Thereupon, he transmitted to him the Dao of the Three Primes and the True Unity, adding, "This may only be imparted to those recorded in the registers of immortals. This scripture is secret; do not transmit it to the unworthy."

*The Supplementary Biographies of Transcendents* recounts: The former worthy Yang traveled through renowned mountains and wandered among grotto-heavens. From Ge Yong, he learned the Dao and, because of the site of Ge’s transformations, built a dwelling there. After a year, one night he suddenly beheld a radiance bright as daylight, and the Perennial Immortal descended. Asked about his cultivated Dao, the former worthy bowed deeply and replied, "This humble one is but dust and earth, witnessing the swift passage of years. Ever fearful of sudden demise, my sole aspiration is to transcend the world." Moved by compassion, the Perennial Immortal bestowed upon him the Essence of Cinnabar. After ingesting it, he ascended in broad daylight.

Zou Tongwei’s purity and clarity, Li Gen’s marvels.
*The Continuation of the Arrayed Biographies* states: The Daoist Zou Tongwei, of unknown origin, possessed a spirit of purity and clarity. He often roamed famous mountains, and many knew him. At times, he would vanish, leaving none able to fathom his whereabouts. Once his elixir was completed and ingested, he ascended in flight from atop a wineshop.

*The Master Who Embraces Simplicity* records: Li Gen, a native of Xuchang, could transform his shape, conceal his form, and enter fire and water. All marveled at him. A female disciple secretly read his plain-text writings and learned that he had been cultivating the Dao for seven hundred years, counted day by day. Some say Li Gen’s eyes had square pupils, for the *Scriptures of the Transcendents* state: "At eight hundred years, a child’s pupils become square." Li Gen often told his disciples, "Though I have not yet attained equality with Heaven and Earth, I am no longer a man of the lower realm."

Zhong Ruoxu’s yellow calf, the spontaneous Cinnabar Brother.
*The Western Pure Poetry Talks* relates: When Zhong Ruoweng was military commissioner of Pingdong, a certain adept sought an audience. He stood in the courtyard led by a cowherd boy pulling a yellow calf. Ruoxu, struck by the strangeness of the scene, pointed to the cowherd and asked, "Can this Daoist compose a poem about him?" The adept smiled and said, "No need for my words—this lad can do it." Thereupon, the boy wrote in large characters:
*Grass spreads o’er the wilds for six or seven li,*
*Flute plays the evening wind with three or four notes.*
*Returning well-fed as dusk descends,*
*Straw cloak unremoved, he treads the bright moon.*

After the adept departed, townspeople saw him outside the city gates, singing loudly beneath two large jars suspended from his eaves. When they rushed forward, he vanished. The jars were merely two openings—could this have been Lü Dongbin?

*The Leisurely Conversations of the Secret Pavilion* records: Zhao Ziran, a Daoist of Phoenix Mountain in Chizhou, dreamed that the Dark True Lord gave him a branch of paulownia leaves to eat. Thereafter, he ceased eating, and his spirit and qi became extraordinary. He composed a poem:
*I once wished to dwell on a mountain isle,*
*Idly sleeping in a jade cavern’s chill.*
*Cinnabar Brother now leads the dance,*
*Coming and going astride a cloudy simurgh.*

When asked the name "Cinnabar Brother," he replied, "It is the crane."

The upright Yundang pitied the tiger; Junping herded geese.

*The Transmission of Daoist Learning* records: Xie Yundang of Liyang saw a starving tiger trapped in a cage. Moved by its plight, he opened the gate and released it. The tiger prostrated itself for a long while before departing.

*The Idle Drumming of Secluded Leisure* states: Goose-Sheep Mountain lies twenty *li* north of Changsha County. Originally called Bundle-Flower Mountain, it is also known as Stone-Treasure Mountain. At its summit stands an immortal’s altar and cinnabar radiance.

*The Miscellaneous Records of Xiangzhong* relates: In former times, a local man named Cheng Junping, at the age of fifteen, was sent by his elder brother to herd geese and sheep. Suddenly, he encountered an immortal elder who led him into this mountain. Later, his brother searched for him and found Junping in the mountains. When asked where the geese and sheep were, Junping pointed to a white stone and said, “This is they.” Then, ashamed, he rose and followed his brother away. Ten days later, he returned to the foot of the mountain and transformed back into stone—a form that remains to this day.

Bi Tian’s poem reads:
*In what year did the feathered guest refine elixirs here?*
*Still lingers the empty radiance, steadfast o’er frail cliffs.*
*In cloudy heights, hawk and hound—surely the immortal has gone far;*
*Below the mountain, geese and sheep—stubborn as stone marrow.*
*How oft has Xiang’s isles shifted with the sea’s vast change?*
*Liaocheng’s walls no longer see Lingwei’s return.*
*In what year shall the immortal’s chariot come again?*
*Then all shall be sent soaring up the Nine Passes.*

Gold seeks Pengzi; needles are entrusted to Granny Tian.

*The Records of Divine Transcendents* states: Tang-period recluse Wang was the fourth nephew of Wang Shan, a constable of Luoyang. When Shan traveled to the capital for his official posting, he crossed the Tianjin Bridge. The fourth nephew, clad in coarse cloth and straw sandals, his appearance rustic and unrefined, was at first unrecognized by Shan. The nephew said, “Uncle, now that you are bound for the selection, your nephew has but a small gift to offer.” He then produced five taels of gold, its hue like a cock’s comb, and said, “Seek out Zhang Pengzi in the gold market to appraise it, and he will give you two hundred thousand cash.
