I have lately dwelt in the Lesser Existence Grotto of Mount Wangwu, but now I am moving my household to Mount Emei.” When Shan went to inquire, the nephew had already departed. In the gold market, he indeed found Pengzi, who, upon seeing the gold, exclaimed in astonishment and delight, “This is the alchemical gold of Daoist Wang the Fourth Nephew! Its value is not fixed.” Thereupon, he paid Shan the sum as instructed.

*The Tales of the Marvelous* recounts: Xu Bingyan, ascending the perilous plank path of Bashu, suddenly fell with his horse into a cliffside cavern. There he encountered the Primordial Sovereign of the Great One, who said, “The horse you ride is a dragon from my grotto, punished for raging and damaging crops and exiled among men. Yet you, poor as you are, bear the bone of an immortal, and so you have met it. Return and release it by the bend of the Wei River—do not harness it again.”

A jade maiden said, “When the Dragon Son returns, ask County Mistress Tian for needles and send some back with him.” Thereupon, he mounted his horse and flew like the wind, reaching the village of Haoxian in the space of a meal. He inquired and purchased a hundred needles from Mistress Tian, tying them to the horse’s mane before releasing it by the banks of the Wei. Indeed, it transformed into a dragon and departed. Mistress Tian, it is said, was also an immortal.

Xiahou was a master of sleep, while Li He excelled in song.

*The Supplementary Records of Eminent Figures* states: The hermit Xiahou wandered between Mount Mao and Mount Tiantai during the late Dazhong era. He carried only a cloth sack and a bamboo staff, sometimes sleeping in the open beneath altars or among the trees. Those who glimpsed him saw only dense clouds and mist, never his form. Whenever he climbed mountains or crossed rivers, he would close his eyes and fall into a deep sleep. His companions heard his snores, yet his steps never faltered, his feet never stumbled. Upon reaching their destination, he would awaken at once. Thus, he was called the Sleeping Immortal.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* records: Li He often ingested the qi of the Five Stars and thereby attained the Dao. Skilled in song, whenever he sang, birds and beasts would gather to listen.

Yang Qinzhen was diligent in her practice and harmonized with the numinous through sleep.

*The Biographies of Female Immortals* recounts: Yang Qinzhen was originally a peasant girl who married Wang Wei. Her husband, poor and toiling in the fields, relied on her tireless labor as a wife, earning her the name “Diligent Daughter-in-Law” among his kin. One day, she bathed, donned new clothes, and vanished. That night, neighbors heard celestial music and an extraordinary fragrance wafting from the northwest. The following night, the music and fragrance returned, even more intense. Magistrate Li Deng, hearing of this, led a crowd to investigate and found the woman lying whole upon her bed. When asked about her comings and goings, she replied: “A celestial procession came to welcome me to the Cloud Terrace Peak of Mount Hua, where four female immortals already awaited. We spoke warmly, saying, ‘Born in the same impure world, sharing mortal flesh, how suddenly we have transcended the dust! What night is this, that we gather here in joy? Let each compose a poem to express our feelings.’ Thus, we took turns reciting verses.” Qinzhen’s poem read:

*This human world is but vain turmoil,*
*Our lives fleeting as the ephemeral flower.*
*Who could have known that tonight,*
*I would turn my gaze toward the mist and clouds?*

Later, Qinzhen ascended as an immortal once more.

*The Elegant Talks of the Prefectural Pavilion* states: Wu Heling was a Daoist priest dwelling on Mount Nanyue, commonly called “Wu the Supple.” Fond of sleep, he could go for weeks without eating. He often said, “If one wishes for leisure, one must be soft—movement brings no rest.” Though he had never studied literature, he suddenly composed an *Ascension Song*:

*The Jade Emperor’s music summons the office of immortality,*
*Dragons exhale clouds, phoenixes lend their strength.*
*Before my eyes, pavilions appear in an instant,*
*Strange grasses and wondrous flowers beyond recognition.*
*I gaze upon the world from the Great Veil,*
*The world no larger than a hand’s span.*
*If I had not been hasty in ascending,*
*I would have carried it all to Yingzhou to sell.*

Liu Dun shared the same boat, while Gongchui shared the same bamboo slip.

*The Notable Worthies’ Poetry Talks* records: Jin the Duke once owned a garden outside the Baokang Gate of the capital. Within the garden, the scenery of the Immortal Wandering Grotto was most refined. The Daoist priest Liu Dun composed a poem for the *Immortal Ascension Pavilion* and presented it to the Duke:

*Oft drunk on the Immortal Ascension Pavilion’s height,*
*The Immortal Wandering Grotto lies deep, void of men.*
*One day, when the crane-drawn chariot roams the azure sea,*
*Together we’ll gaze upon Penglai’s isle in spring.*

The Duke did not understand the poem. Later, when he was exiled to the south, Liu Dun came to visit him at the cliffs. The Duke was then pondering the poem’s meaning and realized that Dun was no ordinary man. He invited him to share a boat and drink upon the sea. The Duke said, “Today’s demotion was foretold in your verse.”

*The Continuation of Yuan’s Strange Records* states: The late Huaihai Military Commissioner Li Shen once encountered an old man who asked, “Do you recognize me from your youth?” The man said, “I am Tang Ruoshan. Are you not Li Shen?” Li replied, “My surname is Li, but I am not called Shen.” The old man said, “You should be styled *Gongchui*, for your name is recorded in the immortal register. Tonight, the Perfected of Luofu will hold a gathering. Would you follow me to attend?” He then drew from his sleeve a slip of paper, seemingly formless, and stretched it lengthwise and crosswise until it became several feet long and wide, resembling a boat. The old man and Li boarded it together. He instructed Li to close his eyes. Soon, Li felt the wind and waves surge as if crossing a vast river or sea. In an instant, they arrived at a mountain where pavilions and halls stood in tiers, and the sound of flutes and pipes echoed faintly. Over ten elegant immortals came to welcome them, saying, “Gongchui has indeed come. The mortal world is turbid, and the sea of suffering is deep. Without your name in the immortal register, how could you have found your way here?” They asked, “Can you stay?” Li replied, “I have yet to establish a family and cannot refuse. I fear I would cause my brothers the same worry as Huang Chuping.” They said, “Since you long to return, though the immortal roster lists you as a guest, your worldly ties are still heavy. Yet, high office and a noble name shall be yours. Strive for it.” They then sent him back. Thereafter, Li changed his name to Shen and styled himself *Gongchui*. He indeed passed the imperial examinations, rising to the highest ranks of general and minister.

*Green-browed Lu* and *Blue-eyed Ruan*.
*The Duyang Miscellany* records: In the Yongzhen era of the Tang, Nanhai presented Lu Meiniang, a girl of fourteen. Her brows were green and long, hence her name. From childhood, she was clever and unmatched in skill. She could inscribe the eight scrolls of the *Scripture of Numinous Treasure* on a foot of silk, the characters as small as millet grains yet strokes distinct. She was also adept at crafting *flying cloud canopies*—dividing a single skein of silk into three strands, dyeing them five colors, and weaving them into a golden canopy. Within it appeared the images of the Ten Continents, the Three Isles, terraces, halls, qilin, and phoenixes, along with thousands of children holding banners and staffs. Emperor Shunzong, marveling at her craft, called her a divine being and ordained her a Daoist priestess. She returned to Mount Nanyue and was styled *Xiaoyao*. Ruan Ji, styled *Sizong*, served as Commandant of the Northern Army during the Jin. Unrestrained by etiquette, he could make *blue-and-white eyes*. Once, on Mount Sumen, he met Sun Deng and discussed ancient matters, the pivot of spirit, and the circulation of qi. Sun remained silent. Ruan then let out a long whistle and departed. Halfway down the ridge, he heard a sound like the cry of phoenixes resonating through the valleys—it was Sun’s whistle.

*The Silver Cauldron of Zhao Wu* and *The Jade Tablet of Shi Liang*.
*The Records of the Hidden and Subtle* states: Feng Zhaowu was a drunkard from Yuhang. Once, while sailing, a violent storm blew his ship to an island. Suddenly, he caught a whiff of a strange fragrance. In the distance, he saw a Daoist priest sitting on the western shore. Zhaowu hurried over to bow. Beneath a rock sat the Daoist, beside him a silver cauldron with a large pearl floating on its surface. The Daoist asked, “Where have you come from?” Zhaowu replied that he was destitute and wished to go to Silla. The Daoist said, “Your face shows no sign of foreign fortune. Return to your boat, and I shall send a fair wind to carry you back to Mingzhou.” Zhaowu pleaded, “All my life, I have been mired in wine. My diet is meager, and my body is emaciated.” The Daoist laughed and said, “Just drink this broth.” He ladled half a dipper from the cauldron and gave it to him. It was truly the liquid of sweet dew. The Daoist also advised him on the principles of livelihood, saying, “Just trade in horses, and you will be self-sufficient.” Zhaowu asked the Daoist’s name. He replied, “I am the Perfected of Yin.” Suddenly, a wind arose. The Daoist urged him to board a small boat. After drifting for a night, at sunrise, he found himself already in Mingzhou.

*The Tang Anecdotes* records: At the beginning of the Yuanhe era, Ma Shiliang, a petty official of Wannian County, committed an offense. Wang Shuang, the capital prefect, was severe and cruel, and Shiliang fled to Mount Taibai. Reaching the shore of the Tangu Creek, he hid beneath a large willow. At dawn, a five-colored cloud descended from the sky, and an immortal maiden appeared within it. By the water’s edge lay a golden mallet and a jade tablet. She struck it several times, and blue lotuses emerged, each petal unfolding in turn. When they fully opened, the maiden plucked three or four and ate them, then rode the cloud away. Shiliang saw the mallet and tablet still there. He struck them, and after a moment, more lotuses appeared. He ate seven or eight and suddenly felt his body grow light.

*Guo Xian’s Wine-Spitting* and *Ban Meng’s Ink-Swilling*.
*The Collection of the Worthy and the Self* states: In the seventh year of Wude, Guo Xian served as Superintendent of the Imperial Household. He accompanied the emperor to the southern suburban sacrifice. While seated, he suddenly turned to the northeast, took a mouthful of wine, and sighed thrice. The emperor asked the reason. He replied, “The state of Qi is on fire. I did this to suppress it.” Later, it proved true.

*The Three Caverns’ Pearl Bag* states: Ban Meng—some say a woman—could fly for an entire day. She could also sit in empty space and speak with people, or enter the earth and vanish. At first, she would sink to her feet, then her chest, until only her headwear remained visible, and after a while, she would disappear entirely. She could hold ink in her mouth, spread paper before her, chew the ink, and spit it out to form characters, filling the page with meaningful text. After ingesting cinnabar, she grew younger at four hundred years of age and entered Mount Daye.

*Daorong’s Tiger Trench* and *Dragon Might’s Crow Traces*.

*The Record of Responses* recounts: In the Northern Qi dynasty, Youwu Daorong, in his youth, became a Daoist. He encountered an immortal from Mount Nanyue, who taught him talismanic water, incantations, yin-yang calculations, astronomy, and the properties of medicinal herbs—all of which he mastered. Once in Liaoyang, a tiger approached within ten paces of his horse. While others fled in terror, Daorong calmly drew his staff across the ground, forming a deep trench, and the tiger fled at once.

*The Supplement to the Biographies of Immortals* tells of the Dragon Might Elder, a nameless recluse on Mount Bao. In the twelfth year of King Helü of Wu, as the king led his troops past the mountain to observe the enemy state, he noticed a cave and wished to know its depth. He asked the recluse to explore it fully. Holding a torch, the elder traveled day and night for one hundred seventy-four days before returning. He described a golden city with jade towers, radiant as daylight, its windows and doors inlaid with flowing purple jade and yellow gold. Above the city gate hung a plaque reading *Detached Palace of the Queen of Heaven*. Within the resplendent halls, he found a scroll of plain silk inscribed with crow-traced seal script. Upon his return, he presented the text to the king, who later sent envoys to show it to Confucius, saying: “Our lord once traveled to Mount Bao, where a red crow delivered this book before his chariot. We now present this sacred text to you, wishing to know its fortune or misfortune.” Confucius opened the case and exclaimed: “When Yu the Great completed his flood-control works, he wandered to the slopes of Mount Zhong, where he received the *Supreme Numinous Treasure True Scripture* bestowed by the Yellow Emperor and Emperor Yan. One copy was hidden in a stone chamber within a famous mountain, and another was entrusted to the water spirit. A perfected one was destined to present it to a king. But as for a red crow delivering it—I have never heard of such a thing.”

*Zhong Yue’s Pine Bow*, *Yaoguang’s Reed-Burning*.

From *Accounts of Illustrious Worthies*:

The Daoist Wu Chongyue practiced grain avoidance. Once, he climbed to the top of a pine tree to bow in homage to Zhou Wei, the Fiscal Commissioner of Fujian. Zhou invited him to join his retinue, and they traveled together to Dehua County. East of the county stood an ancient pine, eighty or ninety feet tall, upon which perched a crane’s nest. Zhou commanded Wu to ascend. Like a gibbon, Wu climbed with ease, rising straight up until he emerged beyond the nest. There, he stood upright, pacing with flying steps, his hands grasping nothing, bowing upon the slender branches as if on level ground. The pine’s branches, supple and yielding, rose and fell with his steps, yet remained unharmed. Zhou presented him with a poem:

*With a hat of paper mulberry bark and robes of cloth,*
*You’ve swallowed the cinnabar clouds—your life shall be long.*
*Amid the worldly, your spirit delights in the Dao,*
*Transcending the dust, your bearing forsook grain long ago.*
*Who passed you the wondrous scriptures within your pillow?*
*You’ve newly transcribed the remedies behind your elbow.*
*Atop the hundred-foot pine, how many flying steps?*
*Where cranes nest on the branches, you bow to the August Sovereign.*

From *Records of Karmic Responses*:

Wu Yaoguang was skilled in fire manipulation. The King of Wu amassed thousands of bundles of reeds, had Yaoguang sit upon them, then wrapped him in thousands more. A fierce wind suddenly arose, and the reeds were set ablaze. When the flames subsided, all assumed Yaoguang had been reduced to ashes. Yet there he sat, unscathed amid the embers, rising to shake off his robes. He obtained a sacred talisman, and his name was inscribed in the immortal register of Lord Bo Wei.

From *Supplement to the Biographies of Transcendents*:

One Shi Deyi recounted: During the Xiantong era, autumn rains persisted for ten days, and mountain torrents overflowed. One morning, as the skies cleared, he saw an object drifting sideways in the river. Using his staff, he drew it ashore and opened it to find the *Three Section Talismans of Lord Lao*. Though it had floated in the water, it was not wet. Marveling at this, he took it home. That night, a radiant light appeared, and two youths seemed to follow him. They spoke: “We are the Talisman Attendants. The Supreme Treasure Talismans have long circulated among mortals, but errors in transcription have distorted their essence. With the text corrupted, ghosts and spirits no longer heed them, and their efficacy has waned. Now, this correct version is entrusted to a child of the Dao, to relieve the people’s suffering—none shall fail to see its power.” Years later, the youths announced that Lord Lao would descend upon Mount Taibai. Shi went to pay homage and never returned.

*The True Records* states: In his youth, Zhao Bowen studied the Dao under Master Deng Ye of Zhang, and in later years at Mount Song, he transmitted the *Scripture of the Jade Girdle and Golden Pendants* to Yuanqiu Linzi. Now, he too has attained the Dao, entering the Huayang Grotto-Heaven to oversee the celestial registers and record the names of seekers.

Han boys drew lines in the earth; Qin women built walls.

*The New Record of the Cinnabar Terrace* recounts: In the early Han, four or five boys were playing by drawing lines on the road. One sang:
*"Don the azure skirt, enter the Gate of Heaven,
Bow to the Golden Mother, pay homage to the Wooden Duke."*
No one understood, save Zhang Zifang, who went and bowed twice—these were the jade lads of the Eastern King.

The so-called Golden Mother is the Queen Mother of the West; the Wooden Duke, the King Father of the East. Immortals bow to the King Father and salute the Queen Mother.

*The Extensive Records* notes: During the Kaiyuan era of the Tang, the Prefect of Daizhou, fearing that the many wandering monks on Mount Wutai might give rise to heretical affairs, expelled all who were not resident clergy. The displaced monks fled into the mountains. One, named Faming, ventured deep into the secluded ravines of Mameng Mountain, where a stone cave allowed passage. Crossing the stream and scaling the bank, he walked two *li* to a thatched hut. Within, a woman clad in woven grass, her countenance refined and lovely, stared in astonishment and asked: "What manner of being are you?" The monk replied, "I am human." The woman laughed: "Could a human truly bear such a form?"

The monk replied, "I am a disciple of the Buddha, and thus shave my head and beard as the Buddha requires." The monk then asked where this place was. The woman said, "I am from Qin. I followed Meng Tian to build the Great Wall, but the toil was unbearable, so I fled here. By eating grass roots, I have managed not to die." The monk took his leave and departed. Later, he prepared provisions and returned, but lost his way and could not find the place again.

Bo Yong’s cranes cried out; Cang Zhi’s roosters crowed.

*The Imperial Anthology* records: Minister Wang Boyong fell ill and grew feeble. That evening, over ten ethereal cranes soared in the sky, their calls ringing clear. On the twentieth night of the eighth month, the moon shone brightly. At the time, his younger brother, Chuncheng, had been assigned to govern Bozhou, and the household staff came to receive him, all finding the event strange and astonishing. Soon after, the minister passed away, and the cranes dispersed. People of the time believed Boyong was destined to become an immortal official.

*The Lives of Eminent Daoists* states: Daoist Ye Cangzhi, styled Hanxiang, was skilled in talismanic protection. The prefect of Bozhou was plagued by an evil entity and came to request a talisman. Midway through the journey, he encountered defilement and suddenly lost it.

The prefect personally visited, seeing a bamboo tube on the table, its seal tightly affixed—it was the talisman he had earlier requested. Thereupon, he burned incense, reverently received the case, and took it home. The malevolent entity was thus eradicated. The prefect memorialized Ye’s virtue, and Emperor Yizong issued an imperial decree bestowing upon him the Jade Cloud Abbey at Mount Shimen.

One day, Ye prepared wine and summoned his friends, recounting the events of his life before announcing his departure date. When the appointed day arrived, he secretly inscribed upon his door: *"At cock’s crow, I depart."* His disciples then heard the mingled sounds of jade pendants and flutes in the air. In an instant, as the cock crowed, they looked and found he had already transformed. He was seventy-four years old.

*The Comprehensive Records of the Immortals of the Three Caverns*, Scroll Nineteen, concludes.

The Orthodox Unity Daoist Chen Baoguang compiled and edited.

The Elder of Green Mound, a minor star of the Purple Tenuity.

*The Records of Extraordinary Persons* states: The Daoist Wang Yuanzhi of Taizhou was skilled in the *Book of Changes* and could foretell life, death, fortune, and misfortune. He composed *The Essentials of the Changes* in fifteen scrolls. One day, amid thunder, rain, and mist, an old man shouted: *"Where is the book you have leaked? The Supreme Emperor has commanded me to seize the Six Ding thunder spirits to retrieve it."* Terrified, Yuanzhi prostrated himself upon the ground, where six attendants in black robes already bore the book forward. The old man rebuked him: *"The forbidden texts of the Upper Realm are guarded by flying celestial protectors, secreted within the Primordial Capital. Who are you to conceal such trifling teachings?"* Yuanzhi replied: *"The Elder of Green Mound transmitted them to me."* The old man said: *"The Supreme Emperor has descended. Your immortal rank is now attained; the date of your transcendence is deferred by four years and two cycles."* Later, the master underwent liberation by means of the corpse in the Hall of Illuminated Residence.

*The Rustic Records of Xiangzhong* states: During the time when the Great Ancestor (Taizu) was still in obscurity, he and Zhao, the Prince of Han, encountered Chen Xiyi while strolling through the markets of Chang’an. Xiyi said: *"Might we share a drink?"* The Great Ancestor replied: *"Certainly."* He went with Zhao the Learned Scholar, whereupon Xiyi glanced at the Prince of Han and said: *"He will do, he will do."* Upon entering the tavern, the Prince of Han, whose foot was numb, happened to sit at the left of the seat. Chen grew angry and said: *"A mere minor star of the Purple Enclosure, how dare you occupy the higher place?"* He rebuked him and made him move to the right. Li Yu commented on the *Analects*, while Gu Huan expounded the *Classic of Filial Piety*.

*The Unofficial Histories* records: The Vice Director of the Bureau of Merit Titles, Li Yu of Xinzhou, was traveling to Mount Hua with Yang Leng when they suddenly came upon a small grotto. The streams, cliffs, grasses, and trees within seemed unlike the mortal realm. A man in purple robes invited them inside to his dwelling, which resembled an official residence, with many bamboo houses. The halls and chambers were exceedingly clean. He introduced himself as Du Zihua, explaining that he had withdrawn from the world to pursue the Daoist arts and had lived there for several hundred years. He offered them lodging, and the food and drink were both refined and abundant. The meat was from a "galloping sheep," whose form resembled an ox. There were also copies of the *Analects* in his collection. After lingering for several days, he presented each of them with several silver vessels and sent an attendant to guide them back, asking, "Would you care to stay?" The two men hesitated. Zihua laughed, clasped their hands in farewell, and parted. Later, when they searched for the grotto, it was nowhere to be found.

*The Biographies of Daoist Scholars* states: Gu Huan of the Song was skilled in Daoist teachings. His disciple Bao Yunshou had a tree before his gate, over ten spans in girth, upon which dwelled a spirit. Huan sealed the tree with a talisman, and it immediately withered and died. Another man afflicted by malevolent spirits sought Huan’s help. Huan asked, "What books do you have at home?" The man replied, "Only the *Classic of Filial Piety*." Huan said, "Take the passage *Confucius at Rest* and place it by the sick man’s pillow with reverence. He will recover." The afflicted man did so and was indeed cured.

Wang Yuan inscribed verses upon a gate; Yin Rong concealed himself while dredging a well.

*The Biographies of Divine Immortals of the Wang Clan* records: Wang Yuan, styled Fangping, was well-versed in celestial patterns and divination, foretelling good and ill fortune. Emperor Wu of Han inquired of him regarding omens, but he did not answer. Instead, he inscribed over four hundred characters upon the palace gate, foretelling future events. The emperor, displeased, ordered the words scraped away. Though the outer characters were erased, the inner ones reappeared, the ink penetrating deep into the wood.

*The Extensive Records* states: In Zhushan County of Fangzhou, a man named Yin Rong was dredging a well at his home. Though they dug over a thousand feet, no water was found. The laborers, feeling along the walls, suddenly beheld another sky and earth, a world with sun and moon, marked by a placard reading *Mount Heavenly Pillar*. Within the gate, two youths with bright teeth and elaborate headdresses asked, "Why have you come here?" The laborers recounted the entire affair.

A certain text titled *Paoyi’s Transmission of Robberies* states: "Send them off with propriety." They led the laborers to a clear spring to bathe, and rinsed their mouths with white springwater—sweet and fragrant as wine. After half a day’s journey, they beheld palaces all of gold and jade, inscribed with the words *Ladder-to-Immortal Land*. The laborers asked, "What is this country?" They were told, "All immortals newly attained are sent through this checkpoint to this land for cultivation."

Wang Jiao split his skull; Yang the Elder struck his crown.
*The Youyang Miscellany* records: Master Wang Xia was skilled in divination. During the Tianbao era, he once sat in the open air with guests at night, pointing to the stars and moon and saying, "The times are about to change." This was reported by a neighbor, and the emperor, then in his declining years, had him beaten on the head dozens of times before he died. When his skull was split open, the bone was found to be one *cun* and eight *fen* thick. Xia had previously associated with Minister Daxi, and after the An Lushan rebellion was pacified, Jiao, staff in hand and sandals on his feet, visited Daxi’s home—only then was he recognized as an extraordinary man.

*The Beimeng Suoyan* states: In Zichuan of Qi Prefecture, there was a Daoist named Yang the Immortal, whose age no one knew; elders from their youth had seen him. Whenever he walked through the lanes, children would gather to watch. Sometimes he would borrow an iron hammer from a blacksmith’s shop and strike his own crown, or have others strike it with all their might—yet he suffered no harm, saying only that it felt most pleasant. He would enter the mountains to play with tigers and leopards, striking them with his hand so that the fierce beasts would fall prostrate. He often urged people to practice hidden virtue.

During the Changxing era, he entered Shu and resided on Mount Emei before departing.

Xi Jia fled into reclusion; Wang Zhongfu absorbed the radiance.
*The Unofficial History of Jin* records: Xi Jia, styled Kongbin, was twenty years of age and fond of studying the classics and histories. One quiet night, he suddenly heard a voice at the window call out: "Xi Kongbin, come away! To adorn the affairs of the human world is bitter and cannot be harmonized. What you gain is not even a hair’s weight, while what you lose is already a mountain’s mass." Jia thereupon fled into reclusion, traveling west to the isles of the sea, where he taught over three thousand disciples.

*The Divine Immortals of the Wang Clan* states: Wang Zhongfu, from his youth, loved the Dao and the arts of nourishing life. He practiced the method of absorbing radiance and nurturing mist for over forty years. One evening, he dreamed that a divine person told him, "The reason you have not yet ascended is that your brain palace is deficient and your numinous essence has not yet overflowed." Thereupon, he was taught the method of ingesting and consuming, and only then was he able to ascend. He now resides on the Dark Continent.

Cheng the Master carried his sack; Yi Yin bore the cauldron.
*The Biographies of Eminent Daoists* records: Master Cheng’s origins were unknown. When Emperor Ming heard of his name, he summoned him to the palace and lodged him in the Penglai Court to inquire about the arts he cultivated, but Cheng did not answer. Soon after, he begged to return to the mountains, and the emperor assented. He departed carrying a cloth sack, and people all laughed at him. Later, when the curtains were drawn back, a poem was found inscribed on the wall:

*Westward the road to Shu,
Northward the Yan army comes.
I had hoped to ascend in broad daylight,
But first, the Black Dragon drinks the Wei.*

The characters, though scraped and washed, grew ever clearer. Within a few months, An Lushan rebelled, and the emperor fled to Shu.

*The Supplement to the Biographies of Transcendents* records: Yi Yin was a man of Shang. His ancestors had served the Xia as feudal lords. When his mother was with child, she was strolling by the western river when a great flood suddenly surged forth. Startled, she fled to escape the waters, then stood with arms clasped and transformed into a withered mulberry. When the flood receded, her husband came to search for her, assuming she had drowned. Suddenly, he saw the withered mulberry—a tree not there before—and suspected it was his wife transformed. He struck it with a stone, and from within the hollow mulberry came the sound of an infant. He took the child and raised him, thus naming him Yi.

As Yi Yin grew, he mastered the celestial stems and earthly branches, the harmonics of sound and the principles of yin and yang, delving into the profound and scrutinizing the subtle, his resolve set on saving the world. He bore the cauldron to petition Tang, who rejoiced greatly and appointed him as minister. At eighty, he relinquished his position, ceased eating, and feigned death at home. The Supreme Lord commanded the Perfected One of Grand Harmony, Meng Guxi, to transmit to him the method of liberating the form. He entered the stone chamber of Mount Meng Qin to cultivate it and ascended in broad daylight.

*Yuan Da dreamed of a crow; Wenzi struck the molted husk.*
*The Pearl Satchel of the Three Caverns* states: Deng Yuzi, styled Yuan Da, was a native of Xinye in Nanyang. Once, he dreamed of a crow that spat out a seal and gave it to him. Thereafter, whenever the people suffered illness, he would heal them with the seal, inscribing talismans that cured the afflicted.

The *Commentary on the Han Sacrificial Canon* in the *Biographies of Transcendents* records: Cui Wenzi studied transcendence under Wang Ziqiao. Ziqiao transformed into a white cicada husk, and Wenzi, startled, struck it with a halberd. Looking down, he saw the corpse of Wang Ziqiao. In an instant, it transformed into a great crow and flew away.

*Jade Orchid harmonized her belly; the Supreme Transcendent shed her skin.*
*The Collected Biographies of Transcendents* states: Zhang Yulan was the granddaughter of the Celestial Master. From childhood, she abstained from meat. At seventeen, she dreamed of a crimson light descending from the heavens, within which golden characters in seal script followed the light into her mouth, and she conceived. Her mother reproached her, but she never spoke of the dream, confiding only in her handmaiden. One day, she declared, "I cannot bear the shame of living. After my death, cut open my belly to prove my innocence."

That very night, she died without illness. Something like a lotus emerged from her belly, and upon inspection, it was revealed to be ten scrolls of the *Sutra of the Fundamental Principle*, written in gold on plain silk, which circulated among the people. When they buried Yulan, a great wind and rain arose, and her coffin flew onto a tree. The sutra and Yulan herself vanished. Her tomb remains in the Abbey of the Maiden in Wenjiang County, Yizhou.

Furthermore, Dong Shangxian was a girl from Fangyi in Suizhou. At seventeen, her divine bearing was elegant and refined, earning her the name "Supreme Transcendent." One day, purple clouds draped the sky, and celestial music descended into her courtyard. Two azure lads led her to ascend to heaven. Her parents, simple and unenlightened, wailed and called for her without cease.

She ascended some ten yards from the ground before descending to return home, where she stayed for several months, only to repeat the ascent. Later, she shed her skin upon the earth—her garments and form remained intact—and flew away.

Du Qiong composed a rhapsody, and Xu Jian wrote poetry.

*The True Diagnosis* states: On Mount Dengdu, the forests, waters, and marshes resemble those of the mortal world, yet the grains of rice are nearly as large as beans, their taste like mugwort. Of the five grains, only rice is of weighty significance.

Du Qiong’s *Rhapsody on the Weighty Grain* reads:
*"In spring’s lush haze, the verdant weighty grain,
The numinous qi intermingles, and fine crops answer the season.
The four rites fulfilled, the people offer sacrifice.
The divine rice flourishes in vast fields,
Its mighty ears swaying o’er my primal terrace.
Lo, a bright omen—
The Son of Heaven thus prospers.
This is what it means."*

*Miscellaneous Records in Refined Speech* states: Xu Jian, a man of Jiangzuo, often dwelled on the Three Maos Mountains. His age was unknown, his appearance unchanged, and he spoke often of immortals and transcendents. He composed poetry, such as *Inscribed at the Maoshan Abbey*:
*"Ever resentful the pure winds stagnant for a thousand years,
Yet today the grotto-heaven grants free roaming.
The pines’ ancient hues quiet the jade altar,
Phoenix and crane come not—the Azure Lord stands aloof.
The Maos’ well runs cold, the cinnabar already transformed,
The Ming Emperor’s stele broken, dreams still toil.
Clearly there is a path to long life—
Cease sighing o’er graying temples in the red dust."*

Another quatrain, sent to Xu Xuan of Hehe:
*"For days, mist and moon have sealed the towers,
Wishing to present my talents to the noble gate.
My face covered in celestial dust, none recognize me—
Had I followed the flowing waters out of the mountains?"*

Qiu Bo appraised cranes, while Tong Jun pulverized stone hens.

*The Classic of Crane Appraisal* states: Fu Qiu Bo once received it from Prince Wang Zijin. Later, Cui Wenzi studied the Dao under Zijin, obtained the text, and concealed it in a stone chamber on Mount Song. The Duke of Huainan, seeking herbs, found it and thus transmitted it to the world. The classic says: *"The crane is a yang bird, born of the qi of metal and fire. At three years, its crown reddens; at seven, it masters flight; after another seven, it crows at all twelve hours. At sixty, downy feathers grow, and mud cannot soil it. At one hundred sixty, male and female gaze at each other to conceive; at one thousand six hundred, it bears young without eating, gestating within. Its appraisal: a long neck and refined sinews make for fine song; a tortoise-shell back and black-bellied frame enable dance—such is the transcendent’s steed."*

*The Record of Extraordinary Events* states: In the third year of Jian’an, the kingdom of Rongtu presented a silent stone hen, its color like cinnabar, the size of a swallow. It often remained on the ground, crowing in season, its voice carrying far through the land. Upon hearing it, the people would slaughter livestock in sacrifice. Where the sound issued, they would dig and find the hen. If the realm knew great peace, it would soar and flap its jowls as an auspicious sign, also called the "treasure hen." Some listened to the earth to mark the hours. Daoists say: *"The transcendent Tong Jun delved into a cave for miles, obtaining a cinnabar stone hawk. Pounded into medicine and consumed, it granted one a voice and breath, delaying death beyond heaven’s decree."* In the first year of Baoding, the four directions offered rare treasures, including amber. Swallows placed in a quiet chamber would sing and flutter—such was its kind.

The External Official and the Filthy Man; the Farmland Soldier and the Conscript.

*The Unofficial History* states: During the Dali era of the Tang, a certain Gentleman Wang, an External Official, was devoted to the Dao. Though he held court rank, he daily wore plain robes and associated with mountain recluses. One day, while performing a purification rite, he spoke with an old man named Pei. Gentleman Wang found him extraordinary. His wife scolded him, saying: *"You are a court official, yet you befriend a filthy man!"* She sent someone to drive him away. A day later, the man returned, clutching a cloth bundle and leaning on a staff, exuding the air of a hermit. He said: *"The External Official does not truly love the Dao—he merely covets elixirs."* He then took a small iron box from Gentleman Wang, weighing about two pounds, unwrapped a tiny packet containing two sesame-seed-like pills, scattered them atop the box, and piled fire to burn it. After the time it takes to eat a meal, he retrieved it—now transformed into superior gold, its hue like a rooster’s comb. Gentleman Wang gasped in astonishment. The man departed, and his whereabouts thereafter were unknown.

*The Comprehensive Record of Marvels* states: Zeng Gongfengxian, a Farmland Gentleman of the Ministry of Works, governed Huizhou during the Jiayou era. His residence had a vegetable garden, tended by an old conscript who watered and weeded with great diligence. Whatever Zeng desired, the old man secretly provided in advance. Zeng asked: *"How do you always anticipate my wishes?"* The conscript replied: *"It is mere coincidence."* Thereafter, Zeng treated him well. One day, the conscript said to Zeng: *"I am grateful for Your Lordship’s kindness. I am no ordinary man. Tonight at the third watch, I humbly request your presence in the garden—there is a secret matter to report."* Zeng readily agreed, preparing his official robes to visit him. His family protested: *"How could a prefect don his court robes to meet an old conscript in the dead of night?"* He relented. At dawn, word came that the gardener had died, and several taels of silver were found at his waist. Zeng purchased a coffin and buried him in the wilds. Months later, a man arrived from Guangzhou, bearing a letter from the conscript in thanks. When they opened his tomb, only his cloth bundle, headscarf, and sandals remained.

The Pine-Eating Maiden Mao; the Orange-Gathering Lady He.

*The Biographies of Divine Transcendents* states: The Maiden Mao, styled Zhengmei, dwelled in seclusion on Mount Hua. Her body grew hair, and she claimed to have been a palace woman during the Qin. Later, she fled into the mountains, where a Daoist taught her to eat pine needles, thus freeing her from hunger and cold. Her body became light as flight. Chen Tuan often roamed Mount Hua with her, and woodcutters frequently saw her. He gifted her a poem:
*"The medicinal sprouts do not fill the basket,
Yet again I ascend the perilous peak.
Turning my head toward the path home,
Together we enter the emerald mist."*

Another verse:
*"Once I broke pine branches to fashion a fine comb,
Wove chestnut leaves to replace silk robes.
Sometimes, when asked of Qin palace affairs,
She laughs, plucks a transcendent flower, and gazes into the great void."*

*The Continued Biographies of Transcendents* states: He A-liu was a woman of Xuzhou who, at the Cloud Terrace Abbey in Huayin, gathered firewood and drew water for the female Daoist masters for several years. The Daoist women spoke of the sweet oranges and fine fruits of Jiangwu, which were difficult to obtain in the Qin region. A-liu smiled and said: *"This is not hard to procure—such things exist nearby."*

She finished speaking and went out the door. After the time it takes to eat a meal, she returned with five or six sweet oranges tucked in her sleeve and gave them to the Daoist women, saying: *"The Guangxun mandarins are especially fine."* She then produced over ten more, which they divided and ate.

Three years later, a Master Cui, a Daoist adept from Mount Luofu, traveled to Huayin. In conversation, she mentioned that the previous year in the official gardens of Guangzhou, a woman had been caught stealing mandarins—yet when seized, she suddenly vanished. Only then did the others realize that A-liu was no ordinary person.

**Yuan Fang begged for his bones; Dou Zhi concealed them.**

*The New Record of the Cinnabar Terrace* states: Zuo Ci, styled Yuanfang, had mastered the arts of the Nine Elixirs and their transformations. When Cao Cao sought his teachings, Zuo refused. Enraged, Cao intended to kill him, but Zuo begged only for his bones. Cao prepared wine for him, and Zuo said: *"Today I must journey far. I beg to divide this cup of wine with you."* Cao agreed. Zuo drew his hairpin and traced a line through the wine, splitting it in two. He drank half and offered the other half to Cao, who, displeased, did not drink at once. Zuo then begged to finish the cup himself. After drinking, he hurled the vessel at the roof beams, where it trembled like a flying bird. As all eyes turned upward, Zuo had already vanished.

*The Declarations of the Perfected* records: The Lady Mao said: *"The female immortal Dou Qiongying’s seventh-generation ancestor, Dou Zhi, made it his practice to conceal dry bones and revive the dead. His hidden virtue extended to Qiongying’s own person, enabling her to ascend to the Hua Yang Grotto-Heaven."*

**The Ghost Mound of Song River; the Immortal Caverns of Mount Heng.**

*The Talismanic Records of the Heavenly Master* states: East of Yichang lies the county of Jiachuan. Twenty *li* northeast of it flows the Song River, where a cluster of rocks in midstream is called the Heavenly Master’s Ghost Mound. Beside the river, a massive boulder bears raised characters known as the Heavenly Master’s Talisman—sharp as if carved, bold as if inscribed, resembling both seal script and clerical script, yet majestic and forceful, like an armored warrior standing with sword in hand. When evil spirits behold it, who does not tremble in terror? Scholars print it with ink on paper, and it serves to repel malevolent forces.

In the second month of the seventh year of the Yuanhe era, An Xing, having resigned from office, traveled south and passed through Yichang. Zhang Dangshi, a man of Huayin then serving as magistrate of Miangu County, presented him with a Heavenly Master’s Talisman, saying: *"This cannot be obtained outside my homeland."* An placed the talisman in his document case and returned home. Half a year later, he opened the case to find that mice had shredded all his books—yet the talisman remained untouched, not a hair’s breadth damaged. Ah, how wondrous!

Li Anxing, magistrate of Fufeng County in Fengxiang Prefecture under the Xuande administration, recorded these events.

**The Traditions of Divine Transcendents (Wangshi Shenxian Zhuan):**
Wang Daochang, of unknown origin, dwelt in Yangmo Township, Lijing Valley County. Twenty li west of the county seat, one crosses the Jialing River and ascends the Anle Gorge to reach the foot of his mountain. Its peaks rise sheer and towering; east of the gorge lies the Immortal’s Cavern, where he cultivated the Dao. His entire household attained transcendence. The foundations of his dwelling and the remnants of his rice mortar remain to this day.

**Yin Jun drinks aconite; Du Wu vomits cinnabar.**
*Supplement to the Traditions of Divine Transcendents (Xianzhuan Shiyi):*
Yin Jun, of unknown origin, once secluded himself on Mount Jin, abstaining from the five grains. At the time, Minister Li Shuo governed the northern frontier, with Feng Lan and Yan Shou serving as his aides. Yan, who cherished the strange and revered divine transcendents, invited Yin Jun to his official residence, where they shared the same mat daily. A rare fragrance often emanated from Yin’s flesh, deepening Yan’s veneration. Yan had a younger sister who had become a nun; displeased with her brother’s association with Yin Jun, she secretly mixed aconite into his wine. After drinking, Yin suddenly exclaimed, *"This wine is not fine!"* Moments later, he vomited an object—hard and fragrant. Yan split it open to find a true musk gland. That very night, Yin Jun perished, and Yan mourned him deeply. The following autumn, a certain Zhu Taixu encountered Yin Jun on Mount Jin, who said: *"Recently, at the northern frontier, I was poisoned with aconite wine and feigned death. Yet how could such poison corrupt my true essence?"* Astonished, Zhu returned and recounted this to Lord Yan, who declared: *"I knew at once that he had achieved liberation through apparent death."*

*Records of the Uncanny (Yuan Guai Lu):*
In his youth, before attaining prominence, Minister Du Wu once met a Daoist on Mount Changbai who bestowed upon him a pellet of cinnabar. After ingesting it, Du lost all desire for food; his complexion grew radiant, his body light and free of illness. Later, when he became Prefect of Shangzhou, he realized that as a high-ranking official, his abstinence from food might alarm others. Thus, he sought to expel the cinnabar. Over a year later, a Daoist arrived and instructed him to consume animal flesh and blood. Du complied, and soon after, he vomited up the cinnabar.

**The Pei family’s boulder; the Wei elder’s ancient altar.**
*Extensive Records (Guang Ji):*
A son of the Pei family served his parents with filial devotion and, though poor, was ever eager to aid others. One day while traveling, he encountered an old man who led him into Mount Taibai. There, they came upon a massive boulder. The elder struck it with his staff, and the stone parted, revealing a cavern. Inside, myriad wonders unfolded—jade maidens and immortal youths. The old man then guided Pei out and told him: *"Twenty years hence, return here to escape the world."* When the appointed time arrived, the An Lushan rebellion erupted, and the entire Pei clan took refuge in the Western Kunlun Mountains, thus escaping harm.

*Collection of Marvels (Yiwen Ji):*
During the reign of Emperor Daizong, Attendant Censor Wei was dispatched on a mission to Mount Hua to pay homage at the Yellow Emperor’s Altar. Upon reaching the lodgings at the mountain’s base, he encountered an old man and inquired about the altar’s location. The elder replied: *"On the southwestern slope of the Lotus Peak stands an ancient altar, its overgrown remnants still visible—this must be it."* When asked his surname, the old man answered, *"Wei."* He then recounted his lineage, revealing himself as the censor’s great-great-grandfather. Together, they entered the mountain. The elder, leaning on his staff, strode ahead, while Wei, spurring his horse, could not keep pace. Upon reaching a stone chamber, they found two old women, who declared: *"We are your great-grandmother and great-aunt."* Both had transformed, clad in garments of woven leaves, and rejoiced: *"After generations of change, we meet our descendant today!"* Soon after, they accompanied the old man up the mountain to pay homage at the altar, then took their leave. When Wei later sought the place again, he could not find the path. Locals said: *"This elder appears once every two or three years, though none know his whereabouts."*

**Ziyu’s white hair; Changrong’s rosy complexion.**
*Continuation of the Traditions of Divine Transcendents (Xu Xian Zhuan):*
Su Ziyu was precocious and wise in his youth, well-versed in the classics. Upon reading of the Yellow Emperor’s mining of copper from Mount Shou to cast tripods and refine the elixir that enabled his ascension, he set down the scroll and sighed: *"The books of the former scholars, the essence of the Five Constants, constrained by rites and music, bound by names and teachings—how fleeting! In an instant, one’s hair turns white, the body perishes, and what use is an empty name?"* Thereafter, he embraced the radiance of the celestial ford, merging with true quiescence, inhaling and exhaling the qi of the Dao to attain harmony. Later, he encountered the Orthodox Unity Perfected One, who transmitted to him the *Crimson Script of Jade Characters, Supreme Secret of the Celestial Perfected*. Through diligent practice and devotion, he attained communion with the divine and suddenly ascended in broad daylight.

*Extensive Records (Guang Ji):*
Changrong entered Mount Da to cultivate the Dao, styling herself Changrongzi. Though over two hundred years old, her complexion remained as rosy as that of a twenty-year-old. Another account claims she was the daughter of a Shang king who consumed the roots of *penglei* (a kind of knotweed), traveling between heaven and earth, seen by mortals across generations.

**Tongyuan gazes toward the palace; Xu returns to the mountain.**
*Brush Strokes of the Ink Guest (Moke Hui Xi):*
During the Zhenzong era, a Daoist named Zi Tongyuan resided at the Chengtian Abbey in Shanzhou. Over a hundred years old, he withstood heat and cold, drank wine daily, and often went without food. When the emperor performed the Feng and Shan sacrifices, Tongyuan followed the procession from Mount Luo to the Taiyi Grotto. One day, nearing the end of his life, he summoned officials and commoners alike to expound on the essentials of life and death. At midnight, he bathed and purified himself, then faced the palace and departed. His body was as light as a cicada’s sloughed husk.

*Jottings from Chouchi (Chouchi Biji):*
Xu Ze of Donghai secluded himself on Mount Tiantai, fasting to nurture his nature. The Perfected One of the Supreme Ultimate, Xu Jun, descended and said: *"At eighty, you shall become the teacher of a king, and only then will you attain the Dao."* When Emperor Yang of the Sui heard of his reputation and summoned him, Xu remarked: *"I am now eighty-three. The summons has come—Xu Jun’s words are proven true."* He then journeyed to Yangzhou. The emperor requested instruction in the Dao, but Xu declined, citing inauspicious timing.

A few days later, he died, yet travelers along the roads all saw him walking back to the mountains, saying he had been released to return. He then distributed scriptures among his disciples and departed.

Left Chou carved an image to venerate, while Gao Yuan took leave of the emperor with lofty words.

*Supplementary Accounts of Immortals (Xianzhuan Shiyi):*
Left Chou was a minister of the Yellow Emperor. When the Yellow Emperor ascended to heaven, Chou carved a wooden likeness of him and led the feudal lords in paying homage. After seven years, the Yellow Emperor did not return. During the reign of Emperor Zhuan, Chou also ascended as an immortal and departed. It is said that the practice of carving wooden images began with him.

*Supplementary Accounts of Immortals (Xianzhuan Shiyi):*
Pu Gaoyuan, a native of Baxi, was a disciple of Master Wang Pu of the Yellow Register. His words were strange and his conduct unpredictable, so all called him mad. On the sixteenth day of the seventh month in the twelfth year of the Datong era, he rode the clouds from the summit of South Peak straight to Jinling to take leave of the emperor. Emperor Wu of Liang was seated in audience when Pu suddenly appeared in his hall. The emperor asked his purpose, and Pu replied: *"I dwelt in Baxi, attained the Dao of the Supreme Clarity, and am now returning to heaven—I have come only to bid you farewell."* With that, he flew away. At the time, Wang Xiaoji, the governor of Yizhou, reported the matter to the throne. The emperor also issued an edict inquiring into it and ordered the gentleman of the palace, Liu Xiaoxian, to compose a stele commemorating his deeds, which still exists today.

Han Yong hobbled with a staff, while Zi Zhen rode a steed.

From *Records of Marvels Gathered*: Gu Chengzhi traveled from Guang to Xiangtan when he heard of a lofty figure named Han Yong and went to pay his respects. At their first meeting, Han treated him like an old friend and said: *"You bear the qi of the Dao—might you abandon this journey?"* Gu declined, citing his pursuit of official rank. Han replied: *"This journey of yours will bring success in the examinations. One day, we shall meet again outside Banhe Gate in the capital."* With that, they parted.

The following spring, Chengzhi indeed passed the imperial exams. One day, while strolling outside Banhe Gate, he saw a man hobbling with a staff—it was Han. Remembering their earlier agreement, Chengzhi was astonished and treated him to wine at a tavern. Han then presented a poem:

*"Virtue and learning already set you apart,
The Way of immortality I now entrust to you.
If fleeting fame is truly not your aim,
Claim for yourself a wisp of cloud in leisure’s embrace."*

They parted once more, and from then on, no word of Han was ever heard again. Later, Gu ceased eating and also displayed many strange and wondrous traits.

*The Accounts of Eminent Daoists*: Master Pan Shizheng was most filial in serving his mother. When she passed, he built a hut beside her grave. The Daoist Liu Aidao saw this and marveled, saying: *"The steed of the Three Clarities—who but you shall ride it?"* He wore a jade crown of *jingdu*, wrapped in a purple-and-gold brocade robe.

*The Divine Immortals of the Wang Clan*: Lord Wang, whose courtesy name was Jingdu, wore a crimson ceremonial robe adorned with nine-colored phoenixes. Upon his head sat the Jade Crown of Soaring Divinity, and in his hand he held a nine-colored staff. He governed the Southern Vermilion Terrace, keeper of the registers of life and death. To know his name, to preserve his spirit, and to cultivate for nine years was to refine the divine herb of immortality. Then would rosy clouds descend in flight to welcome him, and his form ascend to the Jade Clarity Palace.

Also, the Lady of the Southern Pole, fourth daughter of the Queen Mother of the West, was titled the Lady of Purple Primordial Radiance. She wore a brocade robe and a skirt of kingfisher feathers. During the reign of Emperor Ping of Han, she often descended into the stone chamber of Yangluo Mountain. Her attainment of the Dao was most precious, and her literary compositions were worthy of record.

*The Accounts of Eminent Daoists*: Zhang Shizhen dwelled on Mount Nanyue, where a divine being transmitted to him the Dao of the Luminous Mirror. He instructed him in its practice, saying: *"That which illumines the principles of things is Heaven; that which illumines the forms of things is the mirror. The Dao of Heaven is clarity; the Dao of the mirror is luminosity. If one can preserve Heaven’s clarity and the mirror’s brightness, settling the mind and quieting the spirit so that inner and outer are harmonized, then the supreme Dao is achieved. But if one inwardly exhausts the will and outwardly wearies the form, leaving the mind unsettled and the spirit unclear, then the supreme Dao remains distant. As the ancients said: ‘Empty the chamber, and whiteness arises of itself; still the mind, and the Dao arrives of itself.’"* Shizhen cultivated this practice for nine years, and upon attaining the Dao, he was promoted to Perfected One. During the Tianjian era of the Liang dynasty, he ascended in broad daylight.

*The Continuation of the Accounts of Immortals*: Jin Keji was a man of Silla, learned and skilled in composition. He entered Mount Zhongnan and devoted himself to secret acts of virtue. In the twelfth month of the eleventh year of the Dazhong era of the Tang, he suddenly submitted a memorial stating: *"Your servant has received the Jade Emperor’s decree to serve as Attendant Minister of Literary Excellence. On the twenty-fifth day of the second month, I shall ascend."* Emperor Xuanzong dispatched envoys to summon him to the palace, but he firmly declined. He further requested to see the Jade Emperor’s edict, but was told that such matters were the purview of other immortals and not to be disclosed to mortals. The emperor then bestowed upon him four palace women, fragrant medicines, gold, and silks, and sent two eunuchs to attend him. On the twenty-fifth day of the second month, as spring scenery shone bright and flowers bloomed in radiant splendor, five-colored clouds filled the sky. Cranes cried, phoenixes soared, white swans played flutes, and jade wheels, feathered canopies, and jeweled banners filled the air with celestial regalia. He ascended to heaven and transcended the mortal realm, his accumulated merit perfected. His immortal traces are recorded here to forever illuminate this glorious age.

*Thus concludes the twentieth scroll of the Comprehensive Records of the Immortals of the Three Caverns.*
